Definition of Tanzih and Proof against Composition
Defines tanzih and presents three proofs against composition in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tanzih (divine transcendence) and the rational proofs that God cannot be composed of parts.

Core Idea
God cannot be composite because composition implies need, and need implies contingency.

In this passage, the author defines tanzih as negating from God anything that does not befit His majesty. He then focuses on the first aspect: negating multiplicity by composition. He provides three logical proofs demonstrating why composition is impossible for the Necessary Being. These arguments form the foundation of Islamic monotheism (tawhid).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author's concise style and logical progression.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage contains three distinct arguments against composition. Locate each one and note the reasoning used.
The first proof focuses on need, the second on the status of parts, the third on mutual need.
3
Understand the logical force
Reflect on why each proof leads to the conclusion that composition is impossible for God. Ask yourself what each argument assumes about the Necessary Being.
The proofs are independent but complementary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثاني : في التنزيهات أي سلب ما لا يليق بالواجب عنه وفيه مباحث : الأول : في نفي الكثرة عنه بحسب الأجزاء ، بأن يتركب من جزأين فأكثر وبحسب الجزئيات بأن يكون الموجود واجبين أو أكثر، واستدل على نفي التركيب، بأن كل مركب محتاج إلى الجزء الذي هو غيره، وكل محتاج إلى الغير ممكن، لأن ذاته دون ملاحظة الغير لا يكون كافياً في وجوده. وإن لم يكن ذلك الغير فاعلاً له خارجاً عنه، وبأن كل جزء منه، فإما أن يكون واجباً فيتعدد الواجب وسنبطل تعدد الواجب، أو لا فيحتاج الواجب إلى الممكن فيكون أولى بالإمكان. وبأنه إما أن يحتاج أحد الجزأين إلى الآخر فيكون ممكناً، ويلزم إمكان الواجب، أو لا فلا يلتئم منهما حقيقة واحدة، كالحجر الموضوع بجنب الإنسان.
He said: The second chapter: on the transcendent acts (tanzihāt), i.e., negating what is not fitting from the Necessary Being, and it contains investigations. The first: on negating multiplicity from Him with respect to parts, by being composed of two or more parts, and with respect to individuals, by there being two or more existent necessary beings. And he gave proof for the negation of composition: that every composite is in need of the part which is other than it, and everything in need of another is contingent, because its essence without consideration of the other is not sufficient for its existence, even if that other is not an efficient cause external to it; and that every part of it is either necessary, then the necessary would be multiple and we will refute multiplicity of the necessary, or not, then the necessary would be in need of the contingent and would be more deserving of contingency; and that either one of the two parts is in need of the other, making it contingent, and the contingency of the necessary would follow, or not, and then no single reality would be formed from them, like a stone placed next to a human.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a definition and a multi-part proof. The author first defines 'tanzih' (transcendence) as negating from God anything unbecoming. Then he introduces the first investigation: negating multiplicity both in terms of parts (composition) and individuals (multiple gods). He then focuses on composition and gives three proofs. First: every composite needs its parts; need implies contingency, so the necessary cannot be composite. Second: each part is either necessary (leading to multiple necessary beings, which is false) or contingent (then the necessary depends on the contingent). Third: the two parts either need each other (contingency) or do not (no real union, like a stone next to a person). The conclusion is that composition is impossible for the necessary being. The three arguments follow a logical order: from dependency, from the status of parts, and from mutual need.

Key Terms
تنزيه
تركيب
مركب
واجب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that these proofs reject attributes of God as distinct from essence.
Correction: The proofs only reject literal composition into parts, not the distinction of attributes; attributes are a different discussion.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does a composite being have to be contingent?
A) Because a composite has parts, and each part is contingent.
B) Because a composite needs its parts, and need implies contingency.
C) Because a composite is made of both necessary and contingent parts.
D) Because a composite cannot be the cause of its own parts.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be composed because composition entails need, and need is the mark of contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first proof says everything in need of another is contingent. You need food, air, and others every moment. If you were composite in your reliance on these, would you be anything but contingent? How does this compel you to acknowledge your absolute dependence on the Necessary Being, not just in theory, but in your daily choices?
?
If God is free from any composition and need, then He is self-sufficient. But you often act as if you are independent, building your life as if you need no one. What is one area of your life where you have been trying to be self-sufficient, and how will you surrender that illusion today?
?
The third proof compares a stone next to a human as not forming one reality. In your own life, what 'parts' are you trying to combine to create your identity? Your job, relationships, achievements—if they don't cohere into a single purpose for God, are they just like a stone next to a human? How will you re-center your life around the only One who is truly one?

First Proof Against Multiple Gods: Composition Argument
Presents the first argument against multiple gods and answers an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational argument against the possibility of multiple necessary beings and how the author defends it against the objection that shared necessity might be accidental.

Core Idea
If two beings shared the same necessity, they would be composite, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Sanusi begins his proof against multiple gods with a logical argument based on composition. He anticipates an objection that the shared attribute might be accidental, and refutes it by showing that necessity (wujūb) must be identical to the essence. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the objection begins with 'لا يقال' and the answer with 'لأنا نقول'.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The passage has a proof, an objection, and an answer. Find each part and understand how they connect.
The proof is from 'واستدل' to 'وهو محال'. The objection is from 'لا يقال' to 'لا يوجب التركب'. The answer is from 'لأنا نقول' to the end.
3
Analyze the proof's logic
Break down the proof: if two necessary beings exist, they must be distinct; distinction involves a differentiating factor; that factor is not the same as the shared factor (necessity); thus each being is composed of two parts, which is impossible for the necessary.
The key move is that composition implies contingency, and the necessary must be simple.
4
Understand the objection and answer
The objection says composition only follows if necessity is constitutive (part of the essence). The answer proves necessity is identical to the essence, not accidental, by showing that if it were accidental, the essence would cause it, leading to circular priority.
The phrase 'علة متقدمة على المعلول' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل على امتناع تعدد الواجب بوجوه : الأول : لو كان الواجب مشتركاً بين اثنين لكان بينهما تمايز لامتناع الاثنينية بدون التمايز وما به التمايز غير ما به الاشتراك ضرورة. فيلزم تركب كل من الواجبين مما به الاشتراك، وما به الامتياز وهو محال . لا يقال هذا، إنما يلزم لو كان الوجوب المشترك مقوماً، وهو ممنوع الجواز أن يكون عارضاً، والاشتراك في العارض مع الامتياز بخصوصه لا يوجب التركب. لأنا نقول وجوب الواجب نفس ماهيته. إذ لو كان عارضاً لها كان ممكناً معللاً بها، إذ لو علل بغيرها لم يكن ذاتياً ، وإذا علل بها يلزم تقدمها على نفسه . لأن العلة متقدمة على المعلول بالوجود، والوجوب . وإذا كان الوجوب نفس الماهية، كان الاشتراك فيها اشتراكاً في الماهية. والماهية مع الخصوصية مركبة قطعاً.
And he gave proof for the impossibility of multiple necessary beings by [several] aspects. The first: If the necessary was shared between two, there would necessarily be differentiation between them, because two-ness is impossible without differentiation. And that by which differentiation occurs is other than that by which sharing occurs, necessarily. So it follows that each of the two necessary beings is composed of that by which sharing occurs and that by which distinction occurs, and that is impossible. It cannot be said: this only follows if the shared necessity is constitutive (muqawwim), and it is not admitted that it could be an accident; and sharing in an accident with specific distinction does not necessitate composition. For we say: the necessity of the necessary is its very essence. For if it were an accident to it, it would be contingent, caused by the essence, because if it were caused by something else it would not be essential; and if it is caused by the essence, it would imply the essence's precedence over itself, because the cause is prior to the effect in existence. And if necessity is the very essence, then sharing in it is sharing in the essence. And the essence together with particularity is definitely composite.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a proof followed by an objection-and-answer. The author presents the first proof against multiple necessary beings: if two shared the same necessity, they would need to be differentiated; the differentiating factor is not the same as the shared factor, so each would be composed of a common essence and a differentiating accident, making them composite—impossible for the necessary. An objector argues that composition only follows if the shared necessity is constitutive (part of the essence), but it could be an accidental attribute. The author answers that necessity cannot be an accident, because if it were accidental, the essence would cause it, leading to circular priority. Therefore, necessity is identical to the essence, so sharing necessity means sharing essence, and essence with particularity yields composition.

Key Terms
تعدد الواجب
تمایز
اشتراك
وجوب
ماهية
عارض
مقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That necessity (wujūb) could be an accidental attribute separate from the essence of the necessary being.
Correction: The author proves that necessity must be identical to the essence; otherwise, it would be contingent and cause circular priority. Therefore, sharing necessity implies sharing essence, leading to composition.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that necessity (wujūb) could be an accidental attribute?
A) Because an accidental attribute would make the necessary being contingent.
B) Because if it were accidental, the essence would cause it, leading to the essence's priority over itself.
C) Because accidents can only exist in composite beings.
D) Because the Quran denies it.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against multiple necessary beings shows that any sharing of necessity would force composition, making the necessary composite—an impossibility, because necessity is the very essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God is absolutely simple and uncomposed, how would that change the way you pray to Him? Do you ever treat God as if He has parts or qualities that could be separated? Commit to one specific way you will remind yourself of His oneness tomorrow.
?
The objection in this argument sounds plausible: why can't necessity be just an accident? Where in your own life do you accept plausible-sounding excuses to avoid the full consequences of tawhid? What would it cost you to truly accept that God's essence is identical to His attributes?
?
Imagine you are arguing with someone who believes in multiple gods. After this proof, they still resist. What is the deeper resistance in your own heart against submitting to the absolute oneness of God? Identify one doubt or excuse you have used, and determine to replace it with this proof.

Second Objection & Independent Proof against Multiplicity
Handles a second objection and offers an independent proof against multiple necessary beings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to multiplicity of the necessary being and the independent proof that refutes it.

Core Idea
The particularity distinguishing two necessary beings cannot be accidental without causing dependence.

This passage presents a second objection against the possibility of multiple necessary beings. The objector suggests that the particularity (khāṣṣiyya) distinguishing two necessary beings could be an accident. The answer demonstrates that such an accident would inevitably imply dependence, which contradicts necessary existence. The author then reframes this as an independent proof: the determination (ta'ayyun) that distinguishes hypothetical multiple necessary beings must be identical to or derived from the essence, or it would entail need—both impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening phrase 'If it is said...' to grasp the objector's claim that particularity could be accidental.
This is a common line of argument: try to explain away the distinction as non-essential.
2
Trace the answer's logic
Notice how the answer breaks down the possibilities: the accident is caused by the essence, its attributes, or an external cause. Each leads to contradiction.
Pay attention to the phrase 'leading to need' — this is the key flaw.
3
Recognize the independent proof
The passage then generalizes the reasoning: if multiple necessary beings existed, their determining factor (ta'ayyun) must be either identical to the essence or caused by it, or else it would require an external cause, implying need.
The proof is structured as a disjunction (either A or B or C) leading to impossibility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Even though the text cuts off, the conclusion is that multiplicity is impossible. Consider why each option fails.
This proof reinforces tawhid through rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن تكون الخصوصية من العوارض . قلنا : لأنها تكون معللة بالماهية ، أو بما تقوم بها من الصفات وهو ينافي التعدد المفروض ، إذ الواجب لا يكون بدون تلك الخصوصية أو بأمر منفصل؛ فيلزم الاحتياج المنافي للوجوب الوجود. وهذا يصلح أن يجعل دليلاً مستقلاً. بأن يقال : لو تعدد الواجب فالتعين الذي به الامتياز إن كان نفس الماهية الواجبة أو معللاً بها أو بلازمها فلا تعدد وإن كان معللاً بأمر منفصل فلا [؟]
If it is said: Why is it not permissible that the particularity is among the accidents? We say: Because it would be caused by the essence, or by the attributes subsisting in it, and that contradicts the supposed multiplicity, for the necessary being cannot exist without that particularity; or by a separate thing, leading to need, which negates necessity of existence. And this can serve as an independent proof. For it could be said: If the necessary being is multiple, then the determination (ta'ayyun) by which distinction occurs: if it is the necessary essence itself, or caused by it, or by its necessary concomitant, then there is no multiplicity; and if it is caused by a separate thing, then [no multiplicity?] …
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by an independent proof. The objector suggests that the particularity (khāṣṣiyya) distinguishing two necessary beings could be an accident. The answer: such an accident would be caused either by the essence or its attributes, which contradicts the scenario of two independent necessary beings (since each would depend on the other's essence?), or by an external cause, leading to need—impossible for the necessary. The author then notes that this reasoning can stand as an independent proof: if two necessary beings existed, the determining factor (ta'ayyun) that distinguishes them must either be the essence itself (then no distinction), caused by it or its concomitant (still not separate), or caused externally (then need). Thus multiplicity is impossible. The text is cut off, but the conclusion is clear.

Key Terms
خصوصية
عوارض
تعين
امتياز
معلل
منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the 'determination' (ta'ayyun) is a separate entity added to the essence.
Correction: The proof shows that determination must be identical to or caused by the essence, otherwise the necessary being would depend on an external cause, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the independent proof, what happens if the determination (ta'ayyun) is caused by an external separate thing?
A) It leads to multiplicity of necessary beings.
B) It leads to need (iḥtiyāj), contradicting necessity.
C) It makes the determination an accident.
D) It proves that the essence is composite.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of multiple necessary beings is established by analyzing the determination: it must be identical to or caused by the essence, or else it implies need—a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah alone is the Necessary Being, and no other being shares that necessity, does your heart truly recognize that nothing else is worthy of ultimate reliance? In what areas of your life do you still act as if something else is independently necessary for your happiness or success?
?
The proof shows that any supposed partner would be dependent and thus imperfect. How does this truth confront the hidden shirk in your soul—the moments when you fear people more than Allah, or trust your own plans more than His decree? What one practical change can you make today to realign your trust?
?
The determination (ta'ayyun) that distinguishes beings ultimately traces back to Allah's unique necessity. When you look at your own existence and see your complete dependence on Him, does it fill you with humility and gratitude, or do you still harbor a sense of self-sufficiency? What would it mean for you to live each moment acknowledging that you are not necessary, but entirely contingent upon Him?

Why God Cannot Be Called a Body Even Metaphorically
Author states prohibition of applying 'body' to God even in a metaphorical sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'body' cannot be applied to Allah even in a metaphorical sense, based on both revelation and reason.

Core Idea
Calling Allah a body is forbidden by revelation and misleading by reason.

Sanusi begins by establishing a fundamental rule: even if someone intends a harmless meaning (like 'existing'), it is not permissible to call Allah a body. The Lawgiver has not permitted it, and reason warns that it leads to anthropomorphism and Christian Trinitarian errors. This lesson clarifies this foundational prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the two types of prohibition: sam'an (by revelation) and 'aqlan (by reason).
The author mentions both categories to cover all sources of knowledge.
2
Identify the two reasons
Underline the two reasons given: (1) the Lawgiver did not permit it, (2) it suggests anthropomorphic views and Christian doctrines. Reflect on why each is important.
The second reason shows that even metaphorical usage can lead to serious misbelief.
3
Ask: why start here?
Consider why Sanusi opens the refutation with this prohibition. It sets the stage: before examining arguments, he establishes that the very term is inappropriate.
This prevents the opponent from claiming that they use 'body' in a different sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يجزأ على ذلك، ولا على إطلاق الجسم عليه بمعنى الموجود . إما سمعاً فلعدم إذن الشارع، وإما عقلا فلإيهامه بما عليه المجسمة من كونه جسماً بالمعنى المشهور، ولما عليه النصارى من أنه جوهر واحد ثلاثة أقانيم على ما سيجيء . وأما القائلون بحقيقة الجسمية والحيز والجهة فقد بنوا مذهبهم على قضايا وهمية كاذبة تستلزمها، وعلى ظواهر آيات وأحاديث مشير بها. أما الأول : فكقولهم كل موجود فهو إما جسم أو حال في جسم والواجب يمتنع أن يكون حالاً في الجسم لامتناع احتياجه فيتعين أن يكون جسماً . وكقولهم : كل موجود إما متحيز أو حال في التحيز ويتعين كونه متحيزاً لما مر، وكقولهم : الواجب إما متصل بالعالم أو منفصل عنه ، وأياً ما كان يكون في جهة منه . وكقولهم : الواجب إما داخل في العالم فيكون متحيزاً أو خارجاً عنه فيكون في جهة منه ، ويدعون في صحة هذه المنفصلات، وتمام انحصارها الضرورة والجواب : المنع كيف وليس تركيبها عن الشيء ونقيضه، أو المساوى لنقيضه، وأطبق أكثر العقلاء على خلافها، وعلى أن الموجود إما جسم أو جسماني، أو ليس بجسم، ولا جسماني، وكذا باقي التقسيمات المذكورة، والجزم بالانحصار في القسمين، إنما هو من الأحكام الكاذبة للوهم، ودعوى الضرورة مبنية على العناد والمكابرة، أو على أن الوهميات كثيراً ما تشتبه بالأوليات .
It is not permissible to do that, nor to apply the term 'body' to Him in the sense of 'existing' — neither by revelation because the Lawgiver did not permit it, nor by reason because it suggests what the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) hold, that He is a body in the well-known sense, and also what the Christians hold, that He is one substance and three hypostases, as will come. As for those who affirm the reality of corporeality, location, and direction, they have built their doctrine on false imaginary propositions that entail them, and on apparent meanings of verses and hadiths that suggest them. As for the first: such as their saying, 'Every existent is either a body or an accident in a body, and the Necessary Existent cannot be an accident in a body because of the impossibility of His needing it, so He must be a body.' And their saying, 'Every existent is either located in space (mutahayyiz) or an accident in what is located, and He must be located for the same reason.' And their saying, 'The Necessary Existent is either connected to the world or separate from it, and in either case He would be in a direction from it.' And their saying, 'The Necessary Existent is either inside the world, in which case He would be located, or outside it, in which case He would be in a direction from it.' And they claim necessity (al-darura) for the validity of these disjunctions and their complete exhaustiveness. The answer: denial — how so? For their composition is not from a thing and its opposite, or equivalent to its opposite. Most rational people have agreed contrary to them, and on the fact that the existent is either a body or a corporeal (jismani) or is neither a body nor corporeal. And likewise the remaining mentioned divisions. And certainty of exhaustiveness in the two divisions is only among the false judgments of the imagination (al-wahm). And the claim of necessity is based on obstinacy and contrariness, or on the fact that imaginary premises (al-wahmiyyat) often resemble primary ones (al-awwaliyat).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage refutes the rational arguments of those who affirm that God is a body (jism) or has location (hayyiz) and direction (jiha). It begins by stating that it is not permissible to apply the term 'body' to God even in the sense of 'existing' (al-mawjud), because scripture does not allow it and rational consideration warns against leading to anthropomorphism. Then the author turns to the proponents of corporeality (al-qā'ilūn bi-ḥaqīqat al-jismiyya). They base their doctrine on false wahmiyya (imaginary) premises and on apparent meanings of texts. The author first presents their rational arguments (al-awwal): they claim that every existent is either a body or an accident (ḥāl) in a body; the Necessary Existent cannot be an accident, so He must be a body. Similarly, they argue that every existent is either located in space (mutahayyiz) or an accident in what is located, so God must be located. They also present dichotomies about connection to the world and interior/exterior. They claim these disjunctions are exhaustive and necessary. The author's answer (al-jawāb) is simple denial (al-manʿ). He points out that their disjunctions are not genuine contradictions (not a thing and its opposite). Moreover, most rational people (al-ʿuqalāʾ) reject them, agreeing instead that an existent can be a body, a corporeal (jismānī, i.e., something associated with a body), or neither. The claimed exhaustive division into two categories is a false judgment of the imagination (al-wahm). The claim of necessity (ḍarūra) is either obstinacy or confusion of wahmiyyāt with awwaliyyāt (self-evident truths).

Key Terms
جسم
مجسمة
سمع
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if we mean 'existing' by 'body', it is permissible to call Allah a body because the word is used metaphorically.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly prohibits even that metaphorical usage, because the Lawgiver did not allow it and because it leads to serious misunderstandings.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi say that applying 'body' to Allah even in the sense of 'existing' is not permissible by reason?
A) Because the Lawgiver forbade it
B) Because it suggests anthropomorphism and Christian doctrines
C) Because reason cannot understand such a usage
D) Because the word 'body' is always literal in Arabic

Key Takeaway
Even metaphorical application of 'body' to Allah is forbidden by revelation and reason due to its misleading implications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah, do you ever use analogies or terms that might subtly lead others to think of Him as limited or physical? What is one specific phrase you will stop using and what will you replace it with?
?
The author says even metaphorical use of 'body' is prohibited because it opens the door to error. Are there any well-meaning descriptions of Allah you have heard or used that might cause similar misunderstandings? How will you ensure your speech about Allah aligns with revelation and reason from now on?
?
Imagine you hear someone describing Allah as having a 'hand' or 'face' without proper qualification. Instead of dismissing it, how does this lesson compel you to gently correct that person using both the evidence from revelation and the rational dangers of such language?

The Anthropomorphists' Four Rational Arguments
Author presents the four rational arguments of those who affirm corporeality for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four rational arguments used by anthropomorphists to claim that God must be a body or located in space.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists reduce existence to a false dichotomy: body or accident.

After establishing the prohibition, Sanusi turns to the arguments of those who affirm corporeality (al-qā'ilūn bi-ḥaqīqat al-jismiyya). They base their claims on what appear to be rational divisions, but are actually imaginary premises. In this lesson, we will study the four arguments they present.

Guided Path
1
Read the four arguments
Read the passage containing the four 'qawluhum' statements. Identify each one and note the pattern: each is a disjunction (either X or Y) leading to a conclusion.
The arguments are similar: they claim that every existent must be either A or B, then rule out B for God, so God must be A.
2
Understand the first argument
The first: every existent is either a body or an accident. God cannot be an accident (because accidents need a subject), so God is a body.
This is the classic argument of the mujassima.
3
Understand the other arguments
The second: every existent is either located (mutahayyiz) or an accident in a location. Third: God is either connected to the world or separate, so in a direction. Fourth: God is either inside the world (located) or outside (in a direction).
All rely on the same false exhaustive dichotomy.
4
Notice the claim of necessity
At the end, the anthropomorphists claim that these disjunctions are necessary (ḍarūra) and exhaustive. Pay attention to this claim, as it will be refuted next.
The claim of necessity is central to their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يجزأ على ذلك، ولا على إطلاق الجسم عليه بمعنى الموجود . إما سمعاً فلعدم إذن الشارع، وإما عقلا فلإيهامه بما عليه المجسمة من كونه جسماً بالمعنى المشهور، ولما عليه النصارى من أنه جوهر واحد ثلاثة أقانيم على ما سيجيء . وأما القائلون بحقيقة الجسمية والحيز والجهة فقد بنوا مذهبهم على قضايا وهمية كاذبة تستلزمها، وعلى ظواهر آيات وأحاديث مشير بها. أما الأول : فكقولهم كل موجود فهو إما جسم أو حال في جسم والواجب يمتنع أن يكون حالاً في الجسم لامتناع احتياجه فيتعين أن يكون جسماً . وكقولهم : كل موجود إما متحيز أو حال في التحيز ويتعين كونه متحيزاً لما مر، وكقولهم : الواجب إما متصل بالعالم أو منفصل عنه ، وأياً ما كان يكون في جهة منه . وكقولهم : الواجب إما داخل في العالم فيكون متحيزاً أو خارجاً عنه فيكون في جهة منه ، ويدعون في صحة هذه المنفصلات، وتمام انحصارها الضرورة والجواب : المنع كيف وليس تركيبها عن الشيء ونقيضه، أو المساوى لنقيضه، وأطبق أكثر العقلاء على خلافها، وعلى أن الموجود إما جسم أو جسماني، أو ليس بجسم، ولا جسماني، وكذا باقي التقسيمات المذكورة، والجزم بالانحصار في القسمين، إنما هو من الأحكام الكاذبة للوهم، ودعوى الضرورة مبنية على العناد والمكابرة، أو على أن الوهميات كثيراً ما تشتبه بالأوليات .
It is not permissible to do that, nor to apply the term 'body' to Him in the sense of 'existing' — neither by revelation because the Lawgiver did not permit it, nor by reason because it suggests what the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) hold, that He is a body in the well-known sense, and also what the Christians hold, that He is one substance and three hypostases, as will come. As for those who affirm the reality of corporeality, location, and direction, they have built their doctrine on false imaginary propositions that entail them, and on apparent meanings of verses and hadiths that suggest them. As for the first: such as their saying, 'Every existent is either a body or an accident in a body, and the Necessary Existent cannot be an accident in a body because of the impossibility of His needing it, so He must be a body.' And their saying, 'Every existent is either located in space (mutahayyiz) or an accident in what is located, and He must be located for the same reason.' And their saying, 'The Necessary Existent is either connected to the world or separate from it, and in either case He would be in a direction from it.' And their saying, 'The Necessary Existent is either inside the world, in which case He would be located, or outside it, in which case He would be in a direction from it.' And they claim necessity (al-darura) for the validity of these disjunctions and their complete exhaustiveness. The answer: denial — how so? For their composition is not from a thing and its opposite, or equivalent to its opposite. Most rational people have agreed contrary to them, and on the fact that the existent is either a body or a corporeal (jismani) or is neither a body nor corporeal. And likewise the remaining mentioned divisions. And certainty of exhaustiveness in the two divisions is only among the false judgments of the imagination (al-wahm). And the claim of necessity is based on obstinacy and contrariness, or on the fact that imaginary premises (al-wahmiyyat) often resemble primary ones (al-awwaliyat).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage refutes the rational arguments of those who affirm that God is a body (jism) or has location (hayyiz) and direction (jiha). It begins by stating that it is not permissible to apply the term 'body' to God even in the sense of 'existing' (al-mawjud), because scripture does not allow it and rational consideration warns against leading to anthropomorphism. Then the author turns to the proponents of corporeality (al-qā'ilūn bi-ḥaqīqat al-jismiyya). They base their doctrine on false wahmiyya (imaginary) premises and on apparent meanings of texts. The author first presents their rational arguments (al-awwal): they claim that every existent is either a body or an accident (ḥāl) in a body; the Necessary Existent cannot be an accident, so He must be a body. Similarly, they argue that every existent is either located in space (mutahayyiz) or an accident in what is located, so God must be located. They also present dichotomies about connection to the world and interior/exterior. They claim these disjunctions are exhaustive and necessary. The author's answer (al-jawāb) is simple denial (al-manʿ). He points out that their disjunctions are not genuine contradictions (not a thing and its opposite). Moreover, most rational people (al-ʿuqalāʾ) reject them, agreeing instead that an existent can be a body, a corporeal (jismānī, i.e., something associated with a body), or neither. The claimed exhaustive division into two categories is a false judgment of the imagination (al-wahm). The claim of necessity (ḍarūra) is either obstinacy or confusion of wahmiyyāt with awwaliyyāt (self-evident truths).

Key Terms
جسم
حال
متحيز
جهة
منفصل
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the divisions (body/accident, inside/outside, etc.) are logically exhaustive and necessary, so the arguments are sound.
Correction: The author will show that these are not genuine contradictions (laysa tarkībuhā ʿan al-shayʾ wa naqīḍihi) and that most rational people accept a third option.

Checkpoint
How many rational arguments do the anthropomorphists present in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The anthropomorphists base their claims on four false disjunctions that they mistakenly consider necessary and exhaustive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah, do you ever subconsciously picture a form or location? What does this passage teach you about the danger of basing your concept of God on false, ‘obvious’ categories?
?
The anthropomorphists claimed necessity for their disjunctions. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that you treat as ‘obvious’ without realizing they may be based on imaginary premises? Will you examine them today?
?
This passage shows how even rational arguments can lead to error if built on false foundations. Where in your own understanding of Allah might you be relying on imagination rather than revelation and reason? What concrete step can you take to purify your tawhid?

Refutation: False Imagination and Confusion
Author refutes the anthropomorphists' arguments by denying their exhaustiveness and explaining the source of their error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the anthropomorphists' arguments by denying their exhaustiveness, showing they are not genuine contradictions, and attributing their necessity claim to false imagination (wahm) and confusion with primary truths.

Core Idea
The disjunctions are not genuine contradictions; most rational people accept a third category.

Having presented the anthropomorphists' arguments, Sanusi now delivers his refutation. He denies the exhaustiveness of their disjunctions, points out that most rational thinkers disagree with them, and explains that the claim of necessity stems from the false judgments of the imagination (wahm) or from confusing wahmiyyat with awwaliyyat (primary truths).

Guided Path
1
Read the answer
Read the passage from 'wal-jawāb al-manʿ' to the end. Notice the author's straightforward denial: 'al-manʿ' (denial).
He does not engage with each argument individually but attacks the core assumption.
2
Understand the denial
The author says: 'How so? Their composition is not from a thing and its opposite.' This means the disjunctions are not true logical contradictions; there is a third possibility.
For example, an existent can be neither body nor accident but something else (like a spirit or abstract).
3
See the alternative division
Most rational people ('āmmat al-ʿuqalā') hold that an existent is either a body (jism), a corporeal (jismānī), or neither. This refutes the exhaustive two-part division.
The category 'jismānī' covers things that are not bodies but are associated with bodies (e.g., accidents).
4
Recognize the source of error
The claim of necessity is either obstinacy (ʿinād) or confusion: imaginary premises (wahmiyyāt) are often mistaken for self-evident truths (awwaliyyāt).
The imagination (wahm) falsely judges that these divisions are necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation in your life where you assumed only two options were possible—perhaps a relationship, a career decision, or a theological question. How might a third option have changed your judgment? This lesson warns that false imagination creates false dichotomies. Commit this week to examining one binary assumption you hold and ask: is there a third category?
?
The author says imaginary premises often resemble self-evident truths. Consider a belief you hold that feels 'obviously true'—something you've never questioned. Could it be that you are mistaking a cultural assumption for a necessary truth? What would it take for you to honestly test that belief against the actual first principles affirmed by most rational people? This lesson demands intellectual humility. Are you willing to question your 'obvious' convictions today?
?
The claim of necessity (ḍarūra) is called 'obstinacy and contrariness' when baseless. Have you ever insisted on a position with the words 'It's just obvious' or 'How could anyone think otherwise?' Was that insistence truly grounded in necessary knowledge, or was it a defensive reflex? In what area of your faith or life are you being obstinate rather than open to rational correction? Ask Allah to grant you the humility to listen to truth even when it challenges your comfort.

The Two Methods of Interpreting Anthropomorphic Hadiths: Tafwid and Ta'wil
Sanusi presents two methods for reconciling anthropomorphic hadiths with rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two legitimate methods (tafwid and ta'wil) for interpreting hadiths that seem to attribute physical characteristics to God, and how they reconcile scripture with rational proofs.

Core Idea
Scripture and reason are not in conflict: either delegate the meaning to God or interpret it appropriately.

Muslim theologians have long grappled with hadiths that appear to describe God in physical terms. How can we affirm God's transcendence while accepting these texts? The great scholar al-Sanusi presents a clear answer: these texts are speculative in transmission, not certain, and they must be understood in light of decisive rational proofs. He then outlines two valid approaches: the safer path of tafwid and the wiser path of ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the problem
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the hadith example 'charity falls into the hand of the Most Merciful' and the apparent conflict with God's transcendence.
2
Identify the classification of these hadiths
Find where Sanusi classifies these texts as 'speculative auditory' (ẓanniyyāt samʿiyya) in opposition to 'certain rational' (qaṭʿiyyāt ʿaqliyya). Understand why this classification leads to the conclusion that they are not literal.
3
Understand the two methods
Focus on the two methods: tafwid (delegating the true meaning to God) and ta'wil (interpreting in a way consistent with rational proofs). Note the Quranic verse reference and the two readings.
Tafwid is considered the safer path, ta'wil the wiser path.
4
Reflect on the legitimacy of both
Consider why both methods are considered valid within Sunni theology and how they preserve both scripture and reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الصدقة تقع في كف الرحمن» إلى غير ذلك. والجواب : أنها ظنيات سمعية في معارضة قطعيات عقلية، فيقطع بأنها ليست على ظواهرها ويفوض العلم بمعانيها إلى الله تعالى اعتقاد حقيقتها، جرياً على الطريق الأسلم الموافق للوقف على إلا الله في قوله تعالى: ﴿وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله﴾. أو تأول تأويلات مناسبة موافقة لما دلت عليه الأدلة العقلية على ما ذكر في كتب التفسير، وشروح الحديث سلوكاً للطريق الأحكم الموافق للعطف في إلا الله والراسخون في العلم.
'Indeed charity falls into the hand of the Most Merciful' and so forth. The answer: they are speculative auditory (ẓanniyyāt samʿiyya) in opposition to certain rational (qaṭʿiyyāt ʿaqliyya), so it is certain that they are not upon their apparent meanings, and knowledge of their meanings is delegated to God Almighty while believing in their reality, following the safer path that agrees with stopping at 'except God' in His statement: {And none knows its interpretation except God}. Or they are interpreted with suitable interpretations that agree with what the rational proofs indicate, as mentioned in books of exegesis and commentaries on hadith, following the wiser path that agrees with the conjunction in 'except God and those firmly rooted in knowledge.'
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning hadiths that seem to attribute physical characteristics to God, such as the hadith 'charity falls into the hand of the Most Merciful.' The author first presents the objection implicitly: such texts appear problematic because they suggest bodily parts. Then he gives the answer. The answer classifies these texts as 'speculative auditory' (ẓanniyyāt samʿiyya), meaning they are transmitted but not absolutely certain in meaning, and they appear to conflict with 'certain rational' (qaṭʿiyyāt ʿaqliyya) proofs that affirm God's transcendence. Therefore, it is determined that these texts are not to be taken literally. Two methods are then proposed: (1) tafwīḍ—delegating the true meaning to God while affirming the reality of the attribute, which is the safer path (al-aslam) and corresponds to the reading that stops at 'except God' in verse 7 of Āl ʿImrān; (2) ta'wīl—interpreting the texts in a way that harmonizes with rational proofs, which is the wiser path (al-aḥkam) and corresponds to the reading that includes 'and those firmly rooted in knowledge' as knowers of interpretation. Thus the author shows that scripture and reason are not in real conflict, and two legitimate approaches exist to reconcile them.

Key Terms
ظنيات سمعية
قطعيات عقلية
تفويض
تأويل
الراسخون في العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these hadiths must be either fully literal or fully metaphorical; there is no middle ground.
Correction: The author allows both tafwid and ta'wil as valid, neither of which is a literal reading nor a metaphorical reading that denies reality.

Checkpoint
What are the two methods for handling hadiths that seem to attribute physical characteristics to God according to the author?
A) Literal interpretation and figurative interpretation
B) Tafwid (delegation) and Ta'wil (interpretation)
C) Denial and affirmation
D) Consensus and analogy

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphic hadiths are not taken literally; scholars either delegate their meaning to God (tafwid) or interpret them consistently with reason (ta'wil).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a hadith that seems to describe God in a very human way, do you feel a tension between your reason and your faith? Which of the two methods—tafwid or ta'wil—are you most naturally drawn to, and what does that say about how you handle uncertainty in your beliefs?
?
Think about a time when you were unsure about a religious matter and either rushed to a conclusion or avoided the question altogether. How can Sanusi's balanced approach—allowing both a safer path (tafwid) and a wiser path (ta'wil)—change the way you engage with difficult texts going forward?
?
Are there any attributes of God that you find hard to reconcile with your rational understanding? If so, are you willing to stop at 'except God' (tafwid) and trust that His reality is beyond your full comprehension, or do you feel the need to find an interpretation? What would make that step of trust difficult for you, and what would make it easier?

Four Connectors of Analogy from Witnessed to Absent
Enumeration of four connectors for analogy from witnessed to absent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of connectors that make analogy from the witnessed to the absent valid in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Four connectors guarantee that reasoning from creation to Creator is not arbitrary.

This lesson introduces the foundational argument of early theologians for establishing divine attributes. The argument uses analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen God, but it requires specific connectors to ensure validity. The passage below, following Imam al-Haramayn, lists these connectors and applies them to the attribute of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the structure: first a general statement, then enumeration of four connectors, then an example.
2
Identify the four connectors
List the four types of connectors mentioned: cause, condition, essence, and evidence. Understand each one through the brief definitions given.
The connectors are 'illa, sharṭ, ḥaqīqa, and dalīl.
3
Apply the example of knowledge
See how the connector 'cause' is used with the attribute of knowledge. The judgment 'being knowing' is caused by knowledge, so it applies to God as it does in the witnessed.
The same reasoning works for power, life, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا وجوه
(۱)
الأول : طريقة القدماء وهو اعتبار الغائب بالمشاهد وتقريره على ما ذكره إمام الحرمين أنه لا بد في ذلك من جامع للقطع بأنه لا يصح في الغائب الحكم بكونه جسماً محدوداً بناء على أنا لا نشاهد الفاعل إلا كذلك .
والجوامع أربعة : العلة والشرط، والحقيقة والدليل.
فإنه إذا ثبت في الشاهد كون الحكم معللاً بعلة كالعالمية بالعلم أو مشروطاً بشرط كالعالمية بالحياة، أو تقررت حقيقة في محقق ككون حقيقة العالم من قام به العلم ؛ أو دل دليل على مدلول عقلاً، كدلالة الأحداث على المحدث لزم ذلك في الغائب.
وقد ثبت في الشاهد أن حقيقة العالم من قام به العلم . وأن الحكم بكون العالم عالماً معلل بالعلم، فلزم القضاء بذلك في الغائب.
وكذا الكلام في القدرة والحياة ، وغيرهما . وهذا احتجاج على المعتزلة القائلين بصحة قياس الغائب على الشاهد عند شرائطه
We have [several] points. First: the method of the ancients, which is considering the absent by way of the witnessed. Its explanation according to what Imam al-Haramayn mentioned is that there must be a connector for certainty, because it is not valid in the absent to judge that it is a limited body based on the fact that we only observe the agent as such. The connectors are four: cause, condition, essence, and evidence. For if it is established in the witnessed that the judgment is caused by a cause—like being knowing by knowledge—or conditioned by a condition—like being knowing by life—or an essence is established in a thing—like the essence of a knower is one in whom knowledge subsists—or an evidence indicates a thing intellectually—like the indication of events for an originator—then that is necessary in the absent. It has been established in the witnessed that the essence of a knower is one in whom knowledge subsists, and that the judgment of being knowing is caused by knowledge, so the judgment is necessarily applied in the absent. Similarly for power, life, and others. This is an argument against the Mu'tazila who claim the validity of analogy from the witnessed to the absent under its conditions.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) for establishing attributes in God by the method of the early theologians (ṭarīqat al-qudamā'), which is reasoning from the witnessed (shāhid) to the absent (ghā'ib). The argument requires a connector (jāmi') that guarantees the transfer of the judgment. The author, following Imam al-Haramayn, states that there are four types of connectors: (1) cause ('illa) – e.g., knowledge causes the attribute of being knowing; (2) condition (sharṭ) – e.g., life is a condition for knowledge; (3) essence (ḥaqīqa) – e.g., the reality of a knower is one in whom knowledge subsists; (4) evidence (dalīl) – e.g., the existence of contingent events points to a creator. The passage then applies the example of knowledge: in the witnessed, the essence of a knower is one who possesses knowledge, and the judgment of being knowing is caused by knowledge, so the same applies to God. The same applies to power, life, etc. The argument is directed against the Mu'tazila who accept such analogy only under specific conditions. The passage sets up the foundation for the subsequent discussion on the reality of attributes.

Key Terms
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghā'ib)
جامع (jāmi')
علة ('illa)
شرط (sharṭ)
حقيقة (ḥaqīqa)
دليل (dalīl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that analogy from the witnessed to the absent is arbitrary or based on mere resemblance.
Correction: The Ash'ari method requires specific connectors (cause, condition, essence, evidence) to ensure the transfer of judgment is certain, not arbitrary.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, how many types of connectors (jāmi') are there for transferring a judgment from the witnessed to the absent?
A) Two: cause and condition
B) Three: cause, condition, and essence
C) Four: cause, condition, essence, and evidence
D) Five: adding proof and indication

Key Takeaway
The four connectors—cause, condition, essence, and evidence—are essential for validly transferring judgments from the witnessed world to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely affirm that God knows all things. But can you honestly say that your belief is built on a solid connector—like the cause of knowledge—rather than mere imitation? If asked, could you demonstrate using this passage's method that God must be knowing?
?
When you think about God's power or life, do you ever wonder if you are just projecting human limitations onto Him? This passage shows that the essence (and not the accident of body) transfers. Have you ever examined your own heart to see if you truly believe that God's attributes are real and not just metaphors?
?
You might use words like 'God is wise' easily, but is that belief rooted in a rigorous analogy that would satisfy the ancients? Or is it a comfortable phrase that you have never tested? This lesson demands that your faith be reasoned. Will you today take one attribute and mentally trace its connector from creation to Creator, or will you continue assuming without certainty?

The Strong Objection on Attributes' Eternity and Three Responses
Author presents a strong objection about qiyam al-ma'na bi al-ma'na and three responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the strong objection against the Ash'ari view on the eternity of attributes and the three responses offered by scholars.

Core Idea
The eternity of attributes poses a logical problem of meaning-subsisting-in-meaning.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful objection raised by opponents of the Ash'ari school regarding the eternity of God's attributes. The objection claims that if attributes are eternal, it necessitates an invalid subsistence of a meaning (the attribute) in another meaning (the essence). The author then surveys three responses from Ash'ari scholars, evaluating their strength.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage and identify the core objection: that eternal attributes imply qiyam al-ma'na bi al-ma'na.
Note that the premises are 'ilzamiyya' (argumentative) not 'tahqiqiyya' (investigative).
2
Explore the three responses
Identify the three rebuttals: (1) denying that baqa' is an additional attribute, (2) permitting qiyam al-ma'na bi al-ma'na for non-material entities, (3) avoiding describing attributes as 'baqi'.
The author criticizes the third as weak.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this debate matters for the doctrine of divine attributes and how the responses attempt to preserve tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من شبه المخالفين
قال : والقوي إلزاماً لزوم قيام المعنى بالمعنى في بقاء الصفات والدفع بأنها لا تتصف بالبقاء أو باقية ببقاء الذات أو بقاؤها نفسها ضعيف.
قال : والقوي إلزاماً يعني أن من الشبه القوية في هذا الباب، وإن كانت مقدماتها إلزامية لا تحقيقية، أنها لو كانت له صفات قديمة، لزم قيام المعنى بالمعنى لأن القديم يكون باقياً بالضرورة. وعندكم أن بقاء الشيء صفة زائدة عليه قائمة به، وأن قيام المعنى بالمعنى باطل. فمن الأصحاب من لم يجعل البقاء صفة زائدة، بل استمراراً للوجود ، ومنهم من جوز في غير المتحيز قيام المعنى بالمعنى، وإنما الممتنع قيام العرض بالعرض، لأن معناه التبعية في التحيز والعرض لا يستقل بالتحيز، فلا يتبعه غيره، بل كلاهما يتبعان الجوهر. ومنهم من امتنع عن وصف الصفات بالبقاء، فلم يقل علمه باق وقدراته باقية ، بل قال هو باق بصفاته، وهذا ضعيف جداً. لأن الدائم الموجد أزلاً وأبداً من غير طريان فناء عليه أصلاً، اتصافه بالبقاء ضروري، ولا يفيد التحرز عن التكلم به.
From the objections of the opponents. He said: And the strong one in terms of imposition is the necessitation of the subsistence of a meaning (ma'na) in a meaning (ma'na) regarding the eternity (baqa') of the attributes, and the rebuttal that they are not characterized by eternity, or are eternal by the eternity of the essence, or that their eternity is itself, is weak. He said: 'And the strong one in terms of imposition' means that among the strong objections in this chapter, even if their premises are argumentative (ilzamiyya) not investigative (tahqiqiyya), is that if attributes were eternal, then the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning would be necessitated, because the eternal (qadim) is necessarily eternal (baqi). And according to you, the eternity of a thing is an additional attribute (sifa za'ida) subsisting in it, and that the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning is invalid. Among the companions (al-ashab), some did not make eternity an additional attribute, but rather a continuation of existence; and among them, some permitted, in the case of the non-material, the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, and the only impossible thing is the subsistence of an accident ('arad) in an accident, because its meaning is dependence in occupying space (tahayyuz), and an accident does not independently occupy space, so nothing else follows it; rather both follow the substance (jawhar). And among them, some refrained from describing the attributes as eternal, so they did not say 'His knowledge is eternal' and 'His powers are eternal', but rather said 'He is eternal with His attributes', and this is very weak. For the everlasting (da'im), who is brought into existence (mujad) eternally without any attainment of annihilation upon him at all, his being characterized by eternity is necessary, and it does not benefit avoidance of speaking about it.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a strong objection raised by opponents against the Ash'ari position on the eternity (baqa') of God's attributes. The objection argues that if attributes are eternal (qadima), then this implies the subsistence of a meaning (the attribute) in another meaning (the essence), which is considered invalid. The author then lists three responses from Ash'ari scholars to rebut this objection. First, some deny that eternity (baqa') is an additional attribute (sifa za'ida); rather it is the continuation of existence itself. Second, some permit the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning for non-material entities, since the prohibition only applies to accidents (a'rad) which depend on substances for spatial occupation. Third, some avoid describing the attributes as 'eternal' altogether, saying only the essence is eternal with its attributes. The author criticizes the third response as weak, because the Everlasting (al-da'im) is necessarily characterized by eternity, and avoiding the term does not prevent the logical implication.

Key Terms
قيام المعنى بالمعنى
بقاء
صفة زائدة
عرض
جوهر
تحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qiyam al-ma'na bi al-ma'na is absolutely impossible in all cases, including for non-material entities like divine attributes.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that the impossibility is specific to accidents (a'rad) which depend on substances for spatial occupation; it does not apply to non-material entities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'strong objection' (al-qawi ilzaman)?
A) That eternal attributes imply the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning.
B) That the attributes are not eternal.
C) That the essence is eternal without attributes.
D) That the attributes are identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of divine attributes raises a logical challenge of meaning-subsisting-in-meaning, which Ash'ari scholars address through various responses, but the most robust is to deny that eternity is an additional attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the divine attributes, do you ever feel a tension between your intellect and revelation? How do you handle that tension, and what does this lesson teach you about scholarly rigor in such matters?
?
The third response avoided describing attributes as eternal to sidestep the objection, but the author called it weak. Are there beliefs you hold that you avoid examining too closely because they might raise uncomfortable questions? Will you commit to facing those questions with honesty?
?
The scholars engaged deeply with subtle logical objections. How often do you invest such intellectual effort in understanding your faith? What would it mean for you to become a person who does not shy away from difficult theological issues?

Fifth Response: Attributes Eternal by Their Own Eternity
A response, objection, and answer about self-eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that attributes are eternal by their own essence, how an objection arises, and how the distinction of aspects (ikhtilaf al-idafa) resolves it.

Core Idea
Each attribute is eternal by itself, but relations differ, preventing contradiction.

This passage presents a fifth response in the debate about divine attributes and eternity. The author argues that each attribute is eternal by an eternity that is itself (e.g., knowledge is eternal by being knowledge). An objector raises a seeming contradiction: this would make the essence knowing by what is eternity, etc. The answer uses the distinction of aspects (ikhtilaf al-idafa) to show that relations shift, avoiding impossibility. The passage also considers a further refinement questioning the claim that eternity is an additional attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Note the key terms: ikhtilaf al-idafa, jawhar, kawn.
2
Identify the core claim and objection
Find the core claim: the attribute is eternal by an eternity that is itself. Then locate the objection: this leads to the essence being knowing by what is eternity, which is impossible.
3
Understand the resolution
Examine the answer (qulnā) that uses ikhtilaf al-idafa: the essence is knowing by what is an eternity for knowledge, not by what is an eternity for itself. This removes the impossibility.
Also note the further objection about the universality of the claim that eternity is an additional attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : إن الصفة باقية ببقاء جوهر هو نفسها، والعلم مثلاً علم للذات، فيكون به عالماً وبقاء لنفسه، فيكون به باقياً، كما أن بقاء الله تعالى بقاء له، وبقاء للبقاء أيضاً، وهذا كالجسم يكون كائناً بالكون، والكون يكون كائناً بنفسه، وجاز حصول باقيين ببقاء واحد، لأن أحدهما كان قائماً بالآخر، فلم يرد إلى قيام صفة بذاتين بخلاف حصول متحركتين بحركة، وأسوديين بسواد . فإن قيل : معلوم أن الشيء إنما يكون عالماً بما هو علم، قادراً بما هو قدرة، باقياً بما هو بقاء إلى غير ذلك. وها هنا قد لزم كون الذات عالماً، وقادراً بما هو بقاء، والعلم باقياً بما هو علم، والقدرة باقية بما هو قدرة وهو محال قلنا : اختلاف الإضافة بدفع الاستحالة، فإن المستحيل هو أن يكون الشيء عالماً أو قادراً بما هو بقاء له، وباقياً بما هو علم أو قدرة له . واللازم هو أن الذات عالم أو قادر بما هو بقاء للعلم أو للقدرة، والعلم أو القدرة باق بما هو علم أو قدرة للذات . ولقائل أن يقول : فحينئذ حتى لا يبقى قولكم بقاء الباقي صفة زائدة عليه، قائمة به على إطلاقه . وأيضا إذا جاز كون بقاء العلم نفسه مع القطع بأن مفهوم البقاء ليس مفهوم العلم. فلم لا يجوز مثله في الصفات مع الذات بأن يكون عالماً بعلم نفسه، قادراً بقدرة هي نفسه، باقياً ببقاء هو نفسه إلى غير ذلك ، ولا يستلزم إلا كون الجميع واحداً بحسب الوجود لا بحسب المفهوم والاعتبار.
And among them is one who said: The attribute is eternal by an eternity of a substance (jawhar) that is itself. For example, knowledge is knowledge for the essence, so by it He is knowing, and it is an eternity for itself, so by it it is eternal. Just as the eternity of God Most High is an eternity for Him and also an eternity for the eternity. This is like the body being existent by existence (kawn), and the existence itself being existent by itself. It is permissible for two eternal things to occur by one eternity, because one of them subsists in the other, so it does not lead to the subsistence of an attribute in two essences, unlike the occurrence of two moving things by one movement, or two black things by one blackness. For if it is said: It is known that a thing is only knowing by what is knowledge, powerful by what is power, eternal by what is eternity, and so forth. Here it leads to the essence being knowing and powerful by what is eternity, and knowledge being eternal by what is knowledge, and power being eternal by what is power, and that is impossible. We say: The difference of aspect (ikhtilaf al-idafa) repels the impossibility, for the impossible is that a thing be knowing or powerful by what is an eternity for it, and eternal by what is knowledge or power for it. But the required is that the essence is knowing or powerful by what is an eternity for knowledge or power, and knowledge or power is eternal by what is knowledge or power for the essence. And one may say: Then your statement that 'the eternity of the eternal is an additional attribute subsisting in it' does not remain universally applicable. Also, if it is permissible for the eternity of knowledge itself (while it is certain that the concept of eternity is not the concept of knowledge), then why not permit the like in the attributes with the essence: that He is knowing by a knowledge that is Himself, powerful by a power that is Himself, eternal by an eternity that is Himself, and so forth? This only necessitates that all are one in terms of existence, not in terms of concept and consideration.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a fifth response: the attribute is eternal by an eternity that is itself (i.e., each attribute has its own eternity identical to itself). For example, knowledge is eternal by being knowledge, and it is also a knowledge for the essence—so the essence is knowing by it, and it is eternal by itself. This is analogous to God's eternity being both His eternity and an eternity for the eternity itself, like the existence of a body and the existence of that existence. The objector argues that this would lead to contradictions: the essence would be knowing by what is eternity, and knowledge would be eternal by what is knowledge, which seems impossible. The answer (qulnā) is that the distinction of aspects (ikhtilaf al-idafa) removes the impossibility: the essence is knowing by what is an eternity for knowledge, not by what is an eternity for itself; and knowledge is eternal by what is knowledge for the essence, not by what is knowledge for itself. An additional objection is raised: if this is allowed, then the statement that 'eternity is an additional attribute' is undermined. Furthermore, if the eternity of knowledge can be knowledge itself (since the concept differs but the existence is one), then why not allow that God is knowing by a knowledge that is His essence, powerful by a power that is His essence, etc., making them all one in existence but distinct in concept? The passage ends with this consideration, showing the flexibility of the conceptual framework.

Key Terms
اختلاف الإضافة
بقاء النفس
جوهر
كون
وجود
مفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The response implies that the essence is knowing by what is eternity (a contradiction).
Correction: The distinction of aspects (ikhtilaf al-idafa) shows that the essence is knowing by what is an eternity for knowledge, not by what is an eternity for itself; this resolves the apparent contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does the answer (qulnā) resolve the objection that the essence would be knowing by what is eternity?
A) By denying that the essence is knowing at all.
B) By asserting that the essence is knowing by what is an eternity for knowledge, not by what is an eternity for itself.
C) By claiming that knowledge and eternity are the same concept.
D) By stating that the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The argument maintains that attributes are self-eternal, and apparent contradictions are resolved by noting different relational aspects (ikhtilaf al-idafa).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage uses 'ikhtilaf al-idafa' to resolve an apparent contradiction. Think of a time when you misunderstood an attribute of Allah because you failed to distinguish the aspect of your own perception. How will you now train your mind to see the different relations in Allah's names so that your worship becomes more precise and less prone to anthropomorphic confusion?
?
The argument ends with the possibility that all attributes are one with the essence in existence. If that were true, how would it affect the way you call upon Allah by His names? Are you more attached to the distinct concepts (e.g., The All-Knowing, The All-Powerful) or to the single reality behind them? Examine your heart: do you seek the attributes or the Essence?
?
The objector’s challenge forced a deeper logical refinement. When you face difficulties in understanding Allah, are you quick to dismiss them or do you engage in disciplined theological reflection, as the scholars did? What does your response reveal about your commitment to tawhid?

The Need for a Specifier in Divine Power
First objection: divine power needs a specifier; answer denies both implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against divine power as choice and the Ash'ari response that denies infinite regress or the collapse of the proof of the Creator.

Core Idea
The will itself suffices as the specifier, preventing infinite regress and preserving the proof of the Creator.

This lesson examines the first objection to the Ash'ari view that God acts by power and will, not necessity. The opponent argues that if God's power attaches to one of two equals, it needs a specifier, leading either to infinite regress or undermining the proof of the Creator. The answer shows that the will itself provides the specification, avoiding both problems.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the objection and answer to grasp the logical structure. Identify the opponent's claims and the response.
Notice the use of 'first' to introduce the objection.
2
Identify the two implications
The opponent argues that if a specifier is needed, infinite regress follows; if not needed, the proof of the Creator collapses. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The proof of the Creator is based on the impossibility of preponderance without a specifier.
3
Analyze the answer's first denial
The answer denies that needing a specifier leads to infinite regress, because the will itself can be the specifier by its essence, as in human choice.
Compare with the alternative view that power attaches by essence.
4
Analyze the answer's second denial
The answer denies that not needing a specifier collapses the proof. It distinguishes between preponderance without specifier (meaning existence without cause) and specification of one possible without a separate motive.
The latter does not entail the former.
5
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why this argument matters: it defends both divine freedom and the cosmological argument for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: لو كان الباري تعالى فاعلاً بالقدرة والاختيار دون الإيجاب، فتعلق قدرته بأحد مقدوريه بالمتساويين، بالنظر إلى نفس القدرة دون الآخرين، افتقر إلى مرجح بنقل الكلام إلى تأثيره في ذلك المرجح، ولزم التسلسل في المرجحات، وإن لم يفتقر لزم انسداد باب ثبات الصانع، لأن مبناه على امتناع الترجح بلا مرجح، وافتقار وقوع الممكن إلى مؤثر. والجواب: منع الملازمتين. أي لا نسلم أنه لو افتقر تعلق القدرة إلى مرجح لزم التسلسل الجواز أن يكون الترجيح هو الإرادة التي تتعلق بأحد المتساويين لذاتها، كما في اختيار الجائع أحد الرغيفين، والهارب أحد الطريقين، ولا يخفى أن هذا أولى مما قال في المواقف اقتداء بالإمام أن القدرة تتعلق لذاتها. ولا نسلّم أنه لو لم يفتقر إلى مرجح لزم انسداد باب إثبات الصانع، فإن المفضي إلى ذلك جواز ترجيح الممكن بلا مرجح بمعنى تحققه بلا مؤثر، لا ترجيح القادر أحد مقدوريه بلا مرجح بمعنى تخصيصه بالإيقاع من غير داعية. ولا يلزم من جواز هذا جواز ذلك.
The first: If the Most High Creator were an agent by power and choice without necessitation, then His power's attachment to one of two equal possibles with respect to power itself without the others would need a specifier, transferring the discussion to its effect on that specifier, leading to infinite regress in specifiers. And if it did not need, it would necessitate closing the door of establishing the Maker, because its foundation is the impossibility of preponderance without a specifier, and the dependence of the occurrence of the possible on an effect. The answer: Denial of the two implications. That is, we do not grant that if the attachment of power needs a specifier, infinite regress is necessary; it is possible that the specification is the will that attaches to one of the two equals by its essence, as in the choice of the hungry person one of two loaves, and the fleeing person one of two paths. And it is not hidden that this is more fitting than what was said in al-Mawaqif following the Imam that power attaches by its essence. And we do not grant that if it did not need a specifier, the door of establishing the Maker would be closed; for what leads to that is the permissibility of a possible's preponderance without a specifier, meaning its existence without an effect, not the powerful one's specification of one of his possibles without a specifier, meaning his specifying it to bring about without a motive. And the permissibility of this does not necessitate the permissibility of that.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument of the opponent and its answer. The opponent argues: if God acts by power and choice rather than by necessitation, then when His power is attached to one of two equally possible things (without external differentiation), it requires a specifier (مرجح) to determine why one rather than the other is chosen. This specifier would itself require another specifier, leading to infinite regress. If no specifier is needed, then the proof of the Creator (which relies on the impossibility of an effect without a cause) collapses. The answer denies both implications. First, it is possible that the specifier is the eternal will (الإرادة) which by its very nature attaches to one of two equals, just as a hungry person chooses one loaf over another without a further specifier. This is superior to the claim that power itself attaches by essence. Second, the statement that 'no specifier is needed' would only collapse the proof if it meant that a possible can come into existence without any cause; but here it means the powerful one can specify one possible without an external motive, which is different. The permissibility of the latter does not entail the permissibility of the former.

Key Terms
قدرة
مرجح
إرادة
تسلسل
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the need for a specifier in divine choice leads to infinite regress or undermines the proof of the Creator.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer shows that the will itself is the specifier, stopping regress, and that not needing an external specifier for choice does not imply that possibilities can exist without any cause.

Checkpoint
What does the answer offer as the specifier in the attachment of power to one of two equals?
A) The eternal will by its essence.
B) The power itself by its essence.
C) An external cause.
D) The object's own nature.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari answer preserves divine freedom and the cosmological argument by positing the will as the internal specifier, avoiding both infinite regress and the collapse of the proof of the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s will alone suffices as a specifier, without needing any external motive, then why do you often treat your own will as if it needs endless justifications or external validations before you act? Are there decisions you've delayed because you were waiting for a 'specifier' that was already within you?
?
The answer distinguishes between an agent’s free choice (which does not require an external cause) and a possible’s existence without any cause. In your life, do you sometimes mistake your own freedom for lawlessness, thinking that a choice without an external reason is the same as a random event? How does this lesson correct that confusion?
?
When you face a genuine choice between two equally good options, do you trust that your will—guided by faith and reason—is enough to choose? Or do you fall into infinite regress, seeking more and more reasons until you are paralyzed? What would it mean for you today to act with the confidence that your will, like God’s, can be an intrinsic specifier?

The Inclinational State: Beyond Mere Belief in Benefit
The author refutes the claim that only belief in benefit motivates, citing an inclinational state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the human will involves an inclinational state distinct from the cognitive awareness of benefit.

Core Idea
We find within ourselves an inclination that is more than just knowing what's beneficial.

The author now responds to an objection that we have no awareness of any preponderant factor besides the motive understood as belief in benefit. He argues that we actually experience an inclinational state that can arise from motives or from other causes. This inclinational state, together with an extraordinary cause, provides the preponderance and specification. So the will's distinctiveness from the motive becomes evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the opponent's claim.
The opponent claims we have no awareness of any preponderant factor besides the motive as belief in benefit. Note the negation 'لا نسلم'.
3
Notice the author's counter.
The author says we find an inclinational state (حالة ميلانية) that may or may not arise from motives. This is the key refutation.
4
Understand the role of the extraordinary cause.
The inclinational state, along with the 'extraordinary cause' (السبب الغريب), brings about preponderance and specification.
5
Reflect on the conclusion.
The passage ends by asserting that the claim that will is distinct from motive is more worthy of being self-evident (ضرورية).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا نسلم أنه لا شعور لنا بمرجح سوى الداعي بمعنى اعتقاد المصلحة والمنفعة، بل نجد من أنفسنا حالة ميلانية منبعثة عن الدواعي أو غير منبعثة مع السبب الغريب في الترجيح والتخصيص، فدعوى كون الإرادة مغايرة للداعي، أجدر بأن تكون ضرورية.
Then we do not concede that we have no awareness of a preponderant factor other than the motive in the sense of belief in benefit and advantage. Rather, we find within ourselves an inclinational state, arising from motives or not arising, along with the extraordinary cause in preponderance and specification. So the claim that will is distinct from the motive is more worthy of being necessary.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents another objection-and-answer. The opponent claims that we are not aware of any preponderant factor besides the motive (الداعي) understood as belief in benefit (اعتقاد المصلحة). The author refutes this by asserting that we do find within ourselves an inclinational state (حالة ميلانية) that may arise from motives or may arise from something else, along with an extraordinary cause (السبب الغريب) that brings about preponderance and specification (الترجيح والتخصيص). This means that will involves an affective inclination that is not reducible to mere cognitive awareness of benefit. Therefore, the claim that will is distinct from the motive (الإرادة مغايرة للداعي) is more worthy of being considered self-evident (ضرورية).
