Choosing Between Equals: Will Is Not Perception of Benefit
A counter-argument using equal alternatives to disprove identity of will and benefit perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phenomenon of choosing among equal alternatives proves that the will is distinct from the perception of benefit.

Core Idea
Choosing between equals shows will is not just seeing advantage.

This lesson presents a powerful counter-argument from everyday experience. If will were simply recognizing benefit, we could never act when benefits are balanced. But we do — the thirsty person chooses one identical cup. This reveals that will is something more than perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, noting its structure as a counter-argument (معارضة). The author presents a conditional and then disproves it with examples.
Pay attention to the logical flow: if A then B, but not B, therefore not A.
2
Understand the counter-argument
The author argues: if will were perception of benefit, voluntary actions could never occur without such perception. But we observe voluntary actions without perceived benefit in cases of equal alternatives, so the premise is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the thirsty person with two identical cups of water, and the fleeing person with two equally good escape routes. In neither case is there a perceived benefit for choosing one over the other, yet a choice is made.
These examples illustrate فرص التساوي (equal opportunities).
4
Draw the conclusion
Since voluntary choice occurs without a perceived preponderant benefit, will cannot be identical to perception of benefit. Will is a distinct faculty.
This argument is foundational for understanding the nature of human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أورد بطريق المعارضة أن الإرادة لو كانت هي الشعور بما في الفعل أو الترك من المصلحة لما وقع الفعل الاختياري بدونه ، ضرورة واللازم باطل ، لأن العطشان يشرب أحد القدحين، والهارب يسلك أحد الطريقين من غير شعور بمصلحة راجحة من فعل هذا ، أو ترك ذاك عند فرص التساوي في نظر العقل .
Then he brought forward by way of counter-argument that if will were the perception of the benefit in action or omission, a voluntary action would not occur without it, necessarily. But the consequent is false, because the thirsty person drinks one of two cups, and the fleeing person takes one of two roads, without perception of a preponderant benefit in doing this or omitting that, when the alternatives are equal in the view of reason.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a counter-argument (معارضة) against the view that will is identical to perception of benefit. The author argues by necessity: if will were mere perception of benefit, then no voluntary action could occur without such perception. But this is false, because we observe cases of choice among equally balanced alternatives. For example, a thirsty person faced with two identical cups of water chooses one without perceiving any preponderant benefit in drinking that particular cup rather than the other. Similarly, a fleeing person chooses one of two roads without perceiving a greater benefit in taking that road. Since choice occurs even without perceived advantage, will must be something other than mere perception of benefit. This argument uses the principle of indifference (فرص التساوي) to disprove the identity of will and perception.

Key Terms
المعارضة
الشعور
المصلحة
الفعل الاختياري
فرص التساوي
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That will is simply the perception of benefit or advantage in an action.
Correction: The example of choosing between equal alternatives shows that will operates even without perceived benefit, so will must be a distinct faculty.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that voluntary action can occur without perception of a preponderant benefit?
A) A scholar writing a book
B) A thirsty person drinking one of two identical cups
C) A merchant buying goods
D) A worshipper praying

Key Takeaway
The example of choosing between equal alternatives demonstrates that the will is a distinct faculty from mere perception of benefit, because choice occurs even when no benefit differentiates the options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between equally good options, do you secretly insist one is better to avoid admitting your will is the real decider? What would it mean to honestly acknowledge that your will, not your reason, made the final call?
?
Think of a moment you chose one path over another with no clear reason. What did that choice reveal about who you truly are—beyond your calculations? Will you now live more aware that it is your will, not your advantages perceived, that drives your actions?
?
If your will can choose without seeing benefit, how many of your 'rational' decisions are actually rationalizations? Are you ready to stop hiding behind reasons and own the raw choices of your soul?

The Objection from the Mountain's Quaking
First objection to the vision proof and its answer distinguishing essential possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the mountain's quaking invalidates the condition for vision is answered by distinguishing between immediate and future conditions and by invoking essential possibility.

Core Idea
The condition for vision is future and essentially possible, even if the mountain quakes now.

The objector argues that since the mountain was quaking during Prophet Musa's request, the condition of stability was impossible, thus vision is impossible. The answer shows that the condition was for a future time, and even during motion, stability is essentially possible because rest can replace motion. This lesson unpacks that careful reasoning and clarifies key concepts like essential possibility and conditional condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the objector's claim: that the condition was impossible because the mountain was quaking. Then note the answer: the condition was after the sighting (future), not immediate.
Pay attention to the particle 'fa' indicating sequence.
2
Understand essential possibility
The answer introduces the concept of essential possibility: even during motion, stability is possible because rest can replace motion. Essential possibility does not change with time.
Contrast with actual possibility at a given moment.
3
Follow the sub-argument on generality
The text addresses an objection that the more general (essential possibility) would entail the more specific (future stability). The answer clarifies that the generality is conceptual, not existential.
This shows a sophisticated distinction between logical and temporal possibility.
4
Distinguish types of conditions
The final part distinguishes between a condition as mere dependency and a conditional condition (shart ta'liqi) that completes the cause and necessitates the conditioned.
This distinction is crucial for understanding why the condition for vision is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاعتراض على الوجه الثاني من طريق الاستدلال فمن وجوه: أحدها: أنا لا نسلم أنه علق الرؤية على استقرار الجبل مطلقاً أو حالة السكون ليكون ممكناً بل عقيب النظر بدلالة الفاء وهو حالة تزلزل واندكاك، ولا نسلم إمكان الاستقرار حينئذ. والجواب: أن الاستقرار حالة الحركة أيضاً ممكن بأن يحصل بدل الحركة السكون لأن الإمكان الذاتي لا يزول ولهذا صح جعله دكاً. فإنه لا يقال جعله دكاً إلا فيما يجوز أن لا يكون كذا، وإنما المحال هو اجتماع الحركة والسكون، وهذا كما أن قيام زيد حال قعوده يمكن وبالعكس واجتماعهما محال. وما يقال: إن الاستقرار مع الحركة محال إن أريد به الاجتماع فمسلم، لكن ليس هو المعلق عليه، وإن أريد المقيد بالمعية فممنوع. فإن قيل: قد جعلتم الأعم وهو الإمكان الذاتي مستلزماً للأخص وهو الاستقبالي. قلنا العموم والخصوص بينهما إنما هو بحسب المفهوم دون الوجود، لأن الممكن الذاتي ممكن أبداً، وقد يقال في الجواب إنه علقها على استقرار الجبل من حيث هو من غير قيد، وهو ممكن في نفسه ويرد عليه أنه واقع في الدنيا فيلزم وقوع الرؤية فيها، اللهم إلا أن يقال المراد استقرار الجبل من حيث هو لكن في المستقبل وعقيب النظر بدليل الفاء وإن فلا يرد السكون السابق أو اللاحق. فإن قيل: وجود الشرط لا يستلزم وجود المشروط. قلنا: ذاك في الشرط بمعنى ما يتوقف عليه الشيء ولا يكون داخلاً فيه وأما الشرط التعليقي فمعناه ما يتم به عليه العلة، وآخر ما يتوقف عليه الشيء، وما جعل بمنزلة الملزوم لما علق عليه.
As for the objection to the second aspect of the method of inference, it is from several aspects: First: We do not concede that He conditioned vision upon the stability of the mountain absolutely or a state of rest so that it might be possible, but rather [it was conditioned] after the sighting, indicated by the particle fa, and it was in a state of trembling and being crushed, and we do not concede the possibility of stability at that time. The answer: That stability even in a state of motion is possible, because rest can occur in place of motion, for essential possibility does not cease, and for this reason it is correct to call it 'dakk' (crushed). For 'dakk' is only said of something that may not be so. The impossible is only the simultaneous occurrence of motion and rest, just as Zayd's standing while sitting is possible, and vice versa, but their coexistence is impossible. And what is said: 'Stability with motion is impossible' — if what is meant is coexistence, it is conceded, but that is not what is conditioned; if what is meant is being restricted to accompaniment, it is denied. If it is said: 'You have made the more general, which is essential possibility, entail the more specific, which is future stability.' We say: The generality and specificity between them are only in terms of concept, not existence, because the essentially possible is possible forever. And it may be said in answer that He conditioned it upon the stability of the mountain in itself without qualification, and that is possible in itself. But it is objected that it [the mountain's stability] actually occurs in this world, so the occurrence of vision in this world would be necessary. However, it may be said that what is meant is the stability of the mountain in itself but in the future and after the sighting, as indicated by the particle fa, so the previous or subsequent rest does not apply. If it is said: 'The existence of the condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned.' We say: That is regarding the condition in the sense of that on which a thing depends and is not part of it. As for the conditional condition (al-sharṭ al-ta‘līqī), its meaning is that by which the cause is completed, and the last thing on which a thing depends, and what is made to be like the necessitated for what is conditioned upon it.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage handles the first objection against the proof for the possibility of vision. The objector claims that the verse conditions vision on the mountain's stability, but the mountain was quaking, so the condition was impossible, thus vision is impossible. The answer distinguishes: the condition was not stability in a state of rest but after the sighting (i.e., in the future). Even during motion, stability is essentially possible because rest can replace motion; the impossible is only simultaneous coexistence. The discussion further clarifies that essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) is perpetual and does not change with time; thus the condition remains possible. A sophisticated objection about the condition not necessitating the conditioned is answered by distinguishing between a dependent condition (sharṭ bi-ma‘nā mā yatawaqqaf ‘alayhi al-shay’) and a conditional condition (sharṭ ta‘līqī) which is the final completing cause that does necessitate.

Key Terms
استقرار (istiqrār)
إمكان ذاتي (imkān dhātī)
شرط تعليقي (sharṭ ta‘līqī)
دك (dakk)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mountain's quaking at the moment of request makes the condition of stability impossible, thus vision is impossible.
Correction: The condition is future, and essential possibility means stability is possible even if currently quaking; the objector confuses immediate actuality with future potential.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what makes the condition of the mountain's stability possible despite its quaking?
A) The mountain was actually stable at the moment
B) The condition was for a future time after the sighting
C) The mountain's quaking was not real
D) Vision does not require a condition

Key Takeaway
The mountain's stability is a future condition, and essential possibility ensures it remains valid even during motion, so the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own desire to see Allah in the Hereafter, are you allowing doubts based on apparent impossibilities (like the mountain's quaking) to weaken your hope? How can you affirm the essential possibility of what Allah has promised, even when circumstances seem contrary?
?
The passage distinguishes between a condition that merely accompanies and one that completes the cause. In your daily worship, are you treating the conditions of prayer (like wudu) as mere formalities or as the very means that complete your connection to Allah?
?
The objector insisted on an immediate impossibility. Are there areas of your life where you have prematurely declared something impossible for Allah to bring about, forgetting that His timing and conditions are not limited by your immediate perception? What specific situation right now requires you to trust in His future promise rather than your current assessment?

Second Objection: Irrelevance of Intent to Prove Possibility
Objection and refutation about the intent of the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection to the proof of vision is refuted by showing that possibility is logically implied regardless of the objector's intent.

Core Idea
Intent does not cancel logical entailment: possibility follows from a conditional with a possible condition.

The second objection claims the proof's aim was only to show non-occurrence, not to establish possibility. However, the refutation demonstrates that any conditional argument with a possible condition necessarily implies the possibility of the conditioned. Thus, even if the objector did not intend to prove possibility, the logic of his own statement does.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection: the aim is not to prove possibility or impossibility, but to show that vision did not occur because its condition did not occur.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation states that the claimant of impossibility necessarily implies possibility, whether intended or not, and that possibility has been established.
Notice that the refutation does not rely on the objector's intent but on logical entailment.
3
Connect to the broader argument
See how this defends the proof for vision's possibility: even objections that aim to avoid the issue of possibility end up affirming it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن ليس المقصد ههنا إلى إثبات إمكان الرؤية أو امتناعها. بل إلى إثبات أنها لم تقع لعدم وقوع المعلق عليه. ورد بأن المدعي لزوم الإمكان قصد أو لم يقصد وقد ثبت.
Second: That the aim here is not to prove the possibility or impossibility of vision, but rather to prove that it did not occur due to the non-occurrence of that upon which it is conditioned. And it is refuted by saying that the claimant [of impossibility] necessarily implies possibility, whether he intended it or not, and it has been established.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: the verse's purpose was only to show that vision did not happen (because its condition did not occur), not to prove its possibility or impossibility. The refutation is straightforward: even if the objector did not intend to prove possibility, his argument logically entails that the condition was possible (since it was stipulated), and thus the conditioned (vision) is also possible. Logical entailment does not depend on intent.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
امتناع (imtinā‘)
معلق عليه (mu‘allaq ‘alayhi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the objector's aim was only to prove non-occurrence, then the question of possibility is irrelevant.
Correction: The refutation shows that any conditional statement with a possible condition logically implies the possibility of the conditioned, so the objector's argument inevitably entails possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the refutation of the second objection?
A) The objector's intent is irrelevant; possibility is logically implied
B) The verse explicitly states vision is impossible
C) The condition was impossible, so no implication occurs
D) The proof was about miracles, not possibility

Key Takeaway
Even if an argument does not explicitly aim to prove possibility, if it relies on a conditional with a possible condition, possibility is logically entailed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make an argument about religion, do you ever assume that your intention exempts you from the logical consequences of your premises? How has this lesson exposed a flaw in that assumption? What will you change in how you reason about matters of faith?

?

Third Objection: Non-Occurrence Does Not Prove Impossibility
Objection: non-occurrence of condition implies eternal impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that non-occurrence of a condition entails eternal impossibility is logically corrupt.

Core Idea
Non-occurrence does not prove impossibility; possibility is about essence, not actuality.

This lesson presents the third objection against the possibility of vision of Allah. The objection tries to use the non-occurrence of a condition to prove that the conditioned is impossible forever. However, as we will see, this reasoning is fallacious. The author dismisses it as 'extremely corrupt,' and you will learn why.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage once and its English translation. Let the author's words set the context.
Notice the structure: premise, conclusion, evaluation.
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the condition (mountain's stability), the conditioned (vision), and the claim of equal times. See how the objector concludes impossibility.
3
Grasp the corruption
Realize why the author calls it extremely corrupt: non-occurrence of a condition does not make the conditioned essentially impossible; it just didn't happen.
This distinguishes actuality from possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: أنه لما لم يوجد الشرط لم يوجد المشروط، وهو الرؤية في المستقبل فانتفت أبداً لتساوي الأزمنة، فكانت محالاً، وهذا في غاية الفساد.
Third: That since the condition did not exist, the conditioned did not exist, and it is vision in the future; so it is negated forever because times are equal, so it is impossible. And this is extremely corrupt.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This lesson presents the third objection, which argues that because the condition (the mountain's stability) never occurred, the conditioned (vision) never occurred. Since times are equivalent, vision is impossible for all eternity. The author dismisses this reasoning as 'extremely corrupt' (fī ghāyat al-fasād). The corruption lies in assuming that non-occurrence in one time implies impossibility for all times; a thing may be possible but simply not occur due to the condition not being fulfilled. The answer is not elaborated here, but the judgment is clear.

Key Terms
شرط (sharṭ)
مشروط (mashrūṭ)
فساد (fasād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Extremely corrupt
B) Sound and valid
C) Needs further evidence
D) Acceptable but weak

Key Takeaway
Non-occurrence of a condition does not make the conditioned impossible forever; possibility is determined by essence, not by actual events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a long-unanswered dua, do you ever quietly assume that Allah will never grant it? How does this lesson's distinction between 'not yet' and 'impossible' challenge that assumption, and what will you do the next time that thought arises?

This lesson shows that missing a condition does not make a possible thing impossible. Is there an area in your life where you have stopped hoping because a condition hasn't been met? Will you now examine whether that thing is truly impossible in itself or just waiting for its condition—and will you renew your hope in Allah's power?

Fourth Objection: Condition and Possibility
Argues that conditional statements may intend to deny the conditioned, not affirm possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection regarding conditional statements and the role of contextual indications in determining whether possibility is implied.

Core Idea
Context can change the meaning of a conditional, but the Qur'anic context here points to hope, not despair.

This lesson presents the fourth objection raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah. They argue that a conditional statement on a possible thing only indicates possibility if the speaker intends to affirm the occurrence of the conditioned. But in the verse under discussion, the context suggests despair, so no possibility is implied. The refutation counters that the verse actually indicates hope more than despair, supported by the conditional structure itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the two conditions the objector sets for a conditional to imply possibility.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation argues that the verse's conditional structure indicates hope (iṭmā‘), not despair. Notice how the refutation relies on the linguistic form of the conditional.
The refutation is brief but points to a forthcoming detailed discussion of contextual indications.
3
Reflect on the role of context
Consider how the objector uses context to negate possibility, while the author uses it to affirm hope. This shows how context can be interpreted differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها أن التعليق بالجائز إنما يدل على الجواز إذا كان القصد إلى وقوع المشروط عند وقوع الشرط. وأما إذا كان القصد إلى الإقناط الكلي عن وجود المشروط بشهادة القرائن كما في هذه الآية فلا. ورد بأن الآية على الإطماع أدل منها على الإقناط، وسيجيء الكلام على القرائن.
Fourth: That conditioning upon a possible thing only indicates possibility if the intent is the occurrence of the conditioned upon the occurrence of the condition. But if the intent is total despair of the existence of the conditioned, as evidenced by the contextual indications (qarā’in) as in this verse, then it does not [indicate possibility]. And it is refuted that the verse indicates hope (iṭmā‘) more than it indicates despair (iqnāṭ), and discussion on the contextual indications will come.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This lesson presents the fourth objection: conditional statements on possible things only show possibility if the speaker intends to affirm that the conditioned will occur when the condition occurs. But if the context shows the speaker intends to create total despair of the conditioned ever occurring (as argued for this verse), then no possibility is implied. The refutation argues that the verse's context actually indicates hope (iṭmā‘) rather than despair; the conditional phrasing gives hope that if the condition were to happen, vision would occur, and the lack of the condition does not negate possibility. This is supported by the conditional structure itself.

Key Terms

إقناط (iqnāṭ)
إطماع (iṭmā‘)
قرائن (qarā’in)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any conditional clause with a possible condition always affirms the possibility of the conditioned, regardless of context.
Correction: The objector argues context can change meaning, but the author shows that the context in this verse actually favors possibility.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what does the verse indicate more than it indicates despair?
A) Anger
B) Hope
C) Indifference
D) Necessity

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection is refuted by showing that the verse's conditional structure indicates hope, not despair, so possibility is implied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a conditional promise from Allah (e.g., 'If you are grateful, I will increase you'), do you instinctively focus on the possibility of fulfillment or on the obstacles that make it seem impossible? What qarā’in in your life lead you to iqnāṭ rather than iṭmā‘?
?
The objector claimed that context can turn a conditional into a statement of despair. Have you ever twisted a clear promise of Allah into a source of hopelessness by focusing on your own shortcomings? How would your relationship with Him change if you consistently read His conditionals as invitations of hope?
?
This lesson shows that the verse's wording itself indicates hope more than despair. Are you willing to trust the apparent meaning of Allah's words over the negative interpretations your mind imposes? What specific verse will you commit to reading with iṭmā‘ today?

First Objection: Correctness as Relational
Al-Taftazani quotes and refutes the first objection that correctness is relational and needs no real cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first objection against the proof of vision's cause fails and how Imam al-Haramayn's reinterpretation of 'cause' resolves it.

Core Idea
Correctness is relational, but vision requires a real existent object.

Al-Taftazani begins addressing objections to the previous proof that vision requires a cause. He first quotes Imam al-Haramayn's general reframing of the term 'cause', then presents the first objection that correctness is merely relational. This passage shows how quickly the discussion moves from abstract definitions to concrete theological concerns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice how the objection claims that correctness (sihha) is nothing but possibility (imkan), which is a relational concept (i'tibari) and thus does not need an existing cause.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يفتقر إلى علة موجودة' – this is the core of the objection.
2
Understand the key terms
Familiarize yourself with the terms 'imkan' (possibility), 'i'tibari' (relational), and 'huduth' (origination). All of these are mental considerations, not external realities.
Recall that in Islamic theology, 'i'tibari' concepts have no independent existence in the external world.
3
Analyze the refutation
See how the refutation (wajh al-indifa') argues that something with no external reality cannot be the object of vision. This forces the opponent to admit that the cause must be something real.
The key phrase is 'ما لا تحقق له في الأعيان لا يصلح متعلقاً للرؤية' – it establishes the necessity of external existence for vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه بوجوه يندفع أكثرها بما دل عليه كلام إمام الحرمين من أن المراد بالعلة ها هنا ما يصلح متعلقاً بالرؤية لا المؤثر في الصحة على ما فهمه الأكثرون. فالاعتراض الأول : أن الصحة معناها الإمكان وهو أمر اعتباري لا يفتقر إلى علة موجودة بل يكفيه الحدوث الذي هو أيضاً اعتباري ووجه اندفاعه أن ما لا تحقق له في الأعيان لا يصلح متعلقاً للرؤية بالضرورة.
And it was objected to with aspects, most of which are repelled by what the words of Imam al-Haramayn indicate: that what is meant by 'cause' here is what is fit to be the object of vision, not the effect on correctness, as most understood. So the first objection: that correctness (al-sihha) means possibility (al-imkan), which is a relational matter (amr i'tibari) that does not need an existing cause; rather, origination (al-huduth) suffices for it, which is also relational. And the aspect of its repulsion is that what has no realization in external entities cannot be fit as an object of vision by necessity.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage introduces a series of objections against the preceding proof, along with a general response from Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). The general point is that the term 'cause' in the proof should be understood as that which is fit to be the object of vision (mutallaq li-l-ru'ya), not as the efficient cause (mu'aththir) that brings about correctness—which is how most understood it. This reframes the discussion. The first objection claims that correctness (sihha) of vision is merely possibility (imkan), which is a relational concept (i'tibari) and thus does not require an existing cause; origination (huduth) suffices, and origination is also relational. The reply (wajh indifa') is that something with no external reality (tahqaq fi'l-a'yan) cannot be the object of vision: vision requires an actually existing object. Therefore, the cause must be something real, not merely relational.

Key Terms

الصحة
الإمكان
الاعتباري
الحدوث
متعلق الرؤية
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'cause' here means efficient cause (mu'aththir), as most understood it.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn clarifies that 'cause' in this context refers to what is fit to be the object of vision, not an efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to the general response from Imam al-Haramayn, what does 'cause' (al-illa) refer to in this context?
A) The efficient cause that produces correctness
B) The quality that makes the object fit to be seen
C) The state of the viewer
D) The divine attribute of power

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because vision requires an actually existing object, not a mere relational concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson teaches that vision requires a real object. What in your life are you pretending to see clearly, but in fact you are only dealing with a relational concept of it? Can you commit to seeking the real thing?

The Second Objection: Possibility as Common Cause Refuted
Second objection and its refutation regarding possibility as common cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility (imkan) cannot serve as the common cause for vision and how the refutation relies on the relational nature of possibility and the absurd consequence of seeing non-existent things.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental concept, not an external reality—it cannot be the cause of vision.

The second objection argues that possibility is as common as existence, so why not take it as the cause? The refutation shows that possibility is merely relational and has no external existence. Moreover, if possibility were the cause, even non-existent objects would be seeable—an obvious falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is stated first, followed by the refutation.
The refutation has two distinct parts: the nature of possibility and the consequence of applying it.
2
Understand why possibility is rejected
Focus on the first part of the refutation: possibility is i'tibari (relational) and has no external reality. Why does this prevent vision from attaching to it?
Vision requires a real object; a mere concept cannot be seen.
3
Consider the absurd consequence
The second part of the refutation: non-existent things are described as possible. If possibility were the cause, we could see non-existents, which is necessarily false. This seals the argument.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لا حصر للمشترك بينهما في الحدوث والوجود، فإن الإمكان أيضاً مشترك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هو العلة، ووجه اندفاعه أن الإمكان اعتباري لا تحقق له في الخارج، فلا يمكن تعلق الرؤية به. كيف والمعدوم متصف بالإمكان فيلزم أن تصح رؤيته وهو باطل بالضرورة.
The second: that there is no exclusivity of the common factor between them to origination and existence, for possibility (al-imkan) is also common; so why is it not permissible that it be the cause? And the aspect of its repulsion is that possibility is relational, having no realization in the external world; thus vision cannot be attached to it. Moreover, the non-existent is described as possible, so the correctness of seeing it would follow, which is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This is the second objection. It argues that the common factor between substance and accident is not limited to origination (huduth) and existence (wujud); possibility (imkan) is also a common feature, so why can't possibility be the cause enabling vision? The reply is twofold: first, possibility is a relational concept (i'tibari) with no external reality (tahqaq fi'l-kharij), so vision cannot be attached (ta'alluq) to it. Second, if possibility sufficed, then even non-existent things (ma'dum) are described as possible; that would entail that seeing non-existent things is valid, which is necessarily absurd. Thus, possibility is not a valid cause.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الاعتباري
الخارج
المعدوم
تعلق الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility can serve as the common cause for vision because it is a shared attribute.
Correction: Possibility is a relational concept with no external existence, and if it were the cause, non-existents would be seeable—an absurdity.

Checkpoint
Why is possibility (al-imkan) rejected as the common cause for vision?
A) It is not common between substance and accident
B) It is relational and would allow seeing non-existent things
C) It is not shared by the Necessary Being
D) It is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
Possibility is rejected as a common cause because it is relational and would absurdly allow seeing non-existent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider a fear or worry you have about the future—something you think is 'possible' and affects your decisions. According to this lesson, possibility is merely a mental concept with no external reality. Does that reveal that your fear may be rooted in an illusion? What would it look like to base your actions only on what is truly real and present, rather than on mere possibility?
?
The lesson argues that if possibility were a real cause, then non-existents would be visible—which is absurd. Have you ever treated a 'what-if' scenario as if it were already real, shaping your emotions or choices around it? When you catch yourself doing that, what one step can you take to return your attention to what actually exists before you?
?
You believe in the possibility of Jannah or the punishment of the grave—but the author says possibility causes nothing. Can your belief in the unseen be sustained by mere 'possibility,' or must it be anchored in the revealed truth of the Qur'an and Sunnah? What one action today will demonstrate that your vision is attached to what is truly real—Allah's promise—and not to empty possibility?

Third Objection: Different Causes for Different Objects and Its Refutation
The third objection argues each kind could have its own cause, refuted by showing vision attaches to common essence, not particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third objection fails because vision attaches to a shared identity, not to the specific categories of substance or accident.

Core Idea
Vision targets shared identity, not substance or accident.

The third objection attempts to break the argument for a common cause by suggesting each category could have its own cause. The refutation, however, reveals a deeper insight: the object of vision is not the particular nature of substance or accident but a shared identity. This passage clarifies the true object of perception and thereby saves the principle of a single cause for the possibility of seeing both.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand why the objector thinks separate causes are possible—because seeing a substance is not like seeing an accident, and even if similar, one type can have multiple causes.
Note the two sub-arguments: (1) difference in kinds, (2) same type can have multiple causes.
2
Examine the refutation's key move
The reply argues that the object of vision is not the particularity of substance or accident, but a shared identity (al-huwiyya al-mushtaraka). This is proven by our ability to perceive things without knowing their category.
3
Reflect on the example of Zayd
Consider the example of seeing Zayd: we see him as a single identity without analyzing his constituent substances and accidents. This shows that vision attaches to what is common, not to the specific categories.
This example grounds the abstract argument in everyday experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن صحة رؤية الجوهر لا تماثل صحة رؤية العرض. إذ لا يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر! فلم لا يجوز أن يعلل كل منهما بعلة على الانفراد . ولو سلم تماثلهما فالواحد النوعي قد يعلل بعلتين مختلفتين كالحرارة بالشمس والنار، فلا يلزم أن يكون له علة مشتركة، ووجه اندفاعه أن متعلق الرؤية لا يجوز أن يكون من خصوصيات الجوهرية أو العرضية، بل يجب أن يكون مما يشتركان فيه للقطع بأنا قد نرى الشيء وندرك أن له هوية ما من غير أن ندرك كونه جوهراً أو عرضاً، فضلاً عن أن ندرك ما هو زيادة خصوصية لأحدهما ككونه إنساناً أو فرساً سواداً أو خضرة، بل ربما قد نرى زيداً بأن يتعلق رؤية واحدة بهويته من غير تفصيل لما فيه من الجواهر والأعراض، ثم قد نفصله إلى ما له من تفاصيل الجواهر والأعراض. وقد نغفل عن التفاصيل بحيث لا نعلمها عندما سئلنا عنها، وإن استقصينا في التأمل، فعلم أن ما يتعلق به الرؤية هو الهوية المشتركة لا الخصوصيات التي بها الافتراق وهذا معنى كون علته صحة الرؤية مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض.
The third: that the correctness of seeing a substance is not similar to the correctness of seeing an accident. For one does not take the place of the other; so why is it not permissible that each be caused by a separate cause? And even if their similarity is granted, a specific unity may be caused by two different causes, like heat by the sun and fire, so it does not follow that it must have a common cause. And the aspect of its repulsion is that the object of vision cannot be among the particularities of substantiality or accidentally; rather, it must be something in which they share, because we are certain that we sometimes see a thing and perceive that it has some identity (huwiyya) without perceiving whether it is a substance or accident, let alone perceiving what is a further particularity of one of them, such as being a human or horse, black or green. Indeed, we may see Zayd in that a single vision attaches to his identity without detailing what substances and accidents are in him, then we later detail him into the details of substances and accidents. And we may be oblivious to the details such that we do not know them when asked, even if we investigate fully through reflection. Thus it is known that what vision attaches to is the shared identity (al-huwiyya al-mushtaraka), not the particularities by which they differ. And this is the meaning of its cause being common between substance and accident.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its reply. The objector argues that the correctness of seeing a substance is not the same as that of seeing an accident; they are different, so each could have its own separate cause. Even if they were similar, the same type of effect can have different causes (like heat from sun or fire), so a common cause is unnecessary. The reply focuses on the object of vision (mutallaq al-ru'ya). It argues that what we see is not the particularity of being a substance or accident; we can perceive a thing without knowing whether it is a substance or accident. For example, we see a person (Zayd) as a single identity without analyzing the substances and accidents composing him. We may later learn the details, but initially we are oblivious. Therefore, the object of vision is the shared identity (al-huwiyya al-mushtaraka), not the differentiating particularities. Hence, the cause of the possibility of vision must be something common, not particular. This refutes the need for separate causes.

Key Terms
تماثل
علة مشتركة
متعلق الرؤية
الهوية المشتركة
الجوهرية
العرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the vision of each category (substance and accident) requires a distinct cause because they are different kinds of objects.
Correction: The author shows that vision is of the shared identity, not the category, so the cause of the possibility of vision is common to both.

Checkpoint
What does the reply to the third objection identify as the true object of vision?
A) The particular substance
B) The particular accident
C) The shared identity (al-huwiyya al-mushtaraka)
D) The combination of both

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing substances and accidents has a single cause because vision targets what is common between them: their shared identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When was the last time you acted on that common cause? Write down one principle that should govern all your actions, and commit to reviewing it before your next decision.

Fourth Objection: Conditions or Hindrances for Vision
Objection and refutation regarding conditions for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why conditions or hindrances related to the particularities of substance, accident, or necessity do not affect the intrinsic possibility of seeing God, only its actual realization.

Core Idea
Possibility of vision is based on existence; conditions only affect actual occurrence.

The fourth objection attempts to undermine the previous argument by introducing the idea that the specific nature of God (necessary being) might actually be a hindrance to vision, rather than a neutral factor. The response draws a crucial distinction between the theoretical correctness of vision and its concrete realization. Let's examine how the objection is formulated and why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: even if existence is common to all things, the particularity of substance/accident might be a condition for vision, and the particularity of the Necessary might be a hindrance. This prevents the conclusion that God is visible.
Note that the objection grants the earlier premise but adds a nuance.
2
Identify the key distinction in the reply
The reply distinguishes between the correctness (sihha) of vision and its actual realization (tahaqquq). Find where this distinction is made in the text.
This distinction is the core of the refutation.
3
Apply the distinction
Ask yourself: why does the distinction undermine the objection? Conditions or hindrances only affect whether vision actually occurs, not whether it is intrinsically possible.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this argument protects the possibility of seeing God from being denied by accidental factors. It shows that theological arguments must focus on essential rational grounds, not contingent circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن بعد ثبوت كون الوجود هو العلة، وكونه مشتركاً بين الجوهر والعرض وبين الواجب لا يلزم من صحة رؤيتهما صحة رؤيته لجواز أن تكون خصوصية الجوهرية أو العرضية شرطاً لها أو خصوصية الواجبية مانعاً عنها. ووجه اندفاعه أن صحة رؤيته الشيء الذي له الوجود الذي هو المتعلق للرؤية ضرورية بل لا معنى لصحة رؤيته إلا ذلك، ثم الشرطية أو المانعية إنما يتصور لتحقق الرؤية لا لصحتها.
The fourth: that after establishing that existence is the cause, and that it is common between substance and accident and between the Necessary, it does not follow from the correctness of seeing them the correctness of seeing Him, because it is possible that the particularity of substantiality or accidentally is a condition for it, or the particularity of necessity (al-wajibiyya) is a hindrance from it. And the aspect of its repulsion is that the correctness of seeing the thing that has existence, which is the object of vision, is necessary; indeed, there is no meaning to the correctness of seeing it except that. Then conditionality or hindrance is only conceivable for the actual realization (tahaqquq) of vision, not for its correctness (sihha).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This is the fourth objection. Even if existence is the common cause, it does not automatically mean that seeing God is possible, because the particularity of being a substance or accident might be a necessary condition (shart) for the possibility of vision, while the particularity of being necessary (al-wajibiyya) might be a hindrance (mani') that prevents it. The reply distinguishes between the correctness (sihha) of vision and its actual realization (tahaqquq). The correctness of seeing something that has existence is necessary; the meaning of 'correctness of vision' is simply that the object has existence, which makes it theoretically perceptible. Conditions or hindrances only affect whether the vision actually occurs (tahaqquq), not whether it is possible (sihha). Therefore, even if substantiality were a condition for actual vision—which it is not—it would not undermine the possibility grounded in existence. Similarly, the necessary nature of God does not negate the possibility of seeing Him; it might affect actual occurrence but not intrinsic possibility.

Key Terms
شرط
مانع
خصوصية الجوهرية
خصوصية الواجبية
تحقق الرؤية
صحة الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's necessary nature is a hindrance, then seeing Him is impossible overall.
Correction: The hindrance only concerns actual realization, not the intrinsic possibility. The possibility is grounded solely in existence, which God possesses.

Checkpoint
According to the reply to the fourth objection, what do conditions like substantiality or accidentally affect?
A) The possibility (sihha) of vision
B) The actual occurrence (tahaqquq) of vision
C) The existence of the object
D) The power of the viewer

Key Takeaway
Conditions or hindrances affect only the actual occurrence of vision, not its intrinsic possibility, which is based solely on existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you confused the possibility of something (like knowing God) with its actual realization in your life? What condition have you mistakenly treated as a barrier to possibility rather than just to actual occurrence?
?
The author insists that existence alone guarantees the correctness of vision. What does this teach you about God's generosity and the nature of His creation? Are you living as if His existence alone is enough for your connection with Him, or do you add hidden conditions?
?
If hindrances only affect actual realization, not possibility, then what does that say about your excuses for not seeking vision of God in this life? Which 'hindrance' have you been hiding behind, and will you now treat it as a challenge to actualize rather than an excuse to deny possibility?

Why Possibility Cannot Be the Cause of Visibility
Refutes the claim that possibility could be the common cause of visibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of possibility (imkan) cannot serve as the common cause of visibility, because it is a mental concept (i'tibari) lacking external existence.

Core Idea
Only real attributes can cause visibility; mental constructs cannot.

The second objection against the claim that existence is the cause of visibility introduces possibility (imkan) as another common attribute. The author refutes this by showing that possibility is purely mental, not real, and therefore cannot ground actual vision. This lesson walks through the objection and its decisive response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's challenge: why can't possibility be the cause since it is also common? Then see the author's reply.
The objection hinges on the idea that commonality alone is insufficient.
2
Understand 'i'tibari'
The key repelling point is that possibility is i'tibari (mental) with no external existence. Understand why a mental attribute cannot be the cause of a real event like seeing.
This is a central concept in kalam: the distinction between real and mental attributes.
3
Consider the reductio about the non-existent
The author strengthens the argument: the non-existent is described as possible, but we cannot see it. This proves that possibility cannot be the cause of visibility.
This is a clear logical proof from absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لا حصر للمشترك بينهما في الحدوث والوجود، فإن الإمكان أيضاً مشترك فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هو العلة، ووجه اندفاعه أن الإمكان اعتباري لا تحقق له في الخارج، فلا يمكن تعلق الرؤية به. كيف والمعدوم متصف بالإمكان فيلزم أن تصح رؤيته وهو باطل بالضرورة.
The second [objection]: that the common [attribute] between them is not restricted to origination and existence, for possibility is also common, so why is it not permissible that it be the cause? And the repelling of it is that possibility is mental (i'tibari) with no realization in the external, so vision cannot attach to it. How [could it]? For the non-existent is described by possibility, yet it would entail that its vision be valid, and that is necessarily false.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the claim that existence is the common cause of visibility. The objector argues that if commonality is enough, then possibility (al-imkan) is also common between substance and accident. The answer repels this by stating that possibility is a mental construct (amr i'tibari) with no external reality; vision (ru'ya) requires an actual external object. Moreover, a non-existent thing (ma'dum) can be described as possible, but we cannot see it, which proves that possibility cannot be the cause of visibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commonality among attributes is sufficient to make any one of them the cause of a phenomenon like visibility.
Correction: Only attributes that are real (exist externally) can be causes. Possibility is a mental construct, so it cannot be a real cause.

B) Because possibility is a mental concept with no external reality.

Key Takeaway

Third Objection: Separate Causes for Substance and Accident
Objection and answer on the cause of visibility for substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the object of vision must be the common identity (al-huwiyya al-mushtaraka) rather than the particularities of substance or accident.

Core Idea
What we see is not substance or accident but their shared existence.

This passage presents a third objection against the claim that substance and accident are seen through a single cause. The objector argues that since substance and accident are different, their visibility might have different causes. The answer shows that vision attaches to what they share—the common identity—not their specific natures. Let's examine this reasoning in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its reasoning
Read the passage carefully, noting the objector's claim that the validity of vision for substance and accident is not similar, so each could have a separate cause.
The objector also uses the example of heat from sun or fire to argue that a single type can have multiple causes.
2
Understand the answer: common identity
Focus on the answer: the object of vision is not the specific nature (being a substance or accident) but the common identity. The author provides an example of seeing Zayd without analyzing his substances and accidents.
This shows that vision attaches to the shared attribute, not the particularities.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters for the doctrine of ru’yat Allah. The common identity being the object of vision implies that the same cause (validity of vision) applies to all visible things, whether substance or accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن صحة رؤية الجوهر لا تماثل صحة رؤية العرض. إذ لا يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر! فلم لا يجوز أن يعلل كل منهما بعلة على الانفراد. ولو سلم تماثلهما فالواحد النوعي قد يعلل بعلتين مختلفتين كالحرارة بالشمس والنار، فلا يلزم أن يكون له علة مشتركة، ووجه اندفاعه أن متعلق الرؤية لا يجوز أن يكون من خصوصيات الجوهرية أو العرضية، بل يجب أن يكون مما يشتركان فيه للقطع بأنا قد نرى الشيء وندرك أن له هوية ما من غير أن يدرك كونه جوهراً أو عرضاً، فضلاً عن أن ندرك ما هو زيادة خصوصية لأحدهما ككونه إنساناً أو فرساً سواداً أو خضرة، بل ربما قد نرى زيداً بأن يتعلق رؤية واحدة بهويته من غير تفصيل لما فيه من الجواهر والأعراض، ثم قد نفصله إلى ما له من تفاصيل الجواهر والأعراض. وقد نغفل عن التفاصيل بحيث لا نعلمها عندما سئلنا عنها، وإن استقصينا في التأمل، فعلم أن ما يتعلق به الرؤية هو الهوية المشتركة لا الخصوصيات التي بها الافتراق وهذا معنى كون علته صحة الرؤية مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض.
The third [objection]: that the validity of vision of a substance is not similar to the validity of vision of an accident. For one does not suffice as the other. So why is it not permissible that each be caused by a separate cause? And if their similarity is granted, the specific unity may be caused by two different causes, like heat by the sun and fire, so a common cause is not necessary. And the repelling of it is that the object of vision (muta'alliq al-ru'ya) cannot be from the particularities of substance-ness or accident-ness, but rather must be from what they share, for it is certain that we sometimes see a thing and perceive that it has some identity (huwiyya ma) without perceiving whether it is substance or accident, let alone perceiving what is an additional particularity for one of them, like being human or horse, black or green. Nay, perhaps we see Zayd such that a single vision attaches to his identity without detailing what it contains of substances and accidents, then we detail it into its details of substances and accidents. And we may be oblivious to the details such that we do not know them when asked about them, even if we investigate with reflection. So it becomes known that what vision attaches to is the common identity, not the particularities by which they differ. This is the meaning of its cause (validity of vision) being common between substance and accident.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The objector says: the visibility of a substance and an accident is not identical; each could have its own cause. Even if they are similar, a single type can have multiple causes (e.g., heat from sun or fire). The answer explains that the object of vision (muta'alliq al-ru'ya) is the common identity (al-huwiyya al-mushtaraka), not the specific differentiating characteristics. We often see an object without knowing if it is substance or accident; we perceive only a 'whatness'. For example, we see Zayd as an individual without analyzing all his substances and accidents. Thus, the cause of visibility is the shared attribute—existence—not the particular essence.

Correction: The author argues that the common identity is what is seen, so a single cause suffices.

A) The specific nature of substance or accident.
B) The common identity shared by substance and accident.
C) The particular qualities like black or white.
D) The accidental properties only.

Key Takeaway
The object of vision is the common identity, not the differentiating particularities.

Objection: All Existents Would Be Visible
Objection and answer: existents visibility consequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that all existents would be visible is refuted by appealing to God's habitual action rather than impossibility.

Core Idea
Not seeing sounds is God's habit, not because it's impossible.

The opponent argues that if existence alone suffices for visibility, then even sounds, tastes, and thoughts should be seen. This seems absurd. But the defender responds by distinguishing between what is impossible and what is merely not part of God's usual course. This lesson explores that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the list of existents that are never seen: sounds, tastes, smells, beliefs, etc.
The objection tries to reduce the claim to absurdity.
2
Understand the answer
The answer denies that the conclusion is false. It says we do not see these things because God does not create the vision in us—not because it's impossible.
3
Distinguish between jari al-‘ada and istib’ad
Identify the two key terms: 'habit' (jari al-‘ada) and 'improbability' (istib’ad). The defender says the opponent's argument is based on mere improbability, not logical impossibility.
This is a classic Ash'ari move.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this answer preserves the original claim that existence is sufficient for visibility, while explaining away counterexamples by appeal to God's freedom to create or not create sight.
This reinforces the idea that God's power is not limited by our expectations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه يلزم على ما ذكرتم صحة رؤية كل موجود حتى الأصوات والطعوم والروايح والاعتقادات، والقدر والإرادات وأنواع الإدراكات وغير ذلك من الموجودات وبطلانه ضروري.

والجواب : منع بطلانه وأن ما لا يتعلق بها الرؤية بناء على جري العادة بأن الله تعالى لا يخلق فينا رؤيتها لا بناء على امتناع ذلك، وما ذكره الخصم مجرد استبعاد.
The second [additional objection]: that according to what you mentioned, the validity of seeing every existent would follow, even sounds, tastes, smells, beliefs, power, wills, types of perceptions, and other existents, and its falsity is necessary. And the answer: denial of its falsity, and that what vision does not attach to is based on the course of habit (jari al-'āda) that God does not create vision of them in us, not on the impossibility of that. And what the opponent mentioned is mere improbability (istib'ād).
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the second additional objection: if existence suffices for visibility, then all existents, including sounds, tastes, etc., should be visible, which is obviously false. The answer denies the falsity of that conclusion. It argues that the fact that we do not see sounds, etc., is due to God's habit (jari al-'āda)—He does not create vision of them in us—not because it is absolutely impossible. The objector's claim is merely a sense of improbability (istib'ād), not a proof.

Misconception: If existence suffices for visibility, then everything must actually be seen by us.
Correction: Visibility is a potential that God may or may not actualize. We do not see sounds because God habitually does not create sight of them, not because they are inherently invisible.

The objection that all existents would be visible is refuted by distinguishing between what is impossible and what is merely not part of God's habitual creation of vision.

Refuting the Objection of Createdness in the Vision Argument
Refutes the proof by analogy with createdness (al-makhluqiyya).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of 'createdness' (al-makhluqiyya) does not refute the argument for the possibility of seeing Allah, as it is a purely mental concept.

Core Idea
Createdness is a mental concept, not a real attribute that requires a cause.

In this passage, the author addresses a third objection to the proof for vision: that the same reasoning would imply that God is created, which is absurd. He explains why createdness is different from real properties like visibility, and acknowledges that another objection (by tangibility) is stronger. Let's work through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: if createdness is common to substance and accident like visibility, then its cause would be existence, implying God is created. Note the problem.
2
Understand the answer
The author distinguishes createdness as a purely mental concept (amr i'tibari) that does not require a real cause, unlike the validity of vision which is realized upon existence.
Contrast 'amr i'tibari' with real attributes that have actual existence.
3
Consider the concession
Notice that the author concedes the objection by tangibility is strong, showing intellectual honesty. Then the conclusion clarifies that 'cause' here means the object of vision.
The proof's weakness is acknowledged, but the main point about vision stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن (۲) نقض الدليل بصحة المخلوقية، فإنها مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض ولا مشترك بينهما يصلح علة لذلك سوى الوجود فيلزم صحة مخلوقية الواجب وهو محال.

والجواب : أنها أمر اعتباري محض لا يقتضي علة. إذ ليست مما يتحقق عند الوجود وينتفي عند المعدم كصحة الرؤية. سلمنا لكن الحدوث يصلح ها هنا علة . لأن المانع من ذلك في صحة الرؤية، إنما هو امتناع تعلق الرؤية بما لا تحقق له في الخارج، وأما النقض بصحة الملموسة فقوي والإنصاف أن ضعف هذا الدليل جلي، وعلى ما ذكرنا من أن المراد بالعلة ها هنا متعلق الرؤية يكون المرئي من (۳) كل شيء وجوده.
(۲) في (ب) بزيادة حرف (أن).
(۳) في (ب) المرمي بدلاً من (المرئي).
The third [additional objection]: that the proof is refuted by [the attribute of] createdness (al-makhluqiyya), for it is common between substance and accident, and there is no common [attribute] between them fit to be a cause for that other than existence, so the validity of createdness of the Necessary would follow, and that is impossible. And the answer: that it [createdness] is a purely mental concept (amr i'tibari mahd) that does not require a cause. For it is not something that is realized upon existence and ceases upon non-existence like the validity of vision. We grant [your analogy], but origination (huduth) is a suitable cause here. Because the obstacle to that in the case of validity of vision is only the impossibility of vision attaching to what has no external reality. As for the refutation by the validity of being tangible (al-malmūsa), it is strong, and fairness demands that the weakness of this proof is clear. And according to what we mentioned, that the intended by 'cause' here is the object of vision, the visible of everything is its existence. (2) In (b) an addition of the letter 'an'. (3) In (b) 'al-marmī' instead of 'al-mar'ī'.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the third additional objection: the same reasoning used for visibility can be applied to 'createdness' (makhluqiyya), which is also common between substance and accident, and its cause would then be existence, implying that the Necessary (God) is created, which is absurd. The answer distinguishes createdness as a purely mental concept (amr i'tibari) that does not require a real cause; it does not have the same status as the validity of vision, which is a real property (it is realized when the object exists and ceases when it does not). The author concedes that the rebuttal using tangibility (malmusa) is strong, acknowledging the proof's weakness. The conclusion is that by 'cause' here we mean the object of vision: the visible of everything is its existence.

اعتباري
الحدوث
الملموسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Createdness is a real attribute like visibility and thus can be used to refute the argument for vision.
Correction: Createdness is a purely mental concept (amr i'tibari) that does not require a cause, so it cannot be used in the same way as real attributes.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say createdness (al-makhluqiyya) does not refute the argument?
A) Createdness is not common between substance and accident.
B) Createdness is a mental concept that does not require a cause.
C) Createdness is unique to contingent beings.
D) The proof only applies to visibility.

Key Takeaway
Createdness (al-makhluqiyya) is a purely mental concept and does not refute the argument for vision; the proof's real weakness lies in the objection from tangibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The First Proof: Humans Cannot Know Allah's Essence
Presents the debate and first proof that humans cannot know Allah's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that humans cannot know Allah's essence in its true reality, and the distinction between affirming His existence and conceiving His essence.

Core Idea
What we know of Allah are negations, attributes, and relations—not the essence itself.

In this passage, the author introduces a major theological debate: can human beings know Allah's essence (dhat) as it truly is? Many investigators argue that we cannot, and they give two proofs. Here we examine the first proof, which distinguishes between knowing Allah's existence, attributes, negations, and relations, and knowing His actual essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very compressed style.
2
Identify the main claim and the proof
Find the statement that humans cannot know Allah's essence and the evidence given: that what we know are only negations, attributes, and relations.
3
Note the objection and response
The author anticipates an objection: if existence is identical to essence, then knowing existence is knowing essence. See how he responds by distinguishing between affirmation and conception.
This is a key nuance.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Think about the difference between affirming that something exists (tasdiq) and conceiving its specific reality (tasawwur). Why does this matter for theology?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: (المبحث الثاني)
المبحث الثاني
في العلم بحقيقته تعالى
كثير من المحققين على أنه غير حاصل للبشر لأن ما يعلم منه وجود وصفات وسلوب، وإضافات، ولأن ذاته تمنع الشركة، والمعلوم لا يمنعها بدليل افتقارها إلى بيان التوحيد، ثم هو كاف في صحة الحكم عليه .
اختلفوا في العلم بحقيقة الله تعالى للبشر، أي في معرفة ذاته بكنه الحقيقة فقال بعدم حصوله كثير من المحققين خلافاً لجمهور المتكلمين، ثم القائلون بعدم الحصول جوزوه خلافاً للفلاسفة . احتج الأولون بوجهين .
أحدهما : أن ما يعلم منه البشر هو السلوب والإضافات والأحسن أن يقال هو الوجود بمعنى أنه كائن في الخارج والصفات بمعنى أنه حي عالم قادر ونحو ذلك. والسلوب بمعنى أنه واحد أزلي أبدي ليس بجسم ولا عرض، وما أشبه ذلك، والإضافات بمعنى أنه خالق ورازق ونحوهما ، وظاهر أن ذلك ليس علماً بحقيقة الذات لا يقال الوجود عين الذات عند كثير من المحققين، فالعلم به علم به. لأنا نقول : قد أشرنا إلى أن معنى العلم بوجوده التصديق بأنه موجود ليس بمعدوم، لا تصور وجوده الخاص بحقيقته، وكذا الكلام في الصفات.
He said: (Second Investigation)
Second Investigation
On knowledge of His essence, may He be exalted.
Many investigators hold that it is not attained by humans because what is known of Him is existence, attributes, negations, and relations, and because His essence prevents participation (shirkah), and what is known does not prevent it, as evidenced by its need for an explanation of tawhid. Moreover, it suffices for making judgments about Him.
They disagreed about knowledge of Allah's essence, may He be exalted, for humans—that is, knowing His essence in its true reality. Many investigators said it is not attained, contrary to the majority of theologians. Then those who said it is not attained allowed it, contrary to the philosophers. The former argued with two proofs.
The first: that what humans know of Him are the negations, attributes, and relations. Better it is said: it is existence in the sense that He exists in externality; attributes in the sense that He is living, knowing, powerful, and so forth; negations in the sense that He is one, eternal, everlasting, not a body nor an accident, and the like; and relations in the sense that He is Creator, Provider, and similar. It is apparent that this is not knowledge of the essence itself. It cannot be said that existence is identical to essence according to many investigators, so knowledge of it is knowledge of Him. For we say: we have already indicated that the meaning of knowledge of His existence is the affirmation that He exists, not non-existent, not the conception of His specific existence pertaining to His essence. Similarly for the attributes.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage introduces the debate on whether humans can know Allah's essence (haqiqah) in its true reality. It states that many investigators deny this possibility, contrary to most theologians, and even among those who deny it some allow it whereas philosophers deny it absolutely. The deniers give two proofs. The first proof argues that what humans know of Allah are only negations (salub), attributes (sifat), and relations (idafat)—for example, His existence (that He is), His being living, knowing, powerful, His being one, eternal, not a body, etc. These are not knowledge of the essence itself. An objection is raised: if existence is identical to essence, then knowing existence is knowing essence. The author answers that 'knowledge of His existence' means affirming that He exists, not conceiving His specific existence as it is in itself. The same applies to the attributes. Thus the first proof stands that we do not know the essence per se.

Key Terms
حقيقة
سلوب
إضافات
تصديق
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing that Allah exists is equivalent to knowing His essence.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between affirming existence (tasdiq) and conceiving the essence (tasawwur). The first proof argues that we only have the former, not the latter.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what do humans know of Allah?
A) His essence as it is
B) Negations, attributes, and relations
C) Only His existence
D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that human knowledge of Allah is limited to His attributes and relations; we cannot know His essence directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know Allah,' what exactly do you know? Have you ever confused your knowledge of His attributes with knowing His essence? How does this affect your worship?
?
This lesson says we cannot know Allah's essence. Does that make you feel distant or humble? What would it mean to you if you could know His essence? Could you still be a servant?
?
You have been taught that Allah's existence is known. But if existence is not the essence, then what is the difference in your mind? Are you content to know Him only through negations and relations?

Second Proof: The Essence is a Particular Preventing Sharing
Presents the second proof: Allah's essence is a particular that prevents conceptual sharing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that Allah's essence is a real particular whose conception inherently prevents participation, and why this shows that human knowledge does not reach the essence itself.

Core Idea
Allah's essence is a real particular; our known concepts are universals that can be shared.

This passage presents the second proof that humans cannot know Allah's essence. It argues that Allah's specific essence is a real particular (juz'i haqiqi) that prevents sharing, while everything we know of Him (like existence) are universals. This is why proving tawhid requires a proof: if we knew the essence directly, we would not need evidence. The author also addresses an objection about the concept of the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Notice the structure: claim, evidence, implication, objection, clarification.
2
Identify the central claim
The passage states that Allah's specific essence is a 'real particular' (juz'i haqiqi) whose conception prevents participation (shirkah). Understand what this means: a real particular is something that cannot be predicated of multiple subjects.
Real particulars are like 'this specific person' – unique.
3
Understand the implication
If what we know of Allah (e.g., His existence) prevented participation, then tawhid would be self-evident without proof. But we need proof, so what we know must be universal concepts, not the essence itself.
This is the key inference linking the nature of our knowledge to the need for proof.
4
Examine the objection and clarification
An objection says the Necessary Being is a universal that prevents multiplicity. The author clarifies: that applies to the concept of 'Necessary Being', not the specific essence that is described as necessary.
This prevents a common misunderstanding – don't confuse concept with reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أن ذاته المخصوصة جزئي حقيقي يمنع تصوره الشركة فيه، ولا شيء مما يعلم منه كذلك، ولهذا يفتقر في بيان التوحيد أي نفي الشركة إلى الدليل، ولو كان المعلوم منه يمنع الشركة لما كان كذلك، وما يقال إن الواجب كلي يمتنع كثرة أفراده، فمعناه أن مفهوم الواجب كذلك لا الذات المخصوص الذي يصدق عليه أنه واجب.
And the second: that His specific essence is a real particular (juz'i haqiqi) whose conception prevents participation (shirkah) in it, and nothing that is known of Him is like that. For this reason, explaining tawhid—i.e., negating partnership—requires proof. If what is known of Him prevented participation, it would not be so. And what is said—that the Necessary Being is a universal that prevents the multiplicity of its individuals—its meaning is that the concept of the Necessary is like that, not the specific essence that is truly described as being Necessary.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that humans cannot know Allah's essence. It argues that Allah's specific essence (al-dhat al-mukhusşah) is a real particular (juz'i haqiqi). A real particular is such that its conception inherently prevents sharing—that is, it cannot be conceived as applying to more than one entity. In contrast, everything that humans actually know of Allah (existence, attributes, etc.) does not have this property; those concepts can be shared by multiple beings (e.g., 'existent' applies to many). Therefore, if we truly knew His essence, we would not need a proof to negate partnership (tawhid); but we do need proof, showing that our knowledge is not of the essence itself. An objection arises: some say the Necessary Being (al-wajib) is a universal that prevents multiplicity of individuals. The author clarifies: that applies to the concept of 'necessary being', not to the specific essence that is qualified as necessary. Thus the second proof stands.

Key Terms
جزئي حقيقي (real particular)
شركة (participation/sharing)
مفهوم الواجب (concept of the Necessary)
ذات مخصوصة (specific essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing that Allah is the Necessary Being is equivalent to knowing His essence.
Correction: The concept of 'Necessary Being' is a universal that can be predicated of multiple beings in thought, but the specific essence that has that attribute is a real particular that cannot be shared.

Checkpoint
Why does the second proof claim that 'nothing that is known of Him prevents participation'?
A) Because all known concepts are universals
B) Because His essence is a universal

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that Allah's essence is a real particular that cannot be shared, unlike universal concepts we know, proving we do not grasp His essence directly.

?

When you say 'Allah is All-Powerful', do you truly feel that this concept refers exclusively to Him, or have you ever compared His power to something else? How does this impact your tawhid in your heart?
?
This lesson proves that if your knowledge of Allah prevented partnership, you would not need effort to maintain tawhid. But you struggle with shirk in your intentions, your fears, your love. Why does your heart still run after other powers? What practical step will you take today to turn back to the One whose essence cannot be shared?
?
This lesson humbles your intellect: you cannot know His essence, only conceptual handles. When you pray, do you resign to this limitation, or does it drive you to seek Him more? This very lesson is a door—will you stand on the threshold of concepts, or will you ask Allah to grant you a glimpse of His exclusive reality?
