Objections to the Proofs and the Affirmative View
Two objections against the proofs and the affirmative view on knowability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against the proofs for the knowability of Allah's essence, their resolutions, and the divergent views on the possibility of knowing Allah's reality.

Core Idea
The dispute over knowing Allah's essence hinges on whether we discuss His reality or His identity.

This passage continues the discussion on whether Allah's essence can be known. After presenting proofs that humans only know negations and attributes, the author now considers two objections that challenge those proofs. The first objection questions the claim about what all humans know, the second objects that oneness is known decisively. The response clarifies that the real debate concerns Allah's reality, not His identity, and contrasts the views of those who deny and those who affirm knowability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow: objections, answer, and then a deeper point about reality versus identity.
The passage uses a very concise style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection challenges the claim that all humans know only negations, attributes, etc. The second objection points out that oneness is known through decisive proofs, so that seems to be particular knowledge. Find these in the text.
The second objection starts with 'وقد يقال على الأخير'.
3
Understand the answer to the second objection
The answer says that oneness is a universal concept—it can apply to multiple beings hypothetically. So it does not indicate particular knowledge of Allah's identity.
This shows the distinction between universal and particular.
4
Grasp the shift to reality vs identity
The author then states that the real discussion is about the reality (haqiqah) of the Necessary, not His identity (huwiyyah). This reframes the whole debate.
This will help you understand the positions that follow.
5
Examine the two positions on knowability
Those who deny knowability base it on Allah's lack of composition, so definition/description cannot capture reality. Those who affirm say His reality is simply being Necessary Existent with attributes, and some (Mushaykhiyya) claim to know His essence exactly.
Compare these positions and see how they relate to the earlier discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرد على الوجهين :
·
أنا لا نسلم أن معلوم كل أحد من البشر ما ذكرتم ومن أين لكم الإحاطة بأفراد البشر ومعلوماتهم. وقد يقال على الأخير إن من جملة ما علم منه الوحدانية بأدلتها القاطعة ، اعتبار ذلك لا نتصور الشركة ولا الافتقار إلى بيان التوحيد فيجاب: بأن هذا أيضاً كلي إذ لا يمتنع فرض صدقه على كثيرين وإن كان المفروض مما لانعم يتوجه أن يقال : الكلام في حقيقة الواجب لا في هويته، ولهذا ترى القائلين بامتناع المعلومية، يجعلون امتناع اكتسابه بالحد والرسم مبنياً على أنه لا تركب فيه، وأن الرسم لا يفيد الحقيقة لا على أن الشخص لا يعرف بالحد والرسم، والقائلين بحصول المعلومية يقولون إنه لا حقيقة له سوى كونه ذاتاً واجب الوجود يجب كونه قادراً عالماً حياً سميعاً بصيراً إلى غير ذلك من الصفات حتى اجترأ المشايخية من المعتزلة فقالوا : إنا نعلم ذاته كما يعلم هو ذاته من غير تفاوت.
And objections are made against the two proofs:
· We do not grant that what every human knows is what you have mentioned. Where do you get encompassing knowledge of all individuals of humans and their objects of knowledge? And it may be said against the latter [proof] that among what is known of Him is oneness (wahdaniyyah) through its decisive proofs; considering that, we do not conceive of participation nor the need for an explanation of tawhid. It is answered: that this too is universal, for it is not impossible to suppose its truth for many, even if the supposed is such. It must be said: the discussion is about the reality of the Necessary, not about His identity (huwiyyah). For this reason, you see those who hold the impossibility of knowability make the impossibility of attaining Him by definition (hadd) and description (rasm) based on the fact that He has no composition, and that description does not convey the reality—not that the person is not known by definition and description. And those who affirm knowability say: He has no reality other than being an essence that is necessary of existence, which must be powerful, knowing, living, hearing, seeing, and other attributes—until the Mushaykhiyya of the Mu'tazila dared to say: We know His essence as He knows His essence, without difference.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the previous proofs and then states the position of those who affirm knowledge of Allah's essence. The first objection challenges the claim that all humans know only negations, attributes, etc.—the objector says you cannot know what every human knows. The second objection targets the second proof: we know oneness (wahdaniyyah) by decisive proofs, and in that there is no room for participation or need for proof of tawhid—so that seems to be knowledge of something that prevents sharing. The answer is that oneness is also a universal concept; it can apply to multiple beings hypothetically, so it does not prevent sharing in conception. The real discussion is about the reality of the Necessary, not His specific identity. Then the author explains that those who deny knowability base their denial on the fact that Allah has no composition, so He cannot be defined by genus and differentia, and description does not capture the true essence. On the other hand, those who affirm knowability (like the majority of theologians) say that Allah's reality is nothing but being a Necessary Existent with attributes; some even go to extremes, like al-Mushaykhiyya from the Mu'tazila, who claimed to know His essence exactly as He knows it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Oneness (wahdaniyyah) is a particular knowledge that cannot be shared.
Correction: Oneness is a universal concept that can be hypothetically applied to multiple beings, so it does not constitute particular knowledge of Allah's identity.

The debate over knowing Allah's essence centers on whether we can grasp His reality through definition and description, given His simplicity.

When you recite 'Allahu Ahad,' do you stop at the universal meaning, or do you sense the weight of the particular Reality that no description can capture? First Rational Proof: Impossibility of Two Independent Agents on One Effect
Al-Taftazani presents the first rational proof with objection and Ghazali's supplementary argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that every possible act must be caused by Allah alone, and how the objection of mere permissibility is refuted.

Core Idea
One effect cannot have two independent agents—only Allah's power suffices.

Al-Taftazani begins with a rational argument: the servant's act is possible, and every possible is subject to Allah's power. If the servant also had independent power over it, two agents would act on one effect—an impossibility. He then addresses an objection and supplements with al-Ghazali's argument from the necessity of a single specific cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core proof
Read the first rational proof: the servant's act is possible, and every possible is under Allah's power. If it were also under the servant's power, two independent agents would act on one effect, which is impossible.
This is a classic kalam argument from the impossibility of two independent causes.
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims that Allah's power over the act means only that He could influence it, not that it actually occurs by Him. This would avoid the conjunction of agents.
The objector distinguishes between potential power and actual causation.
3
Refute the objection
Al-Taftazani responds that even the permissibility of the act occurring by both powers would lead to the possibility of the impossible, which is absurd.
The refutation hinges on the logical impossibility of two independent agents producing the same effect.
4
Study Ghazali's alternative argument
Ghazali argues that possibility necessitates a specific cause, because a non-specific cause has no reality. Since possibility is uniform across all possibles, they must depend on one cause, which must be necessary and free-acting.
This argument reinforces that all created acts ultimately depend on Allah alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأدلة العقلية على أن فعل العبد واقع بقدرة الرب
قوله لنا عقليات وسمعيات)
(أما العقليات فوجوه :
الأول: أن فعل العبد لو كان بقدرته لزم اجتماع المؤثرين لما مر من شمول قدرة
الله تعالى) .
استدل على كون فعل (۱) العبد واقعاً بقدرة الله تعالى بوجوه عقلية وسمعية. فالأول من الوجوه العقلية أن فعل العبد ممكن ، وكل ممكن مقدور الله تعالى لما مرّ في بحث الصفات، ففعل العبد مقدور الله تعالى، فلو كان مقدوراً للعبد أيضاً على وجوه التأثير لزم اجتماع المؤثرين المستقلين على أثر واحد، وقد بين امتناعه في بحث العلل .
فإن فيل : اللازم من شمول قدرته كون فعل العبد مقدوراً له بمعنى دخوله تحت قدرته وجواز تأثيره فيه، ووقعه بها نظراً إلى ذاته، لا بمعنى أنه واقع بها ليلزم المحال . قلنا : جواز وقوعه بها مع وقوعه بقدرة العبد يستلزم جواز المحال وهو محال ، وفيه نظر ومن تلفيقات الإمام في بيان كون (۲) كل ممكن واقعاً بقدرة الله تعالى، إن الإمكان محوج إلى السبب، ولا يجوز أن يكون محوجاً إلى سبب لا بعينه، لأن غير المعين لا تحقق له، وما لا تحقق له لا يصلح سبباً لوجود شيء، فتعين أن يكون محوجاً إلى سبب معين ، ثم الإمكان أمر واحد في جميع الممكنات، فلزم افتقارها كلها إلى ذلك السبب. والسبب الذي يفتقر إليه جميع الممكنات لا يكون ممكناً بل واجباً ليكون الكل بإيجاده. وقد ثبت أنه مختار لا موجب فيكون الكل واقعاً بقدرته واختياره وفي بيان كون كل مقدور الله واقعاً بقدرته وحده ، أنه لو لم يقع بقدرة الله تعالى وحاله ،
Rational proofs that the act of the servant occurs by the power of the Lord.
His statement: 'We have rational and transmitted proofs.'
'As for the rational proofs, they are several:
The first: that if the act of the servant were by his power, then the conjunction of two agents would be necessitated, because of what has passed of the comprehensiveness of the power of God, exalted.'
He argued that the act of the servant occurs by the power of God, exalted, with rational and transmitted proofs. The first of the rational proofs is that the act of the servant is possible, and every possible is predestined by God, exalted, as passed in the discussion of attributes. So the act of the servant is predestined by God, exalted. Therefore, if it were also predestined by the servant in the mode of influence, then the conjunction of two independent agents on one effect would be necessitated, and its impossibility was demonstrated in the discussion of causes.
If it is said: The necessary consequence of the comprehensiveness of His power is that the act of the servant is predestined by Him in the sense of being under His power and the permissibility of His influencing it, and its occurrence by Him in consideration of its essence, not in the sense that it occurs by Him so that the impossible is necessitated. We say: The permissibility of its occurrence by Him together with its occurrence by the power of the servant necessitates the permissibility of the impossible, which is impossible. And in it is consideration, and from the compilations of the Imām in explaining that every possible occurs by the power of God, exalted: that possibility necessitates a cause, and it is not permissible that it necessitates a non-specific cause, because the non-specific has no realization, and what has no realization is not suitable as a cause for the existence of anything. So it is determined that it necessitates a specific cause. Then possibility is one thing in all possibles, so their dependence on that cause is necessitated. And the cause upon which all possibles depend cannot be possible but rather necessary, so that everything is by its bringing into existence. And it has been established that He is free-acting, not necessitating, so everything occurs by His power and choice. And in explaining that every predestined thing occurs by the power of God alone, that if it did not occur by the power of God, exalted, alone...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational proof that the servant's act is brought about by Allah's power. The author begins by stating that there are rational and transmitted proofs. The first rational proof is that the servant's act is possible (mumkin), and every possible is predestined by Allah based on the discussion of attributes. If the act were also predestined by the servant in the mode of influence, this would entail the conjunction of two independent agents on one effect, which was shown impossible in the discussion of causes. An objection is then raised: the comprehensiveness of Allah's power only means that the act is under His power and that He could influence it, not that it actually occurs by Him. The answer is that the mere possibility of its occurrence by both would lead to the possibility of the impossible, which is impossible. The author notes that there is consideration here. He then provides a supplementary argument from al-Ghazali: possibility necessitates a specific cause, because a non-specific cause has no reality. Since possibility is uniform across all possible things, they all depend on one specific cause. That cause must be necessary, not possible, and since it is established that Allah is free-acting (mukhtār), not a necessitating agent, everything occurs by His power and choice. The final sentence, which is cut off, begins to explain that every predestined thing must occur by Allah's power alone.

مقدور (maqdur)
اجتماع المؤثرين (ijtima' al-mu'aththirin)
إمكان (imkan)
سبب (sabab)
مختار (mukhtar)
موجب (mujib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection successfully shows that Allah's power over an act does not entail its actual occurrence by Him, thereby allowing two independent powers over the same act without conflict.
Correction: Al-Taftazani refutes this: even the permissibility of both powers acting would entail the possibility of two independent agents on one effect, which is impossible. Thus, the act must be solely by Allah's power.

Checkpoint

B) Because a non-specific cause has no reality and cannot be a cause.
C) Because only one necessary cause can exist.
D) Because the servant's power is defective.

Key Takeaway
The first rational proof shows that the servant's act must be by Allah's power alone; any other independent power would lead to the impossible conjunction of two agents on one effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something in your life, do you truly believe that Allah's power alone brought it about, or do you subtly attribute it to your own ability and effort? How would your heart change if you constantly reminded yourself that every possible act is within Allah's power, and that He is the sole independent agent? What is one specific way you can practice this belief today?
?
Al-Ghazali argued that all possible things depend on one necessary cause. Think of a recent situation where you worried about the outcome. Did you treat that situation as if its cause was uncertain, or did you entrust it to Allah as the only cause? How does this proof challenge your reliance on secondary means? Will you tomorrow explicitly tell yourself that Allah alone is the cause of all outcomes, including the ones that depend on your actions?
?
The proof shows that the servant cannot have independent power—all power belongs to Allah. Does this make you feel helpless or free? Reflect on a habit or sin you struggle with: do you feel trapped by your own powerlessness, or do you find hope in the fact that Allah's power is over everything, including your own actions? How can you use this truth to turn back to Allah in repentance and reliance, starting this very moment?

The Second Rational Proof: The Servant Does Not Create His Acts
Second rational proof: modus tollens showing servant cannot create acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that the servant cannot be the creator of his own voluntary acts, based on the necessary knowledge of details.

Core Idea
If you create an act, you must know every detail; you don't, so you don't create.

This lesson explores the second rational argument (al-wajh al-thānī) used by theologians to deny that humans create their own actions. The proof is a simple but powerful logical deduction: producing an act requires specific intention, which requires knowledge. Since we often perform actions without detailed awareness, we cannot be the true creators. The passage grounds this in everyday examples like a sleeping person or a walker.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical form
Recognize that the argument is a modus tollens: if P then Q, not Q, therefore not P. Here P = 'servant creates acts', Q = 'servant knows details'.
2
Examine the implication (P→Q)
The author argues that creation requires specific intention (qasd) which requires knowledge. Read the explanation of why bringing about different quantities/types needs a specificer.
The Quranic verse 'Would He who created not know?' supports this link.
3
Observe the falsity of Q
The author gives examples: a sleeper performing voluntary acts without awareness, a walker traversing a path without attending to every part. These show that detailed knowledge is absent.
Think of your own experiences: walking without counting steps.
4
Conclude the argument
Since Q is false (we don't know details), P must be false: the servant does not create his own acts.
This proof reinforces that only Allah is the true creator of all actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثاني) الدليل الثاني: الثاني لكان عالماً بتفاصيله وبطلان اللازم يظهر في النايم والماشي والناطق والكاتب). الوجه الثاني من الوجه العقلية : أن العبد لو كان موجداً لأفعاله لكان عالماً بتفاصيلها واللازم باطل، أما الملازمة فلأن الإتيان بالأزيد والأنقص والمخالف ممكن فلا بد لرجحان ذلك النوع وذلك المقدار من مخصص هو القصد إليه ، ولا يتصور ذلك إلى بعد العلم به، ولظهور هذه الملازمة يستنكر الخلق بدون العلم كقوله تعالى (ألا يعلم من خلق) ويستدل بفاعلية العالم على عالمية الفاعل، وأما بطلان اللازم فلوجوه منها أن النائم تصدر عنه أفعال اختيارية لا شعور له بتفاصيل كمياتها وكيفياتها، ومنها أن الماشي إنساناً كان أو غيره يقطع مسافة معينة في زمان معين من غير شعور له بتفاصيل الأجزاء والأحياز التي بين المبدأ والمنتهى، ولا بالآنات
He said: 'Second, the second proof: second, he would be knowledgeable of its details, and the falsity of the consequent appears in the sleeper, the walker, the speaker, and the writer.' The second of the rational proofs: If the servant were the bringer-into-existence of his acts, he would be knowledgeable of their details, and the consequent is false. As for the implication: because bringing about more, less, or contrary is possible, so there must be for the preponderance of that type and that quantity a specificer which is the intention towards it, and that is not conceivable except after knowledge of it. And due to the obviousness of this implication, creation without knowledge is disapproved, as His saying 'Would He who created not know?' (Quran 67:14), and one infers from the effective agency of the knower the knowledge of the agent. As for the falsity of the consequent, it is due to aspects, among them: the sleeper performs voluntary acts without being aware of the details of their quantities and qualities; and among them: the walker, human or otherwise, traverses a specific distance in a specific time without awareness of the details of the parts and spaces between the start and end, nor of the moments.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents the second rational proof. It argues that if the servant were the actual creator (mūjid) of his own acts, he would have to know all the details of those acts in order to specifically intend them, because producing different types, quantities, and variations requires a determinate intention based on knowledge. The Quranic verse 'Would He who created not know?' is cited to support the link between creating and knowing. However, we observe that many voluntary acts occur without detailed knowledge, such as the actions of a sleeping person, or a person walking who does not consciously attend to every step, every part of the path, or every moment. Thus, since the consequent (detailed knowledge) is false, the antecedent (the servant creating his acts) must also be false. The proof is structured as a modus tollens: if P (servant creates) then Q (knows details), but not Q, therefore not P.

Key Terms
موجد (bringer into existence)
تفاصيل (details)
مخصص (specificer)
قصد (intention)
ملازمة (implication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that unconscious actions (e.g., sleepwalking) show humans create acts without knowledge, so the servant must be the creator.
Correction: The argument uses lack of knowledge to disprove creation: if you don't know the details, you cannot be the specific creator. Unconscious actions are not created by you; they are created by Allah.

Checkpoint
What verse does the author cite to support the link between creating and knowing?
A) 'He is the All-Knowing, the All-Wise.'
B) 'Would He who created not know?'
C) 'And Allah created you and what you do.'
D) 'No soul knows what it will earn tomorrow.'

Key Takeaway
The lack of detailed knowledge of our own actions proves that we are not the true creators; only Allah creates with full knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a daily action like walking or speaking, do you pay attention to every tiny movement and its details? How does your lack of full awareness remind you that you are not the true creator of your actions? What change in your daily awareness of Allah's control could this bring?
?
The proof says that if you were the creator, you would know every detail. Think of a time you felt proud of an achievement. Did you really create that success? How does this lesson challenge you to redirect all gratitude and reliance to Allah alone?
?
Are you willing to admit that even your most intentional acts—like writing or driving—are ultimately from Allah, not from you? What would it look like to live with this truth constantly in mind? What habit can you adopt to remind yourself that every act is a gift from the All-Knowing Creator?

Preponderance Without a Preponderator: Objection, Answer, and Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents an objection, answer, and al-Razi's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem of preponderance without a preponderator, the attempted solution appealing to God's stronger power, and al-Razi's refutation based on the nature of power and its object.

Core Idea
Two equal powers cannot differ in strength, so the problem remains unresolved.

Al-Taftazani presents a classic objection against Ash'ari theology: if two powers are equally independent, why does one effect occur? The answer invokes God's greater strength, but al-Razi refutes it by arguing that the object of power cannot vary in intensity. This lesson unpacks the entire dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Note the key terms like 'preponderance without a preponderator' and 'independence of effect.'
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the core problem: if two powers (divine and human) are equal and independent, what determines which one's effect actually occurs? Without a preponderator, it would be arbitrary.
This is a logical problem known as 'tarjih bila murajjih'.
3
Analyze the answer and al-Razi's refutation
The answer claims God's power is stronger despite equal independence. Al-Razi refutes this by noting that the object of power is indivisible (it either exists or not), so power cannot be 'stronger' or 'weaker'—only more general.
Al-Razi's argument hinges on the nature of maqdur.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for debates about divine and human agency. If al-Razi is correct, then the problem of preponderance remains unresolved, challenging certain Ash'ari positions.
This connects to broader issues in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما أن يقع أحدهما دون الآخر فيلزم الترجح بلا مرجح ، لأن التقدير استقلال كل من القدرتين بالتأثير من غير تفاوت . وأجيب : بأنه يقع مراداً الله تعالى لكون قدرته أقوى. إذ المفروض استواؤهما في الاستقلال بالتأثير وهو لا ينافي التفاوت في القوة والشدة . ودفعه الإمام الرازي بأن المقدور لا يقبل التجزيء ولا يتفاوت بالشدة والضعف فيمتنع أن يكون الاقتدار عليه قابلاً لذلك، بل يلزم تساوي القدرتين في القوة غاية الأمر أن إحداهما تكون أعم وأشمل ، وهو لا يوجب كونه أشد وأقوى. وعليه منع ظاهر.
Or that one of them occurs without the other, so preponderance without a preponderator is necessitated, because the assumption is that each of the two powers is independent in effecting without disparity. And it was answered: That it occurs as willed by God Almighty because His power is stronger. For the supposed equality in independence of effect does not negate disparity in strength and intensity. And Imam al-Razi refuted it by saying that the object of power does not admit of division and does not vary in intensity and weakness, so it is impossible for the ability to control it to be subject to that; rather, the equality of the two powers in strength is necessitated; the utmost is that one of them is more general and comprehensive, and that does not necessitate it being stronger and more intense. Upon it, the objection is apparent.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the problem of preponderance without a preponderator in the context of divine and human power. The objection states that if two powers are equal and independent, and one effect occurs while the other does not, there must be a preponderator to tip the balance, otherwise preponderance without a preponderator would follow. The answer given is that the occurrence is according to God's will because His power is stronger, even though both powers are independent in causing the effect; independence does not entail equality in strength. Al-Razi then refutes this answer on the grounds that the object of power (maqdur) does not admit of degrees of intensity—it is either created or not—so the power over it cannot vary in strength. Therefore, the two powers must be equal in strength, differing only in generality. Thus, the answer is invalid. The passage ends by noting that this objection (from al-Razi) is apparently valid.

Key Terms
الترجح بلا مرجح
قدرة
مقدور
استقلال
تفاوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equality in independence of effect implies equality in all aspects of power, so the answer that God's power is stronger is ad hoc.
Correction: Independence does not preclude differences in strength or generality. However, al-Razi shows that because the maqdur is indivisible, strength cannot differ; only generality can.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason al-Razi rejects the answer that God's power is stronger?
A) Because God's power is not subject to comparison.
B) Because the object of power does not admit of intensity, so powers cannot differ in strength.
C) Because the answer was proposed by the Mu'tazila.
D) Because the objection itself is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's refutation shows that the notion of a 'stronger' power over a single indivisible object is logically untenable, so the problem of preponderance without a preponderator remains unresolved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own ability to choose, do you ever assume that your will operates independently like God's? How does this passage's insistence that the object of power is indivisible force you to reconsider the nature of your own power?
?
This lesson shows that logical problems arise when we attribute human concepts of strength to God. Are you willing to accept that your understanding of 'stronger' may be deeply flawed when applied to the Divine? What would it take for you to release that assumption?
?
Consider the object of power: a single, indivisible act. Is there any act in your life that you think you could do without God's permission? Can you name one, and explain why this lesson refutes that thought?

First Mu'tazilite Proof: Free Will and Moral Categories
First rational proof: free will necessary for moral distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof of the Mu'tazilites for free will, which argues that moral and religious distinctions are intuitively perceived and would be meaningless without human independence.

Core Idea
Without free will, praise, blame, and all moral categories lose their meaning.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if human actions were entirely determined by God, the basic categories of morality and religion would collapse. In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the first of their rational proofs, which appeals to our intuitive awareness of these distinctions. This argument is foundational to the debate on free will and divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of categories that would be invalidated if the servant lacked independence.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if not independence, then void.'
2
Identify the categories listed
The passage enumerates many moral and religious distinctions. Group them into categories: moral (praise/blame, command/prohibition), religious (reward/punishment, promise/threat, sending messengers, revealing books), theological (disbelief/faith, evil/good, prophet/satan, glorification/raving), and volitional (actions by own will vs another's will).
The list is comprehensive, covering practical, religious, and theological domains.
3
Understand the intuitive argument
The passage concludes that the differentiation between these categories is 'perceived intuitively' (mudrakah bi al-wijdan). This means the distinctions are self-evident and not learned from revelation alone.
Intuition here refers to immediate, pre-theoretical awareness.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the Mu'tazilites thought this argument was decisive. If these distinctions are real and intuitive, then human agency must be real, otherwise God would be unjust in praising or blaming.
This argument challenges the Ash'arite view that God creates all acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنهم من احتج عليه) عقلاً ونقلاً أما العقليات فوجوه : الأول: أنه لولا استقلال العبد لبطل المدح والذم والأمر والنهي والثواب والعقاب وفوائد الوعد والوعيد، وإرسال الرسل، وإنزال الكتب، والفرق بين الكفر والإيمان والإساءة والإحسان وفعل النبي والشيطان، وكلمات التسبيح والهذيان وكذا بين ما يقع بأعضاء العبد على وفق إرادته وإرادة غيره مع أن التفرقة مدركة بالوجدان .
He said: (And among them are those who argued against him) rationally and scripturally. As for rational proofs, they are aspects: First: That if the servant did not have independence, then praise and blame, command and prohibition, reward and punishment, the benefits of promise and threat, sending messengers, revealing books, the distinction between disbelief and faith, evil and good, the act of the prophet and the satan, words of glorification and senseless raving, and likewise the distinction between what occurs by the limbs of the servant in accordance with his will and the will of another, while the differentiation is perceived intuitively.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the first rational argument of the Mu'tazilites against the Ash'arite position that God creates all acts. It begins with an introductory phrase: 'He said: (And among them are those who argued against him) rationally and scripturally.' Then it lists the rational proofs. The first argument is that without the servant's independence (istiglal), many fundamental moral and religious categories would be invalidated. These include praise and blame, command and prohibition, reward and punishment, the benefits of promise and threat, the sending of messengers, the revelation of books, the distinction between disbelief and faith, between evil and good, between the act of a prophet and that of a satan, between words of glorification and senseless raving, and the distinction between actions that occur by the servant's limbs according to his will and those according to another's will. The argument asserts that all these distinctions are intuitively perceived (bi al-wijdan), implying that they are self-evident and would be meaningless if the servant were not an independent agent. The conclusion is that the servant must have free will to preserve these moral and religious realities.

Key Terms
استقلال العبد
المدح والذم
بالوجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is purely empirical or based on scripture.
Correction: The argument is rational and relies on intuitive (wijdani) necessity, not empirical observation or revelation alone.

Checkpoint
What does the first argument claim would be invalidated if the servant lacks independence?
A) Only reward and punishment
B) Praise and blame, command and prohibition, and other moral distinctions
C) Only divine attributes
D) The existence of God

Key Takeaway
The first rational proof for free will argues that moral and religious distinctions are intuitively perceived and would be meaningless without human independence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praised someone today, did you implicitly affirm that they could have done otherwise? How does this Mu'tazilite argument unsettle the foundations of your everyday moral judgments?
?
Think of a moment you felt trapped into an action. Did you still hold yourself accountable? According to this lesson, your sense of ownership runs deeper than circumstances—will you now examine where you hide behind compulsion?
?
If the distinction between meaningful words and senseless raving is intuitively known, what does that say about the prayers you recite? Are they truly your own, or do you utter them mechanically? Will you let this truth transform your worship starting tonight?

Human Will Verses and Asharite Response
Author presents verses and Ash'ari answer about human will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari school responds to Quranic verses that seem to affirm independent human will, by affirming human will while subordinating it to God's will.

Core Idea
Human will is real but dependent on God's will.

This lesson examines the fifth category of evidence used by those who argue for human autonomy: Quranic verses that say 'whoever wills let him believe.' The Ash'ari answer is not to deny human will but to qualify it: the servant's will is itself willed by God. We will see how the author lists verses and then gives the reply.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the list of Quranic verses and the concise answer.
2
Identify the core Ash'ari claim
Find the phrase 'التعليق بمشيئة العبد مذهبنا لكن مشيئته بمشيئة الله' – this is the heart of the answer. It affirms human will while making it dependent on God.
3
Reflect on the supporting verse
The answer ends with 'وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله' – consider how this verse is used as proof that human will is subordinate to God's will.
This verse is from Qur'an 76:30 or 81:29.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : الآيات الدالة على أن فعل العبد بمشيئته كقوله تعالى فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر اعملوا ما شئتم ) (۱) اعملوا ماشئتم (۲) ولمن شاء منكم أن يتقدم أو يتأخر (٣) و فمن شاء ذكره ) (٤) و فمن شاء اتخذ إلى ربه سبيلاً ) (۳) والجواب : أن التعليق بمشيئة العبد ،مذهبنا لكن مشيئته بمشيئة الله تعالى. وما تشاءون إلا أن يشاء الله )
The fifth: The verses indicating that the action of the servant is by his will, such as His saying, the Exalted: 'So whoever wills let him believe and whoever wills let him disbelieve' (1) 'Do whatever you will' (2) 'And whoever among you wills to advance or delay' (3) 'And whoever wills to remember it' (4) 'And whoever wills to take to his Lord a way' (3) And the answer: The conditional by the will of the servant is our doctrine, but his will is by the will of God the Exalted. And 'You do not will unless God wills.'
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth category of scriptural evidence: verses that seem to attribute effective will to the human servant. The author lists several Quranic verses, such as 'So whoever wills let him believe' (Q 18:29), 'Do whatever you will' (Q 41:40), 'Whoever among you wills to advance or delay' (Q 74:37), etc. The Mu'tazila and Qadariyya use these verses to argue that humans create their own actions independently. The Ash'ari answer (al-jawab) affirms that human will is real — the action is indeed linked to the servant's will — but immediately qualifies: 'his will is by the will of God.' This is the Ash'ari position of kasb (acquisition): the human wills, but God creates the will and the action. The proof text is Q 81:29 (or 76:30): 'And you do not will unless God wills.' Thus, the verses are not evidence for absolute human autonomy but for delegated will within divine determination.

Key Terms
مشيئة العبد (human will)
التعليق بمشيئة العبد (conditional by human will)
مشيئة الله (God's will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny that humans have any will or choice; actions are forced by God.
Correction: The passage shows that Ash'aris affirm human will ('التعليق بمشيئة العبد مذهبنا') but subordinate it to God's will. They do not deny human will but qualify its independence.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the verses that say 'whoever wills let him believe'?
A) These verses are abrogated
B) Human will is independent of God
C) Human will is real but dependent on God's will
D) These verses apply only to prophets

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position affirms human will but subordinates it to God's will, as expressed in the Qur'anic verse 'you do not will unless God wills.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take credit for a success, do you genuinely feel that your will to act was a gift from Allah? How does that realization sit with your pride?
?
Think of a moment you insisted on your own plan despite praying istikhara. Did you truly believe you had independent will, or did you forget the verse 'you do not will unless God wills'?
?
If every inclination of your heart is ultimately from Allah, what changes in the way you approach repentance and spiritual struggle? Will you ask Him more earnestly to change your desires?

Ali's Response on Qada and Qadar
Ali explains qada and qadar do not negate human responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine decree (qada and qadar) does not negate human free will and moral responsibility, as explained by Imam Ali.

Core Idea
Divine decree does not mean compulsion: reward and punishment still apply.

This passage records an incident where a soldier worries that his efforts are worthless because of predestination. Imam Ali responds with a clear distinction between divine foreknowledge and human coercion, showing that accountability remains intact. We will examine the argument, the misconception, and the affirmation of moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the Story
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the narrative context: a man asks Ali about their march to Sham and Ali confirms it was by decree.
Notice the man's initial misunderstanding about reward.
2
Identify the Error
The man concludes that if the march was by decree, then his effort has no reward. Ali corrects this view.
This error is the core misconception addressed.
3
Understand Ali's Rebuttal
Ali explains that if decree were compulsion, reward, punishment, command, and prohibition would be meaningless. He then labels those who hold such a view as the Qadariyya and Magians of the nation.
Ali uses logical consequence to refute the error.
4
Reflect on the Affirmation
Ali affirms that Allah commands by choice, forbids as a warning, and does not overpower nor is compelled. The lesson ends with a Quranic verse against those who think creation is in vain.
This reinforces that human responsibility is real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروى الأصبغ بن نباتة أن شيخاً قام إلى علي بن أبي طالب بعد انصرافه من صفين ، فقال أخبرنا عن مسيرنا إلى الشام أكان بقضاء الله وقدره فقال والذي فلق الحبة وبرأ النسمة ما وطئنا موطئاً ولا هبطنا وادياً ولا علونا قلعة إلا بقضاء وقدر، فقال الشيخ عند الله احتسب عنائي ما أرى لي من الأجر شيئاً فقال له مه أيها الشيخ عظم الله أجركم في مسيركم وأنتم سائرون وفي منصرفكم وأنتم منصرفون، ولم تكونوا في شيء من حالاتكم مكرهين ولا إليها مضطرين، فقال الشيخ كيف والقضاء والقدر ساقنا، فقال ويحك لعلك ظننت قضاء لازماً، وقدراً حتماً لو كان كذلك لبطل الثواب والعقاب والوعد والوعيد والأمر والنهي، ولم تأت من الله لمذنب، ولا محمدة لمحسن، ولم يكن المحسن أولى بالمدح من المسيء ولا المسيء أولى بالذم من المحسن تلك مقالة عبدة الأوثان، وجنود الشياطين وشهود الزور، وأهل العمي عن الصواب، وهم قدرية هذه الأمة ومجوسها. إن الله أمر تخييراً ونهى تحذيراً، وكلف يسيراً، لم يعص مغلوباً، ولم يطع مستكرهاً، ولم يرسل الرسل إلى خلقه عبثاً، ولم يخلق السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا فويل للذين كفروا من النار .
And Asbagh ibn Nubata narrated that a man stood up to Ali ibn Abi Talib after his return from Siffin and said: 'Tell us about our march to Sham, was it by the decree (قضاء) of Allah and His predestination (قدر) ?' He said: 'By Him who splits the seed and creates the soul, we did not tread a single step, descend a valley, or climb a hill except by decree and predestination.' The man said: 'I reckon my effort as accounted for by Allah; I see no reward for me.' He said to him: 'Gently, O sheikh! May Allah magnify your reward in your march while you are marching, and in your return while you are returning. You were not in any of your states forced nor compelled.' The man said: 'How is that, when decree and predestination have driven us?' He said: 'Woe to you! Perhaps you thought it is a necessary decree and an inevitable predestination. If it were so, then reward and punishment, promise and threat, command and prohibition would be nullified. No blame would come from Allah upon a sinner, nor praise upon a doer of good. The good-doer would not be more deserving of praise than the evil-doer, nor the evil-doer more deserving of blame than the good-doer. That is the saying of idol worshippers, the soldiers of the devils, false witnesses, and people blind to the truth. They are the Qadariyya (قدرية) of this nation and its Magians. Allah commanded by choice and forbade as a warning, and obligated easy tasks. He was not disobeyed because He was overpowered, nor obeyed under compulsion. He did not send the messengers to His creation in vain, and He did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them false. That is the assumption of those who disbelieve, so woe to those who disbelieve from the Fire.'
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a narrative objection-and-answer. A man asks Ali about the Battle of Siffin, and Ali confirms it was by divine decree. The man then erroneously concludes that if it was by decree, there is no reward for his effort. Ali rebukes him and explains that divine decree does not mean compulsion. He argues that if decree were necessary, the entire system of reward, punishment, commands, and prohibitions would collapse. He then identifies such a belief as that of idolaters and devils, and labels them the Qadariyya and Magians of this nation. Finally, he affirms that Allah commands with free choice, forbids as a warning, and does not overpower nor is compelled. The passage ends with a Quranic verse condemning those who think creation is in vain.

ثواب
عقاب
اختيار
قدرية
مجوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If everything is by divine decree, then human actions are compelled and there is no reward or punishment.
Correction: Ali explains that decree is not compulsion; Allah commands by choice and forbids as a warning, and reward/punishment are based on that.

Checkpoint

A) People's efforts
B) Reward and punishment, promise and threat, command and prohibition
C) The need for prophets
D) The existence of heaven and hell

Key Takeaway
Divine decree and human responsibility coexist: Allah's knowledge does not force our choices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Ash'ari Position on Moral Good and Evil: Reason vs. Revelation
Author defines Ash'ari position on moral good and evil against Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari view that moral good and evil are determined solely by divine command and prohibition, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
Good and evil are defined by God's command, not by reason.

This lesson explores a foundational debate in Islamic theology: how do we know what is morally good and evil? The Ash'ari school argues that reason alone cannot determine moral value in God's sight; only revelation can. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid lays out the Ash'ari position and contrasts it with the Mu'tazila, who hold that reason can perceive moral truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the dense style; key terms are defined within the text.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of good and evil
The author separates two senses: (1) perfection/imperfection or compatibility with purpose (undisputed), and (2) deserving praise/blame from God (disputed). Identify these in the text.
This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding the dispute.
3
Contrast Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions
Compare the statements: Ash'aris say 'good is by command, evil by prohibition'; Mu'tazila say 'command is for good, prohibition for evil'. Understand the reversal of priority.
The difference is whether command follows an inherent quality or creates it.
4
Reflect on the role of revelation and intellect
Consider the author's statement that for Mu'tazila, revelation is a 'revealer' (kashif) not a 'legislator' (mubayyin). For Ash'aris, revelation is the sole determinant of moral value in God's judgment.
This has major implications for theology and ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث لا حكم للعقل بالحسن والقبح بمعنى استحقاق المدح والذم عند الله تعالى، خلافاً للمعتزلة، وأما بمعنى صفة الكمال والنقص أو ملاءمة الغرض أو الطبع وعدمها فلا نزاع فعندنا الحسن بالأمر، والقبح بالنهي، بل عينهما وعندهم الأمر للحسن، والنهي للقبح حتى لو لم يدركا بالعقل ضرورة أو نظراً كان الشرع كاشفاً لا مبيناً . في الحسن والقبح جعل هذا من مباحث أفعال الباري تعالى مع أنها لا تتصف بالحسن والقبح بالمعنى الذي يذكره أعني المأمور به والنهي عنه نظراً إلى أنهما بخلقه، ومن آثار فعله، وإلى أنهما بتفسير الخصم يتعلقان بأفعال الباري إثباتاً ونفياً. وقد اشتهر أن الحسن والقبح عندنا شرعيان وعند المعتزلة عقليان ، وليس النزاع في الحسن والقبح بمعنى صفة الكمال والنقص كالعلم والجهل، وبمعنى الملاءمة للغرض وعدمها كالعدل والظلم وبالجملة كل ما يستحق المدح أو الذم في نظر العقول ومجاري العادات، فإن ذلك يدرك بالعقل . ورد الشرع أم لا ، وإنما النزاع في الحسن والقبح عند الله تعالى بمعنى استحقاق فاعله في حكم الله تعالى المدح أو الذم عاجلاً والثواب والعقاب آجلاً. ومبنى التعرض للثواب والعقاب على أن الكلام في أفعال العباد ، فعند ذلك بمجرد الشرع بمعنى أن العقل لا يحكم بأن الفعل حسن أو قبيح في حكم الله تعالى، بل ما ورد الأمر به فهو حسن ، وما ورد النهي عنه فقبيح من غير أن يكون للعقل جهة محسنة أو مقبحة في ذاته ولا بحسب جهاته واعتباراته حتى لو أمر . بما نهى عنه صار حسناً وبالعكس وعندهم للفعل (۱) جهة محسنة أو مقبحة في حكم الله
The third investigation: The intellect has no judgment of good and evil in the sense of deserving praise and blame from God, exalted is He, contrary to the Mu'tazila. As for in the sense of the attribute of perfection and imperfection, or compatibility with purpose or nature and its lack, there is no dispute. For according to us, good (al-husn) is by command, and evil (al-qubh) by prohibition; indeed, they are identical [with command and prohibition]. According to them, command is for good, and prohibition for evil, such that if they were not perceived by the intellect necessarily or by reflection, revelation would be a revealer (kashif) not a legislator (mubayyin). Regarding good and evil, this is made one of the investigations of the acts of God the Exalted, even though they are not described as good and evil in the sense he mentions, i.e., the commanded and prohibited, considering that they are by His creation, and from the effects of His act, and that they, according to the opponent's interpretation, are related to the acts of God affirmatively and negatively. And it is well-known that good and evil according to us are revelational (shar'iyyan) and according to the Mu'tazila rational ('aqliyyan). And the dispute is not about good and evil in the sense of the attribute of perfection and imperfection like knowledge and ignorance, nor in the sense of compatibility with purpose and its lack like justice and injustice. In summary, everything that deserves praise or blame in the view of intellects and common customs, that is perceived by the intellect whether revelation comes or not. Rather, the dispute is about good and evil with respect to God, exalted is He, i.e., the sense that the doer deserves praise or blame in God's judgment immediately, and reward and punishment subsequently. And the basis for addressing reward and punishment is that the discussion is about the acts of servants. Thus, that is solely by revelation (shar'), meaning that the intellect does not judge that an act is good or evil in God's judgment; rather, whatever is commanded is good, and whatever is prohibited is evil, without the intellect having any aspect that makes good or evil in its essence nor according to its aspects and considerations, so that even if He commands what He prohibited, it becomes good, and vice versa. According to them, the act has (1) an aspect that makes good or evil in God's judgment.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a definition and clarification of the Ash'ari position on moral good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh) against the Mu'tazila. It begins by stating the core thesis: the intellect has no judgment of good and evil in the sense of deserving praise (madḥ) and blame (dhamm) from God. This is contrary to the Mu'tazila. The author then clarifies that other meanings of good and evil—such as attribute of perfection/imperfection (like knowledge vs. ignorance) or compatibility with purpose (like justice vs. injustice)—are not disputed; they are rationally knowable. The Ash'ari position is that good is defined by God's command (al-amr) and evil by His prohibition (al-nahy); they are identical, not that command follows an inherent goodness. The Mu'tazila hold that command follows the goodness of an act, and prohibition follows its evilness; if these were not perceived by reason, revelation would merely uncover (kāshif) what is already true, not declare (mubayyin) what becomes obligatory. The author then explains why this topic is included among discussions of God's acts, even though God's acts are not described as good/evil in this sense, because the opponent relates these moral properties to divine acts. The well-known summary is that for Ash'aris good and evil are revelational (shar'iyyān), for Mu'tazila rational ('aqliyyān). The author stresses that the dispute is narrowly about divine recompense: whether an act in itself, by its essential or accidental attributes, makes the doer deserve reward or punishment. The Ash'ari view is that only revelation determines this; the intellect cannot deem an act good or evil in God's judgment. An act becomes good simply by being commanded, and evil by being prohibited, even if God were to change the ruling. The Mu'tazila hold that the act has an intrinsic aspect (jiha muḥassina or muqabbiḥa) that grounds the divine ruling.

معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'aris deny any rational knowledge of good and evil entirely.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they affirm rational knowledge of other meanings (e.g., perfection/imperfection), and only deny that reason can determine what deserves praise/blame from God.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position described, what determines whether an act is good or evil in God's judgment?
A) The intrinsic nature of the act.
B) The command and prohibition of God.
C) The rational perception of humans.
D) The compatibility with human purposes.

Key Takeaway
For Ash'aris, moral value is conferred by divine command, not discovered by reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a conflict between what your culture considers reasonable and what God has commanded, where does your heart truly turn for moral authority? Are you willing to submit your reason to revelation even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The passage teaches that no act has intrinsic moral worth before God apart from His command. If you truly believed this, how would it change the way you evaluate your own 'good deeds' in your daily life? Would you seek God's command more earnestly?
?
Consider an area of your life where you have rationalized disobedience—perhaps a small compromise you tell yourself is 'not that bad' because it seems harmless or even beneficial. How does the Ash'ari position challenge that rationalization? Will you repent and realign with God's explicit command today?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
The Two Meanings of al-Husn wa al-Qubh
The author distinguishes two meanings of husn and qubh and states the brief positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct meanings of 'good' and 'evil' in Islamic theology and why the Ash'ari-Mu'tazili dispute concerns only one of them.

Core Idea
The dispute about good and evil is not about perfection but about divine reward.

Before delving into the Ash'ari position, it is crucial to clarify what exactly is being debated. The author of al-Maqasid distinguishes two senses of 'good' (husn) and 'evil' (qubh): one relating to intrinsic perfection or suitability, and another relating to divine reward and punishment. This lesson isolates the first meaning and shows where the real disagreement lies.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two meanings
Read the passage and identify the two distinct meanings of husn and qubh mentioned. Notice which one is disputed and which is agreed upon.
The author explicitly says 'there is no dispute' (لا نزاع) about the second meaning.
2
Compare the positions
Observe how the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions are briefly stated: for Ash'aris, good is by command; for Mu'tazilites, command follows good.
This sets up the deeper discussion in the next section.
3
Understand the role of reason
Reflect on the phrase 'the Law is a revealer, not a declarer' (الشرع كاشف لا مبين). What does this imply about the Mu'tazilite view?
The Mu'tazilites hold that reason can know good and evil independently.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث . لا حكم للعقل بالحسن والقبح بمعنى استحقاق المدح والذم عند الله تعالى، خلافاً للمعتزلة، وأما بمعنى صفة الكمال والنقص أو ملاءمة الغرض أو الطبع وعدمها فلا نزاع فعندنا الحسن بالأمر، والقبح بالنهي، بل عينهما وعندهم الأمر للحسن، والنهي للقبح حتى لو لم يدركا بالعقل ضرورة أو نظراً كان الشرع كاشفاً لا مبيناً . في الحسن والقبح جعل هذا من مباحث أفعال الباري تعالى مع أنها لا تتصف بالحسن والقبح بالمعنى الذي يذكره أعني المأمور به والنهي عنه نظراً إلى أنهما بخلقه، ومن آثار فعله، وإلى أنهما بتفسير الخصم يتعلقان بأفعال الباري إثباتاً ونفياً. وقد اشتهر أن الحسن والقبح عندنا شرعيان وعند المعتزلة عقليان ، وليس النزاع في الحسن والقبح بمعنى صفة الكمال والنقص كالعلم والجهل، وبمعنى الملاءمة للغرض وعدمها كالعدل والظلم وبالجملة كل ما يستحق المدح أو الذم في نظر العقول ومجاري العادات، فإن ذلك يدرك بالعقل . ورد الشرع أم لا ، وإنما النزاع في الحسن والقبح عند الله تعالى بمعنى استحقاق فاعله في حكم الله تعالى المدح أو الذم عاجلاً والثواب والعقاب آجلاً. ومبنى التعرض للثواب والعقاب على أن الكلام في أفعال العباد ، فعند ذلك بمجرد الشرع بمعنى أن العقل لا يحكم بأن الفعل حسن أو قبيح في حكم الله تعالى، بل ما ورد الأمر به فهو حسن ، وما ورد النهي عنه فقبيح من غير أن يكون للعقل جهة محسنة أو مقبحة في ذاته ولا بحسب جهاته واعتباراته حتى لو أمر بما نهى عنه صار حسناً وبالعكس وعندهم للفعل جهة محسنة أو مقبحة في حكم الله تعالى، يدركها العقل بالضرورة كحسن الصدق النافع، وقبح الكذب الضار، أو بالنظر كحسن الكذب النافع، وقبح الصدق الضار، أو بورود الشرع، كحسن صوم يوم عرفة، وقبح صوم يوم عيد
The third section. There is no judgment of reason regarding beauty and ugliness in the sense of deserving praise and blame with God, contrary to the Mu'tazilites. As for the sense of perfection and deficiency or suitability to purpose or nature and their absence, there is no dispute. For us, beauty is by command, and ugliness by prohibition; rather they are identical. For them, command is for beauty, and prohibition for ugliness, even if they are not perceived by reason necessarily or reflectively; the Law is a revealer, not a declarer. Regarding beauty and ugliness, this is placed among the investigations of the acts of God Most High, although they are not characterized by beauty and ugliness in the sense he mentions, namely the commanded and the prohibited, considering that they are by His creation and from the effects of His act, and that they, in the opponent's interpretation, relate to the acts of God affirmatively and negatively. It is well known that beauty and ugliness according to us are legal, and according to the Mu'tazilites are rational. The dispute is not about beauty and ugliness in the sense of perfection and deficiency like knowledge and ignorance, nor in the sense of suitability to purpose and its lack like justice and injustice; in short, everything that deserves praise or blame in the view of intellects and the course of habits is perceived by reason, whether the Law comes or not. Rather the dispute is about beauty and ugliness with God Most High in the sense of the doer's deserving in God's judgment praise or blame immediately and reward or punishment in the afterlife. The basis for addressing reward and punishment is that the discussion concerns the acts of servants. Therefore, in that, [it is] solely by the Law, meaning that reason does not judge that an act is beautiful or ugly in God's judgment. Rather, whatever is commanded is beautiful, and whatever is prohibited is ugly, without reason having a beautifying or uglying aspect in itself, nor according to its aspects and considerations, even if He commands what He prohibited, it becomes beautiful, and vice versa. According to them, an act has a beautifying or uglying aspect in God's judgment, which reason perceives necessarily, like the beauty of beneficial truthfulness and the ugliness of harmful lying, or reflectively like the beauty of beneficial lying and the ugliness of harmful truthfulness, or by the coming of the Law like the beauty of fasting on the day of Arafa and the ugliness of fasting on the day of Eid.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is an exposition defining the disputed topic of al-husn wa al-qubh (beauty and ugliness, i.e., good and evil) and stating the Ash'ari position. The author begins by asserting that reason has no judgment regarding husn and qubh in the sense of deserving praise (madḥ) and blame (dhamm) from God, contra the Mu'tazilites. He clarifies that there is no dispute about the other meaning: husn and qubh as perfection/defect or suitability/unsuitability to purpose or nature—these are rationally known. For the Ash'aris (us), husn is by command (amr) and qubh by prohibition (nahy); indeed they are identical (ʿaynihimā) in that the command itself makes it good, the prohibition makes it bad. For the Mu'tazilites, command is for husn and prohibition is for qubh, meaning they perceive pre-existing goodness or badness; the Law only reveals what reason already knows or can deduce. The author then explains that this topic is placed among the investigations of God's acts (afʿāl al-Bārī), even though God's acts are not described as husn/qubh in the sense of commanded/prohibited, because they are His creation. The well-known statement is that for us (Ash'aris) husn/qubh are sharʿiyyān (legal), for the Mu'tazilites ʿaqliyyān (rational). He reiterates that the dispute is not about the meaning of perfection/defect or suitability, as those are rationally perceived regardless of revelation. The real dispute is about husn/qubh in God's judgment concerning the doer's deserving praise/blame now and reward/punishment in the afterlife. The basis of reward/punishment is that the discussion is about human acts (afʿāl al-ʿibād). Thus, according to Ash'aris, reason does not judge an act to be good or bad in God's sight; rather, what is commanded is good and what is prohibited is bad, without any inherent aspect of goodness or badness in the act itself or in its attributes. Even if God were to command what He prohibited, it would become good, and vice versa. For the Mu'tazilites, acts have an aspect that makes them good or bad in God's judgment, which reason perceives either necessarily (e.g., beneficial truthfulness is good, harmful lying is bad) or reflectively (e.g., beneficial lying is good, harmful truthfulness is bad) or by revelation (e.g., fasting on Arafa is good, fasting on Eid is bad).

القبح
العقل
الشرع
الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari-Mu'tazili dispute is about whether reason can know good and evil in any sense.
Correction: The dispute is only about the meaning of deserving divine reward/punishment. Both schools agree that reason can know perfection/defect and suitability.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which meaning of 'good' (husn) is there no dispute between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites?
A) Good as deserving reward from God
B) Good as perfection or suitability to purpose
C) Good as what is commanded
D) Good as what reason perceives

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari-Mu'tazili dispute over good and evil is limited to the sense of deserving divine reward or punishment, not to all senses of moral evaluation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you decide whether an action is right or wrong, do you instinctively rely on your own reason or on divine command? How does this lesson confront your default approach? Are you willing to let God's command override your personal sense of good and evil?
?
Can you think of a specific situation where you considered something 'good' simply because it suited your desires or seemed reasonable, without checking if God has commanded or prohibited it? What would it look like to align your judgment with the Ash'ari view that goodness is identical to divine command?
?
The lesson teaches that the dispute is only about one sense of good/evil. Does this distinction change how you view the role of reason in your faith? Have you ever mistakenly thought that your moral intuitions alone could guarantee divine reward? What practical step can you take today to ensure your moral decisions are grounded in revelation rather than personal preference?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
The Ash'ari Position on Good and Evil
The author elaborates the Ash'ari position and contrasts it with the Mu'tazilite view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that moral value is determined solely by divine command and prohibition, and how it contrasts with the Mu'tazilite view that reason independently knows good and evil.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, an act is good because God commands it, not because it has intrinsic goodness.

Having clarified the meaning of the dispute, the author now presents the Ash'ari position in full. This lesson examines how the Ash'aris ground moral obligation in revelation, and how they differ from the Mu'tazilites who hold that reason can independently perceive good and evil in God's judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the well-known statement
Read the passage and note the famous claim: 'Husn and qubh are legal for us, rational for them.' What does 'legal' mean here?
Legal means determined by divine law, not by reason.
2
Identify the real dispute
The author clarifies that the dispute is not about rational perception of perfection/defect, but about the basis of divine reward and punishment.
Reason can know some goods and evils in a general sense, but not in God's judgment.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari position
The passage states that reason has no beautifying or uglying aspect; an act's value in God's eyes is solely based on command and prohibition. Even if God commanded what He prohibited, it would become good.
This emphasizes divine sovereignty over morality.
4
Contrast with Mu'tazilite view
The Mu'tazilites believe acts have inherent aspects that reason can perceive, either necessarily, reflectively, or through revelation as a guide.
The examples illustrate the three modes of moral perception.

The real dispute is about husn/qubh in God's judgment concerning the doer's deserving praise/blame now and reward/punishment in the afterlife. The basis of reward/punishment is that the discussion is about human acts (afʿāl al-ʿibād). Thus, according to Ash'aris, reason does not judge an act to be good or bad in God's sight; rather, what is commanded is good and what is prohibited is bad, without any inherent aspect of goodness or badness in the act itself or in its attributes. Even if God were to command what He prohibited, it would become good, and vice versa. For the Mu'tazilites, acts have an aspect that makes them good or bad in God's judgment, which reason perceives either necessarily (e.g., beneficial truthfulness is good, harmful lying is bad) or reflectively (e.g., beneficial lying is good, harmful truthfulness is bad) or by revelation (e.g., fasting on Arafa is good, fasting on Eid is bad).

Key Terms
الشرع
العقل
الاستحقاق
المعتزلة
الجهة المحسنة
الجهة المقبحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris completely deny any rational knowledge of good and evil, making morality arbitrary.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm rational knowledge of good and evil in the sense of perfection/defect and suitability, but deny that reason determines what deserves divine reward or punishment.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position in this passage, what happens if God commands an act that He previously prohibited?
A) It remains evil because of its inherent nature.
B) It becomes good because God commanded it.
C) Reason would still judge it evil.
D) It is neither good nor evil.

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, an act's moral value in God's sight is entirely determined by divine command, not by any inherent quality or rational judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something you believe is good, do you occasionally base that judgment on your own reasoning before consulting what Allah has commanded? What might that reveal about your reliance on revelation over intellect?
?
If the Sharia declared that something you inherently feel is evil (like lying) is actually good in some context, would you submit to that judgment fully, or would your mind resist? Where does that resistance come from?
?
The Ash'ari position teaches that moral value is entirely dependent on God's command. Are you willing to let go of your own moral intuitions and accept that without any internal reservation? How would this transform your daily decisions?

The Difference Between Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite Views on Command and Prohibition
The author answers an objection about the difference between the two schools on command and prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise difference between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite positions on how divine command and prohibition relate to an act's moral value, specifically through the concepts of mūjibāt (causes) and muqtaḍayāt (consequences).

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, command causes moral value; for Mu'tazilites, command reveals it.

This passage presents a clear objection-and-answer format that crystallizes the fundamental disagreement between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite schools on the nature of moral value. The author distinguishes two terms—mūjibāt and muqtaḍayāt—to show how each school understands the relationship between divine command/prohibition and the goodness or badness of actions. Reading this will sharpen your grasp of the debate's core.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the structure: a question followed by an answer.
The answer starts with 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the key terms mūjibāt and muqtaḍayāt
Notice how the author contrasts these two terms to explain the difference. Mūjibāt means causes, muqtaḍayāt means consequences or requirements.
This is the heart of the distinction.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters: it determines whether moral values are objective and independent of God's command (Mu'tazilite) or dependent on God's command (Ash'ari).
The passage also mentions that command/prohibition 'reveal' (kashafa) pre-existing values for the Mu'tazilites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فأي فرق بين المدعيين في هذا القسم . قلنا الأمر والنهي عندنا من موجبات الحسن والقبح بمعنى أن الفعل أمر به فحسن ونهى عنه فقبح، وعندهم من مقتضايته بمعنى أنه حسن فأمر به، أو قبح فنهى عنه، فالأمر والنهي إذا وردا كشفا عن حسن وقبح سابقين حاصلين للفعل لذاته أو لجهاته، ثم لكل من الفريقين تعريفات للحسن والقبيح يتناول بعضها فعل الباري وفعل غير المكلف والمباح دون البعض وقد بينا تفصيل ذلك في شرح التنقيح، وفوائد شرح مختصر الأصول
If it is said: What is the difference between the two claimants in this section? We say: Command and prohibition according to us are causes (mūjibāt) of beauty and ugliness, meaning that an act is commanded so it is good, and prohibited so it is ugly. According to them, they are consequences (muqtaḍayāt) of it, meaning that it is good so He commanded it, or ugly so He prohibited it. Thus command and prohibition, when they come, reveal pre-existing beauty and ugliness that belong to the act by itself or by its aspects. Then each of the two groups has definitions of beauty and ugliness, some of which include the act of God, the act of the non-obligated person, and the permissible, while others do not. We have detailed that in Sharḥ al-Tanqīḥ and Fawāʾid Sharḥ Mukhtaṣar al-Uṣūl.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer that clarifies the precise difference between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite positions regarding the relationship between divine command/prohibition and moral qualities. The objection asks: What is the difference between the two claims in this section? The answer is given by distinguishing two key terms: mūjibāt (causes) and muqtaḍayāt (consequences/requirements). According to the Ash'aris (us), command and prohibition are mūjibāt (causes) of husn and qubh: because an act is commanded, it becomes good; because it is prohibited, it becomes bad. The command/prohibition itself produces the moral quality. According to the Mu'tazilites (them), command and prohibition are muqtaḍayāt (consequences) of an already existing moral quality: the act is good, so God commands it; or it is bad, so God prohibits it. Thus, when command or prohibition comes, it does not create husn/qubh but reveals (kashafa) a pre-existing husn/qubh that the act possesses either by its essence (li-dhātihi) or by its aspects (li-jihātihi). The author then notes that each school has definitions of husn and qubh that may or may not include God's acts, the acts of the non-obligated (ghayr al-mukallaf), and permissible acts (mubāḥ), and refers to his other works for further detail.

Key Terms
موجبات
مقتضايات
كشف
الحسن
القبح
الأمر
النهي
المدعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari view means God's commands are arbitrary.
Correction: The passage shows that for Ash'aris, command/prohibition are the cause of moral value, not arbitrary. The act becomes good because it is commanded, not that it is good before the command.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, how do the Mu'tazilites view the relationship between command/prohibition and an act's goodness or badness?
A) The command makes the act good, and the prohibition makes it bad.
B) The command and prohibition reveal pre-existing goodness or badness in the act.
C) Goodness and badness are only known by reason, not by revelation.
D) There is no relationship between command/prohibition and moral qualities.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that command and prohibition are causes (mūjibāt) of an act's moral value, while the Mu'tazilite position is that they reveal (kashafa) a pre-existing moral value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you instinctively treat God's command as the ultimate cause of what is right and wrong, or do you rely on your own sense of goodness and expect God to command accordingly? Which approach does your daily life actually reflect? How might you realign your thinking with the Ash'ari view this lesson presents?
?
The Mu'tazilite view assumes that moral values exist independently and that God's commands merely confirm them. Have you ever assumed that your own moral intuitions are correctly aligned with divine law without seriously checking them against revelation? What would it cost you to let go of that assumption and submit completely to God's command as the source of moral value?
?
In a situation where God's command seems to contradict what you perceive as good, do you find it easier to trust that the command itself makes the act good (Ash'ari) or to assume your perception of goodness is mistaken? Recall a specific moment when you struggled with a divine ruling. How does the distinction in this lesson clarify where your trust should lie, and what specific step will you take today to act on that trust?
