Ash'aris vs Mu'tazila: Command as Cause or Consequence of Morality
Author answers distinction between Ash'ari and Mu'tazila on good/evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental difference between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila positions on whether divine command determines moral value or reveals it.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, command creates morality; for Mu'tazila, morality precedes command.

This passage addresses a core dispute in Islamic theology: what makes an action good or evil? Is it God's command itself, or is there an inherent quality that His command merely uncovers? The author presents both views concisely, showing how each school defines the relationship between divine legislation and moral value.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a clear sense of the question and the author's answer.
Notice the contrast between 'موجبات' (causes) and 'مقتضيات' (implications).
2
Identify the two positions
Find where the author states the Ash'ari view (الأمر والنهي عندنا من موجبات الحسن والقبح) and the Mu'tazila view (وعندهم من مقتضايته). Contrast their implications.
The Mu'tazila view implies that actions have an objective moral quality independent of divine command.
3
Reflect on 'prior goodness and evil'
The author says command and prohibition reveal 'prior goodness and evil' in the action's essence or aspects. Consider what this means for divine freedom and the basis of morality.
This phrase is key to understanding the Mu'tazila rationalist approach.
4
Note the author's reference
The author mentions his earlier works (Sharh al-Tanqih, etc.) for further details. Understand that this is a summary of a larger discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فأي فرق بين المدعيين في هذا القسم .
قلنا الأمر والنهي عندنا من موجبات الحسن والقبح بمعنى أن الفعل أمر به فحسن ونهى عنه فقبح، وعندهم من مقتضايته بمعنى أنه حسن فأمر به، أو قبح فنهى عنه، فالأمر والنهي إذا وردا كشفا عن حسن وقبح سابقين حاصلين للفعل لذاته أو لجهاته، ثم لكل من الفريقين تعريفات للحسن والقبيح يتناول بعضها فعل الباري وفعل غير المكلف والمباح دون البعض وقد بينا تفصيل ذلك في شرح التنقيح، وفوائد شرح مختصر الأصول .
For it is said: What is the difference between the two claimants in this section? We say: The command and prohibition according to us are among the causes of goodness and evil, meaning that an action commanded is good and prohibited is evil. According to them, they are among their implications, meaning that it is good so He commanded it, or evil so He prohibited it. So when command and prohibition come, they reveal prior goodness and evil accruing to the action in its essence or its aspects. Then for each of the two groups there are definitions of good and evil, some of which include the act of the Creator and the act of the non-obligated and the permissible, and some do not. We have detailed that in Sharh al-Tanqih and Fawa'id Sharh Mukhtasar al-Usul.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage answers a question about the difference between the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila regarding the relationship between divine command/prohibition and the goodness/evil of actions. The author first states the Ash'ari position: command and prohibition are causes (mujibat) of goodness and evil; that is, an action becomes good because it is commanded and evil because it is prohibited. Then he states the Mu'tazila position: command and prohibition are consequences (muqtadayat) of goodness and evil; that is, an action is good in itself, so God commands it, or evil so He prohibits it. On the Mu'tazila view, command and prohibition merely reveal a prior, inherent goodness or evil in the action, either in its essence or its aspects. The author notes that each group has definitions of good and evil that partially overlap (e.g., both may include God's acts and the acts of those not obligated, but not all definitions include the permissible). He refers to his earlier works for full details.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح
الأمر والنهي
موجبات
مقتضيات
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position denies that actions have any aspects that can be considered good or evil; they think morality is purely arbitrary.
Correction: The passage acknowledges that even for Ash'aris, actions have 'aspects' (jihaat) that can be considered, but the decisive factor is God's command. The Ash'ari view is not about arbitrariness but about the ultimate grounding of morality in divine will.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position, what is the relationship between command/prohibition and the goodness/evil of an action?
A) Command and prohibition reveal a prior inherent goodness or evil in the action.
B) Command and prohibition are the causes that make an action good or evil.
C) Command and prohibition are irrelevant to goodness and evil.
D) Goodness and evil are known only by reason, not by revelation.

Key Takeaway
Ash'aris believe divine command determines moral value; Mu'tazila believe divine command reveals inherent moral value.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's command alone determines what is good and evil, what does that mean for your personal moral judgments? Do you find yourself sometimes ranking your own reasoning above His command? How does that challenge your submission?
?
The Mu'tazila believe God commands what is inherently good. Does your heart tend to think that some commands of God 'make sense' while others are harder to accept? How might this passage expose a hidden assumption that you are the judge of God's wisdom?
?
In your daily life, do you obey God's commands because they are His commands, or because you see the goodness in them? How would your obedience change if you sincerely embraced the Ash'ari view that the very act of being commanded makes it good?

The Scriptural Proof from Quran 17:15
Sanusi cites Quran 17:15 as scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' serves as a scriptural proof that moral value depends on revelation, not inherent nature.

Core Idea
Punishment and moral judgment require divine revelation, not just reason.

Ahl al-Sunna begin with a clear Quranic foundation. The verse shows that Allah's punishment is contingent on sending a messenger, implying that moral accountability arises from revealed law, not from the intrinsic nature of actions. This lesson unpacks that verse and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse in context
Read the Arabic quote and its translation. Notice that the verse is from Surah al-Isra, verse 15, and is used to argue that punishment requires prior revelation.
The verse is a key proof-text against rationalist ethics.
2
Understand the implication
The verse directly opposes the Mu'tazila claim that reason alone can know good and evil. If reason could know, why would punishment wait for a messenger?
3
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how this scriptural proof fits with the rational proofs that follow. The verse establishes the principle that moral knowledge is revealed, not innate.
This is the first of several proofs in this section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا :
أدلة أهل السنة
وجوه :
من السمع قوله تعالى وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا ) (۱) ومن العقل
الأول : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح لذاته لما اختلف حسناً وقبحاً، كالقتل حداً وظلماً،
والضرب تأديباً وتعذيباً، والكذب أو الصدق إنقاذاً وإهلاكاً.
الثاني : لو كانا بالذات لما اجتمعا كما في أخبار من قال لأكذبن غداً أو هذا الذي
تکلم به کاذب .
الثالث : العبد لا يستقل بفعله لما سبق وعندهم لامدح ولا ذم من الله تعالى إلا على ما يستقل العبد به .
وأما الاستدلال بأنهما لو كانا حقيقين، وهما ثبوتيان لكونهما مقتضى اللاحسن واللاقبح العدميين لزم من اتصاف الفعل بهما قيام المعنى بالمعنى، بل قيام الموجود بالمعدوم لأنهما لكونهما الداعي والصارف يتقدمان الفعل وبأنه إذا اختلفت الأفعال حسناً وقبحاً بالذات، أو الاعتبار ويبطل اختيار الباري في شرعية الأحكام وتعيين الحلال والحرام فضعيف) .
He said to us: The proofs of Ahl al-Sunna are aspects: From scripture, His saying the Most High: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). And from reason: First: If an action were good or evil in its essence, it would not vary in goodness and evil, like killing as a legal punishment versus as oppression, beating as discipline versus as torture, and lying or truthfulness in saving versus destroying. Second: If they were by essence, they would not coexist, as in the report of one who says 'I will lie tomorrow' or 'This is what he spoke as a lie.' Third: The servant is not independent in his action because of what preceded; and according to them there is no praise or blame from God except for what the servant is independent in. As for the argument that if they were real and positive because they are required by non-good and non-evil which are negative, then the attribution of the action to them would entail the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, rather the subsistence of the existent in the non-existent, because since they are the motive and the deterrent they precede the action, and that if actions differ in goodness and evil by essence or consideration, it would invalidate the choice of the Creator in legislating rulings and designating the permissible and forbidden—so this is weak.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs of Ahl al-Sunna (the Ash'aris) against the Mu'tazila doctrine that good and evil are inherent in actions. The author introduces three types of proof: one from scripture, and three from reason. The scriptural proof is the Quranic verse 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15), indicating that punishment depends on revelation, not on rational judgment alone. The first rational proof: If an action were intrinsically good or evil, it would not vary in moral judgment, but we see killing can be good (as legal punishment) or evil (oppression), so the goodness/evil depends on the intention and context, not essence. The second proof: If goodness/evil were inseparable from essence, contradictory judgments could not coexist, but in the case of someone saying 'I will lie tomorrow' or labeling a statement as a lie, the same action (speech) can be both good and evil depending on circumstances. The third proof: The Mu'tazila claim that praise and blame from God are based on what the servant independently does, but the Ash'aris hold that the servant is not independent; thus the Mu'tazila principle fails. Then the author critiques a weak argument against the Ash'ari position: if good and evil are positive attributes, then since they arise from non-good and non-evil (negative), attributing them to an action would require a meaning to subsist in another meaning, or an existent in a non-existent, which is impossible. The author dismisses this as weak, and also notes that if actions were good/evil by essence, it would eliminate God's freedom to legislate. But he does not elaborate on the weakness.

Key Terms
السمع
الرسول
التعذيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this verse only applies to pre-Islamic nations and not to general ethics.
Correction: The Ash'aris use it universally: no one is punished without a divine message, so moral obligation is not based on reason.

Checkpoint
What does the verse 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' imply about moral knowledge?
A) Moral knowledge is innate in all humans.
B) Moral knowledge is based on reason alone.
C) Punishment requires prior revelation from Allah.
D) Punishment can occur without any warning.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse establishes that moral obligation and punishment depend on divine revelation, not on rational assessment of actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you instinctively justify it by thinking 'I'm not that bad' or 'others do worse'? How does this verse's clear condition for punishment shatter those rationalizations and force you to see every sin as a violation of God's revealed command?
?
Have you ever made a moral decision based solely on your own reasoning, ignoring what revelation says? What does this verse teach you about the danger of trusting your own judgment over God's word?
?
Think of someone you know who never received clear Islamic guidance. This verse says they are not punished until a messenger comes. Does this soften your attitude toward them? How does it challenge you to be a messenger of truth to those around you?

First Rational Proof: Variation of Moral Judgment
Sanusi presents the first rational proof with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof: if actions were good or evil by essence, they would not vary in moral judgment depending on context.

Core Idea
Actions like killing or lying can be good or bad based on intent, not essence.

The first rational proof observes that the same action can be considered good in one situation and evil in another. If goodness were intrinsic, such variation would be impossible. This lesson examines examples like killing as punishment vs. murder, and lying to save vs. to destroy.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and examples
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the examples: killing can be legal punishment (good) or oppression (evil); beating can be discipline (good) or torture (evil); truth/lies can save or destroy.
The pattern is the same act, different moral judgments.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is a conditional: if good/evil were by essence (property of the act itself), then the act could not be both good and evil. But we see the act can be both. So essence cannot be the cause.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Apply the reasoning
Think of another action that varies in moral judgment (e.g., speaking). How does this proof undermine the Mu'tazila position?
This shows moral value comes from external factors like intention and divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أدلة أهل السنة
وجوه :
من السمع قوله تعالى وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا ) (۱) ومن العقل
الأول : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح لذاته لما اختلف حسناً وقبحاً، كالقتل حداً وظلماً،
والضرب تأديباً وتعذيباً، والكذب أو الصدق إنقاذاً وإهلاكاً.

تکلم به کاذب .
الثالث : العبد لا يستقل بفعله لما سبق وعندهم لامدح ولا ذم من الله تعالى إلا على ما يستقل العبد به .
وأما الاستدلال بأنهما لو كانا حقيقين، وهما ثبوتيان لكونهما مقتضى اللاحسن واللاقبح العدميين لزم من اتصاف الفعل بهما قيام المعنى بالمعنى، بل قيام الموجود بالمعدوم لأنهما لكونهما الداعي والصارف يتقدمان الفعل وبأنه إذا اختلفت الأفعال حسناً وقبحاً بالذات، أو الاعتبار ويبطل اختيار الباري في شرعية الأحكام وتعيين الحلال والحرام فضعيف) .

The author introduces three types of proof: one from scripture, and three from reason. The scriptural proof is the Quranic verse 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15), indicating that punishment depends on revelation, not on rational judgment alone. Checkpoint
According to the first rational proof, why can't good and evil be intrinsic to an action?
A) Because all actions are neutral.
B) Because the same action can be both good and evil in different contexts.
C) Because actions are always good if intended well.
D) Because reason cannot judge actions.

Key Takeaway
The same action can be good or evil depending on context and intention, so moral value is not part of the action's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent action you did—like speaking a truth or a lie—that could be seen as good or evil depending on your motive. What was your true intention? Are you willing to submit your motive to Allah's judgment today?
?
Have you ever condemned someone's action without knowing their context or intention? How does this proof expose the arrogance of absolute moral judgments that ignore the full picture? What will you do next time you feel quick to judge?
?
This proof shows that even killing can be good if it is a just punishment. Is there a belief or habit you hold that you consider 'obviously good' but which might actually be evil in Allah's sight because of your intention? Ask Him to purify your heart.

Second Rational Proof: Coexistence of Good and Evil
Sanusi presents the second rational proof using language examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof: if good and evil were intrinsic, contradictory judgments would be impossible, but we can say things like 'I will lie tomorrow' where the same speech act is both a lie (evil) and a statement (good or neutral).

Core Idea
Contradictory moral judgments on the same action show it cannot be intrinsically good or evil.

The second proof uses a subtle case: a person says 'I will lie tomorrow,' or labels a statement as a lie. Here, the same utterance is considered both a lie (evil) and a statement that may be true (good). If good and evil were intrinsic, such coexistence would be impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Examine the example
Read the example: someone says 'I will lie tomorrow' or 'This is what he spoke as a lie.' In the first, the statement itself is a lie (if true) or a promise (if false). In the second, the speaker labels a statement as a lie, making the label true if the statement is false, but the label might itself be a lie if the original statement was true.
This is a logical puzzle from language.
2
Understand the contradiction
If good/evil are intrinsic, then the same speech act cannot be both good and evil. But in these cases, the act seems to have both qualities depending on how we view it. This shows that moral value is not in the act itself.
3
Reflect on the implication
The proof demonstrates that moral judgments can be relative to perspective, undermining the Mu'tazila claim that actions have fixed moral properties.
This is a more advanced proof used by scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا :
أدلة أهل السنة
وجوه :
من السمع قوله تعالى وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا ) (۱) ومن العقل
الأول : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح لذاته لما اختلف حسناً وقبحاً، كالقتل حداً وظلماً،
والضرب تأديباً وتعذيباً، والكذب أو الصدق إنقاذاً وإهلاكاً.
الثاني : لو كانا بالذات لما اجتمعا كما في أخبار من قال لأكذبن غداً أو هذا الذي
تکلم به کاذب .
الثالث : العبد لا يستقل بفعله لما سبق وعندهم لامدح ولا ذم من الله تعالى إلا على ما يستقل العبد به .
وأما الاستدلال بأنهما لو كانا حقيقين، وهما ثبوتيان لكونهما مقتضى اللاحسن واللاقبح العدميين لزم من اتصاف الفعل بهما قيام المعنى بالمعنى، بل قيام الموجود بالمعدوم لأنهما لكونهما الداعي والصارف يتقدمان الفعل وبأنه إذا اختلفت الأفعال حسناً وقبحاً بالذات، أو الاعتبار ويبطل اختيار الباري في شرعية الأحكام وتعيين الحلال والحرام فضعيف) .
He said to us: The proofs of Ahl al-Sunna are aspects: From scripture, His saying the Most High: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). And from reason: First: If an action were good or evil in its essence, it would not vary in goodness and evil, like killing as a legal punishment versus as oppression, beating as discipline versus as torture, and lying or truthfulness in saving versus destroying. Second: If they were by essence, they would not coexist, as in the report of one who says 'I will lie tomorrow' or 'This is what he spoke as a lie.' Third: The servant is not independent in his action because of what preceded; and according to them there is no praise or blame from God except for what the servant is independent in. As for the argument that if they were real and positive because they are required by non-good and non-evil which are negative, then the attribution of the action to them would entail the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, rather the subsistence of the existent in the non-existent, because since they are the motive and the deterrent they precede the action, and that if actions differ in goodness and evil by essence or consideration, it would invalidate the choice of the Creator in legislating rulings and designating the permissible and forbidden—so this is weak.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs of Ahl al-Sunna (the Ash'aris) against the Mu'tazila doctrine that good and evil are inherent in actions. The author introduces three types of proof: one from scripture, and three from reason. The scriptural proof is the Quranic verse 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15), indicating that punishment depends on revelation, not on rational judgment alone. The first rational proof: If an action were intrinsically good or evil, it would not vary in moral judgment, but we see killing can be good (as legal punishment) or evil (oppression), so the goodness/evil depends on the intention and context, not essence. The second proof: If goodness/evil were inseparable from essence, contradictory judgments could not coexist, but in the case of someone saying 'I will lie tomorrow' or labeling a statement as a lie, the same action (speech) can be both good and evil depending on circumstances. The third proof: The Mu'tazila claim that praise and blame from God are based on what the servant independently does, but the Ash'aris hold that the servant is not independent; thus the Mu'tazila principle fails. Then the author critiques a weak argument against the Ash'ari position: if good and evil are positive attributes, then since they arise from non-good and non-evil (negative), attributing them to an action would require a meaning to subsist in another meaning, or an existent in a non-existent, which is impossible. The author dismisses this as weak, and also notes that if actions were good/evil by essence, it would eliminate God's freedom to legislate. But he does not elaborate on the weakness.

Key Terms
الاجماع
الكذب
الصدق
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this proof is just wordplay with no real substance.
Correction: It is a valid logical argument that exposes the problem of assuming fixed moral attributes in language.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, why does the statement 'I will lie tomorrow' pose a problem for intrinsic good/evil?
A) It shows that lying is always evil.
B) It shows that the same utterance can be considered both a lie and truthful.
C) It shows that intentions don't matter.
D) It proves that Allah chooses moral values.

Key Takeaway
Contradictory moral judgments can apply to the same action, proving that good and evil are not inherent in the action itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you judge an action as good or evil, do you tend to think the action is always that way, or do you consider the circumstances? What does this lesson reveal about the source of your moral judgments?
?
If good and evil are not intrinsic to actions but depend on God's command, how does that affect the way you weigh decisions where the ruling is unclear? Will you actively seek divine guidance rather than rely on your own intuition?
?
Think of a time you were certain an action was always wrong, but later found a context where it could be permissible. How does this proof challenge the pride you might feel in your own moral reasoning? Are you willing to submit your judgments to revelation?

Third Rational Proof: Servant's Independence and Divine Praise
Sanusi presents the third rational proof on servant independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof: the Mu'tazila claim that praise and blame are based on the servant's independent action is invalid because the servant is not independent in their actions.

Core Idea
If the servant is not the real agent, then praise/blame cannot be based on their supposed independence.

The third proof addresses the Mu'tazila claim that divine praise and blame are contingent on the servant's independent choice. The Ash'aris respond that the servant does not act independently; all actions are created by Allah. Therefore, the very basis of the Mu'tazila argument collapses. This lesson explains that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic: 'العبد لا يستقل بفعله لما سبق وعندهم لامدح ولا ذم من الله تعالى إلا على ما يستقل العبد به.' The author asserts the servant's lack of independence and shows the Mu'tazila contradiction.
The proof is concise but requires understanding of Ash'ari occasionalism.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Mu'tazila believe humans have free will and are ultimate agents of their actions, so they deserve praise or blame. Ash'aris deny that, saying Allah is the creator of all actions.
3
Grasp Ash'ari counterargument
Since the servant is not independent, the condition (praise/blame for independent acts) is not met, so the Mu'tazila framework fails. This indirectly supports the Ash'ari view that moral value depends on Allah's command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

أدلة أهل السنة

?
The Mu'tazila believed they could earn praise from Allah by their own independent acts. In your heart, do you sometimes feel that you 'deserve' Allah's reward because of your efforts? Examine that feeling: does it contradict the Ash'ari teaching that all acts are from Allah?

Scriptural Proof: Punishment Requires Revelation
Al-Taftazani cites a Quranic verse as scriptural proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse (17:15) supports the Sunni position that good and evil are not intrinsic but determined by divine command.

Core Idea
Punishment only comes after a messenger is sent, showing reason alone cannot judge good and evil.

This lesson introduces the scriptural proof from the Quran that moral responsibility depends on revelation. Al-Taftazani begins his presentation of Sunni proofs with this verse, establishing that Allah does not punish without first sending a messenger.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how al-Taftazani introduces it as the first proof 'from revelation' (min al-samʿ).
The verse is from Surah al-Isra, 17:15.
2
Understand the implication
Ask yourself: why does this verse argue against intrinsic good and evil? Because if good and evil were known by reason alone, punishment would be possible without a messenger.
This verse shows that moral judgment is dependent on revealed law.
3
Connect to the Sunni position
Recognize that this proof supports the Ashʿarī and Māturīdī view that actions become good or evil only through divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا : أدلة أهل السنة وجوه : من السمع قوله تعالى وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا ) (۱) ومن العقل الأول : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح لذاته لما اختلف حسناً وقبحاً، كالقتل حداً وظلماً، والضرب تأديباً وتعذيباً، والكذب أو الصدق إنقاذاً وإهلاكاً. الثاني : لو كانا بالذات لما اجتمعا كما في أخبار من قال لأكذبن غداً أو هذا الذي تكلم به كاذب . الثالث : العبد لا يستقل بفعله لما سبق وعندهم لامدح ولا ذم من الله تعالى إلا على ما يستقل العبد به . وأما الاستدلال بأنهما لو كانا حقيقين، وهما ثبوتيان لكونهما مقتضى اللاحسن واللاقبح العدميين لزم من اتصاف الفعل بهما قيام المعنى بالمعنى، بل قيام الموجود بالمعدوم لأنهما لكونهما الداعي والصارف يتقدمان الفعل وبأنه إذا اختلفت الأفعال حسناً وقبحاً بالذات، أو الاعتبار ويبطل اختيار الباري في شرعية الأحكام وتعيين الحلال والحرام فضعيف) .
Our master said: The proofs of the People of the Sunnah are aspects: from revelation is His saying the Most High: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). And from reason: First: If an act were good or evil in its essence, it would not differ in goodness and evilness, like killing as a prescribed punishment and as tyranny, and striking as discipline and torment, and lying or truthfulness for rescue and destruction. Second: If they were by essence, they would not combine as in the report of one who says 'Tomorrow I will surely lie' or 'this is false which he spoke.' Third: The servant is not independent in his action due to what preceded, and according to them there is no praise or blame from God Most High except for what the servant is independent in. As for the argument that if they were real, and they are positive because they are required by the good and evil which are negative, then from the description of the act by them would follow the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, nay, the subsistence of an existent in a non-existent because they, being the inviter and the preventer, precede the act; and because if acts differ in goodness and evilness by essence or by consideration and it voids the choice of the Creator in legislating rulings and determining the permissible and forbidden – then it is weak.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs of the People of the Sunnah (Ahl al-Sunna) that good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh) are not intrinsic to acts (dhat al-fiʿl) but are determined by divine command. It begins with a scriptural proof from the Quran (17:15) that punishment only occurs after sending a messenger, implying that reason alone does not determine good and evil. Then it gives three rational proofs: (1) If acts were good or evil in their essence, they would not vary in moral evaluation depending on context—e.g., killing can be good (as legal punishment) or evil (as murder). (2) If goodness/evil were intrinsic, they could not logically coexist in the same act, but we find cases where the same action (e.g., a statement) can be both good and evil depending on intent. (3) The servant (al-ʿabd) is not independent in his actions; therefore praise and blame from God are only for what the servant does independently, implying that moral value is not inherent but assigned. Finally, the passage refutes the opposing claim that good/evil are real qualities (haqiqiyan) and positive (thubutiyan). It argues that if they were real, they would be causes (daʿi and sarif) that precede the act, leading to the absurdity of an attribute subsisting in a non-existent (maʿdum). This shows the weakness of the opposing view.

Key Terms
السمع
الرسول
العذاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good and evil can be known by human reason alone, independent of revelation.
Correction: The verse shows that punishment only occurs after a messenger is sent, indicating that moral values are determined by divine law, not pure reason.

Checkpoint
According to the verse cited, when does divine punishment occur?
A) Immediately after a sin is committed.
B) Only after a messenger is sent.
C) Only after human reasoning.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Divine punishment depends on revelation, not on human reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied solely on your own reasoning to judge an action as good or bad, without consulting revelation? What was the outcome and what did you learn from that experience?
?
This verse says Allah does not punish without sending a messenger. Does that affect how you view people who have never heard of Islam? How does that challenge your assumptions about God's justice?
?
If good and evil are ultimately determined by revelation, how does that change the way you approach moral decisions in your daily life? Are you actively seeking divine guidance or trusting your own opinion?

First Rational Proof: Moral Variation with Context
Al-Taftazani gives the first rational proof based on variation of moral judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that good and evil are not intrinsic because the same act can be good or evil depending on circumstances.

Core Idea
If good/evil were intrinsic, the same act would always be the same—but it varies.

Al-Taftazani presents a rational argument: if an act were good or evil in its essence, it would never change its moral value. Yet we see that acts like killing, striking, and lying can be either good or evil depending on context. This proves that their moral quality is not intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Read the first rational proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the examples given: killing as punishment versus murder, striking as discipline versus torture, lying vs. truth for rescue or destruction.
2
Understand the logic
The argument is: if an act were good or evil by its own essence (li-dhatihi), it would always have that moral value. But we observe that the same type of act can be good in one situation and evil in another. Therefore, the act's moral value is not part of its essence.
For example, killing is good as legal punishment but evil as murder.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the example of lying: lying to save a life is considered good (or at least excused), but lying to harm someone is evil. This variation shows that moral value depends on factors like intention and circumstance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An act like killing is always evil regardless of context.
Correction: Killing can be good in some contexts (e.g., legal punishment), so its moral value is context-dependent.

Checkpoint
Which example does al-Taftazani use to show that the same act can be both good and evil?
A) Giving charity and stealing.

D) Speaking truth and lying.

Key Takeaway
Moral values vary with context, proving they are not intrinsic to acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where you quickly judged someone's action as wrong. Did you consider the context and intention behind it, or did you assume the act itself was evil? How will this lesson change your immediate judgments tomorrow?
?
Can you identify a moral rule you hold that you have never questioned? How would you test it using the same reasoning as al-Taftazani's proof—by finding a counterexample where the same act is considered good in another context?
?
If moral values are not intrinsic, what does that mean for your own accountability? Are you more responsible for examining the context and intentions behind your actions, rather than simply following a checklist of right and wrong? Commit to one change in how you evaluate your own actions from now on.

Second Rational Proof: Coexistence of Good and Evil
Al-Taftazani gives the second rational proof based on coexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that good and evil cannot be intrinsic because they would sometimes have to coexist in the same act, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If good/evil were intrinsic, they could not coexist—but they can, as in a false statement that is both good and evil.

Al-Taftazani's second proof argues that if goodness and evil were intrinsic properties, they could never be present in the same act simultaneously. However, consider someone saying 'Tomorrow I will surely lie'—this statement can be both truthful (good) and a lie (evil) in different respects, showing they are not intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the example: a person says 'Tomorrow I will surely lie' or a statement that is false.
2
Analyze the example
The statement 'Tomorrow I will surely lie' is a truth-telling about a future lie. As a report about the future, it is true, but it also involves an intended lie. So the same act (the utterance) has both good (truthfulness) and evil (lying) aspects.
Alternatively, consider 'this which he spoke is false' – a statement about another statement.
3
Understand the conclusion
If goodness and evil were intrinsic, they could not both inhere in the same act. But they do, therefore they are not intrinsic; they are relative to different considerations.
This proof is similar to the first but focuses on simultaneity rather than variation across contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال لنا : أدلة أهل السنة وجوه : من السمع قوله تعالى وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا ) (۱) ومن العقل الأول : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح لذاته لما اختلف حسناً وقبحاً، كالقتل حداً وظلماً، والضرب تأديباً وتعذيباً، والكذب أو الصدق إنقاذاً وإهلاكاً. الثاني : لو كانا بالذات لما اجتمعا كما في أخبار من قال لأكذبن غداً أو هذا الذي تكلم به كاذب . الثالث : العبد لا يستقل بفعله لما سبق وعندهم لامدح ولا ذم من الله تعالى إلا على ما يستقل العبد به . وأما الاستدلال بأنهما لو كانا حقيقين، وهما ثبوتيان لكونهما مقتضى اللاحسن واللاقبح العدميين لزم من اتصاف الفعل بهما قيام المعنى بالمعنى، بل قيام الموجود بالمعدوم لأنهما لكونهما الداعي والصارف يتقدمان الفعل وبأنه إذا اختلفت الأفعال حسناً وقبحاً بالذات، أو الاعتبار ويبطل اختيار الباري في شرعية الأحكام وتعيين الحلال والحرام فضعيف) .
Our master said: The proofs of the People of the Sunnah are aspects: from revelation is His saying the Most High: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15). And from reason: First: If an act were good or evil in its essence, it would not differ in goodness and evilness, like killing as a prescribed punishment and as tyranny, and striking as discipline and torment, and lying or truthfulness for rescue and destruction. Second: If they were by essence, they would not combine as in the report of one who says 'Tomorrow I will surely lie' or 'this is false which he spoke.' Third: The servant is not independent in his action due to what preceded, and according to them there is no praise or blame from God Most High except for what the servant is independent in. As for the argument that if they were real, and they are positive because they are required by the good and evil which are negative, then from the description of the act by them would follow the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, nay, the subsistence of an existent in a non-existent because they, being the inviter and the preventer, precede the act; and because if acts differ in goodness and evilness by essence or by consideration and it voids the choice of the Creator in legislating rulings and determining the permissible and forbidden – then it is weak.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs of the People of the Sunnah (Ahl al-Sunna) that good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh) are not intrinsic to acts (dhat al-fiʿl) but are determined by divine command. It begins with a scriptural proof from the Quran (17:15) that punishment only occurs after sending a messenger, implying that reason alone does not determine good and evil. Then it gives three rational proofs: (1) If acts were good or evil in their essence, they would not vary in moral evaluation depending on context—e.g., killing can be good (as legal punishment) or evil (as murder). (2) If goodness/evil were intrinsic, they could not logically coexist in the same act, but we find cases where the same action (e.g., a statement) can be both good and evil depending on intent. (3) The servant (al-ʿabd) is not independent in his actions; therefore praise and blame from God are only for what the servant does independently, implying that moral value is not inherent but assigned. Finally, the passage refutes the opposing claim that good/evil are real qualities (haqiqiyan) and positive (thubutiyan). It argues that if they were real, they would be causes (daʿi and sarif) that precede the act, leading to the absurdity of an attribute subsisting in a non-existent (maʿdum). This shows the weakness of the opposing view.

Key Terms
الاجماع
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An act cannot be both good and evil at the same time, so moral values are singular.
Correction: An act can be good from one aspect and evil from another, showing moral values are relative, not intrinsic.

Checkpoint
What is the key problem if goodness and evil were intrinsic, according to the second proof?
A) They would never combine in the same act.
B) They would always be subjective.
C) They would be known by reason.
D) They would be determined by divine command.

Key Takeaway
Good and evil can coexist in one act, proving they are not intrinsic.

Third Rational Proof and Refutation of Opposing Argument
Al-Taftazani gives the third rational proof and refutes the opposing argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational proof that the servant is not independent, and why the opposing argument that good/evil are real and positive is weak.

Core Idea
The servant's lack of independence and the absurdity of making good/evil real attributes show the Sunni position.

Al-Taftazani presents the third rational proof: the servant (human) is not independent in his actions, so praise and blame from Allah are only for actions the servant performs independently. This implies that moral value is not intrinsic but assigned. Then he refutes the opposing view by showing the logical absurdities if good and evil were real (haqiqiyan) and positive (thubutiyan) attributes subsisting in the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the Arabic and translation: 'The servant is not independent in his action due to what preceded, and according to them there is no praise or blame from God except for what the servant is independent in.'
This proof ties moral responsibility to the servant's capacity.
2
Understand the refutation
Read the refutation: 'As for the argument that if they were real, and they are positive... it is weak.' The refutation argues that if good and evil were real attributes, they would precede the act as inviter (daʿi) and preventer (sarif), leading to the absurdity of an attribute subsisting in a non-existent act.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite view that good/evil are real qualities.
3
Connect to the overall argument
Both the third proof and the refutation reinforce that good and evil are not intrinsic but are determined by divine command and human capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
When you do something good, do you feel entitled to praise or recognition? This lesson says praise from Allah is only for what the servant is independent in—and you are not independent. Are you willing to let go of the need for human praise and seek only His pleasure, knowing that even your good deeds are by His grace?
?
The refutation argues that if good and evil were intrinsic, they would void Allah's choice in legislation. Do you sometimes act as if moral values are self-evident and independent of Allah's command, judging things by your own sense of right and wrong? Are you ready to submit your moral intuitions to divine revelation, even when it feels uncomfortable?

First Ash'ari Proof: Punishment Only After Revelation
Al-Taftazani presents the first Ash'ari proof that moral obligation requires revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'ari argument that moral values are not rationally known but depend on revelation.

Core Idea
Revelation, not reason, determines moral obligation and its punishment.

Al-Taftazani presents the first proof from the Ash'ari school against rationalist ethics. The argument shows that if good and evil were inherent in actions and known by reason, then punishment would be due even without a prophet. But the Qur'an explicitly ties punishment to the sending of messengers, so reason alone cannot judge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani introduces two types of proofs before giving the first.
2
Understand the conditional statement
The proof assumes a conditional: if good/evil were rationally known, then punishment would be obligatory even without revelation. Identify the basis for this conditional in Mu'tazilite thought.
The conditional relies on the Mu'tazilite principle that it is obligatory to punish one who deserves it if unrepentant.
3
Grasp the refutation using the Qur'anic verse
The consequent is false because Q 17:15 states punishment only occurs after sending a messenger. Therefore, the antecedent (rational good/evil) must be false.
This shows that the Ash'aris use revelation itself to argue that reason cannot independently determine moral obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك أصحابنا بوجوه يدل بعضها على أن الحسن والقبح ليسا لذات الفعل ولا الجهات واعتبارات فيه وبعضها على أنهما ليسا لذاته خاصة . ، الأول : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح عقلاً لزم تعذيب تارك الواجب، ومرتكب الحرام، سواء ورد الشرع أم لا بناء على أصلهم في وجوب تعذيب من استحقه إذا مات غير تائب واللازم باطل لقوله تعالى وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا (۱)
Our companions adhered to proofs, some of which indicate that good and evil are not in the essence of the act nor in the respects and considerations in it, and some of which indicate that they are not in the essence specifically. The first: If an act were good or evil rationally, it would necessitate punishing the abandoner of the obligatory and the committer of the forbidden, whether the Law has come or not, based on their principle that it is obligatory to punish one who deserves it if he dies unrepentant. But the consequent is false because of His saying the Most High: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' (17:15).
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This lesson presents the first of the proofs from 'our companions' (aṣḥābunā), i.e., the Ashʿarīs, that good and evil are not intrinsic to acts. The introduction states that some proofs show that good/evil are neither in the essence of the act nor in its aspects/considerations, while others show they are not in the essence alone. The first proof argues: If good and evil were rationally known (ʿaqlan), then one who abandons a duty or commits a forbidden act would deserve punishment even without revelation, according to the Muʿtazilite principle that punishment is obligatory for one who dies unrepentant. But this is false because the Quran says punishment only occurs after sending a messenger. Therefore, good and evil cannot be known by reason alone; they depend on revelation.

Key Terms
الحسن والقبح
العقل
الشرع
تعذيب
الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris deny any role for reason in ethics.
Correction: This proof only denies that reason alone can determine moral obligation and punishment; it does not deny reason's role altogether. Reason can know some truths, but moral obligation requires revelation.

Checkpoint
What does the first Ash'ari proof argue would be the consequence if good/evil were rationally known?
A) There would be no need for prophets.
B) Punishment would be due for some acts even without revelation.
C) All acts would be equally good.
D) Human reason would be sufficient for moral guidance.

Key Takeaway
The first Ash'ari proof uses Qur'an 17:15 to show that punishment only follows revelation, so reason alone cannot determine moral obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you confident that the message you have received from Allah is enough to hold you accountable? Have you responded to it with true obedience, or are you living as if you haven't heard it,  relying on God's mercy without action?
?
This proof shows that God does not punish without sending a messenger. Who are the messengers in your life—the scholars, the Quran, the reminders? Are you taking them seriously, or are you ignoring the warnings because you think you are safe?
?
If you believe that punishment only comes after revelation, what does that mean for those around you who have never heard Islam's message clearly? What is your responsibility to ensure they are not left without warning?

The Disjunction: Acts are Compulsive or Accidental
Al-Taftazani presents the disjunction that human acts are either compulsive or accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ashʿarīs argue that if moral values were rational, every human act would be either compulsive or accidental, and thus not subject to moral judgment.

Core Idea
Human acts are either forced or random—neither can be good or evil.

This lesson presents the core of the second Ashʿarī proof. The author argues that the only possible sources for a human act are compulsion or chance, and neither supports rational moral evaluation. By tracing the causal chain, he aims to show that human independence is illusory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow take shape.
Notice the compressed style; each clause builds on the previous.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage classifies every human act as either compulsive or accidental. Understand each definition and why neither can be morally evaluated.
Compulsive = no ability to refrain. Accidental = occurs without a sufficient reason.
3
Examine the causal reasoning
The argument uses a preponderator (murajjiḥ) to analyze acts. Trace each branch: no preponderator → accidental; preponderator from servant → infinite regress; preponderator from outside → either unnecessary (same problem) or necessary (compulsion).
Focus on the logical necessity of ending at an external cause.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Understand why both branches lead to acts that cannot be rationally good or evil. The conclusion is that the original implication is proven.
This sets up the objection and response in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان الحسن والقبح بالعقل لما كان شيء من أفعال العباد حسناً ولا قبيحاً عقلاً، واللازم باطل باعترافكم . وجه اللزوم أن فعل العبد إما اضطراري، و إما اتفاقي. ولا شيء منهما بحسن ولا قبيح عقلاً. أما الكبرى فبالاتفاق ، وأما الصغرى فلأن العبد إن لم يتمكن من الترك فذاك ، وإن تمكن فإن لم يتوقف الفعل على مرجح ، بل صدر عنه تارة ، ولم يصدر أخرى بلا تجدد أمر كان اتفاقياً على أنه يفضي إلى الترجح بلا مرجح ؛ وفيه انسداد باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن توقف فذلك المرجح إن كان من العبد فينقل الكلام إليه ويتسلسل، وإن لم يكن فمعه إن لم يجب الفعل ، بل صح الصدور واللا صدور، عاد الترديد ولزم المحذور، وإن وجب فالفعل اضطراري والعبد مجبور. واعترض بأن المرجح هو الإرادة التي شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص، وصدور الفعل معه عندنا على سبيل الصحة دون الوجوب إلا عند أبي الحسين. ولو سلم فالوجوب بالاختيار لا ينافي الاختيار ولا يوجب الاضطرار المنافي للحسن وصحة التكليف وأجيب أنه قد ثبت بالدليل لزوم الانتهاء إلى مرجح لا يكون من العبد، ويجب معه الفعل، ويبطل استقلال العبد، ومثله لا يحسن ولا يقبح ، ولا يصح التكليف به. عندهم وأما الاعتراض بأنه استقلال في مقابلة الضرورة، ومنقوض بفعل الباري فقد عرفت جوابه .
Second: If good and evil were by reason, then none of the acts of servants would be good or evil rationally, and the consequent is false by your own admission. The aspect of the implication: The act of the servant is either compulsive (iḍṭirārī) or accidental (ittifāqī). Neither of them is good or evil rationally. As for the major premise, it is by agreement. As for the minor premise, the servant either is not able to refrain – then that; or if he is able, then if the act does not depend on a preponderator (murajjiḥ), but rather emanates from him sometimes and not at other times without a new occurrence, it is accidental, leading to preponderance without a preponderator; and in this is the blockage of the door to proving the Maker. And if it does depend, then that preponderator if it is from the servant, the discourse is transferred to it and it leads to infinite regress; if it is not, then with it, if the act is not necessary, but rather both emanation and non-emanation are possible, the equivocation returns and the detriment occurs; if it is necessary, the act is compulsive and the servant is coerced. And it was objected that the preponderator is the will (irāda) which is characterized by preponderating and specifying, and the emanation of the act with it according to us is on the way of permissibility (ṣiḥḥa) not obligation, except according to Abu al-Ḥusayn. And even if it is conceded, obligation by choice does not negate choice nor necessitate compulsion which contradicts goodness and the validity of obligation. And it was answered: It has been established by proof the necessity of ending at a preponderator that is not from the servant, and the act is necessary with it, and the independence of the servant is nullified, and something like this is neither good nor evil, nor is obligation valid with it according to them. As for the objection that it is independence in the face of necessity, and it is refuted by the act of the Creator – you have already known its answer.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This lesson contains the second proof of the Ashʿarīs, which is a complex argument linking moral evaluation to determinism. The proof claims that if good/evil were determined by reason, then no human act would be rationally good or evil. This is shown by analyzing the nature of human acts: every act is either compulsive (iḍṭirārī) – the agent has no power to refrain – or accidental (ittifāqī) – it occurs without a sufficient reason. Neither type is subject to moral judgment. The argument further explores causation: if an act occurs without a preponderator (murajjiḥ), it is accidental; if it depends on a preponderator, that preponderator must ultimately come from outside the servant, making the act compulsive. An objection is raised that the preponderator is the servant's will, which does not compel but merely makes the act possible. The reply asserts that the chain of preponderators must end in a cause outside the servant, which necessitates the act and nullifies the servant's independence, thereby eliminating the basis for moral evaluation. This proof ties into the Ashʿarī doctrine of acquisition (kasb) and the denial of human causal efficacy.

Key Terms
الاضطراري
الاتفاقي
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if an act depends on a preponderator that is not from the servant and makes the act necessary, what is the status of the act?
A) Accidental and not subject to moral judgment
B) Compulsive and not subject to moral judgment
C) Voluntary and rationally good or evil
D) Contingent and morally neutral

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarī proof concludes that rational moral evaluation of human acts is impossible because all acts are either compelled or accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice, do you ever feel that your decision was forced by circumstances? How does that feeling align with the Ashʿarī claim that all acts are either compulsive or accidental?
?
If moral value is not intrinsic to actions but depends solely on God's command, how should that change the way you prioritize divine revelation over your own reasoning in daily moral dilemmas?
?
The argument says accidental acts lead to a logical absurdity that blocks proving the existence of a Creator. Do you base your belief in God on rational arguments like this, or primarily on other grounds? If this proof were flawed, would your faith be shaken?

Objection and Reply: Will as Preponderator
Al-Taftazani records an objection and gives the Ashʿarī reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the second Ashʿarī proof and how the Ashʿarīs defended their position.

Core Idea
Even if the will preponderates, the chain must end in an external cause, nullifying human independence.

The second proof faced an objection: the preponderator is the servant's will, which permits action without necessity. The Ashʿarī response argues that the will itself must be determined by something external, ultimately leading to compulsion and the impossibility of moral evaluation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector claims that the preponderator is the servant's will, which allows the act without compulsion. Note the exception of Abu al-Ḥusayn.
The will is said to 'specify' (takhṣīṣ) one alternative.
2
Understand the concession
Even if one concedes that the act becomes necessary through choice (wujūb bi al-ikhtiyār), the objector says this does not negate choice or imply compulsion.
This is a common Muʿtazilī argument.
3
Examine the Ashʿarī rejoinder
The reply asserts that the chain of preponderators must end in an external cause that necessitates the act, nullifying the servant's independence. Such an act cannot be good or evil.
This is the core of the proof: true independence is impossible.
4
Note the final dismissal
A secondary objection about the act of the Creator is dismissed by referring to an earlier answer.
The author assumes the reader has already encountered this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان الحسن والقبح بالعقل لما كان شيء من أفعال العباد حسناً ولا قبيحاً عقلاً، واللازم باطل باعترافكم . وجه اللزوم أن فعل العبد إما اضطراري، و إما اتفاقي. ولا شيء منهما بحسن ولا قبيح عقلاً. أما الكبرى فبالاتفاق ، وأما الصغرى فلأن العبد إن لم يتمكن من الترك فذاك ، وإن تمكن فإن لم يتوقف الفعل على مرجح ، بل صدر عنه تارة ، ولم يصدر أخرى بلا تجدد أمر كان اتفاقياً على أنه يفضي إلى الترجح بلا مرجح ؛ وفيه انسداد باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن توقف فذلك المرجح إن كان من العبد فينقل الكلام إليه ويتسلسل، وإن لم يكن فمعه إن لم يجب الفعل ، بل صح الصدور واللا صدور، عاد الترديد ولزم المحذور، وإن وجب فالفعل اضطراري والعبد مجبور. واعترض بأن المرجح هو الإرادة التي شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص، وصدور الفعل معه عندنا على سبيل الصحة دون الوجوب إلا عند أبي الحسين. ولو سلم فالوجوب بالاختيار لا ينافي الاختيار ولا يوجب الاضطرار المنافي للحسن وصحة التكليف وأجيب أنه قد ثبت بالدليل لزوم الانتهاء إلى مرجح لا يكون من العبد، ويجب معه الفعل، ويبطل استقلال العبد، ومثله لا يحسن ولا يقبح ، ولا يصح التكليف به. عندهم وأما الاعتراض بأنه استقلال في مقابلة الضرورة، ومنقوض بفعل الباري فقد عرفت جوابه .
Second: If good and evil were by reason, then none of the acts of servants would be good or evil rationally, and the consequent is false by your own admission. The aspect of the implication: The act of the servant is either compulsive (iḍṭirārī) or accidental (ittifāqī). Neither of them is good or evil rationally. As for the major premise, it is by agreement. As for the minor premise, the servant either is not able to refrain – then that; or if he is able, then if the act does not depend on a preponderator (murajjiḥ), but rather emanates from him sometimes and not at other times without a new occurrence, it is accidental, leading to preponderance without a preponderator; and in this is the blockage of the door to proving the Maker. And if it does depend, then that preponderator if it is from the servant, the discourse is transferred to it and it leads to infinite regress; if it is not, then with it, if the act is not necessary, but rather both emanation and non-emanation are possible, the equivocation returns and the detriment occurs; if it is necessary, the act is compulsive and the servant is coerced. And it was objected that the preponderator is the will (irāda) which is characterized by preponderating and specifying, and the emanation of the act with it according to us is on the way of permissibility (ṣiḥḥa) not obligation, except according to Abu al-Ḥusayn. And even if it is conceded, obligation by choice does not negate choice nor necessitate compulsion which contradicts goodness and the validity of obligation. And it was answered: It has been established by proof the necessity of ending at a preponderator that is not from the servant, and the act is necessary with it, and the independence of the servant is nullified, and something like this is neither good nor evil, nor is obligation valid with it according to them. As for the objection that it is independence in the face of necessity, and it is refuted by the act of the Creator – you have already known its answer.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This lesson contains the second proof of the Ashʿarīs, which is a complex argument linking moral evaluation to determinism. The proof claims that if good/evil were determined by reason, then no human act would be rationally good or evil. This is shown by analyzing the nature of human acts: every act is either compulsive (iḍṭirārī) – the agent has no power to refrain – or accidental (ittifāqī) – it occurs without a sufficient reason. Neither type is subject to moral judgment. The argument further explores causation: if an act occurs without a preponderator (murajjiḥ), it is accidental; if it depends on a preponderator, that preponderator must ultimately come from outside the servant, making the act compulsive. An objection is raised that the preponderator is the servant's will, which does not compel but merely makes the act possible. The reply asserts that the chain of preponderators must end in a cause outside the servant, which necessitates the act and nullifies the servant's independence, thereby eliminating the basis for moral evaluation. This proof ties into the Ashʿarī doctrine of acquisition (kasb) and the denial of human causal efficacy.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الوجوب بالاختيار
استقلال العبد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ashʿarī response, why does the servant's will not prevent the act from being compulsive?
A) Because the will itself is the ultimate cause
B) Because the will is determined by an external cause
C) Because the will has no effect on the act
D) Because the act is always accidental

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarīs maintain that any chain of causation must end in an external necessity, making human acts determined and thus outside the scope of rational moral judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every act you perform—every thought, every movement—is ultimately determined by God's will? If so, how does that change the way you see your own choices? Are you willing to thank God for every good deed as His gift and to repent for every sin as your failure, knowing that even your failure is under His control?
?
This passage claims that without an external cause, your acts cannot be good or evil. When you commit a sin, do you feel less responsible because you think God caused it? Or do you still feel accountable? How can you reconcile the Ashʿarī teaching with your heartfelt remorse and desire to change?
?
If your independence is nullified, what is left of your personal striving for righteousness? Will you continue to struggle against your desires knowing that the outcome depends solely on God? Or will you let go of effort and rely only on His decree? Be honest: which path does your heart take, and what would it mean to fully submit to this teaching?

Third Ash'ari Proof: Lying for a Noble Cause
Al-Taftazani argues that lying can be good when saving a prophet, disproving intrinsic evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris use the example of saving a prophet to argue that moral values are not intrinsic but are determined by divine command.

Core Idea
Lying can be good when it saves a prophet – proving moral value depends on context.

This lesson presents the third Ash'ari proof against the idea that acts are inherently good or evil. Al-Taftazani argues that if lying were evil in its essence, it would never be good in any situation. But we find that lying to save a prophet from destruction is obligatory and therefore good, showing that moral value is not intrinsic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation to understand the argument clearly.
Pay attention to the conditional structure.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Notice the first part: 'If the evil of lying were in its essence (لذاته), it would not be absent from it in any case.' This is the key assumption being tested.
3
Examine the counterexample
The passage gives a specific case: lying to save a prophet from destruction. It says this lying is obligatory (يجب قطعاً) and thus good (يحسن). This contradicts the conditional premise's necessary conclusion.
4
Recognize the broader principle
The author then extends the argument: other acts like killing and striking can be good (as punishment) or evil (as tyranny), showing that moral value depends on context.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this proof is important for Ash'ari theology: it supports the idea that good and evil are determined by divine command, not by the inherent nature of actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان قبح الكذب لذاته لما تخلف عنه في شيء من الصور ضرورة ، واللازم باطل فيما إذا تعين الكذب، لإنقاذ نبي من الهلاك، فإنه يجب قطعـاً فيحسن، وكذا كل فعل يجب تارة، ويحرم أخرى كالقتل، والضرب حداً وظلماً.
Third: If the evil of lying were in its essence, it would not be absent from it in any case necessarily. The consequent is false in the case when lying is specifically required for saving a prophet from destruction, for it is absolutely obligatory and thus good. Likewise every act that is obligatory at times and forbidden at others, such as killing and striking as a prescribed punishment and as tyranny.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This lesson presents the third Ashʿarī proof, which uses a concrete example to show that lying cannot be intrinsically evil. The argument states: if lying were evil in its essence (dhātihi), it would never be good in any circumstance. But we find a case – lying to save a prophet from death – where lying is obligatory (wājib) and therefore good. This demonstrates that the moral value of an act depends on the context and divine command, not on the act's inherent nature. The proof also notes that other actions, like killing and striking, can be good or evil depending on whether they are done as a prescribed punishment or as tyranny.

Key Terms
قبح الكذب
الذات
الوجوب
الحسن
إنقاذ نبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lying is always evil regardless of circumstances.
Correction: The third proof shows that lying to save a prophet is obligatory and good, so lying is not always evil.

Checkpoint

A) Lying in trade.
B) Lying to save a prophet from death.
C) Lying to parents.
D) Lying in court.

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses the case of saving a prophet to show that lying can be good, thereby disproving the idea that moral values are intrinsic.

Second Argument: Conditional and Division of Actions
Al-Taftazani presents the conditional and divides actions into compulsory and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second argument against rational good and evil, specifically the conditional statement and the division of human actions into compulsory and accidental.

Core Idea
Human actions are either compulsory or accidental; neither can be judged by reason alone.

Al-Taftazani now presents a powerful argument: if goodness and badness were determined by reason, then human actions would be evaluated rationally. But he shows that all human actions fall into two categories — compulsory or accidental — and neither can be rationally good or bad. This passage lays the foundation for the detailed analysis to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of kalam argumentation.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The passage begins with 'If...then...'. Identify the antecedent and consequent. The antecedent is the assumption of rational good and evil; the consequent is that some human actions would be rationally good or bad.
3
Understand the division of actions
The author divides all human actions into two types: compulsory and accidental. Ask yourself whether these two categories indeed cover all possibilities.
Compulsory means the actor has no power to refrain; accidental means it happens without a specific cause or preponderant factor.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author claims that neither compulsory nor accidental actions can be rationally good or bad. Why might that be? Think about the requirements for rational moral judgment.
This sets up the need for a preponderator, which is explored in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو كان الحسن والقبح بالعقل لما كان شيء من أفعال العباد حسناً ولا قبيحاً عقلاً، واللازم باطل باعترافكم . وجه اللزوم أن فعل العبد إما اضطراري، و إما اتفاقي. ولا شيء منهما بحسن ولا قبيح عقلاً. أما الكبرى فبالاتفاق ، وأما الصغرى فلأن العبد إن لم يتمكن من الترك فذاك ، وإن تمكن فإن لم يتوقف الفعل على مرجح ، بل صدر عنه تارة ، ولم يصدر أخرى بلا تجدد أمر كان اتفاقياً على أنه يفضي إلى الترجح بلا مرجح ؛ وفيه انسداد باب إثبات الصانع ، وإن توقف فذلك المرجح إن كان من العبد فينقل الكلام إليه ويتسلسل، وإن لم يكن فمعه إن لم يجب الفعل ، بل صح الصدور واللا صدور، عاد الترديد ولزم المحذور، وإن وجب فالفعل اضطراري والعبد مجبور. واعترض بأن المرجح هو الإرادة التي شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص، وصدور الفعل معه عندنا على سبيل الصحة دون الوجوب إلا عند أبي الحسين. ولو سلم فالوجوب بالاختيار لا ينافي الاختيار ولا يوجب الاضطرار المنافي للحسن وصحة التكليف وأجيب أنه قد ثبت بالدليل لزوم الانتهاء إلى مرجح لا يكون من العبد، ويجب معه الفعل، ويبطل استقلال العبد، ومثله لا يحسن ولا يقبح ، ولا يصح التكليف به. عندهم وأما الاعتراض بأنه استقلال في مقابلة الضرورة، ومنقوض بفعل الباري فقد عرفت جوابه .
The second: If goodness and badness were by reason, then no human action would be good or bad rationally — and the consequent is false by your own admission. The entailment is that the servant's action is either compulsory or accidental; neither is good or bad rationally. As for the major premise, it is agreed upon; as for the minor, because if the servant is unable to refrain, then that is it; if he is able, but the action does not depend on a preponderant factor, rather occurs sometimes and not at other times without any new occurrence — then it is accidental, and it leads to preponderance without a preponderator, and therein is the closure of the door of proving the Maker. If it does depend on a preponderator, then if that preponderator is from the servant, the discussion recurs and goes into infinite regress; if it is not from him, then if the action is not necessary but rather both occurrence and non-occurrence are possible, the dilemma returns and the problematic follows; if it is necessary, then the action is compulsory and the servant is coerced. And an objection was raised that the preponderator is the will whose property is to preponderate and specify, and the action's occurrence with it according to us is in the mode of permissibility, not necessity, except for Abu al-Husayn. Even if conceded, necessitation by choice does not negate choice nor necessitate coercion opposed to goodness and the validity of moral obligation. And it was answered that it has been established by proof that one must end at a preponderator that is not from the servant, with which the action becomes necessary, and the servant's independence is nullified; something like this is neither good nor bad, nor is moral obligation valid regarding it according to them. As for the objection that it is independence in the face of necessity, and overridden by the divine act, you already know its answer.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a proof that goodness and badness cannot be known by reason alone, as the Mu'tazila claim. The argument is structured as a conditional syllogism: if rational good and evil existed, then some human actions would be rationally good or bad; but the consequent is false (by Mu'tazilite admission), so the antecedent is false. The author justifies the entailment by dividing human actions into two exhaustive categories: compulsory (idṭirārī) and accidental (ittifāqī). Neither can be rationally good or bad. He then unpacks the minor premise: if the servant cannot refrain from an action, it is compulsory. If he can refrain but the action occurs without a preponderant factor, it is accidental and leads to the problematic doctrine of preponderance without a preponderator, which undermines proofs for the Creator. If the action depends on a preponderant factor, and that factor is from the servant, an infinite regress follows; if from outside, and the action is not necessitated, the problem returns; if necessitated, the action is compulsory and the servant is coerced — thus no rational moral judgment applies. The author then gives an objection: the preponderator is the servant's will, which does not necessitate the action (except in Abu al-Husayn's view). The answer is that even if will is involved, it ultimately leads to an external necessitating factor, nullifying the servant's independence. Hence such actions are neither good nor bad rationally.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
اضطراري
اتفاقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two categories does the author divide human actions into?
A) Good and bad
B) Compulsory and accidental
C) Necessary and possible
D) Rational and irrational

Key Takeaway
The second argument begins by asserting that if rational good and evil existed, then human actions would have to be rationally evaluable, but they are not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you decide something is 'obviously good' or 'obviously evil', do you ever stop to ask whether your reason is sufficient, or do you assume your judgment is absolute? How does Al-Taftazani's argument challenge that assumption, and what will you do the next time you feel morally certain about something without consulting revelation?
?
Consider a recent action you performed that you considered morally neutral. According to this argument, it was either compulsory or accidental. Does that description sit well with your sense of responsibility? If it was accidental, how do you reconcile that with your accountability before Allah?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that neither compulsory nor accidental actions can be rationally evaluated. Does this free you from false moral certainty, or does it unsettle you? How will this understanding affect the way you approach moral judgments in your daily life—specifically, where will you now look for guidance?

Second Argument: Preponderator Chain and Objection
Al-Taftazani analyzes the preponderator chain, presents an objection, and gives the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani uses the concept of a preponderator (murajjih) to show that human actions cannot be rationally good or bad, and how he responds to the Mu'tazilite objection.

Core Idea
Every action either depends on an external necessitating factor or leads to infinite regress, undermining rational moral judgment.

After establishing that human actions are either compulsory or accidental, Al-Taftazani delves into the mechanics of action. He considers whether an action depends on a preponderant factor (murajjih) and traces the implications. This lesson covers the detailed analysis, including the objection based on human will and the answer that ultimately the servant's independence is nullified.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Note the conditional chain and the objection-answer structure.
This passage is dense; read it slowly.
2
Trace the conditional branches
The author explores several possibilities: whether the action depends on a preponderator or not, and if so, where the preponderator comes from. Follow each branch and its consequence.
Use the guided breakdown to see the branching structure.
3
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazila object that the preponderator is the servant's will, which does not necessitate the action. Identify the key point: the will is a preponderator but does not compel.
This objection challenges the idea that an external preponderator is needed.
4
Examine the answer
The answer shows that even if the will is a preponderator, it ultimately depends on an external factor that necessitates, nullifying the servant's independence. Reflect on why this refutes rational moral judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second: If goodness and badness were by reason, then no human action would be good or bad rationally — and the consequent is false by your own admission. The entailment is that the servant's action is either compulsory or accidental; neither is good or bad rationally. As for the major premise, it is agreed upon; as for the minor, because if the servant is unable to refrain, then that is it; if he is able, but the action does not depend on a preponderant factor, rather occurs sometimes and not at other times without any new occurrence — then it is accidental, and it leads to preponderance without a preponderator, and therein is the closure of the door of proving the Maker. If it does depend on a preponderator, then if that preponderator is from the servant, the discussion recurs and goes into infinite regress; if it is not from him, then if the action is not necessary but rather both occurrence and non-occurrence are possible, the dilemma returns and the problematic follows; if it is necessary, then the action is compulsory and the servant is coerced. And an objection was raised that the preponderator is the will whose property is to preponderate and specify, and the action's occurrence with it according to us is in the mode of permissibility, not necessity, except for Abu al-Husayn. Even if conceded, necessitation by choice does not negate choice nor necessitate coercion opposed to goodness and the validity of moral obligation. And it was answered that it has been established by proof that one must end at a preponderator that is not from the servant, with which the action becomes necessary, and the servant's independence is nullified; something like this is neither good nor bad, nor is moral obligation valid regarding it according to them. As for the objection that it is independence in the face of necessity, and overridden by the divine act, you already know its answer.

Commentary
This passage is a proof that goodness and badness cannot be known by reason alone, as the Mu'tazila claim. The argument is structured as a conditional syllogism: if rational good and evil existed, then some human actions would be rationally good or bad; but the consequent is false (by Mu'tazilite admission), so the antecedent is false. The answer is that even if will is involved, it ultimately leads to an external necessitating factor, nullifying the servant's independence. Hence such actions are neither good nor bad rationally.
