How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your actions cannot ultimately be the basis for moral judgment, on what then do you rely when you stand before Allah? Do you secretly believe your deeds make you worthy?
?
The objection shows the human heart's tendency to defend its own autonomy. Where in your life are you still insisting on your own independence, refusing to submit to the fact that all power belongs to Allah alone?
?
This lesson argues that any attempt to ground morality in human action leads to incoherence. How does this challenge the way you evaluate yourself or others—do you judge based on outcomes, efforts, or something else? What would it mean for you to let go of that measure and trust Allah's wisdom?

Lying to Save a Prophet: When Means Become Good
Al-Taftazani argues against intrinsic moral qualities using the case of obligatory lying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why lying can become good in specific circumstances, as argued by Al-Taftazani in the third argument against intrinsic moral qualities.

Core Idea
Lying becomes good when it is the only means to fulfill an obligatory end.

This passage presents Al-Taftazani's third argument against the view that moral qualities are intrinsic. He uses a powerful counterexample: lying to save a prophet's life. After stating the argument, he addresses an objection based on the principle of choosing the lesser evil, and then gives a nuanced answer that highlights how the means to a good end can itself become good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the flow: argument, objection, answer.
The passage uses footnotes; ignore them for now.
2
Identify the main claim and counterexample
The argument claims that if lying were intrinsically bad, it would always be bad. The counterexample is lying to save a prophet, which seems obligatory and thus good.
Also note the parallel examples: killing and beating (context-dependent).
3
Follow the objection and response
The objection says the lie remains bad, but saving is good; we choose the lesser evil. The response says the lie itself becomes obligatory as the only means, so it becomes good.
Pay attention to the logic: means take the ruling of their ends.
4
Connect to the broader issue of intrinsic vs. contextual morality
This argument challenges the Mu'tazilite view that moral qualities are fixed. It shows that context and purpose can change moral judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان قبح الكذب لذاته لما تخلف عنه في شيء من الصور ضرورة ، واللازم باطل فيما إذا (۲) تعين الكذب، لإنقاذ نبي من الهلاك، فإنه يجب قطعـاً فيحسن، وكذا كل فعل يجب تارة، ويحرم أخرى كالقتل، والضرب حداً وظلماً. واعترض بأن الكذب في الصورة المذكورة باق على قبحه إلا إذا (۱) ترك إنجاء النبي أقبح منه ، فيلزم ارتكاب أقل القبيحين تخلصاً عن ارتكاب الأقبح، فالواجب الحسن هو الإنجاء لا الكذب. وهذا إذا سلمنا عدم إمكان التخليص بالتعريض وإلا ففي المعاريض مندوحة عن الكذب (۳). والجواب : أن هذا الكذب لما تعين سبباً وطريقاً إلى الإنجاء الواجب كان واجباً فكان حسناً . وأما القتال (۳) ومحصله (٤) الضرب حداً فأمرهما ظاهر.
The third: If the badness of lying were intrinsic, it would never be absent from any situation necessarily — and the consequent is false in the case where lying is determined to save a prophet from destruction, for it is absolutely obligatory and thus good. Likewise every action that is sometimes obligatory and sometimes forbidden, like killing and beating as a legal punishment or unjustly. And an objection was raised that lying in the mentioned case remains bad, unless refraining from saving the prophet is worse, so committing the lesser of two evils is necessary to avoid the greater evil; thus the obligatory good is saving, not lying. And this is if we concede the impossibility of extrication by equivocation; otherwise, there is scope in equivocations to avoid lying. And the answer: this lying, since it is determined as a cause and means to the obligatory saving, becomes obligatory and therefore good. As for fighting and its conclusion, beating as a legal punishment, their matter is clear.

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against intrinsic moral qualities (dhātī). The argument uses the case of lying to save a prophet's life: if lying were intrinsically bad, it would never be good, yet in this case it is obligatory and thus good. The author also mentions similar cases like killing and beating, which change moral status based on context. An objection is then raised that the lie remains bad, but the obligation to save the prophet forces choosing the lesser of two evils, so the act of saving is good, not the lie. The author answers that the lie itself becomes obligatory as the only means to the required end, thus it becomes good. The rejoinder emphasizes that the means takes the ruling of its end.

Fourth Argument: The liar paradox and intrinsic goodness
Al-Taftazani uses a version of the liar paradox to refute intrinsic good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the liar paradox is used to demonstrate that good and evil cannot be intrinsic properties of actions.

Core Idea
Intrinsic moral qualities lead to logical contradictions like the liar paradox.

This passage presents the fourth argument against the Mu'tazilite doctrine of intrinsic moral qualities. Al-Taftazani uses a logical paradox—similar to the famous liar paradox—to show that if good and evil were objective and knowable by reason, then even a simple future statement would force a contradiction. The argument proceeds through several formulations, each exposing the impossibility of intrinsic moral attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the logical structure sink in without trying to analyze it deeply yet.
The passage is dense; do not worry if it seems confusing at first.
2
Understand the first formulation
Focus on the statement 'I will lie tomorrow.' Follow the two cases: if the statement is true, the speaker is truthful (good) but entails lying tomorrow (bad). If false, the speaker is lying (bad) but entails not lying tomorrow (good). In both cases, good and bad combine.
This is the core of the paradox.
3
Examine the alternative formulations
Notice how the argument is restated in different ways: focusing on the future act of lying, or on the principle that the implication of good is good and of evil is evil. Each version reinforces the same contradiction.
The variety shows the robustness of the argument.
4
Study the self-referential version
The final formulation uses a statement like 'This statement is not true,' which directly combines truth and falsehood. This strengthens the case that intrinsic qualities lead to contradictions.
This is the classic liar paradox.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how this argument refutes the Mu'tazilite position without denying moral values altogether. It merely shows that moral qualities cannot be known through reason alone.
The Ash'arite position is that moral value is determined by divine command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الحسن والقبح ذاتيين لزم اجتماع المتنافيين في إخبار من قال لأكذبن غداً. لأنه إما صادق فيلزم لصدقه حسنه ولاستلزامه الكذب في الغد قبحه، وإما كاذب فيلزم لكذبه ،قبحه ولاستلزامه ترك الكذب في الغد حسنه، وقد يقرر اجتماع المتنافيين في اخبار الغدي كاذباً ، فإنه لكذبه قبيح ، ولاستلزامه صدق الكلام الأول حسن أو لأنه إما حسن فلا يكون القبح ذاتياً للكذب ، وإما قبيح فيكون تركه حسناً مع استلزامه كذب الكلام الأول وهو قبيح، ومبني الاستلزام على انحصار الأخبار الغدي في هذا الواحد . وقد يقرر بأنه إما صادق، وإما كاذب، وأياً ما كان يلزم اجتماع الحسن والقبح فيه، ومبنى الكل على أن ملزوم الحسن حسن، وملزوم القبيح قبيح ، وأن كل حسن أو قبح ذاتي، ويمكن تقرير الشبهة بحيث يجتمع الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد فيجتمع الحسن و القبح وذلك إذا اعتبرنا قضية يكون مضمونها الإخبار عن نفسها بعدم الصدق ، فيتلازم فيها الصدق والكذب، كما تقول هذا الكلام الذي أتكلم به الآن ليس بصادق، فإن صدقها يستلزم عدم صدقها
The fourth: If goodness and badness were intrinsic, then the combination of two opposites would be necessary in the statement of someone who says 'I will lie tomorrow.' For if he is truthful, then from his truthfulness his goodness follows, and from its entailing lying tomorrow its badness follows; and if he is lying, then from his lying its badness follows, and from its entailing refraining from lying tomorrow its goodness follows. And it is sometimes formulated that the combination of opposites occurs in the future statement being a lie, for it is bad because of its lying, and good because it entails the truth of the first statement; or because if it is good, then badness is not intrinsic to lying, and if it is bad, then refraining from it is good while it entails the lying of the first statement which is bad. And the entailment is based on the confinement of the future statement to this single one. And it is formulated that it is either truthful or lying, and whichever it is, the combination of good and bad in it is necessary. And the basis of all this is that the necessary condition of the good is good, and the necessary condition of the bad is bad, and that every good and bad is intrinsic. And it is possible to formulate the difficulty such that truth and falsehood combine in a single statement, and thus good and bad combine, and that is when we consider a proposition whose content is self-assertion of untruth, so truth and falsehood are mutually entailed, as when you say 'This statement I am now uttering is not true', for its truth entails its untruth.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against intrinsic moral qualities. It uses a logical paradox similar to the liar paradox. The argument considers someone who says 'I will lie tomorrow.' If this statement is true, then the speaker is truthful (good), but it entails that he will actually lie tomorrow (bad). If it is false, then he is lying (bad), but it entails that he will not lie tomorrow (good). In either case, the same person would be both good and bad in the same respect. The author then gives alternative formulations: focusing on the future act of lying, or on the principle that the implication of good is good and of evil is evil. He concludes by presenting a version where truth and falsehood combine in a single self-referential statement like 'This statement is false,' leading to a direct combination of good and evil. The argument aims to show that intrinsic moral qualities lead to impossible contradictions, thus they cannot be real.

Key Terms
ذاتيين
المتنافيين
الصدق
الكذب
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies moral values altogether.
Correction: It only refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that moral qualities are intrinsic and knowable by reason alone.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the statement 'I will lie tomorrow' create if good and evil are intrinsic?
A) It forces the speaker to be both truthful and a liar at the same time.
B) It results in the combination of good and bad in the same action or person.
C) It proves that lying is always wrong.
D) It shows that future statements cannot be evaluated.

Key Takeaway
The liar paradox demonstrates that intrinsic moral qualities lead to contradictions, supporting the Ash'arite view that moral value is determined by divine command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are there areas in your life where you act as if your own moral sense is absolute, ignoring what God has commanded? What is one step you will take today to align your moral judgments with divine revelation?

The Fourth Objection: Liar Paradox Against Inherent Goodness
Al-Taftazani presents the fourth objection against inherent goodness using the liar paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the liar paradox is used as an objection against the theory that goodness and badness are inherent (dhatiyy).

Core Idea
The liar paradox shows that inherent goodness would entail contradiction.

This lesson presents the fourth objection raised against the Ashʿarī position that moral values are inherent. The objector argues that if actions were inherently good or bad, then self-referential statements like 'I will lie tomorrow' would simultaneously possess both qualities, which is impossible. Al-Taftazani lays out several formulations of this paradox, setting the stage for his resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the passage and identify the central claim of the objection: that if goodness and badness were inherent, then a statement about lying tomorrow would combine both opposites.
The objector is using the principle that the necessary condition of good is good, and of bad is bad.
2
Follow the formulations
Notice how the paradox is presented in different ways: first with a single statement about tomorrow, then with a self-referential 'this statement is not truthful', and finally with a pair of statements about yesterday and tomorrow.
Each formulation aims to force a contradiction from the assumption of inherent moral values.
3
Recognize the underlying principle
The objection relies on the claim that whatever necessitates good is itself good, and whatever necessitates bad is itself bad, and that these qualities are inherent.
This is the key logical move: the consequence inherits the inherent value of its cause.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this paradox is so problematic for the theory of inherent goodness. If successful, it would show that the theory leads to logical absurdity.
Al-Taftazani will later offer a resolution, but here the objection is presented forcefully.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الحسن والقبح ذاتيين لزم اجتماع المتنافيين في إخبار من قال لأكذبن غداً. لأنه إما صادق فيلزم لصدقه حسنه ولاستلزامه الكذب في الغد قبحه، وإما كاذب فيلزم لكذبه قبحه ولاستلزامه ترك الكذب في الغد حسنه، وقد يقرر اجتماع المتنافيين في اخبار الغدي كاذباً، فإنه لكذبه قبيح، ولاستلزامه صدق الكلام الأول حسن أو لأنه إما حسن فلا يكون القبح ذاتياً للكذب، وإما قبيح فيكون تركه حسناً مع استلزامه كذب الكلام الأول وهو قبيح، ومبني الاستلزام على انحصار الأخبار الغدي في هذا الواحد . وقد يقرر بأنه إما صادق، وإما كاذب، وأياً ما كان يلزم اجتماع الحسن والقبح فيه، ومبنى الكل على أن ملزوم الحسن حسن، وملزوم القبيح قبيح، وأن كل حسن أو قبح ذاتي، ويمكن تقرير الشبهة بحيث يجتمع الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد فيجتمع الحسن و القبح وذلك إذا اعتبرنا قضية يكون مضمونها الإخبار عن نفسها بعدم الصدق، فيتلازم فيها الصدق والكذب، كما تقول هذا الكلام الذي أتكلم به الآن ليس بصادق، فإن صدقها يستلزم عدم صدقها وبالعكس، وقد يورد ذلك في صورة كلام غدي وأمسي، فيقال الكلام الذي أتكلم به غداً ليس بصادق أو لاشيء مما أكلم به غداً بصادق خارجية، ثم يقتصر في الغد على قوله ذلك الكلام الذي تكلمت به أمس صادق فإن صدق كل من الكلام الغدي والأمسي يستلزم عدم صدقهما وبالعكس، وهذه مغلطة تحير في حلها عقول العقلاء وفحول الأذكياء، ولهذا سميتها مغلطة جذر الأصم، ولقد تصفحت الأقاويل فلم أظفر بما يروي الغليل، وتأملت كثيراً، فلم يظهر إلا أقل من القليل، وهو أن الصدق أو الكذب كما يكون حالاً للحكم أي للنسبة الإيجابية أو السلبية على اللازم في جميع القضايا، قد يكون حكماً أي محكوما به محمولاً على الشيء بالاشتقاق كما في قولنا هذا صادق، وذاك كاذب، ولا يتناقضان إلا إذا اعتبرا حالين الحكم واحد، أو حكمين على موضوع واحد بخلاف ما إذا اعتبر أحدهما حالاً للحكم، والآخر حكماً لاختلاف المرجع اختلافاً جلياً. كما في قولنا: السماء تحتنا . . صادق أو كاذب أو خفياً .. كما في الشخصية التي هي مناط المغلطة، فأما إذا فرضناها كاذبة لم يلزم إلا صدق نقيضها وهو قولنا هذا الكلام صادق فيقع الصدق حكماً للشخصية لا حالاً لحكمها، وإنما حال حكمها الكذب على ما فرضنا، والصدق حال للنسبة الإيجابية التي هي حكم النقيض وحكم للشخصية التي هي الأصل، فلم يجتمعا حالين لحكم ولا حكمين لموضوع وكذا إذا فرضناها صادقة، وحينئذ فلعل المجيب يمنع تناقض الصدق والكذب المتلازمين بناء على رجوع أحدهما إلى حكم الشخصية والآخر إلى موضوعها. لكن الصواب عندي في هذه القضية ترك الجواب والاعتراف بالعجز عن حل الإشكال.
Fourth: If goodness and badness were inherent, then the combination of two opposites would necessarily follow in the statement of one who says 'I will certainly lie tomorrow.' Because either he is truthful, so necessarily from his truthfulness (comes) its goodness and from its entailing lying tomorrow its badness; or he is a liar, so necessarily from his lying its badness and from its entailing avoiding lying tomorrow its goodness. And it may be formulated that the combination of opposites occurs in the tomorrow statement as a lie: for because of its lie it is bad, and because of its entailing the truth of the first statement it is good; or because if it is good then badness is not inherent to lying, and if it is bad then its avoidance is good while it entails the lie of the first statement which is bad. And the entailment is based on the restriction of tomorrow's statements to this one. And it may be formulated: either truthful or a liar, and whichever it is, the combination of goodness and badness in it necessarily follows. And the basis of all is that the necessary condition of good is good, and the necessary condition of bad is bad, and that every good or bad is inherent. And one can formulate the doubt such that truth and falsehood combine in one statement, so goodness and badness combine—that is when we consider a proposition whose content is the reporting about itself that it is not truthful, so truth and falsehood are mutually entailed in it, as you say: 'This statement I am uttering now is not truthful.' For if it is true, it entails its own untruth, and vice versa. And it may be presented in the form of a tomorrow statement and a yesterday statement: it is said: 'The statement I will utter tomorrow is not truthful' or 'Nothing of what I utter tomorrow is a truthful external statement', then tomorrow is restricted to saying: 'That statement I uttered yesterday is truthful.' For if both the tomorrow statement and the yesterday statement are true, it entails their untruth, and vice versa. This is a fallacy that confuses the minds of the intelligent and the elites of the clever. For this reason I named it the fallacy of 'deaf root'. I have perused the sayings and found nothing that quenches the thirst, and I reflected much, but nothing appeared except the least of little, which is: that truth or falsehood, just as it can be a property (hal) of the judgment—i.e., of the affirmative or negative relation to the concomitant in all propositions—it can also be a judgment (hukm)—i.e., a predicated thing, predicated of a thing derivatively, as in our saying: 'This is truthful' and 'That is a liar.' And they are contradictory only if they are considered as two properties of one judgment, or two judgments on one subject, as opposed to the case when one is considered a property of the judgment and the other a judgment, because of the clear difference of referent—as in our saying: 'The sky is beneath us... truthful or false'—or subtle difference... as in the particular proposition which is the locus of the fallacy. So if we suppose it false, nothing follows except the truth of its contradictory, which is our saying: 'This statement is truthful.' So truth occurs as a judgment of the particular proposition, not as a property of its judgment; rather the property of its judgment is falsehood, as we supposed. And truth is a property of the affirmative relation which is the judgment of the contradictory, and a judgment of the particular proposition which is the original. So they have not combined as two properties of one judgment nor as two judgments on one subject. Likewise if we suppose it true. In that case, perhaps the respondent may deny the contradiction of the mutually entailing truth and falsehood, based on one of them referring to the judgment of the particular proposition and the other to its subject. But the correct view in my opinion in this matter is to refrain from answering and to admit inability to solve the difficulty.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the theory that goodness and badness are inherent (dhatiyy) in actions, followed by a detailed response. The objector argues that if goodness and badness were inherent, then a self-referential statement like 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' would entail a contradiction: both goodness and badness would simultaneously apply to the same statement. Al-Taftazani presents several formulations of this paradox, including the liar paradox and a version involving statements about tomorrow and yesterday. He then provides his own resolution: a distinction between truth and falsehood as properties of a judgment (hal li al-hukm) and as predicates (hukm) of a thing. When truth is treated as a predicate of the statement itself, and falsehood as a property of its judgment, they can coexist without contradiction because they apply to different referents (marji'). He illustrates this with an example. However, he concludes that the best course is to refrain from a definitive answer and admit inability to resolve the difficulty, showing intellectual humility.

Key Terms

قبح
ذاتي
صدق
كذب
مغلطة
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the liar paradox proves beyond doubt that inherent goodness is impossible.
Correction: Al-Taftazani does not accept the objection as conclusive; he later provides a resolution that preserves the possibility of inherent goodness by distinguishing between different aspects of truth and falsehood.

Checkpoint
What principle underlies all the formulations of the objection?
A) The necessary condition of good is good, and of bad is bad.
B) Truth is always good and falsehood is always bad.
C) Self-referential statements are meaningless.
D) Inherent qualities cannot change.

Key Takeaway
The liar paradox is a powerful objection against inherent goodness because it forces a contradiction from the assumption of inherent moral values.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever judge actions as 'good' or 'bad' based solely on your intuition, without considering how they relate to God's commands? The liar paradox shows that such judgments can lead to contradictions. Will you commit to evaluating actions by revelation rather than by what seems inherently right to you?

?

What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you accepted that moral values are not inherent but are instead defined by His command? Are you ready to submit your conscience to that understanding?

Resolving the Liar Paradox: Distinction Between Property and Predicate
Al-Taftazani resolves the liar paradox with a distinction between property-of-judgment and predicate-judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's resolution of the liar paradox through the distinction between truth as a property of a judgment (hal li al-hukm) and truth as a predicate (hukm) of a thing.

Core Idea
Truth and falsehood can coexist when one is a property of judgment and the other a predicate.

Having presented the liar paradox as an objection against inherent goodness, al-Taftazani now offers his own resolution. He distinguishes between truth as a property (hal) of the judgment itself and truth as a predicate (hukm) predicated of a thing. This distinction shows that in self-referential statements, truth and falsehood apply to different referents and therefore do not constitute a genuine contradiction. However, he humbly concludes that the best answer is to admit inability to fully solve the difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the fallacy
Al-Taftazani describes the liar paradox as a fallacy that confuses even the most intelligent. He admits that after research he found little satisfactory solution.
This sets the stage for his contribution: a distinction that resolves the apparent contradiction.
2
Understand the distinction
Truth or falsehood can be either: (1) a property (hal) of the judgment itself, i.e., of the affirmative or negative relation in the proposition; or (2) a judgment (hukm) predicated of a thing derivatively, as in 'This is truthful.'
The two are not the same, and they can apply to different referents.
3
Apply to the liar statement
Consider 'This statement is not truthful' assumed false. Then its judgment is false (property), but its contradictory 'This statement is truthful' is true, and that truth is a predicate of the original statement, not a property of its judgment. Thus truth and falsehood do not combine as two properties of one judgment or as two predicates of one subject.
They are about different things, so no contradiction.
4
Note the final humility
Al-Taftazani concludes that the correct view is to refrain from a definitive answer and admit inability to solve the difficulty. This reflects intellectual honesty.
He presents a solution but acknowledges its limitations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الحسن والقبح ذاتيين لزم اجتماع المتنافيين في إخبار من قال لأكذبن غداً. لأنه إما صادق فيلزم لصدقه حسنه ولاستلزامه الكذب في الغد قبحه، وإما كاذب فيلزم لكذبه قبحه ولاستلزامه ترك الكذب في الغد حسنه، وقد يقرر اجتماع المتنافيين في اخبار الغدي كاذباً، فإنه لكذبه قبيح، ولاستلزامه صدق الكلام الأول حسن أو لأنه إما حسن فلا يكون القبح ذاتياً للكذب، وإما قبيح فيكون تركه حسناً مع استلزامه كذب الكلام الأول وهو قبيح، ومبني الاستلزام على انحصار الأخبار الغدي في هذا الواحد . وقد يقرر بأنه إما صادق، وإما كاذب، وأياً ما كان يلزم اجتماع الحسن والقبح فيه، ومبنى الكل على أن ملزوم الحسن حسن، وملزوم القبيح قبيح، وأن كل حسن أو قبح ذاتي، ويمكن تقرير الشبهة بحيث يجتمع الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد فيجتمع الحسن و القبح وذلك إذا اعتبرنا قضية يكون مضمونها الإخبار عن نفسها بعدم الصدق، فيتلازم فيها الصدق والكذب، كما تقول هذا الكلام الذي أتكلم به الآن ليس بصادق، فإن صدقها يستلزم عدم صدقها وبالعكس، وقد يورد ذلك في صورة كلام غدي وأمسي، فيقال الكلام الذي أتكلم به غداً ليس بصادق أو لاشيء مما أكلم به غداً بصادق خارجية، ثم يقتصر في الغد على قوله ذلك الكلام الذي تكلمت به أمس صادق فإن صدق كل من الكلام الغدي والأمسي يستلزم عدم صدقهما وبالعكس، وهذه مغلطة تحير في حلها عقول العقلاء وفحول الأذكياء، ولهذا سميتها مغلطة جذر الأصم، ولقد تصفحت الأقاويل فلم أظفر بما يروي الغليل، وتأملت كثيراً، فلم يظهر إلا أقل من القليل، وهو أن الصدق أو الكذب كما يكون حالاً للحكم أي للنسبة الإيجابية أو السلبية على اللازم في جميع القضايا، قد يكون حكماً أي محكوما به محمولاً على الشيء بالاشتقاق كما في قولنا هذا صادق، وذاك كاذب، ولا يتناقضان إلا إذا اعتبرا حالين الحكم واحد، أو حكمين على موضوع واحد بخلاف ما إذا اعتبر أحدهما حالاً للحكم، والآخر حكماً لاختلاف المرجع اختلافاً جلياً. كما في قولنا: السماء تحتنا . . صادق أو كاذب أو خفياً .. كما في الشخصية التي هي مناط المغلطة، فأما إذا فرضناها كاذبة لم يلزم إلا صدق نقيضها وهو قولنا هذا الكلام صادق فيقع الصدق حكماً للشخصية لا حالاً لحكمها، وإنما حال حكمها الكذب على ما فرضنا، والصدق حال للنسبة الإيجابية التي هي حكم النقيض وحكم للشخصية التي هي الأصل، فلم يجتمعا حالين لحكم ولا حكمين لموضوع وكذا إذا فرضناها صادقة، وحينئذ فلعل المجيب يمنع تناقض الصدق والكذب المتلازمين بناء على رجوع أحدهما إلى حكم الشخصية والآخر إلى موضوعها. لكن الصواب عندي في هذه القضية ترك الجواب والاعتراف بالعجز عن حل الإشكال.
Fourth: If goodness and badness were inherent, then the combination of two opposites would necessarily follow in the statement of one who says 'I will certainly lie tomorrow.' Because either he is truthful, so necessarily from his truthfulness (comes) its goodness and from its entailing lying tomorrow its badness; or he is a liar, so necessarily from his lying its badness and from its entailing avoiding lying tomorrow its goodness. And it may be formulated that the combination of opposites occurs in the tomorrow statement as a lie: for because of its lie it is bad, and because of its entailing the truth of the first statement it is good; or because if it is good then badness is not inherent to lying, and if it is bad then its avoidance is good while it entails the lie of the first statement which is bad. And the entailment is based on the restriction of tomorrow's statements to this one. And it may be formulated: either truthful or a liar, and whichever it is, the combination of goodness and badness in it necessarily follows. And the basis of all is that the necessary condition of good is good, and the necessary condition of bad is bad, and that every good or bad is inherent. And one can formulate the doubt such that truth and falsehood combine in one statement, so goodness and badness combine—that is when we consider a proposition whose content is the reporting about itself that it is not truthful, so truth and falsehood are mutually entailed in it, as you say: 'This statement I am uttering now is not truthful.' For if it is true, it entails its own untruth, and vice versa. And it may be presented in the form of a tomorrow statement and a yesterday statement: it is said: 'The statement I will utter tomorrow is not truthful' or 'Nothing of what I utter tomorrow is a truthful external statement', then tomorrow is restricted to saying: 'That statement I uttered yesterday is truthful.' For if both the tomorrow statement and the yesterday statement are true, it entails their untruth, and vice versa. This is a fallacy that confuses the minds of the intelligent and the elites of the clever. For this reason I named it the fallacy of 'deaf root'. I have perused the sayings and found nothing that quenches the thirst, and I reflected much, but nothing appeared except the least of little, which is: that truth or falsehood, just as it can be a property (hal) of the judgment—i.e., of the affirmative or negative relation to the concomitant in all propositions—it can also be a judgment (hukm)—i.e., a predicated thing, predicated of a thing derivatively, as in our saying: 'This is truthful' and 'That is a liar.' And they are contradictory only if they are considered as two properties of one judgment, or two judgments on one subject, as opposed to the case when one is considered a property of the judgment and the other a judgment, because of the clear difference of referent—as in our saying: 'The sky is beneath us... truthful or false'—or subtle difference... as in the particular proposition which is the locus of the fallacy. So if we suppose it false, nothing follows except the truth of its contradictory, which is our saying: 'This statement is truthful.' So truth occurs as a judgment of the particular proposition, not as a property of its judgment; rather the property of its judgment is falsehood, as we supposed. And truth is a property of the affirmative relation which is the judgment of the contradictory, and a judgment of the particular proposition which is the original. So they have not combined as two properties of one judgment nor as two judgments on one subject. Likewise if we suppose it true. In that case, perhaps the respondent may deny the contradiction of the mutually entailing truth and falsehood, based on one of them referring to the judgment of the particular proposition and the other to its subject. But the correct view in my opinion in this matter is to refrain from answering and to admit inability to solve the difficulty.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an objection against the theory that goodness and badness are inherent (dhatiyy) in actions, followed by a detailed response. The objector argues that if goodness and badness were inherent, then a self-referential statement like 'I will certainly lie tomorrow' would entail a contradiction: both goodness and badness would simultaneously apply to the same statement. Al-Taftazani presents several formulations of this paradox, including the liar paradox and a version involving statements about tomorrow and yesterday. He then provides his own resolution: a distinction between truth and falsehood as properties of a judgment (hal li al-hukm) and as predicates (hukm) of a thing. When truth is treated as a predicate of the statement itself, and falsehood as a property of its judgment, they can coexist without contradiction because they apply to different referents (marji'). He illustrates this with an example. However, he concludes that the best course is to refrain from a definitive answer and admit inability to resolve the difficulty, showing intellectual humility.

حكم
صدق
كذب
مغلطة
جذر الأصم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Taftazani's admission of inability means he has no solution at all.
Correction: He does provide a logical distinction that resolves the paradox, but he modestly acknowledges that it may not be completely satisfying to all.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, when the liar statement 'This statement is not truthful' is false, where does truth appear?
A) As a property of the judgment of the liar statement
B) As a predicate (judgment) of the liar statement
C) As both a property and a predicate of the liar statement
D) Nowhere, because the statement is false

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani resolves the liar paradox by distinguishing between truth as a property of judgment and truth as a predicate, showing they can coexist without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Taftazani distinguished between truth as a property of a judgment and truth as a predicate. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse the truth of a statement with the truth about a person? How can this lesson help you avoid unfair judgments?
?
Reflect on a time when you were caught in a logical or moral paradox. Did you seek a solution, or did you admit your inability? How does al-Taftazani's example challenge your approach to difficult questions?

Fifth Argument: Accident in Accident
Al-Taftazani presents the fifth argument against intrinsic good/evil based on impossibility of an accident inhering in an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument against intrinsic good/evil, how it relies on the impossibility of an accident inhering in an accident, and the objection and response that follow.

Core Idea
If actions were intrinsically good/evil, goodness would be an accident inhering in an accident—impossible.

This lesson presents a key argument from Islamic theology against the idea that moral qualities are inherent in actions. Al-Taftazani constructs a reductio ad absurdum, showing that such a view leads to a logical impossibility: an accident inhering in another accident. He then addresses objections and clarifies that revealed moral qualities are not subject to this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the technical terms like ʿaraḍ and salb.
2
Identify the main claim and entailment
Find where the author states the conditional and the resulting impossibility. Understand why goodness is considered an accident.
The key phrase is 'لزم قيام العرض بالعرض'.
3
Examine the objection and its three points
The passage raises an objection with three sub-points. List them: (a) both contradictories can be privative, (b) negation forms can be universal, (c) the argument also refutes essential possibility.
These objections challenge the necessity of one contradictory being existential.
4
Understand the author's response
Al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing revealed goodness (ḥasan sharʿī) which is eternal and not an accident, thus outside the scope of the argument.
This shows the argument is limited to intrinsic moral qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : لو كان الفعل حسناً أو قبيحاً لذاته لزم قيام العرض بالعرض وهو باطل باعتراف الخصم وبما مر من الدليل، وجه اللزوم أن حسن الفعل مثلاً أمر زائد عليه ، لأنه قد يعقل الفعل ولا يعقل حسنه أو قبحه، ومع ذلك فهو وجودي غير قائم بنفسه، وهذا معنى العرض، أما عدم القيام بنفسه، فظاهر. وأما الوجود فلأن نقيضه لا حسن وهو سلب إذا لم يكن سلباً لاستلزم محلاً موجوداً فلم يصدق على المعدوم أنه ليس بحسن وهذا باطل بالضرورة وإذا كان أحد النقيضين سلبياً، كان الآخر وجودياً ضرورة امتناع النقيضين ثم إنه صفة (١) للفعل الذي هو أيضاً عرض يلزم قيام العرض بالعرض، واعترض بأن النقيضين قد يكونان عدميين كالامتناع واللامتناع ، وبأن صورة السلب أعني ما فيه حرف النفي لا يلزم من صدقه على المعدوم أن يكون سلباً محضاً لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً كلياً يصدق على أفراد بعضها وجودي، وبعضها عدمي كالأمكن (٣) الصادق على الواجب والممتنع ، وبأنه منقوض بإمكان الفعل، فإنه ذاتي له مع إجراء الدليل فيه، وإنما لم ينقضوا الدليل بأنه يقتضي أن لا يتصف الفعل بالحسن الشرعي للزوم قيام العرض بالعرض، لأن الحسن الشرعي عند التحقيق قديم لا عرض ، ومتعلق بالفعل لا صفة له، وقد بينا ذلك في شرح الأصول.
The fifth: If an action were good or evil by itself, it would entail the inherence of an accident in an accident (qiyām al-ʿaraḍ bi al-ʿaraḍ), which is false by the opponent's admission and by what has preceded of the proof. The aspect of the entailment is that the goodness of an action, for example, is an additional thing over it, because the action may be conceived without conceiving its goodness or evil, and yet it is existent and not self-subsistent, and this is the meaning of accident (ʿaraḍ). As for not being self-subsistent, it is evident. As for existence, because its opposite is 'not-good' which is a privation (salb); if it were not a privation, it would require an existent subject; thus it is not true of the non-existent that it is 'not-good,' and this is false necessarily. And if one of the two contradictories is privative, the other is existential by necessity of the impossibility of the two contradictories. Then it is a quality (ṣifa) of the action, which is also an accident, so the inherence of an accident in an accident follows. And it is objected that the two contradictories may both be privative, such as impossibility and non-impossibility; and that the form of negation, i.e., that which contains the negative particle, does not entail from its truth of the non-existent that it is pure privation, because it may be a universal concept that applies to individuals some of which are existential and some privative, like 'possible' (al-mumkin) which applies to the necessary and the impossible. And that it is refuted by the possibility (imkān) of the action, for it is essential to it when the proof is applied to it. And they did not refute the proof by saying that it entails that the action cannot be described with sharʿī goodness due to the necessary inherence of accident in accident, because sharʿī goodness, upon investigation, is eternal (qadīm), not an accident, and is related to the action, not a quality of it, and we have clarified that in Sharḥ al-Uṣūl.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against the claim that actions are intrinsically good or evil, followed by an objection and the author's response. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if an action were good or evil by itself, then goodness/evil would be an accident (ʿaraḍ) inhering in the action, which is itself an accident. This would involve an accident inhering in another accident, which is impossible. The author explains that goodness is an additional attribute: we can conceive an action without conceiving its moral quality, so it is not essential. It is existent and not self-subsistent, hence an accident. Its opposite 'not-good' is a privation, but if it were not a privation, it would need a subject, so it must be existential. Since one contradictory is privative, the other is existential, so goodness is existential. Then, as a quality of the action (itself an accident), it yields the prohibited inherence. The objection raises three points: (1) both contradictories can be privative (e.g., impossibility and non-impossibility); (2) the form of negation does not guarantee pure privation—it can be a universal concept applying to both existent and non-existent, like 'possible'; (3) the argument is refuted by the possibility (imkān) of the action, which is essential. The author answers by noting that these objections do not disprove the core point regarding sharʿī (revealed) goodness, which is eternal (qadīm) and not an accident, as explained elsewhere.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
عرض
قيام العرض بالعرض
سلب
وجودي
عدمي
إمكان
حسن شرعي
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that no actions can be good or evil at all.
Correction: The argument only targets intrinsic moral qualities; revealed goodness is eternal and not an accident.

Checkpoint
What is the core problem if an action were good by itself, according to the fifth argument?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress
B) It would entail an accident inhering in an accident
C) It would make God imperfect
D) It would contradict experience

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an accident inhering in an accident is used to refute intrinsic moral qualities, while allowing for revealed moral qualities that are eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If actions have no intrinsic goodness or evil, what is your ultimate criterion for judging right from wrong? Do you truly submit to revelation, or do you secretly rely on your own sense of what seems good?
?
The argument reduces moral qualities to accidents that cannot stand on their own. How does this affect your motivation for obeying God? Are you obeying because the action is inherently good, or because you trust that God's command is good, even when your own reason disagrees?
?
This argument might make you feel that morality is arbitrary. Does that disturb you? How does the Ash'ari view—that moral values are grounded in God's eternal will and wisdom—challenge you to deepen your reliance on Him rather than on your own moral intuitions?

The Sixth Argument: Divine Freedom vs. Intrinsic Morality
Taftazani presents the sixth argument against intrinsic goodness/evil, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument against intrinsic goodness and evil, which shows that such a view would negate God's free choice, and the subsequent objection and response.

Core Idea
If actions are intrinsically good or bad, God would lose His free choice.

This passage presents the sixth argument in a series refuting the claim that actions are intrinsically good or bad. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if actions had inherent moral value, then God would be compelled to judge accordingly, which contradicts the consensus that God is absolutely free. Taftazani then addresses an objection about the deterrent of wisdom and clarifies the logical entailment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: argument, objection, response.
2
Identify the main argument
What does the argument claim? How does it derive the negation of God's free choice?
Focus on the entailment: if actions have intrinsic values, God must judge accordingly, so He is not free.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says that even if God does not do evil, He remains capable, and the deterrent of wisdom (ṣārif al-ḥikmah) does not remove choice.
Consider why the objector thinks capability preserves freedom.
4
Examine the response
Taftazani's response: the opponents hold that judgment of good/bad is based on intrinsic attributes, which are fixed, not chosen. Show how this refutes the objection.
The response forces the opponent to explain how choice is compatible with intrinsic values.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final phrase: unless the aim is to force a commitment or relate judgment to actions. What does Taftazani suggest?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : لو حسن الفعل أو قبح لذاته أو لصفاته وجهاته لم يكن الباري مختاراً في الحكم واللازم باطل بالإجماع. وجه اللزوم أنه لا بد في الفعل من حكم ، والحكم على خلاف ما هو المعقول قبيح لا يصح عن الباري. بل يتعين عليه الحكم بالمعقول الراجح بحيث لا يصح تركه، وفيه نفي للاختيار. واعترض بأنه وإن لم يفعل القبيح لصارف الحكمة، لكنه قادر عليه، بتمكن منه، ولو سلم فالامتناع لصارف الحكمة لا ينفي الاختيار على أن الحكم عندكم ، فكيف يكون بالاختيار ، اللهم إلا أن يقصد الإلزام أو يراد جعله متعلقاً بالأفعال.
The sixth: If the action is good or bad by itself or by its attributes and aspects, the Creator would not be free in judgment, and the consequent is false by consensus. The aspect of entailment is that there must be a judgment about the action, and judging contrary to what is rationally understood is bad and not befitting the Creator. Rather, He would be determined to judge by the rationally preferred such that it is not valid to leave it, and in this is a negation of choice. And it was objected that even if He does not do the bad due to the deterrent of wisdom, He is capable of it, able to do it, and if granted, the prevention due to the deterrent of wisdom does not negate choice, while the judgment according to you (opponents) is [based on intrinsic attributes], so how can it be by choice? Unless the aim is to force a commitment or to make it related to actions.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument in a series refuting the view that actions are intrinsically good or bad, whether by their essence, attributes, or aspects. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if actions were intrinsically good or bad, then God would be compelled to judge accordingly, which would negate His free choice (ikhtiyār). The author states that the consequent—that God is not free—is false by consensus. He explains the entailment: every action requires a judgment (ḥukm); judging contrary to what is rationally understood (al-ma‘qūl) would be bad (qabīḥ) and unworthy of God. Therefore, God would be constrained to judge according to the rationally preferred (al-ma‘qūl al-rājiḥ) and could not do otherwise—clearly negating choice. An objection is then raised: even if God does not commit evil due to the deterrent of wisdom (ṣārif al-ḥikmah), He remains capable of it, and the deterrent does not eliminate choice. The author counters that according to the opponents' own view, the judgment of good and bad is based on intrinsic attributes, so how can that judgment be a matter of divine choice? The final phrase suggests either forcing an implication or relating the judgment to actions (presumably a clarification of their stance).

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
باري
مختار
اختيار
حكم
معقول
صارف الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's not doing evil implies He is incapable of it.
Correction: The argument clarifies that capability remains, but the judgment of good/bad cannot be based on intrinsic attributes because that would make the judgment inherent and not a matter of divine choice.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what would be negated if actions were intrinsically good or bad?
A) God's power over actions
B) God's free choice
C) The possibility of evil
D) The existence of wisdom

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument shows that divine freedom is incompatible with actions having intrinsic moral value, as God would be compelled to judge accordingly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes treat moral rules as if they exist independently of God, judging His actions by your own standards of fairness? How does this argument call you to surrender your expectations to His absolute freedom?
?
God's freedom means He is not bound by any external moral code; His judgments flow from His wisdom alone. In what area of your life are you resisting His decisions because they don't fit your sense of 'good'? What would it take for you to trust that His judgment is better than yours?
?
If actions are not intrinsically good or evil, then your own sense of right and wrong is entirely dependent on divine revelation. Are you willing to redefine your morality based solely on what God has commanded, even when it conflicts with your natural inclinations? What is one step you can take today to align your conscience with His word?

The Seventh Argument: Goodness as Motive Preceding Action
Taftazani presents the seventh argument with objection against intrinsic goodness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh argument against intrinsic goodness and the objection that the true motive is knowledge of the qualification.

Core Idea
Goodness cannot motivate action directly; only knowledge of goodness does.

Taftazani presents the seventh argument against the view that actions have intrinsic goodness or badness. The argument shows that if goodness or badness were a motive, it would have to exist before the action, which is impossible. Then an objection clarifies that the real motive is our knowledge of the future qualification, not the quality itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument and objection.
Notice the logical structure: argument -> impossibility -> objection.
2
Understand the argument's logic
Identify the premise: if goodness/badness were a motive, it would precede the action. Then the conclusion: that would require an existent (the quality) to subsist in a non-existent (the action), which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the objection
The objection states that the actual motive is not the quality itself but knowledge (al-‘ilm) of the action being qualified upon occurrence. This avoids the problem because knowledge can exist before the action.
Knowledge is an existent in the mind, not requiring the action's prior existence.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument supports the Ash'ari position that moral values are not intrinsic but determined by revelation. The objection also reinforces that our actions are motivated by knowledge, not by inherent qualities.
This connects to broader debates in kalam about ethical objectivism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : قبح الفعل أو حسنه إذا كان صارفاً عنه أو داعياً إليه كان سابقاً عليه، فيلزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم. واعترض بأن الصارف والداعي في التحقيق هو العلم باتصاف الفعل بالقبح أو الحسن عند الحصول.
The seventh: If an action's badness or goodness were a deterrent from it or an incentive to it, it would be prior to it, so it would entail the subsistence of an existent in a non-existent. And it was objected that the deterrent and incentive in reality is the knowledge of the action being qualified by badness or goodness upon its occurrence.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, also against intrinsic goodness and badness. The argument states: if an action's goodness (ḥusn) or badness (qubḥ) were a deterrent (ṣārif) from the action or an incentive (dā‘ī) towards it, then that quality would have to exist before the action itself. This leads to an impossibility: the subsistence of an existent (the quality) in a non-existent (the action that does not yet exist), i.e., qiyām al-mawjūd bi al-ma‘dūm. The objection clarifies that in reality, the actual deterrent or incentive is not the attribute of goodness or badness itself, but rather the knowledge (al-‘ilm) of the action being qualified as good or bad upon its occurrence. Thus, there is no pre-existing quality without a substrate; knowledge of the future qualification is sufficient to motivate or deter.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
صارف
داعي
علم
قيام الموجود بالمعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the motive for action is the intrinsic quality itself (goodness/badness).
Correction: The motive is actually the knowledge of the quality, not the quality itself.

Checkpoint
What is the objection to the seventh argument?
A) The action must exist before its goodness
B) The motive is actually the knowledge of the quality, not the quality itself
C) The deterrent can be non-existent
D) Goodness and badness are not real

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument shows that if goodness were a motive it would cause an impossibility, but the objection clarifies that the motive is knowledge of the quality, not the quality itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you avoided a sin. Was your avoidance driven by a mere feeling of wrongdoing, or by a clear knowledge of its badness in Allah's sight? If by feeling, how can you transform that into firm knowledge? What step will you take today to learn the Islamic ruling on a struggle you face?
?
The passage says the real motive is knowledge. Do you truly know why you perform your obligatory acts of worship—is it from knowledge of their goodness, or from habit? Commit to studying the wisdom behind one act of worship this week so your intention becomes knowledge-based.
?
When you do a good deed, do you reflect on the knowledge that it is good, or do you simply act? How would your worship change if you consciously reminded yourself before each action of the divine command behind it? Write one reminder you will use before your next prayer.

First Objection: Intuitive Knowledge of Good and Evil
Objection that good and evil are known intuitively, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that goodness and badness are intuitively known does not refute the need for revelation in determining moral obligation.

Core Idea
Intuitive moral knowledge does not settle the theological dispute.

This lesson examines the first objection raised against the Ash'ari position that moral good and evil are not known rationally without revelation. The objectors argue that everyone intuitively knows beneficence is good and aggression is bad. But is that enough to prove moral obligation? The answer reveals a critical distinction that undermines the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is presented and then answered succinctly.
The passage is very concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors claim that the goodness of beneficence and badness of aggression are beyond doubt for any rational person, even without religious commitment. What does this imply about the source of moral knowledge?
This is a common argument from intuition.
3
Understand the answer
The author responds 'Not in the disputed sense.' This concedes the intuition but denies it addresses the specific theological question. What sense is being disputed?
The disputed sense involves reward and punishment from God, not mere approval.
4
Connect to the bigger debate
Reflect on why this distinction matters for kalam. If moral obligation requires divine command, then intuition alone cannot ground it. This is a key move in the Ash'ari argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الإحسان وقبح العدوان ليس موضع شك قال تمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أن حسن الإحسان وقبح العدوان مما لا يشك فيه عاقل وإن لم يتدين : قلنا : لا بالمعنى المتنازع .
The goodness of beneficence and the badness of aggression is not a place of doubt. He said: They cling to aspects: The first: That the goodness of beneficence and the badness of aggression are not doubted by any rational person even if he is not religious. We said: Not in the disputed sense.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer in a kalam debate about whether moral judgments are rationally known or based on revelation. The objectors first assert that the goodness of beneficence and the badness of aggression are intuitively obvious to any rational person, even one without religious commitment. The author responds by making a crucial distinction: he concedes that such judgments are universally acknowledged, but denies that they are good or evil in the sense under dispute. The disputed sense concerns moral obligation (taklīf) that carries reward and punishment from God, not mere conventional or prudential evaluation. Thus the objection fails because it assumes an uncontroversial meaning of 'good' and 'evil' while the argument concerns a specific theological sense.

Key Terms

Checkpoint

A) Because the goodness of beneficence is not known to all rational people.
B) Because the objectors are not religious.
C) Because the admitted goodness is not in the disputed theological sense.
D) Because the objectors ignore revelation.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because it conflates everyday moral intuition with the specific theological sense of moral obligation that is under dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The difference determines whether your action carries theological weight.
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked you why you believed a certain act was good, what would your answer be? Would you point to universal consensus among rational people, or to His revealed word? This lesson warns that consensus is not the same as obligation. Are you willing to surrender your most comfortable moral intuitions to the judgment of revelation, even when they feel self-evident? Commit to one area where you will submit your intuition to scripture.

Second Objection: Preference for Truth and Saving Life
Objection from preference for truth and saving life, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that preference for truth over falsehood proves rational goodness, and the answer that it stems from utility and nature.

Core Idea
Preference for truth is not due to rational goodness but to benefit and human nature.

This lesson examines a key objection against the Ash'ari rejection of rational moral obligation. The objector argues that when faced with equal alternatives, people still prefer truth and saving life, suggesting these acts are intrinsically good. The response shifts the basis from morality to utility and inherent human disposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the objector's claim: preference for truth and saving can only be due to rational goodness.
The objector assumes perfect equality between alternatives.
2
Analyze the answer's two prongs
The answer has two parts: first, the preference is due to benefit (أصلح) and suitability for public interest and human nature; second, the assumption of perfect equality is impossible.
The phrase 'ولا تساوي فإنه محال' is crucial.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why this answer undermines the objection. If preference is based on utility and nature, it does not prove any moral obligation. And if equality never occurs, the argument is built on an impossible premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: من استوى في غرضه الصدق والكذب، وإنقاذ الغريق وإهلاكه يؤثر الصدق والإنقاذ وما ذاك إلا لحسنهما عقلاً. قلنا : بل لكونهما أصلح وأوفق لغرض العامة وأليق برقة الجنسية، على أن هذا القطع إنما هو عند فرض التساوي ولا تساوي فإنه محال .
The second: If truth and falsehood are equal in his purpose, and saving the drowning person and killing him, he prefers truth and saving, and that is only due to their rational goodness. We said: Rather because they are more beneficial and more suitable for the public interest and more fitting to the tenderness of human nature, except that this certainty is only upon the assumption of equality, but equality is impossible.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objector argues that when a person's purpose is indifferent (truth and falsehood equally serve it, or saving and killing equally serve it), the person still prefers truth and saving, and attributes this preference to an intrinsic rational goodness in those acts. The author counters that the preference is not due to rational goodness in the moral sense, but because these acts are more beneficial (أصلح), more in harmony with the public good (أوفق لغرض العامة), and more in keeping with human gentleness (أليق برقة الجنسية). Additionally, the author challenges the premise of perfect equality between alternatives, asserting that it is impossible for two acts to be absolutely equal in all respects; therefore the claimed certainty of preference does not prove rational moral obligation.

Key Terms

أصلح

برقة الجنسية
التساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Preference for truth over falsehood proves that truth is intrinsically rationally good and morally obligatory.
Correction: The preference stems from utility and human nature, not from rational moral obligation. Also, perfect equality between alternatives is impossible, undermining the argument.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does a person prefer truth and saving when alternatives are supposedly equal?
A) Because truth and saving are rationally good in themselves.
B) Because they are more beneficial and suit human nature.
C) Because revelation commands them.
D) Because the person is irrational.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because preference for truth is not based on rational goodness but on benefit and human nature, and perfect equality between alternatives is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says perfect equality between truth and falsehood in purpose is impossible. Think of a time when two moral choices seemed equally beneficial to your goal. Did you still lean toward what is right? What does that reveal about your nature?
?
If someone saved your life, would you think they acted out of rational obligation or human instinct? How would your answer affect how you view your own moral responsibilities toward others?

Breaking the Circularity: Rational Foundations of Morality
Objection that revelation-based ethics leads to circularity, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that a revelation-based ethics leads to circularity and how the author resolves it by appealing to rational knowledge and intrinsic goodness.

Core Idea
Moral circularity is avoided because God's truthfulness is known rationally, and goodness is intrinsic, not command-based.

The third objection targets the Ash'ari view that good and evil are determined by revelation. The critic claims that if morality depends on revelation, then even the foundations of revelation—like God's inability to lie—would also need revelation, creating a vicious circle. Let's examine the objection and its powerful response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage once in Arabic, then the literal translation. Notice the compact argument structure: objection followed by answer.
The key terms 'دور' (circularity) and 'الحسن بالأمر' (goodness by command) are central.
2
Identify the objection's logic
The objector says: if good/evil depend on revelation, then the very basis of revelation (God truthfulness, etc.) would also depend on revelation, creating circularity. Understand why that would be a problem.
Circularity would make the entire system groundless.
3
Trace the answer's reasoning
The answer has two parts: (1) The impossibility of God lying is already proven without circularity (rational proof). (2) Goodness is not defined by God's command but is intrinsic. This breaks the circle.
The phrase 'بل نفسه' is crucial: goodness is by itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان بالشرع لما ثبت أصلاً لأن امتناع كذب الباري، وأمره بالقبح، ونهيه عن الحسن يكون أيضاً بالشرع فيدور. قلنا : قد سبق بيان امتناع كذبه من غير دور على أنا لا نجعل الحسن بالأمر بل نفسه ولا دور حينئذ
The third: If it were by revelation, it would not be established at all because the impossibility of God's lying, and His commanding the bad, and His forbidding the good would also be by revelation, leading to circularity. We said: The explanation of the impossibility of His lying has preceded without circularity, and we do not make goodness by command, but by itself, and then there is no circularity.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its answer. The objector argues that if moral judgments (like good and evil) depend solely on revelation, then the very foundations of revelation—such as the impossibility of God lying, and that God does not command evil or forbid good—would themselves depend on revelation, creating a vicious circle. The author answers by pointing to an earlier argument (not included here) that the impossibility of God's lying is established without circularity, presumably by rational proofs. Furthermore, the author clarifies that they do not define goodness as being caused by God's command; rather, goodness is intrinsic to certain acts (بل نفسه). Therefore, the circularity does not arise because the goodness of an act is not dependent on the command, and the impossibility of God's lying is known rationally.

Key Terms

امتناع كذب الباري
أمره بالقبح
نهيه عن الحسن
دور
الحسن بالأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If goodness is by revelation, then God's attributes must also be known by revelation, making the whole system circular.
Correction: The Ash'ari position avoids circularity because God's attributes (like truthfulness) are known rationally, and goodness is intrinsic to acts, not dependent on commands.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how is circularity avoided?
A) By asserting that God's lying is impossible by revelation alone.
B) By establishing God's impossibility of lying without circularity, and by holding that goodness is intrinsic, not by command.
C) By denying the objection's premise that God could command evil.
D) By saying that revelation itself is not needed for morality.

Key Takeaway
Circularity in moral epistemology is avoided by grounding God's attributes rationally and goodness intrinsically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about a moral rule you follow, is it because you see it as inherently right, or because 'God said so'? What would happen if you had to explain that rule to someone who does not believe in revelation? Are you personally convinced of its rational basis, or do you rely on authority alone?
?
The objection in this passage warns against circular reasoning in faith. Which of your own beliefs might be caught in a similar loop—accepted because of something else that itself depends on the first? Take a moment to trace the foundation of one core belief. Does it rest on reason, or on an assumption you have never examined?
?
If goodness is not merely God's command but exists intrinsically, then your conscience carries an enormous weight: you are expected to discern right from wrong, not just obey. In what area of your life have you avoided this responsibility by outsourcing moral decisions to others? What would it cost you to take ownership of that discernment starting today?

The Fourth Objection: Lying, Miracles, and Prophethood
Objection that rational evil of lying is needed for prophethood, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of a miracle at a liar's hand does not undermine prophethood, by distinguishing between logical possibility and actual occurrence by divine custom.

Core Idea
Miracles confirm prophets not by logical necessity, but by God's established custom.

This passage addresses a key objection against the rational basis of prophethood: if lying is not inherently evil for God, and miracles could occur at the hands of liars, how can we trust any prophet? The answer introduces a crucial distinction between what is possible in itself and what actually occurs by God's customary way of acting. This distinction preserves the evidential value of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: first the objection ('If... then...') then the answer.
The objection assumes that unless lying is evil for God, prophethood cannot be established.
2
Understand the distinction
Focus on the answer: 'ربما يمكن الشيء ويقطع بعدم وقوعه'. This distinguishes between possibility in itself (imkan) and actual non-occurrence due to divine custom (adat).
Custom here refers to God's consistent way of acting, not logical necessity.
3
Apply to prophethood
Reflect on how this distinction defends prophethood: even if a miracle is logically possible at a liar's hand, we know by custom it never happens, so miracles remain reliable signs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - لولم يقبح منه الكذب وإظهار المعجزة عند الكاذب لم تثبت النبوة . :قلنا ربما يمكن الشيء ويقطع بعدم وقوعه كسائر العاديات .
The fourth: If lying by Him were not bad, and the manifestation of a miracle at the hand of a liar, then prophethood would not be established. We said: Perhaps a thing is possible but it is certain not to occur, like other customary matters.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its answer. The objector argues that if lying were not intrinsically evil (and thus impossible for God), and if a miracle could be performed at the hand of a liar, then there would be no way to confirm true prophecy, since a false prophet could also perform miracles. The author responds by drawing a distinction between possibility and actual occurrence: something may be possible in itself (e.g., a miracle appearing at a liar's hand) but we know with certainty that it will not happen due to established custom (عاديات). In other words, God's custom (sunnat Allāh) ensures that miracles only support true prophets, even if it is not logically impossible for a miracle to occur at a liar's hand. Thus prophethood is still established without requiring lying to be inherently evil.

Key Terms
قبح الكذب
إظهار المعجزة
النبوة
العاديات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles must be logically impossible for liars in order for prophethood to be verifiable.
Correction: Miracles can be logically possible at the hands of liars, but divine custom ensures they never actually occur with false claimants.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is prophethood still established even if a miracle could occur at a liar's hand?
A) Because it is logically impossible for a liar to perform a miracle.
B) Because we know by custom that it will not happen, even though it is possible.
C) Because the liar would not want to perform a miracle.
D) Because God only gives miracles to prophets.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is established even if miracles are not logically exclusive to true prophets, because God's custom ensures they only accompany truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to trust God's custom even when evidence seems ambiguous? Where in your life do you demand logical certainty instead of trusting God's consistent ways? How can you cultivate that trust today?
?
The objector demanded that lying be impossible for God. Do you ever impose conditions on God, thinking He must act a certain way for you to believe? What would it take for you to surrender that demand?
?
If God's custom (sunnah) is enough to establish prophethood, what does that say about how you seek guidance? Are you looking for miraculous signs, or are you attentive to the consistent patterns of God's guidance in your life? What one step will you take to be more aware of His custom?

Fifth Objection: Knowledge of God and Blame for Shirk
Objection that rational knowledge of God implies blame for shirk, and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against the rational basis of punishment for shirk and the author's answer that such knowledge comes from established laws and customs, not pure reason.

Core Idea
Knowing God rationally does not automatically entail punishment for shirk — that knowledge comes from revelation.

This lesson examines a specific objection: if rational knowledge of God is sufficient, then anyone who knows God through reason and then commits shirk should rationally know they deserve punishment. The objector presents this as a rational certainty. The author offers a succinct answer, grounding that certainty not in reason alone, but in the established religious laws and customary practices.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's statement: 'Whoever knows Him by His essence, attributes, and favors, then associates partners... knows with certainty that he deserves blame and punishment.'
Notice the strong language: 'علم قطعاً' (knows with certainty).
2
Understand the author's answer
Read the author's reply: 'We said: Because it is known from the establishment of the laws and the continuation of customs upon that.'
The author does not deny the certainty, but traces its source to sharia and custom.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What does this answer tell us about the scope of unaided reason in matters of reward and punishment?
It suggests that moral accountability for shirk is essentially a revealed teaching, not a purely rational deduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : من عرفه بذاته وصفاته وإنعاماته، ثم أشرك به ونسب إليه ما لا يليق به من الزوجية والولد وسائر سمات الحدوث ،والنقصان وأصر على الكفران وعبادة الأوثان، علم قطعاً أنه في معرض الذم والعقاب. قلنا : لما علم من استقرار الشرايع بذلك واستمرار العادات عليه .
The fifth: Whoever knows Him by His essence, attributes, and favors, then associates partners with Him and attributes to Him what is not befitting of Him, such as spouse, child, and all signs of temporality and deficiency, and persists in disbelief and worship of idols, he knows with certainty that he is in a position of blame and punishment. We said: Because it is known from the establishment of the laws and the continuation of customs upon that.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its answer. The objector argues that anyone who knows God through His essence, attributes, and favors, and then commits shirk—ascribing to God what is unbefitting like spouse, child, signs of temporality and deficiency—and persists in disbelief and idol worship, knows with certainty that he deserves blame and punishment. The objector presents this as a rational certainty that does not depend on revelation. The author answers by attributing this knowledge not to pure reason, but to the establishment of religious laws (استقرار الشرايع) and the continuation of customs (استمرار العادات). In other words, it is through the long history of prophetic teachings and customary practices that humans come to know that shirk deserves punishment, not through unaided reason alone.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author clarifies that this certainty is known through the establishment of divine laws and the continuation of customs — i.e., through revelation and tradition, not unaided reason alone.

Key Takeaway
The certainty that shirk deserves punishment is rooted in revealed laws and customs, not in pure reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The polytheists knew God existed but still committed shirk. The lesson says their blame comes from the established laws and customs of revelation. Are you truly grateful for the revelation you have? Or do you sometimes rely on your own intellect to define right and wrong, forgetting that without the prophets you would not know the full weight of your actions before God?
