Preferring Truth and Rescue: Objection and Response
Second objection and response on preferring truth and rescue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that preference for truth and rescue proves rational goodness by appealing to utility and human nature.

Core Idea
Preference for truth is not due to rational goodness but utility and human tenderness.

This lesson presents a second objection against the Ash'ari position that actions are not inherently good or evil. The objector points out that even when outcomes are equal, people still prefer truth and saving life, which seems to indicate rational moral value. The author's response offers an alternative explanation grounded in benefit and human nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim: even when truth and falsehood or saving and killing yield the same goal, one prefers truth and rescue. Note the conclusion that this is due to rational goodness.
The objector is a proponent of rational good/evil.
2
Read the response
Now read the author's response (qulna). It gives three alternative reasons: beneficial (aslah), conformable to public interest (awfaq li-gharad al-'amma), and fitting to human tenderness (aliyaq bi-riqq al-jinsiyya). Also note the final point that equality is impossible.
3
Analyze the shift in basis
Notice how the author shifts from reason to utility and instinct. This is a key Ash'ari move: denying intrinsic moral value and explaining moral intuitions through other factors.
This connects to the broader debate about rational ethics.
4
Consider the impossibility argument
The response ends by questioning the assumption of perfect equality. If equality is impossible, the objection's premise fails. Reflect on the strength of this response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: من استوى في غرضه الصدق والكذب، وإنقاذ الغريق وإهلاكه يؤثر الصدق والإنقاذ وما ذاك إلا لحسنهما عقلاً. قلنا : بل لكونهما أصلح وأوفق لغرض العامة وأليق برقة الجنسية، على أن هذا القطع إنما هو عند فرض التساوي ولا تساوي فإنه محال .
Second: One for whom truth and falsehood, saving a drowning person and killing him are equal in their goal, prefers truth and rescue, and that is only because of their goodness rationally. We said: Rather, because they are more beneficial and more conformable to the general public's interest and more fitting to human tenderness, given that this certainty only occurs under the assumption of equality, and equality is impossible.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its response. The objector argues that even if truth and falsehood, or saving someone and letting them drown, yield the same outcome for the agent, one instinctively prefers truth and rescue. This preference, he claims, shows that these acts are rationally good. The response (qulna) offers an alternative explanation: the preference arises not from rational recognition of intrinsic goodness, but because these actions are more beneficial (aslah) and more conformable to the general public's interest (awfaq li-gharad al-'amma), and more fitting to human tenderness (araq al-jinsiyya). The response further notes that the alleged certainty only holds under the hypothetical assumption that the outcomes are exactly equal, but such perfect equality is impossible, so the argument fails. Thus, the author shifts the basis from reason to utility and temperament.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The preference for truth and rescue proves these acts are rationally good.
Correction: The author argues it's due to utility and human nature, not rational goodness.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, why do people prefer truth and saving life even when goals are equal?
A) Because they recognize the intrinsic rational goodness of these acts.
B) Because these acts are more beneficial and fit human nature, not because they are rationally good per se.
C) Because revelation commands these acts.
D) Because people are irrational.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari response to the objection from preference for truth is that such preference stems from benefit, public interest, and human tenderness, not from perceived rational goodness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which motivation is actually driving your choices in everyday situations? ?
The author says perfect equality between truth and falsehood is impossible. Reflect on a time you told a small lie, telling yourself 'it makes no difference.' How does this lesson expose the flaw in that reasoning? What would it mean for you to reject all such justifications from now on?
?
If human tenderness and public interest are the real reasons you prefer goodness, then what does that say about your heart? Are you acting out of genuine care for others, or merely out of a sense of duty? Which pleases Allah more, and how can you cultivate the former?

Third Objection: Circularity if Good and Evil Are by Revelation
Third objection about circularity, with response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection against the Ash'arite view that good and evil are known through revelation, and how the response resolves it using rational proof.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided by grounding God's truthfulness in reason, not revelation.

This passage presents a key objection to the Ash'arite position that moral values depend on revelation. If good and evil are defined by revelation, then we cannot even prove that God does not lie or command evil, because that would also depend on revelation, creating a vicious circle. The response shows that the impossibility of God lying is proven rationally, and that goodness is not a product of command but an inherent property, thus breaking the circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection statement and identify the circularity being claimed. The objector argues that if good/evil depend on revelation, then the very basis of revelation (God's truthfulness) would also depend on revelation, leading to a circle.
Notice the term 'dawr' (circularity).
2
Examine the first prong of the response
The response first states that the impossibility of God lying (imtina' kadhib al-Bari) has already been demonstrated without circularity. This means reason, not revelation, establishes this principle.
This breaks the circle from the start.
3
Examine the second prong of the response
The response also clarifies that goodness is not 'by command' (bi al-amr) but 'by itself' (bi nafsihi). This means acts have inherent goodness, even if known through revelation, so circularity does not arise.
This distinguishes Ash'arism from pure voluntarism.
4
Understand how both prongs avoid circularity
By establishing a rational basis for God's truthfulness and by affirming inherent moral properties, the response shows that the objection's circularity is not present.
Reflect on why this matters for the broader debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان بالشرع لما ثبت أصلاً لأن امتناع كذب الباري، وأمره بالقبح، ونهيه عن الحسن يكون أيضاً بالشرع فيدور. قلنا : قد سبق بيان امتناع كذبه من غير دور على أنا لا نجعل الحسن بالأمر بل نفسه ولا دور حينئذ.
Third: If it were by revelation, it would not be established at all, because the impossibility of lying by the Creator, and His commanding evil, and His prohibiting good would also be by revelation, leading to circularity. We said: The impossibility of His lying has already been demonstrated without circularity, and we do not make good by command but rather by itself, and there is no circularity then.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its response. The objector argues that if good and evil are determined solely by revelation, then we cannot even establish that God cannot lie or command evil, because those principles would also depend on revelation, creating a vicious circle (dawr). The response (qulna) counters by stating that the impossibility of God lying has already been demonstrated (qad sabaqa bayan) without circularity, using reason. Moreover, the Ash'arite position does not make something good simply because God commands it (the 'command theory' but rather they hold that goodness is a property that exists in the act itself? Actually the response says 'we do not make good by command but rather by itself (nafsahu)'. This is nuanced: the author clarifies that goodness is not created by the command but is a property of the act, known through revelation? However, the text says 'بل نفسه' (but rather itself). This is a key point: the Ash'arites affirm that acts have inherent properties that are revealed, but avoid circularity by basing the impossibility of God's lying on rational proof, not on revelation. Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
امتناع كذب الباري
دور
الحسن بالأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'arites believe that an act is good only because God commands it, with no inherent property.
Correction: The passage states: 'we do not make good by command but rather by itself', indicating that acts have an inherent goodness even if known through revelation.

Checkpoint
How does the author avoid the charge of circularity in the third objection?
A) By admitting that the circularity is unavoidable.
B) By claiming that good and evil are known rationally, not by revelation.
C) By showing that the impossibility of God's lying is proven rationally, not by revelation, and that goodness is not made by command but by the act itself.
D) By denying that the impossibility of God's lying needs proof.

Key Takeaway
The third objection is refuted by showing that God's truthfulness is rationally proven and that moral properties are not created by command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were asked to defend the Ashʿarite position against the charge of circularity, could you articulate the two-part response from this passage? If not, what does that reveal about your readiness to give a reasoned account of your faith?

Fourth Objection: Prophecy and the Rational Evil of Lying
Fourth objection and response on prophecy and rational evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'arite response to the fourth objection preserves prophecy while rejecting rational good and evil through the concept of customary divine practice.

Core Idea
Prophecy is established by God's habitual practice, not by rational necessity.

This lesson presents the fourth objection against the Ash'arite denial of rational good and evil: if lying were not inherently evil and God could support a liar with a miracle, how could we ever trust a prophet's claim? The response introduces a key idea: some things are possible in themselves yet their occurrence is ruled out by God's customary way of acting. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's challenge: if lying is not evil by nature, and if God can show miracles through liars, then no prophet can be reliably identified.
2
Understand the response: possibility vs. occurrence
The response (qulna) introduces a distinction: something can be possible (mumkin) in itself yet its actual occurrence can be ruled out. This is the concept of 'al-'adiyyat' (customary events).
This is a core Ash'arite idea: miracles for liars are logically possible but never happen because God's habit prevents it.
3
Connect to the larger debate on rational good and evil
Consider why this response matters: it allows the Ash'arites to reject rational good and evil while still giving a basis for knowing true prophethood – through reliable custom, not necessary reason.
This preserves the possibility of prophetic knowledge without attributing rational moral properties to actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع - لولم يقبح منه الكذب وإظهار المعجزة عند الكاذب لم تثبت النبوة . قلنا ربما يمكن الشيء ويقطع بعدم وقوعه كسائر العاديات .
Fourth: If lying and the appearance of a miracle at the hand of a liar were not evil from Him, prophethood would not be established. We said: It is possible that a thing is possible yet its occurrence is ruled out, like other customary events (al-'adiyyat).
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and its response. The objector argues that if lying (kadhib) were not inherently evil (qabih), and if it were possible for God to support a false claimant with a miracle, then prophecy would become unreliable and unprovable. The response (qulna) introduces a distinction: something can be possible (mumkin) in itself yet its actual occurrence can be ruled out (yuqta' bi-'adam wuqu'ih) based on customary practice (al-'adiyyat). This is a key Ash'arite concept: miracles are not intrinsically impossible, but God's customary action (sunnat Allah) prevents them from happening to false claimants. Thus, prophecy is established not by rational necessity but by God's habit, which is reliable though not necessary. This response upholds the Ash'arite rejection of rational good and evil while preserving the possibility of knowing true prophets through customary miracles.

Key Terms
الكذب
إظهار المعجزة
النبوة
العاديات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that without rational evil of lying, any miracle would prove any claim, making prophecy indistinguishable from falsehood.
Correction: The author points out that God's habitual practice (al-'adiyyat) prevents this, even if it is not logically impossible. So prophecy is known through reliable custom, not necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, how can prophecy be established if lying is not rationally evil?
A) It cannot be established, so the objection is valid.
B) By rational proof that God would not support a liar.
C) Through God's customary practice that miracles do not occur for liars, even though it is not necessary.
D) By the fact that prophets are morally perfect.

Key Takeaway
Prophecy is established through God's reliable custom (al-'adiyyat) that miracles do not occur for false claimants, even though it is not rationally necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself demanding a rational proof for everything in your faith, or do you truly trust God's habitual custom that He has set since the beginning? This lesson teaches that prophecy is proven not by necessity but by God's reliable habit. Where does your heart stand?
?
If God's protection of the truth depended on your ability to reason, would you ever be certain? Today's lesson shows that God's custom is enough. Are you willing to let go of your need for absolute logical necessity and instead rely on the consistency of His ways?
?
When you hear about miracles, do you see them as mere possibilities or as signs from God that confirm His prophet? This passage reminds us that even though miracles could happen to anyone in principle, God's custom prevents that. Does this certainty move you to deeper gratitude and submission, or do you still secretly wish for a sign tailored to your own demands?

Fifth Objection: Knowing God Yet Associating Partners
Fifth objection and response about rational blame for the polytheist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection regarding the rational blame of a polytheist who knows God, and the Ash'arite response that such blame is known through religious laws and customs, not pure reason.

Core Idea
Blame for shirk is not self-evident; it rests on divine laws and social customs.

This objection challenges the assertion that rational judgments alone determine moral blame. It presents a scenario where someone knows God yet persists in shirk. The response clarifies the source of this knowledge, grounding it in revelation and customary practice rather than pure reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection (الخامس) then the response (قلنا).
The objection assumes that blame for shirk is rationally obvious.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector claims that someone who knows God but commits shirk is certainly subject to blame and punishment. This is presented as a necessary rational judgment.
3
Understand the response
The response ('We said') shifts the basis: the certainty comes from the establishment of religions (الشرايع) and the continuation of customs (العادات), not from pure reason alone.
This is a key Ash'arite position: moral judgments depend on revelation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: if blame were purely rational, then all humans would be accountable even without prophets. The response preserves the necessity of revelation for moral obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : من عرفه بذاته وصفاته وإنعاماته، ثم أشرك به ونسب إليه ما لا يليق به من الزوجية والولد وسائر سمات الحدوث ،والنقصان وأصر على الكفران وعبادة الأوثان، علم قطعاً أنه في معرض الذم والعقاب. قلنا : لما علم من استقرار الشرايع بذلك واستمرار العادات عليه .
Fifth: One who knows Him by His essence, attributes, and favors, then associates partners with Him and attributes to Him what is unfitting, such as spouse, child, and other marks of temporality and deficiency, and persists in ingratitude and idol-worship, it is known with certainty that he is subject to blame and punishment. We said: Because it is known from the establishment of religions (shara'i') regarding that and the continuation of customs (al-'adat) upon it.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its response. The objector argues that if a person knows God's essence, attributes, and blessings, yet still commits shirk (associating partners) and persists in disbelief, common sense dictates that such a person deserves blame and punishment. This, he claims, is a rational judgment independent of revelation. The response (qulna) shifts the basis: the knowledge that such a person is blameworthy comes from 'the establishment of religions (shara'i') regarding that and the continuation of customs (al-'adat) upon it.' In other words, the certainty that idolaters are punished is not rational but comes from the information provided by the divinely revealed laws (shara'i') and the habitual practice of societies (al-'adat) that condemn such acts. This is consistent with the Ash'arite position that moral blame and punishment are known only through revelation and customary consensus, not through pure reason.

العادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that blame for shirk is self-evident and known by pure reason alone.
Correction: The author argues that this knowledge comes from established religions and customs, not from pure reason.

Checkpoint

A) Because reason alone tells us that shirk is evil.
B) Because it is known from the establishment of religions and the continuation of customs, not from pure reason.
C) Because all humans have an innate moral sense.
D) Because the polytheist himself knows it.

Key Takeaway
The certainty that a polytheist deserves blame derives from established religious laws and customs, not from pure reason.

The Mu'tazilite Consensus Argument Refuted
Mu'tazilite argument from universal agreement, with detailed response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite refutation of the Mu'tazilite argument that universal agreement on good and evil proves their rational basis in a theological sense.

Core Idea
Universal agreement on good and evil does not prove divine reward or punishment.

The Mu'tazilites claimed that everyone, even non-believers, agrees that justice is good and injustice is evil. They used this as their strongest proof that good and evil are known by reason alone, without revelation. In this lesson, we examine their argument and the Ash'arite response, which shows that the supposed agreement is only about worldly benefit, not about what deserves divine reward or punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazilites present their strongest proof: universal agreement on the goodness of justice and evil of injustice, even among non-religious people.
Notice the examples: justice, beneficence vs. injustice, ingratitude.
2
Identify the Ash'arite response
Read the response which begins with 'والجواب'. The Ash'arites deny agreement in the disputed sense, which is the theological sense involving divine praise/blame and reward/punishment.
The key phrase is 'منع الاتفاق على الحسن والقبح بالمعنى المتنازع'.
3
Understand the distinction of meanings
The response explains that the actual agreement is about conformity to public interest and custom, not about intrinsic moral properties that entail divine recompense.
This distinction is crucial: moral terms are often used in a non-theological sense.
4
Reflect on the analogy argument
The passage also rejects the Mu'tazilite analogy from worldly praise/blame to divine recompense, noting that non-religious people don't even believe in an afterlife.
Consider why the analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمعتزلة في كون الحسن والقبح عقليين وجوه : الأول : وهو عمدتهم القصوى أن حسن مثل العدل والإحسان، وقبح مثل الظلم والكفران مما اتفق عليه العقلاء حتى الذين لا يتدينون بدين ولا يقولون بشرع كالبراهمة والدهرية وغيرهم، بل ربما يبالغ فيه غير المليين حتى يستقبحون ذبح الحيوانات. [؟] وذلك وذلك مع أنه ذاتي للفعل يعلم بالعقل لما كان كذلك. والجواب : منع الاتفاق على الحسن والقبح بالمعنى المتنازع ، وهو كونه متعلق المدح والذم عند الله تعالى، واستحقاق الثواب والعقاب في حكمه ، بل بمعنى ملاءمة غرض العامة وطباعهم ، وعدمها ، ومتعلق المدح والذم فى مجاري العقول والعادات ولا نزاع في ذلك فبطل اعتراضهم بأنا نعني بالحسن ما ليس لفعله مدخل في استحقاق الذم، وبالقبح خلافه، وأما اعتراضهم بأنه لما ثبت المدح والذم واستحقاق الثواب والعقاب في الشاهد، فكذا في الغائب قياساً ، فلا يخفي ضعفه. كيف، وغير المتشرع ربما لا يقول بدار الآخرة والثواب والعقاب .
For the Mu'tazilites regarding good and evil being rational, there are arguments: First: And it is their strongest proof: that the goodness of justice and beneficence, and the evil of injustice and ingratitude are things agreed upon by rational people, even those who adhere to no religion and do not follow a revealed law, like the Brahmins and the Materialists (Dahriyya) and others. Rather, the non-religious sometimes go so far as to consider the slaughter of animals evil. [؟] and that [؟] with it being intrinsic to the act, known by reason, would not be thus. And the answer: Denial of agreement on good and evil in the disputed sense, which is that they are related to praise and blame with God Almighty, and deserving reward and punishment in His judgment. Rather, [the agreement is] in the sense of conformity to the general public's interest and their natures, or lack thereof, and being related to praise and blame in the course of intellects and customs, and there is no dispute about that. So their objection is invalidated, because we mean by 'good' that whose doing does not entail deserving blame, and by 'evil' the opposite. As for their objection that since praise and blame and entitlement to reward and punishment are established in the witnessed world, so too in the unseen by analogy—its weakness is not hidden. Moreover, the non-religious person might not believe in the abode of the afterlife and reward and punishment.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite argument (their strongest proof, 'umdatuhum al-quswa') and the Ash'arite response. The Mu'tazilites claim that everyone, including non-religious people like Brahmins and materialists (dahriyya), agrees that justice and beneficence are good, and injustice and ingratitude are evil. Furthermore, non-religious people sometimes even consider the slaughter of animals evil, indicating a universal rational recognition. The response (al-jawab) begins by denying agreement in the disputed sense (al-ma'na al-mutanaza'), which is that such acts are related to divine praise and blame and deserve reward and punishment from God. For the Mu'tazilites, good and evil are intrinsic properties known by reason, and they entail divine reward or punishment. The Ash'arite response counters that any universal agreement is only about worldly interests (mulama'a ghard al-'amma) and social customs, not about divine recompense. The response also rejects an argument by analogy from the witnessed world to the unseen (al-gha'ib), noting that even if praise and blame follow good and evil in this world, that does not prove it in the afterlife, especially since non-religious people do not even believe in an afterlife. Thus, the Mu'tazilite 'consensus' argument fails because it conflates different senses of 'good' and 'evil'.

Key Terms
عمدتهم القصوى
العدل والإحسان
الظلم والكفران
البراهمة
الدهرية
المعنى المتنازع
مدح وذم
استحقاق الثواب والعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universal agreement on moral values proves that they are known by reason alone and that God must reward or punish based on them.
Correction: Universal agreement is only about worldly benefits and customs, not about theological consequences. Reason alone cannot determine divine reward or punishment.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'arite response to the Mu'tazilite claim that everyone agrees on the goodness of justice and evil of injustice?
A) The claim is false because some people disagree.
B) The agreement is only about worldly benefit and custom, not about divine reward and punishment.
C) The agreement proves that reason alone can know divine law.
D) The response admits the agreement but says it is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Universal agreement on good and evil does not prove their rational basis in a theological sense; it only proves agreement on worldly interests and customs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel strongly that something is unjust, do you naturally assume God will punish it in the afterlife? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you do to align your moral feelings with revealed criteria?
?
Think of a moral issue where you share universal agreement with others (e.g., helping the needy). Does that agreement guarantee that God sees it as good? How can you verify that your moral compass is calibrated to divine command, not just social custom?
?
The non-religious may agree on justice, but they deny the afterlife. If you base your moral certainty on universal human sentiment, what happens when that sentiment contradicts revelation? Will you be willing to submit your own sense of right and wrong to the divine judgment, even if it feels uncomfortable? Make a concrete commitment to test one moral assumption today against the Quran and Sunnah.

The Mu'tazila's First Argument for Rational Good and Evil and Its Refutation
Presents Mu'tazila's first argument for rational good and evil and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first argument for rational good and evil and the Ash'ari refutation based on distinguishing the disputed meaning of good and evil.

Core Idea
Universal agreement on good and evil is about social custom, not divine reward.

This passage presents one of the central debates in Islamic theology: whether good and evil are known by reason alone or only through revelation. The Mu'tazila argue that even atheists agree on the goodness of justice, so it must be rational. The author (an Ash'ari) responds by clarifying the meaning of 'good' and 'evil' and showing the argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: presentation of the Mu'tazila argument, then the response.
The author first notes that some Ash'aris accept rational good/evil for some actions.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila argue that all rational people, even non-religious, agree that justice is good and injustice is evil. They conclude this is because these qualities are intrinsic and known by reason.
3
Understand the refutation
The author denies that this agreement applies to the theological meaning of good/evil (meriting divine reward/punishment). Instead, it applies to social custom. He also dismisses an analogical argument.
The refutation hinges on distinguishing the disputed sense from the uncontested sense.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters. It shows that reason alone cannot determine moral obligation in the sight of God without revelation.
This is a key difference between Ash'ari and Mu'tazili theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مر ولقوة الأخيرين ذهب البعض منا إلى الحسن والقبح عقلاً في بعض الأفعال . للمعتزلة في كون الحسن والقبح عقليين وجوه : الأول : وهو عمدتهم القصوى أن حسن مثل العدل والإحسان، وقبح مثل الظلم والكفران مما اتفق عليه العقلاء حتى الذين لا يتدينون بدين ولا يقولون بشرع كالبراهمة والدهرية وغيرهم، بل ربما يبالغ فيه غير المليين حتى يستقبحون ذبح الحيوانات. وذلك مع أنه ذاتي للفعل يعلم بالعقل لما كان كذلك والجواب : منع الاتفاق على الحسن والقبح بالمعنى المتنازع ، وهو كونه متعلق المدح والذم عند الله تعالى، واستحقاق الثواب والعقاب في حكمه ، بل بمعنى ملاءمة غرض العامة وطباعهم ، وعدمها ، ومتعلق المدح والذم فى مجاري العقول والعادات ولا نزاع في ذلك فبطل اعتراضهم بأنا نعني بالحسن ما ليس لفعله مدخل في استحقاق الذم، وبالقبح خلافه، وأما اعتراضهم بأنه لما ثبت المدح والذم واستحقاق الثواب والعقاب في الشاهد، فكذا في الغائب قياساً ، فلا يخفي ضعفه. كيف، وغير المتشرع ربما لا يقول بدار الآخرة والثواب والعقاب .
Some of us have gone to [the position of] good and evil being rational in some actions. For the Mu'tazila concerning good and evil being rational there are aspects: The first, and it is their chief argument, that the good of justice and beneficence, and the evil of injustice and ingratitude, are agreed upon by rational people, even those who do not adhere to a religion nor follow a law, like the Brahmins and the Materialists and others. Indeed, the non-religious sometimes go so far as to consider the slaughter of animals evil. And that, while it is intrinsic to the act known by reason, would not be thus [if not rational]. The answer: Denial of agreement upon good and evil in the disputed sense, which is their being grounds for praise and blame with God Almighty, and deserving reward and punishment in His judgment, but rather in the sense of suitability to the common purpose and their natures, or lack thereof, and being grounds for praise and blame in the streams of intellects and customs, and there is no dispute about that. Thus their objection is invalid: that we mean by good that which has no role in deserving blame, and by evil the opposite. As for their objection that since praise, blame, and deserving reward and punishment are established in the witnessed world, so likewise in the absent world by analogy, its weakness is not hidden. How so? For the non-religious perhaps does not affirm the abode of the Hereafter and reward and punishment.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage begins by noting that some from the author's own school (Ash'aris) accept rational good and evil for some actions, then presents the first argument of the Mu'tazila for rational good and evil and its refutation. The Mu'tazila argue that concepts like justice being good and injustice being evil are universally agreed upon by all rational people, even those without religion, indicating that these judgments are intrinsic and known by reason alone. The author refutes this by distinguishing the disputed sense of good/evil as that which merits divine reward or punishment from the uncontested sense of social or customary approval. He argues that the alleged agreement only applies to the latter, not the former. He also dismisses the Mu'tazila's analogical argument from the witnessed world to the unseen as weak, since non-religious people may not even believe in the afterlife.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila's universal agreement argument proves that good and evil are known by reason alone in the theological sense.
Correction: The agreement only pertains to social customs, not to divine reward and punishment, which cannot be known without revelation.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the Mu'tazila's first argument for rational good and evil?
A) That good and evil are known only by revelation.
B) That all rational people agree on the goodness of justice and evil of injustice, even non-religious people.
C) That good and evil are purely subjective.
D) That only Muslims can know good and evil.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's reliance on universal agreement fails because the agreement is only about social custom, not about moral obligation in the sight of God.

How does this realization affect your own emotional response to sin and your reliance on revelation for understanding true moral responsibility?

Are you willing to pay that cost?

The Mu'tazila's Second Argument: Preference for Truth Without Benefit
Presents the Mu'tazila's second argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's second argument for rational good and evil, and why the author refutes it by pointing to social and natural causes rather than intrinsic rational judgment.

Core Idea
Universal preference for truth does not prove rational goodness for divine reward; it reflects social utility.

The Mu'tazila argued that even without knowing revelation, people prefer truthfulness and rescue others, showing these actions are rationally good. This lesson examines that argument and its refutation. We'll see how the author shifts the explanation from intrinsic rational value to social convention and natural sympathy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's argument
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazila claims that truthfulness is preferred even when it brings no personal benefit, and similarly rescuing someone from death. Note the claim that this preference proves intrinsic rational goodness.
Pay attention to the terms 'ذاتي' (intrinsic) and 'ضروري' (necessary).
2
Study the author's refutation
The author offers two main refutations: (1) Preference for truth is due to social utility and the praise of rational people, not divine reward. (2) Rescuing is due to natural human sympathy, not rational moral law. Observe how these explanations deny the Mu'tazila's conclusion.
The author also questions the certainty of preference under absolute equality.
3
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this refutation matters: it defends the Ash'ari position that good and evil are known only through revelation, not reason alone. The observed human behavior does not necessarily indicate rational moral truths.
This contrasts with the Mu'tazila's claim of rational obligation independent of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن من استوى في تحصيل غرضه الصدق والكذب بحيث لا مرجح أصلاً ولا علم باستقرار الشرائع على تحسين الصدق وتقبيح الكذب ، فإنه يؤثر الصدق قطعاً وما ذاك إلا لأن حسنه ذاتي ضروروي عقلي، وكذلك إنقاذ من أشرف على الهلاك حيث لا يتصور للمنقذ نفع وغرض ولو مدحاً وثناء . والجواب : أن إيثار الصدق لما تقرر في النفوس من كونه الملائم لغرض العامة ومصلحة العالم والاستواء المفروض إنما هو في تحصيل غرض ذلك الشخص، واندفاع حاجته لا على الإطلاق كيف والصدق ممدوح، والكذب مذموم عند العقلاء. وعلى مذهبكم عند الله أيضاً بحكم العقل. ولو فرضنا الاستواء من كل وجه، فلا نسلم إيثار الصدق قطعاً، وإنما القطع بذلك عند الفرض والتقدير، فيتوهم أنه قطع عند وقوع المقدر المفروض ، وقد أوضحنا الفرق في فوائد شرح الأصول. وأما انقاذ الهالك فلرقة الجنسية المجبولة في الطبيعة وكله يتصور مثل تلك الحالة لنفسه، فيجره استحسان ذلك الفعل من غيره في حق نفسه إلى استحسانه من نفسه في حق غيره . وبالجملة لا نسلم أن إيثار الصدق والإنقاذ عند من لم يعلم استقرار الشرائع على حستها إنما هو الحسنهما عند الله على ما هو المتنازع بل لأمر آخر.
The second: That if someone is equal in achieving his purpose in truthfulness and lying, such that there is no preference at all and no knowledge of the establishment of laws on deeming truthfulness good and lying evil, then he will definitely prefer truthfulness, and this is only because its goodness is intrinsic, necessary, and rational. Likewise, rescuing someone about to perish, where no benefit or purpose is conceivable for the rescuer, even praise and commendation. The answer: That preference for truthfulness is due to what has been established in souls as being suitable for the common purpose and the welfare of the world. The supposed equalness is only in achieving that person's purpose and fulfilling his need, not absolutely. How so? For truthfulness is praiseworthy and lying is blameworthy among rational people, and according to your doctrine, also with God by rational judgment. If we suppose equalness in every respect, we do not concede that truthfulness is definitely preferred. Rather, certainty is only under the assumption and supposition, so it is imagined that it is certain when the supposed scenario occurs. We have clarified the difference in the benefits of the commentary on the Principles. As for rescuing the perishing, it is due to the compassion of species ingrained in nature, and everyone imagines such a state for himself, so he is drawn from approving that action from others in his own case to approving it from himself in the case of others. In summary, we do not concede that the preference for truthfulness and rescue by one who does not know the establishment of laws on their goodness is due to their goodness with God in the disputed sense, but rather for another reason.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's second argument for rational good and evil and its refutation. The argument claims that even when truthfulness and lying are equally beneficial to an individual, and there is no revealed law, the person will still prefer truthfulness, indicating its intrinsic rational goodness. Similarly, rescuing someone from death without any personal benefit shows rational goodness. The author refutes by pointing out that truthfulness is preferred because it is socially useful and praised by rational people, not because of an intrinsic rational judgment about divine reward. He also argues that the supposed equalness is only in individual need, not absolute, and that the certainty of preference is only hypothetical. For rescuing, he attributes it to natural human sympathy, not rational moral law. Thus, the observed preferences do not prove rational good in the theological sense.

The Mu'tazila's Third Argument and Its Refutation
Presents the third argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's third argument for rational good and evil and the Ash'ari refutation that avoids circularity by redefining good and evil as acts being commanded or forbidden.

Core Idea
Good and evil are defined by revelation, not known before it — thus no circle.

The Mu'tazila argued that if good and evil were only known through revelation, then we could never be sure that revelation is truthful, because we would need to know that lying is evil before trusting the Lawgiver. This would lead to an infinite regress or circularity. The Ash'ari response redefines the terms: good simply means what is commanded, evil means what is forbidden. This cuts the circle cleanly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional statement, a dependency argument, and an answer.
The last sentence from Imam al-Haramayn is cut off, but the main argument and refutation are complete.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's argument
The argument says: if good/evil come only from revelation, they can't be established. Why? Because to know the Lawgiver's command is good, we need to know that lying is evil and commanding evil is foolish. That knowledge would come either from reason (but reason is assumed powerless here) or from revelation (circular).
Track the two possibilities: 'إما بالعقل' and 'وإما بالشرع'.
3
Understand the circularity problem
If we need revelation to know good/evil, but we need to know good/evil to trust revelation, we have a vicious circle. The Mu'tazila use this to argue that good/evil must be known by reason independently.
The phrase 'فيدور' in Arabic literally means 'it circles'.
4
See the Ash'ari answer
The author refutes by redefining good and evil. They are not properties known before revelation; rather, 'good' is simply what is commanded and praised, 'evil' what is forbidden and blamed. This removes the need for independent knowledge.
Notice the shift: 'بل نجعل الحسن عبارة عن كون الفعل متعلق الأمر والمدح'.
5
Note the quote from Imam al-Haramayn
The passage ends with a quote from al-Juwaynī that reinforces the point, though it is incomplete. Reflect on how his statement aligns with the refutation.
The quote likely continues to clarify that good/evil are not known except by revelation in this definitional sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو لم يثبت الحسن والقبح إلا بالشرع لم يثبتا أصلاً، لأن العلم بحسن ما أمر به الشارع أو أخبر عن حسنه وبكذب ما نهى عنه أو أخبر عن قبحه يتوقف على أن الكذب قبيح لا يصدر عنه ، وأن الأمر بالقبيح والنهي عن الحسن سفه وعبث لا يليق به وذلك إما بالعقل والتقدير أنه معزول لا حكم له . وإما بالشرع فيدور. والجواب : أنا لا نجعل الأمر والنهي دليل الحسن والقبح ليرد ما ذكرتم ، بل نجعل الحسن عبارة عن كون الفعل متعلق الأمر والمدح، والقبح عن كونه متعلق النهي والذم . قال إمام الحرمين ومما يجب التنبيه له أن قولنا لا يدرك الحسن والقبح إلا
The third: If good and evil were not established except by revelation, they would not be established at all, because knowledge of the goodness of what the Lawgiver commanded or informed of its goodness, and of the badness of what He forbade or informed of its badness, depends on the fact that lying is bad and does not issue from Him, and that commanding badness and forbidding goodness is foolish and frivolous unbefitting Him. This [knowledge] is either by reason, and it is assumed that it [reason] is invalid with no ruling, or by revelation, leading to circularity. The answer: We do not make command and prohibition the evidence for good and evil as you mentioned, but we make good an expression of the act being related to command and praise, and evil an expression of it being related to prohibition and blame. Imam al-Haramayn said: Among what must be noted is that our statement that good and evil are not known except...
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's third argument and its refutation. The argument is that if good and evil are only known through revelation, then they would be unprovable, since to know that the Lawgiver's commands are good, we would need to know that lying is evil (so He does not lie) and that commanding evil is foolish (which He does not do). That knowledge would require either reason (but if reason is invalid, it cannot provide it) or revelation, leading to circularity. The author refutes by redefining good and evil: they are simply what is commanded/praised or forbidden/blamed, not additional properties that need to be known before trusting the Lawgiver. The quote from Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwaynī) begins but is cut off; it likely continues the point.

Key Terms

الأمر والنهي
الحسن
القبح

إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position that good and evil are defined by revelation leads to an infinite regress or circularity because one cannot trust revelation without knowing that it commands good.
Correction: The Ash'ari redefinition avoids circularity by making 'good' and 'evil' mere labels for what revelation commands or forbids, so there is no independent standard needed before accepting revelation.

Checkpoint

A) By proving that reason alone can know good and evil.
B) By defining good as what is commanded and evil as what is forbidden, not as independent properties.
C) By accepting that revelation is unnecessary.
D) By arguing that the Lawgiver is known through miracles.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position defines good and evil in terms of divine command and prohibition, thus avoiding the circularity that would arise if they were independent properties known before revelation.

Third Objection: Good and Evil Only by Scripture? Ash'ari Answer
Al-Taftazani presents and answers the third objection against rational knowledge of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari redefinition of good and evil as scriptural terms and how it avoids the circularity objection against the sole scriptural source of moral knowledge.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not intrinsic qualities but scriptural designations.

This passage tackles a powerful objection to the Ash'ari position that good and evil are known only through revelation. The objection claims that if scripture is the sole source, then we can never know that scripture itself is good, leading to circularity. Al-Taftazani presents the Ash'ari response, which redefines the very meaning of 'good' and 'evil' to break the circle. Let's see how.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the opening lines of the passage that present the third objection. Notice the logical structure: if good and evil depend solely on scripture, then we cannot know that the Lawgiver's command is good without prior moral knowledge, leading to a vicious circle.
The objection is a classic critique of theological voluntarism.
2
Identify the circularity argument
The objection states that to know that what the Lawgiver commands is good, we must first know that lying is ugly and that commanding evil is foolishness. But that prior knowledge cannot come from scripture (since that would be circular) nor from reason (if reason is denied authority). This is the core of the objection.
Understand the two horns of the dilemma: either reason or scripture, both problematic.
3
Examine the Ash'ari redefinition
Al-Taftazani presents the Ash'ari answer: 'good' does not mean an intrinsic quality but simply that an act is the object of divine command and praise, and 'evil' is the object of prohibition and blame. This redefinition removes the need for any prior moral knowledge.
The key phrase: 'بل نجعل الحسن عبارة عن كون الفعل متعلق الأمر والمدح'.
4
Understand the clarification by Imam al-Haramayn
Imam al-Haramayn clarifies that the statement 'good and evil are only known by scripture' is figurative. It does not mean there is a real quality of goodness attached to the act; rather, the act's goodness is exactly the scriptural praise. This prevents misunderstanding.
He says: 'الحسن عبارة عن نفس ورود الشرع بالثناء على فاعله'.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes by noting that the impossibility of lying on the part of the Lawgiver has been established elsewhere in kalam, without circularity. This reinforces the Ash'ari position without needing rational moral knowledge.
This shows internal consistency in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو لم يثبت الحسن والقبح إلا بالشرع لم يثبتا أصلاً، لأن العلم بحسن ما أمر به الشارع أو أخبر عن حسنه وبكذب ما نهى عنه أو أخبر عن قبحه يتوقف على أن الكذب قبيح لا يصدر عنه ، وأن الأمر بالقبيح والنهي عن الحسن سفه وعبث لا يليق به وذلك إما بالعقل والتقدير أنه معزول لا حكم له . وإما بالشرع فيدور. والجواب : أنا لا نجعل الأمر والنهي دليل الحسن والقبح ليرد ما ذكرتم ، بل نجعل الحسن عبارة عن كون الفعل متعلق الأمر والمدح، والقبح عن كونه متعلق النهي والذم . قال إمام الحرمين ومما يجب التنبيه له أن قولنا لا يدرك الحسن والقبح إلا بالشرع تجوز حيث يوهم كون الحسن زائداً على الشرع موقوفاً إدراكه عليه وليس الأمر كذلك. بل الحسن عبارة عن نفس ورود الشرع بالثناء على فاعله وكذا في القبح، فإذا وصفنا فعلاً بالوجوب، فلسنا نقدر للفعل الواجب صفة بها يتميز عما ليس بواجب، وإنما المراد بالواجب الفعل الذي ورد الشرع بالأمر به إيجاباً، وكذا الحظر. هذا وقد بينا في بحث الكلام امتناع الكذب على الشارع من غير لزوم دور.
The third: If good and evil were only established by scripture, they would not be established at all, because knowledge of the goodness of what the Lawgiver commanded or informed of its goodness, and of the ugliness of what He forbade or informed of its ugliness, depends on the fact that lying is ugly and does not occur from Him, and that commanding the ugly and forbidding the good is foolishness and frivolity unbefitting to Him. That is either by reason, and it is assumed that it has no authority; or by scripture, leading to circularity. The answer: We do not make command and prohibition the proof of good and evil as you mentioned; rather we make good an expression of an act being the object of command and praise, and evil an expression of its being the object of prohibition and blame. Imam al-Haramayn said: It must be noted that our statement 'good and evil are only known by scripture' is a figurative expression, because it suggests that good is something additional to scripture whose knowledge depends on it, but it is not so. Rather good is the very occurrence of scripture praising the doer, and similarly for evil. So when we describe an act as obligatory, we do not posit for the obligatory act a quality by which it is distinguished from what is not obligatory; rather the meaning of obligatory is the act regarding which scripture has issued a command in the sense of requirement, and similarly for prohibition. And we have already shown in the discussion of kalam the impossibility of lying on the part of the Lawgiver without circularity.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a classic argument against the Ash‘ari position that good and evil are known only through revelation. The objection states that if good and evil depend solely on scripture, they could never be known, because to know that what the Lawgiver commands is good requires knowing that the Lawgiver does not command evil, which itself presupposes knowledge of good and evil—a circularity. Also, the Lawgiver's command itself cannot be known to be good unless we know that commanding evil is bad, which again requires prior moral knowledge. The answer redefines the terms: 'good' does not mean an intrinsic quality but simply that an act is the object of divine command and praise; 'evil' means it is the object of prohibition and blame. Imam al-Haramayn clarifies that the Ash‘ari expression 'good and evil are only known by scripture' should not be misunderstood as implying an independent moral reality; rather, goodness is identical with the act of scripture praising the doer. Thus, calling an act 'obligatory' does not signify a real attribute but merely that scripture has commanded it as a requirement. The passage concludes by noting that the impossibility of lying by the Lawgiver has been established in the discussion of kalam without circularity.

Key Terms
حسن
قبح
شرع
عقل
أمر
نهي
مدح
ذم
كذب
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view that good and evil are known only by scripture implies that there is no real difference between good and evil, or that God could arbitrarily call anything good or evil.
Correction: The passage clarifies that good and evil are not arbitrary labels; they are defined by divine command and praise, which are consistent with God's nature. The impossibility of lying on God's part is established rationally, so God's commands are not arbitrary.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari answer in this passage, what does the term 'good' (حسن) mean?
A) An act that has an intrinsic quality of goodness known by reason
B) An act that is the object of divine command and praise
C) An act that benefits the doer in this world
D) An act that is approved by human consensus

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari answer avoids circularity by defining good and evil as identical with scriptural command and praise, not as independent qualities requiring prior rational or scriptural validation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that good and evil are defined by scripture alone. Is there any area of your life where you still trust your own sense of right and wrong more than what Allah has commanded? What would it take to fully submit in that area?
?
The objection suggests that if good and evil are only by scripture, then we cannot know anything about the Lawgiver's truthfulness without circularity. Do you ever find yourself trying to judge Allah's commands by your own standards of fairness? How does this lesson challenge that tendency?
?
Imam al-Haramayn clarifies that 'goodness is the very occurrence of scripture praising the doer.' When you think about your actions, do you seek to align them with what scripture praises, or do you hope that scripture will somehow align with your preferences? Which attitude dominates your heart?

Hanafi Position on Rational Good and Evil
Al-Taftazani explains the Hanafi view on rational good and evil with its distinctions from Mu'tazilism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Hanafis affirm rational knowledge of good and evil while preserving divine sovereignty, distinguishing their view from Mu'tazilism.

Core Idea
Hanafis accept rational moral knowledge without binding God by obligation.

Al-Taftazani presents the sixth objection against pure Ash'arism and then explains how the Hanafis, a school within Ahl al-Sunna, were swayed by earlier objections to adopt a modified rationalism. While they agree with the Mu'tazila that reason can know good and evil, they insist that God remains the sole Creator and judge, and reason is merely an instrument. This lesson clarifies that nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the opening line which states the sixth objection about the redundancy of prophets. The passage then says its answer has already passed, so focus on the Hanafi response.
2
Compare Hanafi and Mu'tazili views
Notice that Hanafis agree with Mu'tazila that some moral judgments are rational, but the passage explicitly distinguishes them: they do not say God is obligated, and they affirm God creates acts. Find these phrases.
Look for the clause: 'ولم يقولوا بالوجوب أو الحرمة على الله تعالى'
3
Understand the instrumental role of reason
The passage states that reason is an instrument (آلة) for knowing good and evil, not a source of obligation. Reflect on how this preserves Ash'ari principles while allowing rational moral knowledge.
This is the key difference from full Mu'tazilism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: لو لم يكن وجوب النظر، وبالجملة أول الواجبات عقلياً لزم إقحام الأنبياء وقد مرّ بجوابه، ولقوة هاتين الشبهتين ذهب بعض أهل السنة وهم الحنفية إلى أن حسن بعض الأشياء وقبحها مما يدرك بالعقل كما هو رأي المعتزلة كوجوب أول الواجبات، ووجوب تصديق النبي وحرمة تكذيبه دفعاً للتسلسل، وكحرمة الإشراك بالله ، ونسبة ما هو في غاية الشناعة إليه على من هو عارف به وبصفاته، وكمالاته و وجوب ترك ذلك ولا نزاع في أن كل واجب حسن، وكل حرام قبيح إلا أنهم لم يقولوا بالوجوب أو الحرمة على الله تعالى، وجعلوا الحاكم بالحسن والقبح والخالق الله تعالى والعقل آلة المعرفة بعض ذلك من غير إيجاب ولا توليد لأفعال العباد هو، بل بإيجاد الله تعالى من غير كسب في البعض ومع الكسب بالنظر الصحيح في البعض.
The sixth: If the obligation of reflection, and in general the first of obligations, were not rational, it would necessitate the redundancy of prophets, and its answer has already passed. And due to the strength of these two equivocal arguments, some Ahl al-Sunna, namely the Hanafis, held that the goodness and ugliness of some things are perceived by reason, as is the view of the Mu‘tazila, like the obligation of the first of obligations, and the obligation to believe the prophet and the prohibition of calling him a liar to avoid infinite regress, and like the prohibition of associating partners with God, and attributing to Him what is most disgraceful, for one who knows Him, His attributes, and perfections, and the obligation to refrain from that. And there is no dispute that every obligatory act is good and every forbidden act is evil. But they did not affirm obligation or prohibition upon God Almighty, and they made the judge of good and evil and the Creator God Almighty, and reason an instrument of knowledge of some of that, without necessitation or generation of the acts of servants. Rather, by God's bringing into existence, without acquisition in some cases and with acquisition through correct reflection in others.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage explains two things: first, the sixth objection is briefly mentioned (the obligation to reflect rationally is needed to avoid rendering prophets superfluous) but its answer is said to have already been given, so the focus shifts to the Hanafi position. The Hanafis, a school within Ahl al-Sunna, were convinced by the strength of the fifth and sixth objections and adopted a modified rationalist view. They agree with the Mu‘tazila that some moral judgments (like the obligation to know God, believe prophets, and avoid shirk) are known by reason independently of revelation. However, they differ crucially: they do not say that God Himself is obligated or that creatures have innate moral obligations to God in a way that obligates God to reward/punish. Rather, they affirm that God is the creator of all acts, and reason is merely an instrument (آلة) for knowing some of what is good and evil, without implying that reason causes or generates the acts. The Hanafi position thus balances rational moral knowledge with Ash‘ari doctrines of divine sovereignty and the createdness of human acts, distinguishing between the knowability of good and evil and the source of moral obligation (only God imposes obligations).

Key Terms
وجوب النظر
أول الواجبات
حنفية
معتزلة
حسن
قبح
عقل
كسب
توليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hanafi view is identical to the Mu'tazili view on rational good and evil.
Correction: The Hanafis distinguish themselves by not affirming obligation or prohibition upon God, and by maintaining that God is the creator of acts and reason is only an instrument, not a cause.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the Hanafi position on rational good and evil from that of the Mu'tazila, according to this passage?
A) The Hanafis deny that reason can know any good or evil at all
B) The Hanafis affirm that God is the creator of acts and reason is only an instrument
C) The Hanafis hold that good and evil are entirely conventional
D) The Hanafis agree with the Mu'tazila on every point

Key Takeaway
The Hanafis affirm that reason can know some moral obligations and prohibitions, but they maintain that God alone creates acts and imposes obligation, making reason an instrument not an independent source of law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God must act a certain way because it seems 'fair' to you? How does this lesson's distinction—that reason knows some good and evil but does not obligate God—challenge that assumption? What will you do the next time you feel God 'owes' you something?
?
In your spiritual journey, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on revelation? The Hanafis say reason is an instrument, not an independent source. Can you identify an area where your intellect has led you away from what you know is true from Scripture? What step will you take to correct that imbalance today?
?
The lesson emphasizes that God alone creates all acts. When you reflect on your own actions, do you truly feel that God is the Creator of every deed, including your mistakes and successes? How does this belief affect your sense of responsibility and your trust in God's wisdom? What will you change in your daily gratitude and reliance?

Good and Ugly as Sharia's Praise and Blame
Defines good and ugly as Sharia's praise and blame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of good and ugly as Sharia's praise and blame, not as inherent qualities.

Core Idea
Good is Sharia's praise; ugly is Sharia's blame.

This passage introduces a foundational Ash'ari principle: moral qualities are not intrinsic to actions but are defined by divine revelation. Before we can discuss obligation or prohibition, we must understand what 'good' and 'ugly' mean in Islamic theology. The author clarifies that these terms refer to God's commendation or condemnation, not to any independent property.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noticing how the author first refutes a misconception then gives the positive definition.
The passage is dense but precise.
2
Understand the misconception
Identify the wrong view that the author rejects: that goodness is an additional quality separate from Sharia.
3
Grasp the definition of good and ugly
See how the author defines 'good' as the Sharia's praise of the doer, and 'ugly' as its blame.
4
Apply to obligation and prohibition
Notice that 'obligatory' means an act commanded by Sharia, not one with a special inherent property.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari theory of moral values.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالشرع تجوز حيث يوهم كون الحسن زائداً على الشرع موقوفاً إدراكه عليه وليس الأمر كذلك. بل الحسن عبارة عن نفس ورود الشرع بالثناء على فاعله وكذا في القبح، فإذا وصفنا فعلاً بالوجوب، فلسنا نقدر للفعل الواجب صفة بها يتميز عما ليس بواجب، وإنما المراد بالواجب الفعل الذي ورد الشرع بالأمر به إيجاباً، وكذا الحظر.
By the Sharia it is permissible where it suggests that goodness (al-husn) is additional to the Sharia, its perception dependent upon it, but this is not the case. Rather, goodness is simply the Sharia's coming with praise for its doer, and similarly for ugliness (al-qubh). So if we describe an act as obligatory (wajib), we do not posit for the obligatory act a quality by which it is distinguished from what is not obligatory; rather what is meant by 'obligatory' is the act which the Sharia has commanded as an obligation, and similarly for prohibition.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of good (husn) and ugly (qubh) from an Ash'ari perspective. The author begins by refuting the notion that goodness is an additional quality independent of Sharia. Instead, goodness is identified with the Sharia's praise of the doer, and ugliness with blame. The definition of obligation (wajib) follows: it is not an inherent quality but simply the act that the Sharia has commanded as an obligatory command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Goodness is simply the Sharia's praise of the doer; it has no separate existence apart from divine revelation.

Checkpoint

B) It is an act that the Sharia has commanded as an obligation.

Key Takeaway
Good and ugly are not intrinsic qualities but are defined by Sharia's praise and blame.

Objection: Certainty of Shirk's Ugliness
Objection and answer about shirk's ugliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the objection that shirk's ugliness is known by pure reason, and why they attribute this certainty to custom and divine law.

Core Idea
Certainty about shirk's ugliness comes from ingrained custom, not pure reason.

This lesson addresses a strong objection: that we are certain shirk is ugly without needing revelation. The Ash'ari response challenges the source of this certainty, showing it actually arises from the constant reinforcement of divine laws and societal customs. You'll see how the objection is answered and why this matters for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Notice the claim that we are 'certain' (qati'un) that shirk is ugly regardless of revelation.
The objection claims this certainty is from reason alone.
2
Understand the answer's key terms
Focus on the terms 'establishment of the laws' (istiqrar al-shara'i) and 'continuation of customs' (istimrar al-'adat). The answer uses these to explain the basis of that certainty.
These two elements make the ugliness 'ingrained' (markuz) in minds.
3
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: that Ash'aris deny any innate moral knowledge. This passage shows they attribute it to custom and law, not pure reason.
This clarifies the Ash'ari position on moral epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: إنا قاطعون بأنه يقبح عند الله تعالى من العارف بذاته، وصفاته أن يشرك به، وينسب إليه الزوجة والولد وما لا يليق به من صفات النقص وسمات الحدوث بمعنى أنه يستحق الذم والعقاب في حكم الله تعالى سواء ورد الشرع أو لم یرد. والجواب : أن مبنى القطع على استقرار الشرائع على ذلك، واستمرار العادات بمثله فى الشاهد فصار قبحه مركوزاً في العقول حيث يظن أنه بمجرد حكم العقل .
The fifth: We are certain that it is ugly (qabih) in the sight of God, for one who knows His essence and attributes, to associate partners with Him, and to ascribe to Him a spouse, child, and what is not fitting of attributes of deficiency and marks of origination, meaning that he deserves blame and punishment in God's judgment, whether the Sharia came or did not come. The answer: That the basis of this certainty is the establishment of the laws (shara'i') upon that, and the continuation of customs similar to it in the witnessed world, so that its ugliness became ingrained (markuz) in minds, where it is thought to be merely by the judgment of reason.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, which claims that we are certain that shirk is ugly independently of revelation. The Ash'ari response explains that this certainty arises from the constant reinforcement of divine laws and customary practices, not from pure reason. People mistakenly attribute this judgment to reason alone, but it is actually based on ingrained custom.

Correction: The passage shows that Ash'aris do not deny the certainty; they explain its origin as ingrained custom and law, not pure innate reason.

Checkpoint

Our certainty that shirk is ugly is not from pure reason but from ingrained custom and divine law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Hanafite Middle Position on Rational Obligation
Sixth objection and Hanafite compromise on rational obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafite compromise on rational obligation, how it differs from the Mu'tazilite view, and the role of reason as an instrument rather than a source of obligation.

Core Idea
Reason knows good and evil, but God alone judges and creates.

This lesson addresses a key objection against rational obligation and presents the Hanafite middle path. The objection warns that without rational obligation, prophets become redundant. The Hanafites, while agreeing with the Mu'tazila that some things are rationally knowable as good or bad, carefully distinguish themselves by affirming that God is the sole creator and judge, and that reason is merely an instrument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the objection and the subsequent Hanafite response.
The passage has two parts: the objection and the Hanafite position.
2
Identify the Hanafite position
Find where the Hanafites agree with the Mu'tazila (that some things are knowable by reason as good/bad) and note the specific examples given.
Examples include the obligation of belief in the prophet and prohibition of shirk.
3
Distinguish key differences
Look for what the Hanafites deny: they do not affirm obligation or prohibition upon God, and they do not hold that reason generates acts. God is the creator and judge.
The intellect is an instrument (ālat al-maʿrifa), not a source of obligation.
4
Reflect on the role of reason
Consider how this view balances rational knowledge with divine sovereignty. How does it avoid the pitfalls of pure rationalism and pure fideism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس: لو لم يكن وجوب النظر، وبالجملة أول الواجبات عقلياً لزم إقحام الأنبياء وقد مرّ بجوابه، ولقوة هاتين الشبهتين ذهب بعض أهل السنة وهم الحنفية إلى أن حسن بعض الأشياء وقبحها مما يدرك بالعقل كما هو رأي المعتزلة كوجوب أول الواجبات، ووجوب تصديق النبي وحرمة تكذيبه دفعاً للتسلسل، وكحرمة الإشراك بالله ، ونسبة ما هو في غاية الشناعة إليه على من هو عارف به وبصفاته، وكمالاته و وجوب ترك ذلك ولا نزاع في أن كل واجب حسن، وكل حرام قبيح إلا أنهم لم يقولوا بالوجوب أو الحرمة على الله تعالى، وجعلوا الحاكم بالحسن والقبح والخالق الله تعالى والعقل آلة المعرفة بعض ذلك من غير إيجاب ولا توليد لأفعال العباد هو ، بل بإيجاد الله تعالى من غير كسب في البعض ومع الكسب بالنظر الصحيح في البعض.
The sixth: If the obligation of reflection (wujub al-nazar), and in summary the first of obligations, were not rational ('aqli), it would necessitate the redundancy of prophets, and its answer has already passed. And due to the strength of these two misconceptions, some of the Ahl al-Sunna, namely the Hanafites, went to the view that the goodness (husn) and ugliness (qubh) of some things are perceived by reason, just as it is the view of the Mu'tazila, like the obligation of the first obligations, and the obligation to believe the prophet and the prohibition of belittling him to avoid infinite regress, and like the prohibition of associating partners with God, and attributing to Him what is extremely repugnant, for one who is knowledgeable of Him and His attributes and perfections, and the obligation to leave that. And there is no dispute that every obligation is good and every forbidden is ugly, except that they did not affirm obligation or prohibition upon God, and they made the judge of good and evil and the Creator God, and the intellect is an instrument of knowledge for some of that, without imposition or generation of the acts of servants. Rather, by the creation of God without acquisition in some, and with acquisition through correct reflection in some.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection and then outlines the Hanafite compromise. The objection states that if the first obligation (reflection) were not rationally obligatory, then prophets would be unnecessary. The answer is referenced as having been given earlier. The author then notes that due to the strength of these two objections, some Hanafites adopted a middle position: they affirm that some things are knowable by reason as good or bad, but they deny that God is obligated or that reason can generate acts. They uphold divine causation without imposing duties on God.

Key Terms

العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Hanafite position on rational obligation is identical to the Mu'tazilite position.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that Hanafites 'did not affirm obligation or prohibition upon God' and that 'the intellect is an instrument... without imposition or generation.' They differ from the Mu'tazila on divine obligation and causal generation.

Checkpoint

C) It makes actions obligatory.
D) It is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafite compromise affirms rational knowledge of good and evil while maintaining that God alone is the creator and judge, and reason is only an instrument.

The Fourth Inquiry: No Ugly Act from Allah – Agreement and Disagreement
Taftazani states the agreed-upon conclusion and contrasts Ash'ari and Mu'tazili justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis agree that Allah does no ugly act or omission, yet they ground this conclusion in fundamentally different principles.

Core Idea
Both sects agree on the conclusion but differ radically on the foundation: Shariah versus reason.

The Fourth Inquiry addresses the crucial question of whether any act of Allah could be considered ugly or obligatory. Taftazani begins by stating the agreed-upon conclusion—Allah does nothing ugly and neglects nothing obligatory—and then shows how the two major theological schools reach the same result from opposite starting points. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the Mu'tazili's internal difficulty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the common ground
Read the Arabic and translation to see the initial statement that there is no disagreement that Allah does no ugly act and leaves no obligatory act. Notice the 'la khilafa' phrase.
The common ground is crucial.
2
Contrast the two rationales
The passage gives 'amma 'indana' for Ash'aris and 'amma 'inda al-mu'tazila' for Mu'tazilis. Compare how each grounds the same conclusion.
Ash'aris deny the categories apply; Mu'tazilis affirm Allah does what is obligatory and avoids what is ugly.
3
Examine the Mu'tazili forced interpretation
The author notes that the Mu'tazila were forced to reinterpret 'obligatory' as 'definitely does' even if leaving is possible. Understand why this is problematic.
This shows the limits of rational obligation for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (المبحث الرابع) لا قبيح من الله تعالى لا قبيح من أفعاله بحكم الشرع . والمعتزلة لما قالوا بوجوب أشياء عليه، وثبوت قبائح بالعقل ذهبوا إلى أن يفعل البتة ما وجب ويترك ما قبح فوقع الايقان على أنه لا يفعل قبيحاً، ولا يترك واجباً، واضطروا في تفسير الواجب عليه تعالى ثم اضطروا إلى أن معناه أنه يفعله البتة، وإن جاز تركه وهو مع كونه رجماً بالغيب مجرد تسمية) .
لا خلاف في أن الباري لا يفعل قبيحاً، ولا يترك واجباً. أما عندنا فلأنه لا قبح منه ولا واجب عليه لكون ذلك بالشرع ، ولا يتصور في فعله . وأما عند المعتزلة فلأن كل ما هو قبيح منه فهو يتركه البتة، وما هو واجب عليه فهو يفعله البتة. وسيجيء ذكر ما أوجبوا عليه
He said: The Fourth Inquiry: No ugly act from Allah. No ugly act from His acts by the ruling of the Shariah. The Mu'tazila, since they asserted obligation of things upon Him and established ugly acts by reason, went to the position that He definitely does what is obligatory and leaves what is ugly, so they were certain that He does no ugly act and neglects no obligatory act. But they were forced in the interpretation of the obligatory upon Him, then they were forced to the meaning that He definitely does it, even if leaving it is possible, and it is, despite being conjecture of the unseen, a mere naming.
There is no disagreement that the Creator does no ugly act and neglects no obligatory act. As for us, because there is no ugly act from Him and no obligation upon Him, because that is by Shariah, and it is not conceivable in His act. As for the Mu'tazila, because every ugly act from Him He definitely leaves, and every obligatory act upon Him He definitely does. Their obligations will be mentioned later.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage opens the Fourth Inquiry and establishes the common ground and point of divergence between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis. The author first states the agreed-upon conclusion: Allah does not do ugly acts and does not omit obligatory acts. However, the reasoning differs. For Ash'aris, 'ugly' and 'obligatory' are defined by Shariah, and Allah is above such rulings; thus, the conclusion follows from the absence of such categories in His case. For Mu'tazilis, who affirm rational obligation and ugliness, they hold that Allah necessarily does what is obligatory and avoids what is ugly. The author notes that the Mu'tazila were forced to reinterpret 'obligatory' as meaning that He definitely does it even if it is possible for Him not to, which he dismisses as mere conjecture and labeling.

Key Terms
قبيح
واجب
شرع
عقل
المعتزلة
أهل السنة
رجماً بالغيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis is about whether Allah can do ugly acts.
Correction: Both sides actually agree that Allah does not do ugly acts. The real disagreement is on the basis: Ash'aris deny the categories apply to Allah, while Mu'tazilis affirm He actively avoids ugliness and does what is obligatory.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position, why does Allah not do ugly acts?
A) Because ugly acts are impossible for Him by nature
B) Because there is no ruling of ugliness from Shariah regarding His acts
C) Because He avoids them out of wisdom
D) Because He is not the doer of ugly acts

Key Takeaway
The agreement that Allah does no ugly act conceals a deep disagreement about the very criteria of ugliness and obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unfair or 'ugly' to you, do you instinctively judge Allah by your own standards of good and evil? How can you train your heart to trust that His wisdom surpasses your reasoning?
?
Have you ever held onto a belief about Allah out of pride or intellectual attachment, even when it required twisting meanings? What would it look like for you to humbly accept that your mind is limited and submit to revelation?
?
The Mu'tazila were forced into 'conjecture of the unseen' to maintain their rational system. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on speculation rather than the clear words of Allah and His Messenger? What concrete step can you take today to prioritize shariah over your own logic?
