Meaning of Wajib and Refutation of Mu'tazili Reinterpretation
Taftazani critiques Mu'tazili definitions of obligation upon Allah and refutes their reinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on divine obligation and how Taftazani refutes the Mu'tazili reinterpretations by appealing to Allah's absolute ownership, wisdom beyond human grasp, and free choice.

Core Idea
Divine obligation cannot be defined by human blame or necessity that negates choice.

In this passage, Taftazani clarifies that nothing is wajib upon Allah in the Shariah sense. He then dismantles the Mu'tazili attempts to define obligation differently, showing that their definitions either imply Allah deserves blame (contradicting His absolute ownership) or imply compulsion (negating His free choice). Finally, he exposes the weakness of later Mu'tazili redefinition and questions their inconsistency in not applying the same reasoning to all acts that are definitely done by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial objection
The opponent first states the Ash'ari position (no obligation in the Shariah sense) and then presents the Mu'tazili two definitions: obligation as deserving blame rationally, or as necessity (luzum) from wisdom.
This sets up the debate framework.
2
Examine the first refutation
Taftazani rejects the first definition because Allah, as absolute owner, does not deserve blame rationally for any act or omission.
Key concept: al-malik 'ala al-itlaq.
3
Examine the second refutation
For the second definition, Taftazani argues we cannot claim that any act of Allah would violate wisdom if left, because His wisdom and purposes may be beyond our understanding. Additionally, 'necessity' implies inability to leave, which negates free choice (ikhtiyar).
Note the double argument: epistemological (we don't know) and theological (necessity contradicts choice).
4
Consider the later Mu'tazili redefinition
Later Mu'tazila redefined wajib as 'He definitely does it, though leaving is possible' like natural regularities (e.g., Mount Uhud staying as it is). Taftazani notes this is a shift in meaning.
This is an attempt to avoid the problem of necessity negating choice.
5
Analyze Taftazani's final answer
Taftazani dismisses this redefinition as mere labeling and an unjustified assertion, unlike the necessary knowledge of custom which Allah creates in all rational people. He also points out their inconsistency in not calling all acts that the Lawgiver informs will happen 'wajib'.
The final answer exposes the arbitrariness of the Mu'tazili position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الذي ثبت من مذهبنا هو أنه لا واجب عليه بمعنى أن شيئاً من أفعاله ليس مما أمر الشارع به وحكم بأن (۱) فاعله يستحق المدح، وتاركه الذم عند الله تعالى والمعتزلة إنما يقولون بالوجوب بمعنى استحقاق تاركه الذم عند العقل، أو بمعنى اللزوم عليه كما في تركه من الإخلال بالحكمة .
قلنا : على الأول لا نسلّم أنه يستحق الذم عقلاً (۲) على فعل أو ترك ، فإنه المالك على الإطلاق. وعلى الثاني لا نسلم أن شيئاً من أفعاله يكون بحيث يحل تركه بحكمة لجواز أن يكون له في كل فعل أو ترك حكم ومصالح لا تهتدي إليها العقول، فإنه الحكيم الخبير على أنه لا معنى للزوم عليه (۳) إلا عدم التمكن من الترك، وهو ينافي الاختيار. ولو سلّم فلا يوافق مذهبهم أن صدور الفعل عنه على سبيل الصحة من غير أن ينتهي الوجوب . ولهذا اضطر المتأخرون منهم إلى أن معنى الوجوب على الله أنه يفعله البتة، ولا يتركه ، وإن كان الترك جائزاً كما في العاديات . فإنا نعلم قطعاً أن جبل (٤) أحد باق على حاله لم ينقلب ذهباً وإن كان جائزاً .
والجواب : أن الوجوب حينئذ مجرد تسمية والحكم بأن الله تعالى يفعل البتة ما سميتموه واجباً جهالة وادعاء من شرذمة بخلاف العاديات ، فإنها علوم ضرورية خلقها الله تعالى لكل عاقل، والعجب أنهم لا يسمون كل ما أخبر به الشارع من أفعاله واجباً عليه مع قيام الدليل على أنه يفعله البتة.
If it is said: What is established from our school is that there is no obligation upon Him in the sense that none of His acts is something the Lawgiver commanded and ruled that its doer deserves praise and its abandoner deserves blame from Allah Almighty. The Mu'tazila only say obligation in the sense of the abandoner deserving blame in reason, or in the sense of necessity (luzum) upon Him, as in abandoning it would be an infringement of wisdom. We reply: As for the first, we do not accept that He deserves blame rationally for an act or omission, for He is the absolute owner. As for the second, we do not accept that any of His acts is such that leaving it would violate wisdom, because it is possible that for every act or omission He has judgments and interests that minds cannot grasp, for He is the Wise, the All-Aware. Moreover, there is no meaning to 'necessity upon Him' except lack of ability to leave, which negates choice. Even if it were granted, it would not agree with their school that the act proceeds from Him in a sound manner without the obligation terminating. For this reason, the later ones among them were forced to the meaning that obligation upon Allah is that He definitely does it and does not leave it, even if leaving is possible as in the case of customary matters. For we know with certainty that Mount Uhud remains as it is, not turned into gold, even if that is possible. The answer: Then obligation is a mere naming, and the judgment that Allah definitely does what you have called obligatory is ignorance and an assertion by a clique, unlike customary matters, for they are necessary sciences that Allah creates for every rational person. It is strange that they do not call every act of His that the Lawgiver informed about 'obligatory' upon Him, despite the evidence that He definitely does it.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage delves into the precise meaning of 'obligation' (wujub) when applied to Allah. The opponent's objection first clarifies the Ash'ari position: nothing is wajib upon Allah in the sense of Shariah command and reward/punishment. The Mu'tazila, however, define wajib either as deserving blame in reason for leaving it, or as necessity (luzum) from the perspective of wisdom. The author refutes both: on the first, Allah is the absolute owner (al-malik 'ala al-itlaq) and does not deserve blame; on the second, we cannot claim that any act of His would violate wisdom if left, because His wisdom is beyond our grasp. Moreover, 'necessity' implies inability to leave, which contradicts choice (ikhtiyar). The author notes that later Mu'tazila were forced to reinterpret 'wajib' as merely 'He definitely does it, though leaving is possible in itself', analogous to natural regularities like Mount Uhud remaining unchanged. The author dismisses this as mere labeling and an unjustified assertion, unlike necessary knowledge of custom which is divinely created. He finds it inconsistent that they do not label all acts that the Lawgiver informs will definitely happen as 'wajib'.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الذم
اللزوم
الحكمة
الاختيار
العاديات
العلوم الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani's response, what does 'necessity' (luzum) upon Allah entail that contradicts His nature?
A) It implies He is forced to act, negating choice.
B) It implies He dislikes the act.
C) It implies He acts without wisdom.
D) It implies He is ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Any concept of obligation on Allah must preserve His absolute ownership and free choice; the Mu'tazili definitions fail by implying blame or compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah is the absolute owner of everything, do you ever find yourself feeling entitled to certain outcomes from Him? In what areas of your life do you need to surrender that sense of entitlement and acknowledge His absolute freedom?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted on rational accountability for Allah. Are there areas in your faith where you prioritize your own reasoning over accepting divine decrees? What specific situation in your life right now requires you to trust His hidden wisdom rather than demand an explanation?
?
The passage calls the Mu'tazili redefinition 'mere naming and ignorance.' When you reflect on your own beliefs, are there comfortable labels you use to avoid confronting the full reality of Allah's sovereignty? Will you commit today to seeking the truth from revelation even when it challenges your assumptions?

Refuting Obligation as Blameworthy by Reason
Sanusi presents and refutes the first meaning of divine obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that God is obligated (wajib) and the Ash'ari refutation of the first interpretation (obligation as deserving blame by reason).

Core Idea
God, as absolute Owner, cannot be subject to blame by human reason.

In this passage, Sanusi engages with the Mu'tazila doctrine that certain actions are obligatory for God. He first clarifies their two definitions of obligation and then refutes the first, which holds that reason can declare God blameworthy. This lesson unpacks that critical argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Mu'tazila definitions
Read the opening lines where the author states the Mu'tazila say obligation in two senses: blame by reason or necessity.
The first definition is what this lesson focuses on.
2
Focus on the first definition
The first definition: obligation means that the abandoner deserves blame according to reason.
3
Study the refutation
Sanusi refutes this by stating that God is the absolute Owner; therefore, no creature can hold Him accountable by reason.
This is a key Ash'ari principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس مما أمر الشارع به وحكم بأن فاعله يستحق المدح، وتاركه الذم عند الله تعالى والمعتزلة إنما يقولون بالوجوب بمعنى استحقاق تاركه الذم عند العقل، أو بمعنى اللزوم عليه كما في تركه من الإخلال بالحكمة.
قلنا: على الأول لا نسلّم أنه يستحق الذم عقلاً على فعل أو ترك، فإنه المالك على الإطلاق. وعلى الثاني لا نسلم أن شيئاً من أفعاله يكون بحيث يحل تركه بحكمة لجواز أن يكون له في كل فعل أو ترك حكم ومصالح لا تهتدي إليها العقول، فإنه الحكيم الخبير على أنه لا معنى للزوم عليه إلا عدم التمكن من الترك، وهو ينافي الاختيار. ولو سلّم فلا يوافق مذهبهم أن صدور الفعل عنه على سبيل الصحة من غير أن ينتهي الوجوب. ولهذا اضطر المتأخرون منهم إلى أن معنى الوجوب على الله أنه يفعله البتة، ولا يتركه، وإن كان الترك جائزاً كما في العاديات. فإنا نعلم قطعاً أن جبل أحد باق على حاله لم ينقلب ذهباً وإن كان جائزاً.
والجواب: أن الوجوب حينئذ مجرد تسمية والحكم بأن الله تعالى يفعل البتة ما سميتموه واجباً جهالة وادعاء من شرذمة بخلاف العاديات، فإنها علوم ضرورية خلقها الله تعالى لكل عاقل، والعجب أنهم لا يسمون كل ما أخبر به الشارع من أفعاله واجباً عليه مع قيام الدليل على أنه يفعله البتة.
It is not among what the Lawgiver commanded and ruled that its doer deserves praise and its abandoner blame in the sight of God. The Mu'tazila only say obligation in the sense of the abandoner deserving blame according to the intellect, or in the sense of necessity upon Him like His leaving [something] would be a violation of wisdom. We say: As for the first, we do not concede that He deserves blame intellectually for an act or omission, for He is the absolute Owner. As for the second, we do not concede that any of His actions is such that leaving it would be permitted by wisdom, because it is possible that He has in every act or omission judgments and interests that minds cannot attain, for He is the Wise, the Aware. Moreover, there is no meaning to 'necessity upon Him' except the inability to leave, which contradicts free choice. Even if conceded, it does not agree with their doctrine that the act proceeds from Him in a valid manner without the obligation reaching an end. For this reason, the later ones among them were forced to [say] that the meaning of obligation upon God is that He will certainly do it and not leave it, even if leaving is possible, as in customary events. For we know with certainty that Mount Uhud remains in its state, having not turned into gold, even if that is possible. The answer: That obligation is then mere naming, and the judgment that God certainly does what you have named obligatory is ignorance and a claim from a small group, unlike customary events, for they are necessary sciences that God creates in every rational person. And it is surprising that they do not name every action of His that the Lawgiver informed of as obligatory upon Him, despite the evidence that He certainly does it.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God is obligated (wajib) to do certain actions or avoid others. The author first explains that the Mu'tazila understand obligation in two ways: either the agent (here, God) deserves blame for not doing something according to reason, or it is necessary (luzum) upon Him to do it because leaving it would violate wisdom. The author refutes both: for the first, God is the absolute Owner, so no one can intellectually blame Him; for the second, God's actions are not bound by human conceptions of wisdom because He may have hidden interests and judgments in every act or omission. The author also argues that necessity would negate free choice, which contradicts the Ash'ari understanding of God's will. He notes that later Mu'tazila redefined obligation as God's certain performance of an act, even though leaving it is possible in itself, analogous to customary events like Mount Uhud remaining a mountain. The author dismisses this as mere labeling and ignorance, contrasting it with the necessary knowledge (ʿulūm ḍarūriyya) of customary patterns that God creates in all rational people. The passage ends with a criticism that the Mu'tazila are inconsistent: they do not call every divinely informed action obligatory, even when evidence shows God certainly does it.

Key Terms
وجوب
استحقاق الذم
المالك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari rejection of obligation on God implies moral nihilism.
Correction: The author affirms that God's wisdom and justice are not subject to human criteria.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the first interpretation of obligation fail?
A) Because God is the absolute Owner, so He cannot be blamed intellectually.
B) Because God never acts against wisdom.
C) Because the Mu'tazila rely on revelation.
D) Because obligation is only from the Lawgiver.

Key Takeaway
The first Mu'tazila definition of obligation (blame by reason) is refuted by the absolute ownership of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself complaining about God's decree, as if He owes you an explanation? How does the truth that He is the absolute Owner silence that complaint? Write one concrete situation where you will apply this truth starting today.
?
When you face a tragedy, do you instinctively feel that God should not have allowed it? What would it take for you to fully accept His ownership and stop judging His actions by your own sense of fairness? Commit to one prayer where you surrender that judgment.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that reason alone can hold God accountable. Is there any area of your life where you are trusting your own reasoning more than God's command? Will you re-read this passage before making your next major decision?

Refuting Necessity and the Later Redefinition
Sanusi refutes the second meaning of obligation and critiques the later Mu'tazila redefinition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of the second Mu'tazila definition (necessity) and their later redefinition, and how the author dismisses it as mere labeling.

Core Idea
Necessity upon God would negate free choice, and redefining it as certain performance is unfounded.

Having refuted the first meaning of obligation, Sanusi now turns to the second: that God is under necessity (luzum) to act wisely. He shows that this contradicts divine free choice and then addresses the later Mu'tazila attempt to reinterpret obligation as certain performance, which he critiques as mere naming without evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the second meaning
The author argues that necessity upon God would imply inability to leave, which negates free choice.
Also, God's wisdom may be beyond human comprehension.
2
Examine the later Mu'tazila redefinition
Later Mu'tazila redefined obligation as certain performance (like customary events such as Mount Uhud remaining a mountain).
3
Understand the author's response
The author dismisses this as mere naming and ignorance, contrasting it with necessary knowledge of habitual patterns.
He also criticizes their inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس مما أمر الشارع به وحكم بأن فاعله يستحق المدح، وتاركه الذم عند الله تعالى والمعتزلة إنما يقولون بالوجوب بمعنى استحقاق تاركه الذم عند العقل، أو بمعنى اللزوم عليه كما في تركه من الإخلال بالحكمة.
قلنا: على الأول لا نسلّم أنه يستحق الذم عقلاً على فعل أو ترك، فإنه المالك على الإطلاق. وعلى الثاني لا نسلم أن شيئاً من أفعاله يكون بحيث يحل تركه بحكمة لجواز أن يكون له في كل فعل أو ترك حكم ومصالح لا تهتدي إليها العقول، فإنه الحكيم الخبير على أنه لا معنى للزوم عليه إلا عدم التمكن من الترك، وهو ينافي الاختيار. ولو سلّم فلا يوافق مذهبهم أن صدور الفعل عنه على سبيل الصحة من غير أن ينتهي الوجوب. ولهذا اضطر المتأخرون منهم إلى أن معنى الوجوب على الله أنه يفعله البتة، ولا يتركه، وإن كان الترك جائزاً كما في العاديات. فإنا نعلم قطعاً أن جبل أحد باق على حاله لم ينقلب ذهباً وإن كان جائزاً.

It is not among what the Lawgiver commanded and ruled that its doer deserves praise and its abandoner blame in the sight of God. The Mu'tazila only say obligation in the sense of the abandoner deserving blame according to the intellect, or in the sense of necessity upon Him like His leaving [something] would be a violation of wisdom. We say: As for the first, we do not concede that He deserves blame intellectually for an act or omission, for He is the absolute Owner. As for the second, we do not concede that any of His actions is such that leaving it would be permitted by wisdom, because it is possible that He has in every act or omission judgments and interests that minds cannot attain, for He is the Wise, the Aware. Moreover, there is no meaning to 'necessity upon Him' except the inability to leave, which contradicts free choice. Even if conceded, it does not agree with their doctrine that the act proceeds from Him in a valid manner without the obligation reaching an end. For this reason, the later ones among them were forced to [say] that the meaning of obligation upon God is that He will certainly do it and not leave it, even if leaving is possible, as in customary events. And it is surprising that they do not name every action of His that the Lawgiver informed of as obligatory upon Him, despite the evidence that He certainly does it.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God is obligated (wajib) to do certain actions or avoid others. Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a trial befalls you that defies your sense of wisdom, do you still submit to God's decree with the same trust, or do you find yourself demanding explanations as if God owes them to you?
?
In what areas of your life do you still secretly presume that God must act according to your understanding of what is 'wise'? Are you ready to surrender that presumption to His unsearchable wisdom?
?
If you were to truly internalize that God's choices are not constrained by human logic, would you still object to His commandments that seem arbitrary? What would change in your obedience today?

The Fifth Research: Burdening Beyond Capacity
Introduction to the fifth research with the Mu'tazila objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that God may command what is beyond human capacity, and how this differs from the Mu'tazila objection based on foolishness and futility.

Core Idea
God's commands are not limited by human capacity or purpose.

The author begins the fifth research by asserting two key doctrines: it is not impossible for God to impose a duty that exceeds a person's ability, nor are His actions motivated by purposes. The Mu'tazila object, arguing that such commands would be foolish and pointless. This lesson introduces the debate and the author's initial rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the key terms 'safah' and 'abath'.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage states the Ash'ari position (permissibility) and contrasts it with the Mu'tazila position (impossibility). Mark both in your mind.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila evidence
The Mu'tazila argue that burdening beyond capacity is foolish (safah) and an action without purpose is futile (abath). These are unbecoming of the Wise (al-Ḥakīm).
This is their main evidence ('umdatuhum).
4
Note the author's claim
The author says 'you have known the weakness of both arguments.' This implies that the reader has already learned why these objections fail, likely from earlier discussions on good and evil.
This connects to the broader Ash'ari framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المبحث الخامس: لا يمتنع تكليف ما لا يطاق، ولا تعلل أفعاله بالأغراض خلافاً للمعتزلة وعمدتهم أن تكليف ما لا يطاق سفه، والفعل الخالي عن الغرض عبث، فلا يليق بالحكيم، وقد عرفت ضعفهما.
جعل أصحابنا جواز تكليف ما لا يطاق وعدم تعليل أفعال الله تعالى بالأغراض من فروع مسألة الحسن والقبح، وبطلان القول بأنه يقبح منه شيء، ويجب عليه فعل أو ترك، لأن المخالفين إنما عولوا في ذلك على أن تكليف ما لا يطاق سفه والفعل الخالي عن الغرض فيما شأنه ذلك عبث وكلاهما قبيح، لا يليق بالحكمة، فيجب عليه تركه. والمعتزلة منهم من ادعى العلم الضروري بقبح تكليف ما لا يطاق، حتى زعم بعض جهلتهم أن غير العقلاء كالصبيان والمعاينة يستقبح ذلك، بل البهائم أيضاً بلسان الحال حيث يحاربون بالقرون والأذناب وكثير من الأعضاء عند عدم الطاقة، وأنت خبير بأن هذا منافرة للطبع وألم ومشقة وتضرر لا قبح بالمعنى المتنازع، ومنهم من أثبته بقياس الغائب على الشاهد، فإن العقلاء حتى الذاهلين عن النواهي الشرعية، بل المنكرين للشرائع يستقبحون تكليف الموالي عبيدهم ما لا يطيقونه، ويذمونهم على ذلك، معللين بالعجز وعدم الطاقة.
والجواب: أن ذلك من جهة قطع المستقبحين بأن أفعال العباد معللة بالأغراض وأن مثل ذلك مناف لغرض العامة، ومصلحة العالم، ولا كذلك تكليف علام الغيوب إما لتنزه أفعاله من الغرض وإما لقصده حكماً ومصالح لا تهتدي إليها العقول.
The fifth research: It is not impossible to burden beyond capacity, nor are His actions motivated by purposes, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Their main evidence is that burdening beyond capacity is foolishness, and an action devoid of purpose is futility, so it is not befitting the Wise One, and you have known the weakness of both [arguments]. Our companions made the permissibility of burdening beyond capacity and the lack of motivation of God's actions by purposes among the branches of the issue of good and evil, and the invalidity of the statement that anything is evil from Him, or that an act or omission is obligatory upon Him, because the opponents relied in that on the fact that burdening beyond capacity is foolishness and the action devoid of purpose in that which is of such nature is futility, both of which are evil, not befitting wisdom, so He must abandon it. Among the Mu'tazila, some claimed necessary knowledge of the evil of burdening beyond capacity, to the extent that some of their ignorant even claimed that non-rational beings like children and the insane consider that evil, even beasts by the tongue of the state, where they fight with horns and tails and many organs when lacking power. And you know that this is natural aversion, pain, difficulty, and harm, not evil in the disputed sense. And among them are those who established it by analogy from the witnessed to the unseen, for rational people, even those heedless of religious prohibitions, indeed deniers of religions, consider evil the burdening of masters upon their slaves what they cannot bear, and blame them for that, citing incapacity and lack of power. The answer: That is because the deemers-of-evil are certain that the actions of servants are motivated by purposes and that such is contrary to the general purpose and the interest of the world. Not so the burdening of the Knower of the Unseen; either because His actions are free from purpose, or because He intends judgments and interests that minds cannot attain.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth research (al-mabḥath al-khāmis) of the section, which asserts two points: it is not impossible for God to command what is beyond a person's capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq), and God's actions are not motivated by purposes (aghrad). The author states that the Mu'tazila oppose these, arguing that burdening beyond capacity is foolish (safah) and an action without purpose is futile (abath), both unbecoming of the Wise. The author then explains that the Ash'ari position (our companions) makes these issues branches of the foundational question of good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ), and the rejection of the idea that anything is intrinsically evil from God or obligatory upon Him. The opponents rely on the claim that such actions are evil. The author then details two Mu'tazila arguments: some claim necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) of the evil of such burdening, even citing non-rational beings and beasts as evidence; the author responds that this is merely natural aversion, pain, and harm, not the moral evil under dispute. Others argue by analogy from human masters to God, since humans blame masters who overburden slaves. The author answers that humans base this on the certainty that human actions have purposes and harming subordinates destroys social order, but God is not similarly constrained—either His actions are free from any purpose, or He has hidden wisdoms beyond human grasp.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
سفه
عبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is inherently evil for God to command the impossible.
Correction: The Ash'ari school holds that God's actions are not subject to human moral judgments; He may command what appears impossible to us, and His actions have no external purpose.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence of the Mu'tazila against burdening beyond capacity?
A) It causes harm to humans.
B) It is foolish and futile, unbecoming of a wise being.
C) It contradicts divine mercy.
D) It leads to social disorder.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position affirms that God's actions are not constrained by human concepts of purpose or capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that feels beyond your capacity, do you trust that He knows your limits better than you do, or do you resent it? How does this lesson change your response to difficult obligations?
?
If God's actions are not motivated by purposes as we understand them, what does that say about your attempts to find personal benefit in every divine decree? Are you willing to submit to His will even when you see no purpose?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that burdening beyond capacity is foolish. But the Ash'aris assert it's possible. How does this truth impact your view of the trials you have faced? Do you believe that Allah may test you beyond what you think you can bear, and that it is still just? What will you do the next time you feel overwhelmed by His command?

The Mu'tazila Arguments and the Ash'ari Response
Detailed presentation of Mu'tazila arguments and Ash'ari responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments of the Mu'tazila (necessary knowledge and analogy) and the Ash'ari response that dismisses both based on the nature of divine actions.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila rely on human analogies; Ash'aris reply that God is beyond such comparisons.

After stating the two positions, the author details the Mu'tazila's attempts to prove that burdening beyond capacity and purposeless actions are evil. They claim either that it is known by necessity or by analogy from human masters. The Ash'ari response cuts to the core: human judgments are based on assumptions about purposes that do not apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
This part contains the detailed arguments and the response. Read it carefully, noting the structure.
The passage first links the issue to the broader question of good and evil.
2
Identify the two Mu'tazila arguments
The Mu'tazila use two arguments: (1) necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī) that such actions are evil, citing even non-rational beings; (2) analogy (qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid) from human masters burdening slaves.
These are distinct strategies; notice how the author responds to each.
3
Understand the Ash'ari critique
The author dismisses the first as mere natural aversion, not moral evil. The second fails because human analogies assume that actions have purposes that are recognizable, but God's actions are not constrained by such purposes.
The key point is that the deemers-of-evil assume a purpose-driven world, which does not apply to God.
4
Appreciate the two possible explanations
The Ash'ari response offers two possibilities: either God's actions are entirely free from purpose, or they have judgments and interests that human minds cannot reach.
Both options remove the basis for the Mu'tazila objection.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why does the analogy from human masters fail?
A) Because human masters are not wise.
B) Because God's actions are not motivated by purposes or have hidden purposes beyond human comprehension.
C) Because slaves have rights that God does not recognize.
D) Because the analogy is not logically valid.

God's actions transcend human analogies; they are either purposeless in the human sense or guided by wisdom beyond our comprehension.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that something difficult or seemingly unfair in your life must be pointless or unjust because you cannot see the reason? How does this lesson challenge that assumption about God's actions?
?
The Mu'tazila analogized God to a human master. In what ways do you unconsciously compare God to human authorities, expecting Him to act as they would? What would it cost you to truly let go of that analogy?
?
If God's wisdom can be hidden from even the most intelligent minds, are you willing to trust Him in a test that makes no sense to you right now? What would it take for you to say, 'I submit, even though I do not understand'?

Two Ash'ari Positions on Divine Action and the Mu'tazila Objection
Introduces two issues and Mu'tazila arguments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two foundational Ash'ari positions on divine tasking and purpose, and the main arguments the Mu'tazila use to oppose them.

Core Idea
Ash'aris affirm Allah can task beyond capacity and acts without purpose; Mu'tazila deem these immoral.

This passage opens a central debate between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila regarding the nature of divine commands and actions. The Ash'ari position is that Allah may command what cannot be done and acts without extrinsic purposes. The Mu'tazila object, arguing that such actions would be foolish or vain, and thus unbefitting of the Wise God. In this lesson, we will examine both sides and the Mu'tazila's specific proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author immediately states the two Ash'ari positions as a starting point.
2
Identify the two Ash'ari positions
The first is that it is not impermissible for Allah to task His servants with what they cannot bear. The second is that His actions are not caused by purposes. Note how these are stated as negations of claims that they are impossible or inappropriate.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's core objection
The Mu'tazila argue that tasking with the unbearable is foolish (سفه) and an act without purpose is vain (عبث). Both are evil and thus unbefitting a wise agent. This is the moral argument against the Ash'ari view.
The author remarks that the weakness of these arguments is already known, hinting at a refutation.
4
Connect to al-husn wa al-qubh
The author explains that these two issues are branches of the broader question of rational good and evil. The Ash'aris deny that God is bound by human moral categories; the Mu'tazila affirm them.
This shows that the debate is not about isolated points but a fundamental disagreement on moral epistemology.
5
Examine the two Mu'tazila proofs
First, some claim necessary knowledge (العلم الضروري) that such tasking is evil, even perceived by children and animals. Second, they use analogy from human masters (قياس الغائب على الشاهد) to argue that rational people universally condemn imposing impossible tasks on slaves.
The author dismisses the first as mere natural aversion and pain, not moral evil in the disputed sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال المبحث الخامس) (لا) يمتنع تكليف ما لا يطاق، ولا تعلل أفعاله بالأغراض خلافاً للمعتزلة وعمدتهم أن تكليف ما لا يطاق سفه، والفعل الخالي عن الغرض عبث، فلا يليق بالحكيم، وقد عرفت ضعفهما) .جعل أصحابنا جواز تكليف ما لا يطاق وعدم تعليل أفعال الله تعالى بالأغراض من فروع مسألة الحسن والقبح ، وبطلان القول بأنه يقبح منه شيء، ويجب عليه فعل أو ترك، لأن المخالفين إنما عولوا في ذلك على أن تكليف ما لا يطاق سفه والفعل الخالي عن الغرض فيما شأنه ذلك عبث وكلاهما قبيح، لا يليق بالحكمة، فيجب عليه تركه والمعتزلة منهم من ادعى العلم الضروري بقبح تكليف ما لا يطاق، حتى زعم بعض جهلتهم أن غير العقلاء كالصبيان والمعاينة، يستقبح ذلك، بل البهائم أيضاً بلسان الحال حيث يحاربون بالقرون والأذناب وكثير من الأعضاء عند عدم الطاقة، وأنت خبير بأن هذا منافرة للطبع وألم ومشقة وتضرر لا قبح بالمعنى المتنازع، ومنهم من أثبته بقياس الغائب على الشاهد، فإن العقلاء حتى الذاهلين عن النواهي الشرعية، بل المنكرين للشرائع يستقبحون تكليف الموالي عبيدهم ما لا يطيقونه، ويذمونهم على ذلك، معللين بالعجز وعدم الطاقة.
He said, the fifth chapter: It is not impermissible to task with what cannot be borne, and His acts are not caused by purposes, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Their main argument is that tasking with what cannot be borne is foolish, and an act devoid of purpose is vain, so it does not befit the Wise. And you have known the weakness of both. Our companions made the permissibility of tasking with what cannot be borne and the non-causation of Allah's acts by purposes branches of the issue of good and evil, and the invalidity of the statement that something evil can come from Him or that an act or omission is obligatory upon Him, because the opponents relied in that on that tasking with what cannot be borne is foolish and an act devoid of purpose in that which is such is vain, and both are evil, not befitting wisdom, so He must avoid it. The Mu'tazila: some of them claimed necessary knowledge of the evil of tasking with what cannot be borne, to the extent that some of their ignorant ones claimed that non-rational beings like children and animals deem that evil, even beasts by the tongue of the situation fight with horns and tails and many limbs when unable, and you are aware that this is repugnance to nature, pain, hardship, and harm, not evil in the disputed sense. And some of them established it by analogy of the unseen on the witnessed, for rational people, even those heedless of religious prohibitions, nay deniers of religions, deem evil the tasking of masters of their slaves with what they cannot bear, and blame them for that, giving as reason incapacity and lack of power.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth chapter of the work by stating two positions of the Ash'ari school: (1) it is not impermissible for Allah to task His servants with that which they cannot bear (تكليف ما لا يطاق), and (2) Allah's actions are not motivated by purposes or causes (لا تعلل أفعاله بالأغراض). The author immediately notes that the Mu'tazila oppose both, basing themselves on the claim that such tasking would be foolish (سفه) and purposeless action vain (عبث), both of which are unbecoming of the Wise (الحكيم). The author then remarks that the weakness of these arguments has already been shown. Next, the author explains that Ash'ari scholars subsume these two issues under the broader question of al-husn wa al-qubh (the rational determination of good and evil). They reject the notion that something evil can proceed from Allah or that an act is obligatory upon Him. The opponents, i.e., the Mu'tazila, rely on the premises that tasking with the unbearable is foolish and purposelessness is vain, both evil, necessitating avoidance by a wise agent. The passage then details two Mu'tazila proofs: First, some claim intuitive necessary knowledge (العلم الضروري) of the evil of such tasking, asserting even children and animals recognize its repulsiveness, as animals fight with their limbs when overpowered. The author dismisses this as mere natural aversion (منافرة للطبع) and pain, not moral evil in the disputed sense. Second, others use an argument from analogy (قياس الغائب على الشاهد): rational people universally condemn masters who impose impossible tasks on slaves, citing incapacity. The passage thus sets up the Mu'tazila's case before the Ash'ari refutation that follows.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
تعلل الأفعال بالأغراض
الحسن والقبح
المعتزلة
العلم الضروري
قياس الغائب على الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'aris deny any purpose in creation, implying that Allah acts whimsically.
Correction: Ash'aris affirm that Allah acts with wisdom, but they reject that He is compelled by an extrinsic purpose or that His actions can be judged by human standards of good and evil.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the Mu'tazila against the permissibility of tasking with what cannot be borne?
A) It is impossible by nature.
B) It is foolish and unbecoming of the Wise.
C) It contradicts divine mercy.
D) It leads to contradiction in divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on divine tasking and purpose is not based on irrationality but on a rejection of anthropomorphic standards for God, while the Mu'tazila rely on human moral intuitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that feels impossible or beyond your capacity, do you secretly question His wisdom? How does this lesson challenge that inner thought, and what will you do the next time it arises?
?
Are you more like the Mu'tazila, demanding that God's ways make sense to your human reason, or like the Ash'aris, submitting to His absolute will even when it seems 'unreasonable'? Take a moment to repent for any judgment you have passed on Allah's decrees.
?
If you truly believed that Allah never tasks a soul beyond its capacity (Quran 2:286), why do you still resist His commands as if they are too hard? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to His authority today?

The Ash'ari Response and an Objection about Punishment
Ash'ari answer to Mu'tazila and an objection about punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila claim that God's command of the impossible is evil due to purposelessness, and how they address an objection regarding punishment.

Core Idea
Divine actions are not bound by human purposes; hidden wisdoms may exist.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the Ash'ari defense against the Mu'tazila claim that tasking with the impossible is evil because it seems purposeless. The Ash'aris argue that God's actions are either free from purpose or contain wisdoms beyond human grasp. An objection then raises the issue of punishment for non-compliance, and the Ash'aris reaffirm that hidden secrets may still be present. This lesson unpacks their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the structure: answer, then objection and response.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila claim being answered
The passage begins with 'The answer' implying a prior claim. The Mu'tazila assert that actions must have purposes and that purposeless command is evil. Grasp this context.
3
Examine the Ash'ari answer
The Ash'aris give two possibilities: either God's actions are free from purpose, or they have hidden wisdoms. Note how this refutes the Mu'tazila analogy from human actions.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection specifies 'tasking of realization and punishment' to exclude other types like challenge. The Ash'ari response maintains the possibility of hidden secrets and affirms entitlement to punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن ذلك من جهة قطع المستقبحين بأن أفعال العباد معللة بالأغراض وأن مثل ذلك مناف لغرض العامة، ومصلحة العالم ، ولا كذلك تكليف علام الغيوب إما لتنزه أفعاله من الغرض وإما لقصده حكماً ومصالح لا تهتدي إليها العقول .فإن قيل : كلامنا في تكليف التحقيق والمعاقبة على الترك لا في التكليف لأسرار أخر كما في التحدي .قلنا: نحن أيضاً إنما نعتبر احتمال أسرار أخر في ذلك التكليف، وفي تثبيت استحقاق العقاب.
The answer: That is from the perspective of those who deem evil, cutting [the matter] that the actions of servants are caused by purposes, and that such [tasking] is contrary to the common interest and the welfare of the world. But it is not so for the tasking of the Knower of the Unseen, either because His actions are free from purpose, or because He intends wisdoms and interests that minds cannot perceive. If it is said: our discussion is about the tasking of realization and punishment upon abandonment, not about tasking for other secrets as in the challenge (tahaddi). We say: we too only consider the possibility of other secrets in that tasking, and in establishing the entitlement to punishment.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila arguments and then an objection and answer. The Ash'ari answer: The Mu'tazila and others who deem such tasking evil have mistakenly assumed that all actions must be for some purpose (غرض) and that an apparently purposeless action is contrary to the general good and world order. However, this reasoning applies to human actions only. When it comes to the tasking of the All-Knowing (علام الغيوب), either His actions are entirely free from purpose (تنزه عن الغرض) or He intends wisdoms and benefits (حكم ومصالح) that human minds cannot grasp. Thus, the analogy fails. Next, an objection is raised: The discussion is specifically about the tasking that entails actual obligation (تكليف التحقيق) and punishment for non-compliance, not about tasking for other hidden reasons such as the challenge (التحدي) where Allah asks for something impossible to show human incapacity. The Ash'ari response: They consider the possibility that even in such tasking there may be other hidden secrets, and also that the entitlement to punishment (استحقاق العقاب) can still be affirmed despite hidden wisdom. Thus, the objection does not refute their position.

Key Terms
الجواب
المستقبحون
غرض
حكم ومصالح
علام الغيوب
تكليف التحقيق
التحدي
استحقاق العقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash'aris deny any purpose in God's actions, making Him arbitrary.
Correction: The Ash'aris actually affirm that God's actions may have hidden wisdoms or be entirely free from purpose as we understand it. They do not deny divine wisdom; they deny that it must be transparent to humans.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'aris respond to the Mu'tazila claim that tasking with the impossible is evil because it is purposeless?
A) They agree it is evil but argue Allah is above morality.
B) They say divine actions are not necessarily for purposes, or have hidden wisdoms.
C) They claim the tasking is not really impossible.
D) They say the Mu'tazila have no proof.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari defense shows that God's actions are not bound by human notions of purpose; hidden wisdoms or pure transcendence mean that what seems evil to us may not be.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or a command from Allah that seems pointless or harmful to you, do you truly trust that He has a hidden wisdom, or do you judge His action by your own limited understanding? What would it take for you to surrender to His hidden purpose today?
?
If you were to fully internalize that God's actions are not bound by human purposes, how would that change the way you react to unanswered prayers or unexplained suffering in your life? Will you take a moment to confess a specific situation where you have doubted His wisdom?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that commanding the impossible is evil because it serves no purpose. Are there times when you expect God to explain His commands or justify His decrees before you obey? Will you commit now to obeying even when you cannot see the reason?

Defining the Subject of Dispute in Taklif
Sanusi defines the subject of dispute by excluding inherently impossible and divinely-decided impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise scope of the disputed impossibility: it concerns what is possible in itself but never occurs, not what is inherently impossible or impossible by divine decree.

Core Idea
The disputed impossibility is about things possible in themselves but never realized, not logical contradictions or divine decrees.

Sanusi begins by specifying exactly what is being debated in the topic of tasking with the impossible. He distinguishes three types of impossibility, eliminating two from the dispute. This precision is crucial for understanding the subsequent arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi defines the subject of dispute by negation.
The passage uses 'لا' to exclude two types.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three types of impossibility: (1) possible in itself but never occurs, (2) inherently impossible, (3) impossible due to prior decree. Determine which is the subject of dispute.
3
Reflect on the exclusion
Why does Sanusi exclude the inherently impossible and the divinely-decided impossible? What is the rationale for each exclusion?
The majority hold the first exclusion; the second is by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم المتنازع) ( في الأول ما أمكن في نفسه ولم يقع متعلقاً لقدرة العبد أصلاً كخلق الأجسام أو عادة كالصعود إلى السماء لا ما امتنع لذاته كجمع النقيضين. فإن الجمهور على امتناع التكليف به بناء على أنه يستدعي تصور المكلف به واقعاً، والمستحيل لا يتصور إلا على سبيل التشبيه والنفي ولا ما امتنع لسابق علم أو إخبار من الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه، فإن التكليف به واقع وفاقاً . ثم النزاع في الجواز، وإلا فالوقوع منفي بحكم النص والاستقراء، وفي التكليف بمعنى طلب تحقيق الفعل والإتيان به واستحقاق العذاب على الترك، وإلا فعل قصد التعجيز واقع وفاقاً، وبهذا يظهر أن تمسك المانعين بمثل ( لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها ) ليس على المتنازع، وكذا تمسك المجوزين بمثل فأتوا بسورة ) وبأن فعل العبد ليس بقدرته وبأن التكليف قبل الفعل والقدرة معه وبأن من علم الله أنه لا يؤمن مكلف بالإيمان مع استحالته منه لاستحالة الجهل على الله تعالى. وفي كلام كثير من المحققين التكليف بالممتنع لذاته كجمع النقيضين جائز بل واقع . لأن مثل أبي لهب مكلف بأن يصدق في جميع ما جاء به، ومن جملته أنه لا يصدقه أصلاً، فقد كلف بأن يصدقه فى أنه لا يصدقه، وهو جمع للنقيضين . والجواب بأن المكلف به ليس إلا تحصيل الإيمان وهو ممكن في نفسه ممتنع لسابق العلم والإخبار، أو بأنه إنما كلف التصديق بما عدا هذا الإخبار ضعيف.
He said: Then the subject of dispute in the first [issue] is what is possible in itself but never falls within the slave's power at all, such as creating bodies, or by custom, such as ascending to heaven; not what is impossible in itself like combining two opposites. For the majority hold that tasking with it is impossible on the basis that it requires conceiving of the tasked thing as real, and the impossible cannot be conceived except by way of analogy and negation. Nor what is impossible due to prior knowledge or divine information that it will not occur, for tasking with it occurs by agreement. Then the dispute is about permissibility; otherwise, occurrence is negated by text and induction. And in tasking meaning the demand to actualize the act and perform it and entitlement to punishment for abandonment; otherwise, an act intending incapacitation occurs by agreement. By this it becomes clear that the adherence of the preventers to [the verse] 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' is not on the subject of dispute, and likewise the adherence of the permittees to [the verse] 'Then bring a chapter' and that the servant's act is not within his power, and that the tasking is before the act and power with it, and that he whom Allah knows will not believe is tasked with faith despite its impossibility from him due to the impossibility of ignorance on Allah. And in the speech of many investigators, tasking with what is impossible in itself like combining two opposites is permissible, nay, it occurs. Because someone like Abu Lahab is tasked with believing in all that he brought, among which is that he does not believe him at all, so he is tasked with believing him in that he does not believe him, and that is combining two opposites. And the answer that the tasked thing is only the acquisition of faith, which is possible in itself but impossible due to prior knowledge and information, or that he was only tasked with believing in what is other than this information, is weak.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage defines precisely what is meant by 'impossible' in the debate. It distinguishes three types: (1) What is possible in itself but never occurs due to lack of power (e.g., creating bodies) or by custom (e.g., ascending to heaven without means). (2) What is impossible in itself (ممتنع لذاته) like combining two opposites. The majority hold that tasking with such is impossible because it requires conceiving the thing as real, but the impossible cannot be directly conceived. (3) What is impossible due to prior divine knowledge or announcement that it will not occur; this type is actually allowed to be tasked by consensus. The real dispute is about permissibility (الجواز), not actual occurrence (الوقوع). Furthermore, the text clarifies that the dispute is about tasking in the sense of demanding actual performance and punishment for non-compliance, not about tasking meant to incapacitate (as in the challenge) which is agreed to occur. The passage then points out that arguments from both sides (preventers citing 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها' and permittees citing 'فأتوا بسورة' or other points) often miss the precise subject of dispute. Finally, it discusses a strong position by many investigators (المحققين) that tasking with an inherent impossibility like combining two opposites is indeed permissible and actually occurs, citing the case of Abu Lahab. He was commanded to believe in the Prophet's message including the verse that he would not believe, thus he was tasked with believing that he would not believe, which is a contradiction. The attempted solutions — either that he was only commanded to believe in what is possible in itself but prevented by prior decree, or that he was only commanded to believe in other parts — are considered weak.

Key Terms
متنازع
ممكن في نفسه
ممتنع لذاته
سابق علم
إخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of impossibility is the actual subject of dispute?
A) What is impossible in itself like combining opposites.
B) What is possible in itself but never occurs due to lack of power or custom.
C) What is impossible due to prior divine knowledge.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
The core dispute concerns tasking with things that are possible but never happen, not logical contradictions or predestined events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that seems beyond your capacity, do you truly believe He has considered your limits, or do you secretly assume He expects the impossible? How does this lesson challenge your assumption?
?
If you know that something—like a particular sin you keep committing—is forbidden but also within your power, why do you still choose to do it? Are you hiding behind the excuse that 'it's too hard' when the truth is you have not truly resolved to leave it?
?
God's prior knowledge of what will not happen does not remove your responsibility. Is there an area of obedience where you have convinced yourself 'it won't happen anyway' and therefore you neglect the effort? How will you change your action today?

The Real Point of Contention: Permissibility and the Meaning of Taklif
Sanusi clarifies the dispute is about permissibility and the meaning of taklif, critiquing evidence from both sides.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute is about permissibility (not actual occurrence) and about tasking as a demand for actual performance (not incapacitation), and how both sides misapply scriptural proofs.

Core Idea
The debate is about whether such tasking is permissible, not whether it actually occurs, and proofs on both sides often miss the mark.

Having defined the subject, Sanusi now clarifies the exact nature of the disagreement. He stresses that the dispute is about permissibility (الجواز), not actual occurrence (الوقوع). He also distinguishes between two meanings of taklif: demanding actual performance versus intending to incapacitate. This distinction helps him show that many scriptural arguments on both sides are irrelevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the nature of the dispute
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the distinctions Sanusi makes.
2
Note the two key distinctions
Identify where Sanusi distinguishes permissibility from occurrence, and demand from incapacitation.
The phrase 'ثم النزاع في الجواز' and 'وفي التكليف بمعنى طلب تحقيق الفعل' are key.
3
Examine the misapplied proofs
Look at the examples from both preventers and permittees. Understand why Sanusi says they miss the subject of dispute.
Preventers cite 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها'; permittees cite 'فأتوا بسورة' and other arguments.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this clarification shapes the rest of the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What does Sanusi say the dispute is about?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from a difficult act of obedience by saying 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity,' without checking whether the difficulty is truly beyond your ability or just beyond your willingness? How does Sanusi's distinction between general capacity and specific impossibility challenge that excuse?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's commands are only about permissibility—not about whether you think you can do them—how would your response to a challenging Islamic obligation change starting today?
?
When you find yourself arguing with Allah about a command that seems impossible for you, are you really disputing its permissibility or just your own desire to avoid it? What step can you take this week to obey a command you usually avoid?

The Case of Abu Lahab and Tasking with Inherent Impossibility
Presents the investigators' argument for tasking with inherent impossibility using Abu Lahab, and critiques the counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of some investigators that tasking with inherent impossibility (like combining opposites) is actually permissible and occurs, using Abu Lahab as an example, and why the counterarguments are weak.

Core Idea
Many scholars hold that tasking with logical contradictions is possible, citing Abu Lahab's command to believe in the very message that predicted his disbelief.

Sanusi ends the discussion by presenting a strong position from many investigators: tasking with what is impossible in itself, like combining opposites, is not only permissible but actually occurs. He gives the example of Abu Lahab, who was commanded to believe in all of the Prophet's message, which includes the verse that he will not believe. This creates a contradiction. Sanusi then dismisses two attempted solutions as weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the investigators' view
Read the Arabic and translation from 'وفي كلام كثير من المحققين' to the end.
2
Understand the Abu Lahab example
Analyze why this is considered an instance of tasking with combining opposites. Abu Lahab is asked to believe he does not believe.
This is a classic example of a logical contradiction.
3
Evaluate the weak answers
Sanusi mentions two weak counterarguments. Identify them and reflect on why they are considered weak.
The first answer says the task is only to acquire faith in general; the second says he was only tasked with believing other parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It distinguishes three types: (1) What is possible in itself but never occurs due to lack of power (e.g., creating bodies) or by custom (e.g., ascending to heaven without means). (2) What is impossible in itself (ممتنع لذاته) like combining two opposites. The majority hold that tasking with such is impossible because it requires conceiving the thing as real, but the impossible cannot be directly conceived. (3) What is impossible due to prior divine knowledge or announcement that it will not occur; this type is actually allowed to be tasked by consensus. The real dispute is about permissibility (الجواز), not actual occurrence (الوقوع). Furthermore, the text clarifies that the dispute is about tasking in the sense of demanding actual performance and punishment for non-compliance, not about tasking meant to incapacitate (as in the challenge) which is agreed to occur. The passage then points out that arguments from both sides (preventers citing 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها' and permittees citing 'فأتوا بسورة' or other points) often miss the precise subject of dispute. Finally, it discusses a strong position by many investigators (المحققين) that tasking with an inherent impossibility like combining two opposites is indeed permissible and actually occurs, citing the case of Abu Lahab. He was commanded to believe in the Prophet's message including the verse that he would not believe, thus he was tasked with believing that he would not believe, which is a contradiction. The attempted solutions — either that he was only commanded to believe in what is possible in itself but prevented by prior decree, or that he was only commanded to believe in other parts — are considered weak.

Key Terms
محققين
تكليف بالممتنع لذاته
جمع النقيضين
أبو لهب
تحصيل الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Abu Lahab example is not a valid instance of tasking with an inherent impossibility because it seems forced or interpretable.
Correction: The passage presents it as a real occurrence and a clear case of combining opposites, as the commanded belief includes a self-negation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Abu Lahab's case considered an example of tasking with inherent impossibility?
A) He was asked to perform a physically impossible task.
B) He was asked to believe a contradiction: that he does not believe.
C) He was asked to do something that only Allah can do.
D) He was asked to believe despite his prior knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The example of Abu Lahab shows that some scholars consider tasking with a logical contradiction to be permissible and actual, challenging the majority view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Abu Lahab was commanded to believe the very prophecy that announced his disbelief, do you feel the weight of divine justice? How does this example force you to confront the paradox between God’s foreknowledge and your own free will? Will you let this tension drive you to deeper submission, or will you retreat into comfortable explanations that dull the edge of the Qur’an’s command?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'I commanded you to believe in all that My Prophet brought — did you accept it all, or did you selectively believe what suited you?' What would you say? Does the example of Abu Lahab expose a tendency in your own heart to pick and choose which parts of revelation to take seriously? Let this reflection humble you and drive you to embrace the whole of the message, even when it challenges your intellect.
?
The investigators cited Abu Lahab as evidence that God can task with inherent contradictions. Others called this weak. When you encounter a theological puzzle that seems to defy logic, do you trust that Allah’s wisdom surpasses human reasoning, or do you feel compelled to resolve it before you can submit? What does your answer say about the state of your iman and your reliance on Allah’s absolute sovereignty?

The Three Ranks of the Unbearable (Ma La Yutaq)
Sanusi classifies the three ranks of taklif ma la yutaq and states their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ranks of what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) and the debate over the permissibility of obligating each, especially the inherently impossible.

Core Idea
Not all impossibilities are equal; the ranks depend on the source of impossibility.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies a key dispute in kalam: Is it permissible for God to obligate what is impossible? He first notes that some scholars even allow obligating the inherently impossible, while others consider any obligation of what God knows will not occur as 'unbearable'. Then he provides a three-rank classification that distinguishes between impossibility due to God's knowledge, inherent impossibility, and impossibility due to lack of power. This classification helps resolve the debate by specifying which kinds of impossibility are actually disputed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: the author first mentions differing views, then states the three ranks, then elaborates each.
Notice the phrase 'فنقول مراتب ما لا يطاق ثلاثة' which introduces the classification.
2
Identify the three ranks
List the three ranks as described: (1) the lowest — due to God's knowledge, will, or information; (2) the highest — inherently impossible; (3) the middle — possible in itself but not within the servant's power. For each, note what the author says about its permissibility or dispute.
The lowest is agreed upon; the highest has hesitation; the middle is the actual point of dispute.
3
Focus on the highest rank
The author discusses whether the inherently impossible can even be conceived, which affects whether it can be obligated. This is a deep point: if we cannot conceive it, how can we be commanded to do it? Reflect on the two ways of conceiving the impossible: by analogy or by negation.
The reference to Ibn Sina's Shifa indicates a philosophical background.
4
Understand the middle rank and the challenge
The middle rank is the one truly disputed. The author distinguishes between demanding actual performance (which is disputed) and the challenge (tahaddi) which is clearly intended to show incapacity and is not a real obligation.
The example of the Quranic challenge illustrates that such obligations are undeniably unbearable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشير إلى تحرير محل النزاع على ما هو رأي المحققين من أصحابنا، فإنه حكى عن بعضهم تجويز تكليف المحال حتى الممتنع لذاته، كجعل القديم محدثاً وبالعكس، وعن بعضهم أن تكليف ما علم الله تعالى عدم وقوعه أو أراد ذلك أو أخبر به كلها تكليف ما لا يطاق . فنقول مراتب ما لا يطاق ثلاثة أدناها ما يمتنع بعلم الله تعالى بعدم وقوعه، أو لإرادته ذلك أو لإخباره بذلك ، ولا نزاع في وقوع التأليف به فضلاً عن الجواز فإن من مات على كفره، ومن أخبر الله تعالى بعدم إيمانه يعد عاصياً إجماعاً وأقصاها ما يمتنع لذاته كقلب الحقائق ، وجمع الضدين أو النقيضين. وفي جواز التكليف به تردد بناء على أنه يستدعي تصور المكلف به ،واقعاً والممتنع هل يتصور واقعاً، ؟ فيه تردد . فقيل : لو لم يتصور لم يصح الحكم بامتناع تصوره وقبل تصوره. إنما يكون على سبيل التشبيه بأن يعقل بين السواد والحلاوة أمر هو الاجتماع . ثم يقال مثل هذا الأمر لا يمكن بين السواد والبياض أو على سبيل النفي بأن يحكم العقل بأنه لا يمكن أن يوجد مفهوم هو اجتماع السواد والبياض كذا في الشفاء وله زيادة تحقيق وتفصيل أوردناها في شرح الأصول. والمرتبة الوسطى ما أمكن في نفسه لكن لم يقع متعلقاً لقدرة العبد أصلاً كخلق الجسم أو عادة كالصعود إلى السماء، وهذا هو الذي وقع النزاع في جواز التكليف به بمعنى طلب تحقيق الفعل والإتيان به واستحقاق العقاب على تركه لا إلى قصد التعجيز وإظهار عدم الاقتدار على الفعل كما في التحدي بمعارضة القرآن، فإنه لا خفاء في وجوب كونه مما لا يطاق .
He points to defining the point of dispute according to the view of the verified scholars among our fellows, for he related from some of them the permissibility of obligating the impossible, even the inherently impossible, like making the eternal originated and vice versa, and from some of them that obligating what Allah knows will not occur, or wills that, or informs of that, all of it is obligation of what cannot be borne. We say: the ranks of what cannot be borne are three: the lowest is what is impossible by Allah's knowledge of its non-occurrence, or by His will of that, or by His informing of that; and there is no dispute about the actual occurrence of such obligation, let alone its permissibility, for whoever dies in his disbelief and whom Allah has informed will not believe is considered a sinner by consensus. The highest is what is inherently impossible, like reversing realities, and combining two opposites or two contradictories. Regarding the permissibility of obligation with it, there is hesitation based on whether it requires actually conceiving the obligated thing, and whether the impossible can be actually conceived? There is hesitation about that. It is said: if it were not conceivable, the judgment of the impossibility of conceiving it would not be valid, and before conceiving it, it can only be by way of analogy, that between blackness and sweetness something is understood which is combination, then it is said such a thing is not possible between blackness and whiteness; or by way of negation, that the mind judges that it is impossible for a concept of combination of blackness and whiteness to exist. Thus in the Shifa, and he has additional verification and detail which we have mentioned in Sharh al-Usul. The middle rank is what is possible in itself but does not occur as related to the servant's power at all, like creating a body, or by custom like ascending to heaven; and this is what the dispute occurred concerning the permissibility of obligating it, meaning demanding actual performance of the act and coming with it and deserving punishment for abandoning it, not with the intention of incapacitating and showing inability to perform the act, as in the challenge of producing something like the Quran; for there is no hiding that it must be something unbearable.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage classifies the three ranks of 'what cannot be borne' (ma la yutaq) in the debate on obligating the impossible. The author first notes the views of some scholars who permit obligating even the inherently impossible, and others who consider obligating what God knows will not occur as 'taklif ma la yutaq'. He then states that the ranks are three: (1) the lowest rank: what is impossible only due to God's knowledge, will, or information—there is consensus that such obligation actually occurs (e.g., the disbeliever is held accountable) and is permissible. (2) the highest rank: what is inherently impossible, like combining opposites—there is hesitation about its conceivability and thus its obligation. The author discusses whether the impossible can be conceived, referencing discussions in Ibn Sina's Shifa. (3) the middle rank: what is possible in itself but not within the servant's power, either inherently (creating a body) or customarily (ascending to heaven). This is the actual point of dispute: is it permissible to obligate such acts in the sense of demanding performance and punishing failure, as opposed to the challenge (tahaddi) which is meant to show incapacity? The author notes that such challenge obligations are unquestionably 'ma la yutaq'.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
ممتنع لذاته
ممتنع بالغير
مراتب
تصور الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of impossibility are the same; obligating any impossible thing is equally impossible.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes three ranks: impossibility due to God's knowledge/will, inherent impossibility, and impossibility due to lack of power. Each has a different ruling regarding the permissibility of taklif.

Checkpoint
What is the middle rank of 'ma la yutaq' according to the author?
A) What is impossible by God's knowledge of its non-occurrence
B) What is inherently impossible like combining opposites
C) What is possible in itself but not within the servant's power
D) What is impossible due to God's will

Key Takeaway
The three ranks of the unbearable help resolve the debate on taklif ma la yutaq by distinguishing between different sources of impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that some things are impossible by God's knowledge but He still commands them. Is there a sin you keep committing that you excuse by saying 'I'll never change' or 'It's impossible for me'? How does the fact that God's knowledge of your failure does not remove your responsibility challenge that excuse? What will you do today to act against that impossibility?
?
The middle rank involves duties that are within your nature but beyond your power alone. Think of a religious duty you have neglected because you felt it was beyond you—like consistent night prayer, perfecting your character, or giving up a beloved sin. Does this lesson convince you that such duties might still be required, and that God may hold you accountable for not trying? What concrete step can you take to move from 'I can't' to 'I will try with Allah's help'?
?
The highest impossibility deals with things like combining opposites—things we cannot even fully conceive. Have you ever justified a doubt about a matter of faith (like an attribute of Allah) by saying 'I can't conceive it, so it can't be true'? How does the lesson's discussion on conceiving the impossible (by analogy or negation) guide you to trust in revelation even when your mind cannot fully grasp? What one belief will you affirm today despite not fully understanding it?

Why Inanimate Objects Cannot Be Obligated
Sanusi answers the objection that obligating an inanimate object is not more unlikely than the middle rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why obligating an inanimate object is impossible in Islamic theology, and the role of understanding (fahm) as a condition for obligation.

Core Idea
Obligation requires understanding; an inanimate object cannot understand.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a clever objection: if God could give life to a stone, why can't He obligate it? The answer reveals a key principle in Islamic theology — the condition for any obligation (taklif) is the capacity to understand. Without that, obligation is meaningless. Sanusi then clarifies that the debate is only about logical possibility, not actual occurrence in God's law.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objector argues that obligating an inanimate object is not more improbable than the middle rank.
The objector assumes God could create life in a stone.
2
Identify the key condition
Focus on Sanusi's reply: 'شرط التكليف الفهم' (the condition of obligation is understanding). Understand why the inanimate object lacks this condition.
Understanding (fahm) is a prerequisite for any moral address.
3
Distinguish permissibility from occurrence
Sanusi points out that the dispute is only about whether it is logically permissible, not about whether it actually happens. Actual obligation is negated by induction and the Quran.
The Quranic verse confirms that Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : تكليف الجماد ليس بأبعد من هذا الجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه الحياة والعلم والقدرة . فكيف لم يقع النزاع في امتناعه حتى للقائلين بجواز تكليف الممتنع لذاته . قلنا : لأن شرط التكليف الفهم، ولا فهم للجماد حين هو جماد، ثم الجمهور على أن النزاع إنما هو في الجواز وأما الوقوع فمنفي بحكم الاستقراء ، وبشهادة مثل قوله تعالى لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها.
For if it is said: Obligating an inanimate object is not more remote than this, for it is possible that Allah creates in it life, knowledge, and power. So why was there no dispute about its impossibility even by those who permit obligating the inherently impossible? We say: Because the condition of obligation is understanding, and there is no understanding for the inanimate object while it is inanimate. Then the majority hold that the dispute is only about permissibility; as for occurrence, it is negated by inductive reasoning and by testimony like His saying, 'Allah does not obligate a soul except what it can bear.'
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the obligation of inanimate objects. The objector argues: if the middle rank (e.g., creating a body) is considered possibly obligatory, then obligating a lifeless object is not more far-fetched, since God could create in it life, knowledge, and power—so why is there no dispute about its impossibility, even among those who permit obligating the inherently impossible? The author's reply: the condition for obligation (taklif) is understanding (fahm), and an inanimate object, while it is inanimate, has no understanding. Furthermore, the majority (jumhur) agree that the dispute is only about permissibility (jawaz), not about actual occurrence (wuqu'), which is negated by induction and by the Quranic verse 'Allah does not obligate a soul except what it can bear.'

Key Terms
تكليف الجماد
شرط التكليف
الفهم
الجمهور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that obligating inanimate objects is impossible because it is inherently impossible (like obligating the impossible).
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the impossibility is due to lack of understanding, not inherent impossibility. The objector's point about God creating life is acknowledged, but the absence of understanding remains.

Checkpoint
What is the reason given in the text for the impossibility of obligating an inanimate object?
A) It is inherently impossible
B) God did not create it with understanding
C) It lacks the condition of understanding
D) The Quran explicitly forbids it

Key Takeaway
Obligating an inanimate object is impossible because it lacks understanding, which is a necessary condition for any obligation, and actual occurrence is further negated by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If ignorance nullifies obligation in the case of inanimate objects, what about your own willful ignorance of important Islamic teachings? What is one area of your faith you have avoided learning about, and will you commit to studying it with a sincere intention?
