Refuting the Prohibiters' Verse Argument
Sanusi refutes the prohibiters' use of the verse about burden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Quranic verse 'Allah does not obligate a soul beyond its capacity' negates actual occurrence but does not negate permissibility.

Core Idea
A verse about obligation does not decide what is possible.

Sanusi begins by showing that many arguments from both sides miss the actual point of dispute. He focuses on an argument used by those who prohibit the possibility of certain acts: the verse about Allah not burdening a soul beyond its capacity. This paves the way for a deeper discussion on the distinction between occurrence and permissibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to see how Sanusi introduces his refutation.
Notice the key phrase 'innama yanfi al-wuqu' la al-jawaz'.
2
Understand the distinction
Sanusi says the verse only negates occurrence, not permissibility. This distinction is key.
3
Reflect on the broader context
Think about how this argument might apply to other disputes in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبما ذكرنا يظهر أن كثيراً من التمسكات المذكورة في كلام الفريقين لم ترد على المتنازع أما للمانعين فمثل قوله تعالى لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها فإنه إنما ينفي الوقوع لا الجواز. فإن قيل : ما علم الله أو أخبر بعدم وقوعه يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، هو جهله أو كذبه تعالى عن ذلك وكل مايلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فهو محال ضرورة امتناع وجود الملزوم بدون اللازم فجوابه منع الكبرى، وإنما يصدق لو كان لزوم المحال لذاته . أما لو كان لعارض كالعلم أو الخبر فيما نحن فيه فلا ، الجواز أن يكون هو ممكناً في نفسه، ومنشأ لزوم المحال هو ذلك العارض، ولعل لهذه النكتة في بعض كتبنا تقريراً آخر
And by what we have mentioned it becomes clear that many of the arguments mentioned in the speech of the two groups do not apply to the point of dispute. As for the prohibiters, like His saying, 'Allah does not obligate a soul except what it can bear'—for it only negates occurrence, not permissibility. For if it is said: What Allah knows or informs will not occur—its supposition of occurrence necessitates an impossibility, namely His ignorance or lying, exalted is He above that; and whatever necessitates an impossibility from supposing its occurrence is itself impossible, by the necessity of the impossibility of the existence of the antecedent without the consequent. The answer is: denial of the major premise; it would only be true if the necessitation of impossibility were essential. But if it is due to an accident, like knowledge or news in our case, then it is not; it is possible that it is possible in itself, and the source of the necessitation of impossibility is that accident. Perhaps for this point in some of our books there is another formulation.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage refutes the arguments of those who prohibit (al-mani'un) and then addresses an objection. The author first notes that many arguments from both sides miss the real point of dispute. The prohibiters use the Quranic verse 'Allah does not obligate a soul except what it can bear'—but this only negates actual occurrence, not permissibility. Then an objection is raised: if God knows or informs that something will not occur, then supposing it occurs would entail an impossibility (God's ignorance or lying), and whatever entails an impossibility is itself impossible. The author's answer: he denies the major premise that 'whatever entails impossibility is impossible' in all cases; it holds only when the entailment is essential (li dhatihi). Here, the impossibility is due to an accident ('arad), namely God's knowledge or news. Therefore, the thing itself can be possible in itself, and the impossibility arises only from that accident. The author hints that he has another formulation of this point elsewhere.

Key Terms
الموانعون
المجوزون
الوقوع
الجواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, the verse 'Allah does not obligate a soul beyond its capacity' negates what?
A) Permissibility
B) Occurrence
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A text that negates actual occurrence does not necessarily negate permissibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a Quranic verse to support a position without carefully checking whether the verse actually addresses that specific claim? How can you guard against that in the future?
?
This lesson draws a sharp line between what Allah commands and what is simply possible. Is there an area of your life where you confuse the two—perhaps assuming that because something is permissible, it must be commanded, or vice versa? What would change if you applied this distinction?
?
When you read a verse like 'Allah does not obligate a soul beyond its capacity,' do you naturally assume it is about human limitations? Sanusi shows it is about obligation, not inherent possibility. How does this affect your understanding of Allah's justice and what He expects from you?

Answering the Knowledge Objection
Sanusi answers the knowledge objection with the essential/accidental distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential and accidental entailment of impossibility, and how it answers the objection that God's knowledge of non-occurrence makes an act impossible.

Core Idea
Impossibility from an accident does not make the thing itself impossible.

Sanusi now addresses a more sophisticated objection: if God knows or informs that something will not occur, then supposing it occurs entails an impossibility (God's ignorance or lying). The objector argues that this makes the act itself impossible. Sanusi's response hinges on a crucial distinction between essential and accidental entailment, a logical principle central to kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Pay attention to the argument: if God knows X won't happen, then assuming X happens leads to a contradiction.
Identify the major premise: 'anything that entails impossibility is itself impossible'.
2
Understand the answer
Sanusi denies the major premise and distinguishes essential vs. accidental necessity.
The key is 'li dhatihi' vs. 'li 'arad'.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider why the impossibility in this case is accidental (due to God's knowledge) and why the thing itself remains possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And by what we have mentioned it becomes clear that many of the arguments mentioned in the speech of the two groups do not apply to the point of dispute. As for the prohibiters, like His saying, 'Allah does not obligate a soul except what it can bear'—for it only negates occurrence, not permissibility. For if it is said: What Allah knows or informs will not occur—its supposition of occurrence necessitates an impossibility, namely His ignorance or lying, exalted is He above that; and whatever necessitates an impossibility from supposing its occurrence is itself impossible, by the necessity of the impossibility of the existence of the antecedent without the consequent. The answer is: denial of the major premise; it would only be true if the necessitation of impossibility were essential. Commentary
This passage refutes the arguments of those who prohibit (al-mani'un) and then addresses an objection. The author first notes that many arguments from both sides miss the real point of dispute. The prohibiters use the Quranic verse 'Allah does not obligate a soul except what it can bear'—but this only negates actual occurrence, not permissibility. Then an objection is raised: if God knows or informs that something will not occur, then supposing it occurs would entail an impossibility (God's ignorance or lying), and whatever entails an impossibility is itself impossible. The author's answer: he denies the major premise that 'whatever entails impossibility is impossible' in all cases; it holds only when the entailment is essential (li dhatihi). Here, the impossibility is due to an accident ('arad), namely God's knowledge or news. Therefore, the thing itself can be possible in itself, and the impossibility arises only from that accident. The author hints that he has another formulation of this point elsewhere.

Key Terms
الملزوم
اللازم
العارض
ذاتي
الكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any logical impossibility from an assumption makes the assumed thing impossible.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that it must be essential entailment; accidental factors do not negate intrinsic possibility.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi deny in his response to the knowledge objection?
A) The minor premise
B) The major premise
C) The conclusion
D) The fact that God knows

Key Takeaway
The impossibility resulting from an accidental factor like God's knowledge does not negate the intrinsic possibility of an act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible, do you assume it's impossible because of God's foreknowledge, or do you still strive, knowing that the impossibility may be external to the act itself? How does Sanusi's distinction free you from fatalism?
?
You often say 'If it's written, it will happen.' But what if the 'writing' (divine knowledge) is an accident that doesn't affect the intrinsic possibility of your choice? How does this change your sense of responsibility for your actions?
?
Can you think of an action you once believed was impossible because you thought God had decreed it so? How would Sanusi's argument challenge that belief and open up a new understanding of your own agency?

Arguments of the Permitters for Obligating the Seemingly Unbearable
Sanusi enumerates four arguments of the permiters for taklif ma la yutaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four arguments (wujuh) presented by the permiters (al-mujawwizun) for the permissibility of obligating what appears unbearable, and Sanusi's critical observation regarding the second and third arguments.

Core Idea
The permiters argue from Quran, power, timing, and divine foreknowledge.

The permiters (al-mujawwizun) advance four distinct arguments to show that taklif ma la yutaq is not only possible but actual. Their first argument cites Quranic verses as cases of incapacitation; the second argues from divine power; the third from the timing of power; and the fourth from the obligation of the disbeliever. As you read, pay attention to how Sanusi notes that the second and third arguments, if taken literally, would lead to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the four arguments and the critical note.
Notice the repeated phrase 'ومنها' marking each new argument.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument uses Quranic verses (2:31 and 2:23) as examples of 'taklif ta'jiz' (obligation of incapacitation) rather than 'taklif tahqiq' (obligation of actualization).
This argument suggests that the obligation is meant to demonstrate inability, not to demand actual performance.
3
Understand the second and third arguments
The second argument states that acts are created by Allah's power, so they are not within the servant's power—yet the obligation is related to the servant's power. The third argument distinguishes between 'qudra' (soundness of causes) and 'istita'a' (ability with the act).
These arguments are connected; note how Sanusi critiques them together.
4
Examine Sanusi's critique
Sanusi observes that if the second and third arguments were taken at face value, they would imply that all obligations are 'taklif ma la yutaq', which is false. This shows a flaw in those arguments.
This critique is important—not all permitter arguments are equally valid.
5
Reflect on the fourth argument
The fourth argument cites the consensus case of a disbeliever known to die in disbelief being obliged to believe, even though belief is impossible from him due to God's foreknowledge.
This argument uses divine foreknowledge to show that obligation can exist despite apparent impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما للمجوزين فوجوه : منها مثل قوله تعالى أنبؤني بأسماء هؤلاء وقوله تعالى فأتوا بسورة من مثله وذلك لأنه تكليف تعجيز، لا تكليف تحقيق ومنها أن فعل العبد بخلق الله تعالى وقدرته ، فلا يكون بقدرة العبد وهي معنى ما لا يطاق وذلك لأن معنى ما لا يطاق أن لا يكون متعلقاً بقدرة العبد، وما وقع التكليف به متعلق بقدرته ، وإن كان واقعاً بقدرة الله تعالى ومنها أن التكليف قبل الفعل والقدرة معه فلا يكون التكليف إلا بغير المقدور، وذلك لأن القدرة المعتبرة في التكليف هي سلامة الأسباب والآلات لا الاستطاعة التي لا تكون إلا مع الفعل ولو . هذان الوجهان لكان جميع التكاليف تكليف ما لا يطاق، وليس كذلك. ومنها من علم الله تعالى منه أنه لا يؤمن ، بل يموت على الكفر مكلف بالإيمان وفاقاً، مع استحالته منه لأنه لو آمن لزم انقلاب علم الله تعالى جهلاً.
And as for the permiters, they have several arguments: Among them is like His saying, 'Tell Me the names of these' and His saying, 'Then bring a surah like it'—that is because it is an obligation of incapacitation, not an obligation of actualization. And among them is that the act of the servant is by Allah's creation and power, so it is not by the servant's power, and this is the meaning of 'what cannot be borne'—because the meaning of 'what cannot be borne' is that it is not related to the servant's power; yet what obligation has fallen on is related to his power, even if it occurs by Allah's power. And among them is that the obligation comes before the act, and power is with the act, so obligation is only with what is not within one's power—this is because the power considered in obligation is soundness of causes and instruments, not the capability that only exists with the act. If these two points were as stated, all obligations would be obligation of what cannot be borne, but it is not so. And among them is that one whom Allah knows will not believe, but will die in disbelief, is obligated with faith by consensus, despite its impossibility from him because if he believed it would necessitate turning Allah's knowledge into ignorance.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage enumerates four arguments of the permiters (al-mujawwizun) for the permissibility of obligating what might seem unbearable (taklif ma la yutaq). First argument: Quranic verses like 'Tell Me the names of these' and 'Bring a surah like it' are obligations of incapacitation (taklif ta'jiz), not actualization (taklif tahqiq). Second argument: The servant's act is created by Allah's power, so it is not within the servant's power—yet 'what cannot be borne' means not related to the servant's power. However, what is obligated is related to the servant's power, even if it occurs by Allah's power. Third argument: Obligation precedes the act, and power (qudra) accompanies the act, so obligation would only be with what is not within one's power; but the qudra considered in obligation is the soundness of causes and instruments, not the ability (istita'a) that only exists with the act. The author notes that if these two points (second and third) were as stated, all obligations would be 'taklif ma la yutaq', but that is not the case, implying a flaw in those arguments. Fourth argument: The one whom Allah knows will not believe is still obligated to believe by consensus, even though belief is impossible from him because if he believed it would entail God's knowledge turning into ignorance. This is a case where obligation is actual despite the apparent impossibility.

Key Terms
المجوزون
تكليف تعجيز
تكليف تحقيق
القدرة
الاستطاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All permitter arguments are sound and prove that taklif ma la yutaq is permissible.
Correction: Sanusi points out that the second and third arguments, if taken at face value, would lead to the absurd conclusion that all obligations are taklif ma la yutaq, which is false. Thus, those arguments are not valid as presented.

Checkpoint
According to the permiters' first argument, what kind of obligation is represented by the verse 'Bring a surah like it'?
A) An obligation of actualization
B) An obligation of incapacitation
C) An obligation of information
D) An obligation of creation

Key Takeaway
The permiters advance four arguments from Quran, divine power, timing of ability, and divine foreknowledge, but Sanusi shows that the second and third are problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command from Allah that feels impossible to obey—whether it's leaving a sin or performing a difficult act of worship—do you use the difficulty as an excuse to give up? This lesson teaches that your obligation is tied to your capacity at the time of the command. What specific command have you been avoiding because you thought it was too hard? Will you take the first step today, trusting that Allah has given you the means?
?
The fourth argument shows that even someone Allah knows will disbelieve is still obligated to believe. Does this unsettle you? Have you ever treated someone as if they are hopeless, using their past or what you think they will do as a reason not to invite them to good? How will you change your attitude toward those you consider 'lost causes'?

The Dispute Is About Permissibility, Not Occurrence
Taftazani clarifies that the dispute concerns jawaz, not waqu', and critiques irrelevant arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the kalam dispute over commanding the impossible is about its permissibility (jawaz), not whether it has actually occurred (wuqu'), and why many arguments miss this distinction.

Core Idea
The dispute is about what Allah can do, not what He has done.

Taftazani cuts through the debate with a simple but crucial clarification. He tells us that the real question is whether it is permissible for Allah to command the impossible, not whether such a command has ever happened. Once we understand this, many of the arguments from both sides lose their force. This passage helps us see the precise point of disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Let Taftazani's own wording set the framework before you analyse.
Notice the key terms: جواز and وقوع.
2
Identify the main distinction
Find where Taftazani states that the majority hold the dispute is about jawaz (permissibility) not wuqu' (occurrence). Understand why this distinction matters.
3
Examine the example of the prohibitors
Look at the example Taftazani gives: the verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.' He says this only negates occurrence, not permissibility. See how he uses this to show that many arguments miss the point.
The verse is relevant only to occurrence, not the permissibility question.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Ask yourself: How does this clarification affect the overall debate? Why is it important to keep jawaz and wuqu' separate?
This helps avoid irrelevant arguments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : لأن شرط التكليف الفهم، ولا فهم للجماد حين هو جماد، ثم الجمهور على أن النزاع إنما هو في الجواز وأما الوقوع فمنفي بحكم الاستقراء ، وبشهادة مثل قوله تعالى لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها وبما ذكرنا يظهر أن كثيراً من التمسكات المذكورة في كلام الفريقين لم ترد على المتنازع أما للمانعين فمثل قوله تعالى لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها فإنه إنما ينفي الوقوع لا الجواز
We say: because the condition of obligation (taklif) is understanding, and there is no understanding for an inanimate object when it is inanimate. Then the majority (jumhur) hold that the dispute is only about permissibility (jawaz), while occurrence (wuqu') is denied by inference from induction (istiqra'), and by the testimony of His saying ‘Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.’ And based on what we have mentioned, it becomes clear that many of the proofs mentioned in the speech of both parties are not directed at the disputed point. For the prohibitors (man'ien), an example is His saying ‘Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity,’ for it only negates occurrence, not permissibility.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage clarifies the nature of the dispute. The author first states the condition of taklif: understanding. Then he asserts that the jumhur agree that the dispute is about jawaz (whether it is permissible for Allah to command what is impossible) not about whether it actually occurred. The occurrence is denied by induction and by the verse. Then he notes that many arguments from both sides miss the point: e.g., the prohibitors' use of the verse 'لا يكلف الله نفساً إلا وسعها' only negates occurrence, not permissibility, so it doesn't refute the permissibility claim.

Key Terms
تكليف
فهم
جمهور
جواز
وقوع
استقراء
مانعون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' proves that commanding the impossible is impermissible.
Correction: The verse only negates actual occurrence (wuqu'), not permissibility (jawaz). The dispute is about permissibility, so the verse is irrelevant to the main point.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of disagreement according to the author?
A) Whether Allah can command something impossible
B) Whether such a command has actually occurred
C) Whether the command requires understanding
D) Whether the verse negates permissibility

Key Takeaway
The debate over commanding the impossible is about permissibility (jawaz), not about whether it has ever happened (wuqu'), and arguments that only negate occurrence are irrelevant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might your understanding of this verse actually compel you to greater effort rather than resignation?
?
The condition for taklif is understanding (fahm). When you recite the Quran or listen to a lecture, do you merely hear it, or do you actively strive to understand? What is one command of Allah that you have not yet truly understood? What step will you take today to remove that ignorance?
?
Taftazani criticizes those who miss the point of the dispute. In your own religious discussions, do you often argue about side issues while missing the core? Think of a recent debate you had. What was the actual heart of the matter? How can you refocus your conversations on the central issue next time?

Three Arguments of the Permitters and the Critique of Two
Taftazani lists three arguments for taklif ma la yutaq and critiques the second and third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments presented by those who permit taklif ma la yutaq and how the author critiques the second and third as leading to an absurd conclusion.

Core Idea
Two of the three arguments for impossible commands backfire and would make all obligations impossible.

In this passage, Taftazani presents the case made by those who claim Allah can command what is impossible. They offer three distinct arguments. After stating them, he critically examines the second and third, showing that if accepted generally, they would undermine all religious obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three arguments
Read the passage carefully and note the three distinct arguments listed by the permitters. Each begins with 'ومنها' (and among them is).
The first argument uses scriptural examples, the second concerns divine creation of acts, and the third deals with timing of power.
2
Understand the critique
Focus on the author's closing statement: 'ولو هذان الوجهان لكان جميع التكاليف تكليف ما لا يطاق' meaning if these two aspects were true, all obligations would be obligations of the impossible. Ask why that is a problem.
The author implies that the second and third arguments, taken generally, lead to a contradiction with known obligations.
3
Reflect on the first argument
Notice that the author does not critique the first argument. Consider why it might be different from the other two.
The first argument is specific to certain verses, while the other two are general principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما للمجوزين فوجوه : منها مثل قوله تعالى أنبؤوني بأسماء هؤلاء وقوله تعالى فأتوا بسورة من مثله وذلك لأنه تكليف تعجيز، لا تكليف تحقيق ومنها أن فعل العبد بخلق الله تعالى وقدرته ، فلا يكون بقدرة العبد وهي معنى يطاق وذلك لأن معنى ما لا يطاق أن لا يكون متعلقاً بقدرة العبد، وما وقع التكليف به متعلق بقدرته ، وإن كان واقعاً بقدرة الله تعالى ومنها أن التكليف قبل الفعل والقدرة معه فلا يكون التكليف إلا بغير المقدور، وذلك لأن القدرة المعتبرة في التكليف هي سلامة الأسباب والآلات لا الاستطاعة التي لا تكون إلا مع الفعل ولو هذان الوجهان لكان جميع التكاليف تكليف ما لا يطاق، وليس كذلك
As for the permitters (mujawwizun), they have aspects: among them is His saying ‘Inform Me of the names of these’ and His saying ‘Then bring a surah like it’ for that is a command of incapacitation (taklif ta'jiz), not a command of realization. And among them is that the act of the servant is by Allah's creation and power, so it is not within the servant's power, and that is the meaning of 'what is not possible' (ma la yutaq), because the meaning of 'what is not possible' is that it is not related to the servant's power, and what has been commanded to be done is related to his power, even if it is realized by Allah's power. And among them is that the command comes before the act, and power is concurrent with the act, so the command is only for what is not within one's power, because the power considered in obligation is the safety of causes and instruments, not the ability (istita'ah) which only occurs with the act. And if these two aspects were the case, all obligations would be obligations of the impossible, and it is not so.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
The author lists three arguments from the permitters. First: the verses 'Inform Me of the names of these' and 'Bring a surah like it' are commands of incapacitation (taklif ta'jiz) to show inability, not actual obligations. Second: the servant's act is created by Allah, so it is not under the servant's power, meaning it is impossible for the servant, but the command is related to his capacity. Third: the command precedes the act, and power is with the act, so the command is for what is not already within power. The author then notes that the second and third arguments, if taken generally, would make all commands impossible, which is false. So they are not valid.

تكليف تعجيز
تكليف تحقيق

ما لا يطاق
استطاعة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that all three arguments of the permitters are equally correct and that the author accepts them all.
Correction: The author critiques the second and third arguments, showing they lead to an absurd conclusion. Only the first argument remains unchallenged in this passage.

Checkpoint

A) First and second
B) Second and third
C) First and third
D) All three

Key Takeaway
The permitters' second and third arguments, if applied universally, would render all obligations impossible, so they are not valid.

The Actual Occurrence of Commanding the Impossible
Taftazani reports the actual occurrence of commanding the impossible, with examples and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that commanding the impossible in itself has actually occurred in Islamic law, using the example of Abu Jahl, and the scholarly response to it.

Core Idea
Can Allah command a contradiction? Some scholars say yes—and give an example.

This passage presents a bold claim: that it is not only theoretically possible for Allah to command something impossible in itself, but that it has actually happened. The case of Abu Jahl is cited as evidence. However, a counter‑interpretation shows that the commanded act was possible in essence, even if prevented by prior knowledge. Let us examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation. Notice that the passage contains a claim, two examples, and a response.
The Arabic uses technical terms like ممتنع لذاته and جمع النقيضين.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that according to many investigators, obligating what is impossible in itself is permissible and actually occurred. This is the central thesis.
The phrase 'جائز بل واقع' indicates both permissibility and actual occurrence.
3
Analyze the Abu Jahl example
Imam al-Haramayn argues: Abu Jahl was commanded to believe the Prophet's report, which included the report that he would not believe. So he was commanded to believe that he does not believe—a contradiction.
This is meant to show that a command to combine contradictories has occurred.
4
Understand the counter‑response
Some scholars respond that the command was not to combine contradictories, but to acquire faith—something possible in itself, even if prevented by prior knowledge.
This distinguishes between impossibility in essence and impossibility due to external factors.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by noting that the commanded act is permissible and actual by agreement, but the disagreement is about its nature.
The author reports the debate without necessarily endorsing one side.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما على تقرير كثير من المحققين، فيدل على أن التكليف بالممتنع لذاته كجمع النقيضين جائز بل واقع . التقرير قال إمام الحرمين في الإرشاد . فإن قيل : ما جوزتموه عقلاً من تكليف المحال، هل اتفق وقوعه شرعاً .؟ - قلنا : قال شيخنا ذلك واقع شرعاً فإن الرب تعالى أمر أبا جهل بأن يصدقه ويؤمن به في جميع ما يخبر عنه ، ومما أخبر عنه أنه لا يؤمن فقد أمره أن يصدقه بأنه لا يصدقه، وذلك جمع بين النقيضين . وكذا ذكر الإمام الرازي في المطالب العالية وقال أيضاً، أن الأمر بتحصيل الإيمان مع حصول العلم بعدم الإيمان أمر يجمع الوجود والعدم ، لأن وجود الإيمان يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم بعدم الإيمان ضرورة أن العلم يقتضي المطابقة ، وذلك بحصول عدم الإيمان وأجاب بعضهم بأن ما ذكر لا يدل على أن المكلف به هو الجمع، بل تحصيل الإيمان، وهو ممكن في نفسه مقدور للعبد بحسب أصله، وإن امتنع لسابق علم أو إخبار للرسول بأنه لا يؤمن فيكون مما هو جائز بل واقع بالاتفاق
And according to the formulation of many investigators, it indicates that the obligation of what is impossible in itself, like combining two contradictories, is permissible and indeed actual. The formulation: Imam al-Haramayn said in al-Irshad: 'If it is said: That which you have deemed rationally permissible of obligating the impossible, has it occurred in the Law? We say: Our teacher said it is actual in the Law, for the Lord commanded Abu Jahl to believe him and have faith in all that he reports, and among what he reported is that he will not believe, so He commanded him to believe that he does not believe, and that is combining two contradictories.' Similarly, Imam al-Razi mentioned in al-Matalib al-Aliya and also said that the command to acquire faith along with the occurrence of knowledge of the absence of faith is a command that combines existence and non-existence, because the existence of faith is impossible to occur with knowledge of the absence of faith necessarily because knowledge requires correspondence, and that correspondence is achieved by the occurrence of the absence of faith. And some of them answered that what was mentioned does not prove that what is commanded is the combination; rather, it is the acquisition of faith, which is possible in itself and within the servant's power according to its origin, even if it is prevented by prior knowledge or the Prophet's report that he will not believe, so it is something permissible and indeed actual by agreement.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents a stronger claim: that commanding what is impossible in itself (like combining two contradictories) is not only permissible but actually occurred. The example from Imam al-Haramayn: Abu Jahl was commanded to believe in the Prophet's report, which includes the report that he will not believe, so he is commanded to believe that he does not believe, which is a contradiction. Imam al-Razi gives a similar argument. However, some scholars respond that the command is actually for acquiring faith, which is possible in itself, even if impossible due to external factors like prior knowledge. So the act is possible in itself, not impossible by essence. The author does not necessarily endorse this; he is reporting.

Key Terms
ممتنع لذاته
جمع النقيضين
إمام الحرمين
تكليف المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The example of Abu Jahl proves that Allah commands what is impossible in itself (like combining contradictories) without qualification.
Correction: Some scholars interpret the command as being for acquiring faith, which is possible in itself, even if its occurrence is prevented by prior knowledge. Thus, the impossibility is not in the essence of the commanded act but due to external factors.

Checkpoint
What is the main claim attributed to many investigators in this passage?
A) Obligating what is impossible in itself is rationally impossible.
B) Obligating what is impossible in itself is permissible and has actually occurred.
C) Obligating what is impossible in itself is only theoretical, never actual.
D) Obligating what is impossible in itself is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The command to Abu Jahl is interpreted by some as a real example of commanding a contradiction, while others argue it was a command to a possible act prevented by external factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself that a command of Allah is impossible for you to fulfill, using circumstances or your own weakness as an excuse? Reflect on how the Abu Jahl example shows that the impossibility is often in your own determination, not in the command itself.
?
In what specific area of your life today are you choosing to disobey Allah while telling yourself 'I cannot obey'? Would you still make that claim if you knew that the command itself is possible, and your inability is only due to your own unwillingness?
?
The scholars argued that the command to Abu Jahl was for a possible act, even if prevented by prior knowledge. How does this change your understanding of your accountability? If you disobey, can you truly say 'I had no choice' when the act was within your power at its origin?

Reward Without Prior Merit is Possible
Rejects Mu'tazilite premise and distinguishes merit from fitness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that reward without prior merit is impossible, by distinguishing between merit and fitness and arguing on two premises.

Core Idea
Reward without merit is possible because it is pure generosity, not injustice.

The Mu'tazilites argued that it is rationally evil for God to reward someone who has not earned it through obedience. The author gives the final answer by first clarifying the Ash'ari position that nothing is evil for God, and then even accepting rational good and evil, showing that conferring benefit without harm is pure generosity and wisdom. The error lies in confusing merit from actions with the fitness of the gift to the recipient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins with 'First, we do not accept...' — this is the final answer (jawab).
2
Identify the two levels of argument
The author argues on two levels: (1) on the Ash'ari view that nothing is evil for God, and (2) on the Mu'tazilite view of rational good and evil. See how he uses 'اما على... واما على...'.
This shows the author is accommodating the opponent's premise.
3
Understand the distinction
The author says the Mu'tazilites failed to distinguish between merit (istihqāq) from works and fitness (liyāqa) of the gift to the recipient. Grasp this distinction.
This is the key refutation.
4
Consider the conclusion
Reflect on how the author concludes that eternal joy is appropriate even without obligation, so how could it be evil?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب: أولاً بأنا لا نسلم أنه لا يحسن الثواب والتعظيم بدون الاستحقاق. أما على أنه لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء فظاهر، وأما على التنزل والقول بالقبح العقلي فلأن إفادة منفعة الغير من غير ضرر للمفيد، ولا لغيره محض الكرم والحكمة، وغلطهم إما نشأ من عدم التفرقة بين الاستحقاق الحاصل بالأعمال، وبين كون المفاد والمنعم به لائقاً بحال المنعم عليه فإن إفادة ما لا ينبغي كتعظيم الصبيان والبهائم لا يعود جوداً ولا يستحسن عقلاً، فتوهموا أن إيصال النعيم إلى غير من عمل الصالحات من هذا القبيل . ولاخفاء في أن هذا إما هو على تقدير التكليف ، وإما على تقدير عدمه وكون الإنسان غير مكلف بأمر ولا نهي . فكيف يتصور قبح إفاضة سرور دائم عليه من غير الحوق ضرر بالغير.
And it is answered: First, that we do not accept that reward and exaltation are not good without merit. As for the view that nothing is evil for Allah, it is obvious. As for descending to the view of rational evil, then conferring benefit upon another without harm to the conferrer or anyone else is pure generosity and wisdom. Their error arose from failing to distinguish between merit obtained through actions and the fitness of the bestowed gift to the state of the recipient. For giving what is not appropriate, like exalting children and animals, is not generosity and is not rationally good. So they imagined that conveying bliss to one who did not do righteous deeds is of this kind. But there is no hiddenness that this is either on the assumption of obligation (taklif) or on the assumption of its absence and that humans are not obligated with any command or prohibition. So how can one imagine evil in bestowing eternal joy upon him without causing harm to others?
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the final answer (jawab) to the Mu'tazilite position. The author first states that he does not accept the premise that reward and exaltation are not good without prior merit. He then argues on two levels: (1) If we adopt the Ash'ari view that nothing is evil for God (no rational good or evil), the point is obvious—God may reward without merit. (2) Even if we accept rational good and evil (as the Mu'tazilites do), conferring benefit without harm to the giver or anyone else is pure generosity (karm) and wisdom (hikma), not injustice. The Mu'tazilites' error stems from confusing two things: merit obtained through works (istihqāq) and the fitness (liyāqa) of the gift to the recipient. Giving something inappropriate (like exalting children or animals) is not generous, but giving eternal bliss to a human being—a form of existence capable of receiving it—is appropriate whether or not they performed good deeds. This holds true whether we assume the existence of obligation (taklif) or not. Therefore, there is no rational evil in God bestowing eternal joy without prior works. Thus the Mu'tazilite argument collapses.

Key Terms
الكرم
الحكمة
الاستحقاق
اللياقة
التفرقة
القبح العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author denies the importance of works entirely; one might think he is saying works do not matter at all.
Correction: The author does not deny the importance of works; he distinguishes between merit (which comes from works) and fitness (which is about appropriateness). Reward without merit is possible, but that does not negate the value of works.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that the author says the Mu'tazilites failed to make?
A) Between reward and punishment.
B) Between merit obtained through actions and the fitness of the gift to the recipient.
C) Between God's essence and attributes.
D) Between this world and the hereafter.

Key Takeaway
Reward without prior merit is not evil because it is pure generosity and wisdom, and the Mu'tazilite error is confusing merit from works with the fitness of the gift to the recipient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt entitled to reward or recognition because of your efforts, forgetting that all goodness is from Allah's generosity? How does this lesson challenge that sense of entitlement?
?
When you see others receiving blessings – health, wealth, or even guidance – despite not 'deserving' them in your eyes, does your heart feel a sting of injustice? What does this teach you about your own understanding of Allah's wisdom?
?
If you truly believed that Allah can give His grace to anyone without regard to their past deeds, how would that change the way you view the people you consider 'unworthy' of His mercy? Are you willing to accept that His generosity is unconstrained by your sense of fairness?

Refutation of the Objection that Reward without Desert is Not Good
Author refutes the claim that reward without desert is not good.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that reward without prior desert is not good, by distinguishing between desert acquired by actions and the suitability of the gift.

Core Idea
Reward without desert is not evil because suitability, not earned merit, determines goodness.

The objection claims that rewarding someone without prior meritorious deeds is not good. The author answers by first denying that anything is evil from Allah, then even accepting rational evil, benefiting another without harm is pure generosity and wisdom. The key move is distinguishing between desert earned by actions and the fittingness of the gift to the recipient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author structures the refutation in two steps.
The passage is dense with theological nuance.
2
Identify the two prongs of the answer
The author gives two responses: first, from the Ash'ari perspective that nothing is evil from God; second, even accepting rational evil, benefiting others without harm is pure generosity and wisdom.
The second prong is a concession (tanazzul).
3
Understand the key distinction
The author says the error is confusing desert (istiḥqāq) earned by actions with suitability (liyāqa) of the gift. The gift must be fitting, not necessarily earned.
Example: honoring a child is not generous because it is inappropriate.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author asks: how can granting permanent pleasure to a human without harming others be conceived as evil? This applies whether or not the person was obligated.
This reinforces that reward without desert is not inherently evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب: أولاً بأنا لا نسلم أنه لا يحسن الثواب والتعظيم بدون الاستحقاق. أما على أنه لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء فظاهر، وأما على التنزل والقول بالقبح العقلي فلأن إفادة منفعة الغير من غير ضرر للمفيد، ولا لغيره محض الكرم والحكمة، وغلطهم إما نشأ من عدم التفرقة بين الاستحقاق الحاصل بالأعمال، وبين كون المفاد والمنعم به لائقاً بحال المنعم عليه فإن إفادة ما لا ينبغي كتعظيم الصبيان والبهائم لا يعود جوداً ولا يستحسن عقلاً، فتوهموا أن إيصال النعيم إلى غير من عمل الصالحات من هذا القبيل. ولاخفاء في أن هذا إما هو على تقدير التكليف، وإما على تقدير عدمه وكون الإنسان غير مكلف بأمر ولا نهي. فكيف يتصور قبح إفاضة سرور دائم عليه من غير الحوق ضرر بالغير.
And it was answered: First, we do not concede that reward and honor are not good without desert. As for the position that nothing is evil from Allah, it is apparent; and as for descending and accepting rational evil, then benefiting others without harm to the benefactor or anyone else is pure generosity and wisdom. Their error arose from not distinguishing between desert (istiḥqāq) acquired by actions and the state of that which is given and bestowed being fitting (lāʾiq) to the recipient; for giving what is not fitting, like honoring children and animals, is not considered generosity nor is it rationally good. So they imagined that granting bliss to one who has not done righteous deeds is of this category. And there is no hidden that this is either on the assumption of obligation (taklīf) or on the assumption of its absence and man not being obligated by command or prohibition. So how can one conceive the evil of bestowing permanent pleasure upon him without inflicting harm on others?
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage contains the answer to the first objection. The objector claimed that reward and honor without prior desert (istiḥqāq) are not good. The response is twofold: (1) From the Ashʿarī perspective, nothing is evil from Allah (qabīḥ) at all, so this objection is irrelevant. (2) Even accepting the rationalist position of good and evil (al-qabḥ al-ʿaqlī), the act of benefiting another without harming anyone is pure generosity (karam) and wisdom (ḥikma). The objector's confusion stems from conflating two distinct concepts: desert earned by works (istiḥqāq ḥāṣil bil-aʿmāl) and the suitability (al-liyāqa) of the bestowed thing to the recipient. The latter is about dignity, not desert. For example, honoring a child or animal is not generous because it is inappropriate. But granting eternal bliss to a human being, even without prior works, is not inappropriate. This is true whether or not the person was obligated (mukallaf). Thus, it is not rationally evil to grant bliss without prior desert.

Key Terms
استحقاق
ثواب
تعظيم
قبح عقلي
كرم
حكمة
لائق
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward must be earned by good deeds to be good or just.
Correction: The goodness of a reward depends on its suitability to the recipient, not on prior desert. Even without deeds, granting bliss to a human is not inappropriate.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, what is the real error of those who deny the goodness of reward without desert?
A) They ignore that God can do anything.
B) They confuse desert by works with suitability of the gift.
C) They deny the existence of rational good and evil.
D) They think that obligation is necessary for reward.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that rewarding without desert is not evil because suitability, not earned merit, determines the goodness of a gift.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that your good deeds earn you a right to Allah’s favor, or does your heart truly rest on His pure generosity, even when your deeds are lacking? What would change if you lived as if reward depends solely on suitability, not your merit?
?
When you consider someone who is not religious but receives worldly blessings, do you feel a sense of injustice? How does this lesson’s distinction between desert and suitability challenge that feeling? What would it cost you to let go of that resentment?
?
If Allah’s generosity is not constrained by human notions of desert, are you willing to extend that same generosity to others—especially those you deem undeserving? What is holding you back from forgiving or helping someone without them meriting it?

Mu'tazila: Lutf, Tawfiq, and 'Isma
Mu'tazila define lutf, divide it into muhassil and muqarrib, and assign tawfiq and 'isma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila definition of divine grace (lutf), its two types (muhassil and muqarrib), and how tawfiq and 'isma relate to it.

Core Idea
Lutf is divine grace that aids free choice without compulsion.

The Mu'tazila developed a nuanced theory of divine grace to reconcile divine omnipotence with human free will. In this passage, they define lutf (grace) and its subtypes, showing how God assists believers toward obedience while preserving their moral responsibility. This concept also explains their interpretation of certain Quranic verses about divine will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Get a sense of the Mu'tazila's technical vocabulary.
Notice the terms muhassil and muqarrib.
2
Identify the two types of lutf
The passage divides lutf into muhassil (effecting) and muqarrib (approximating). Note how each is defined and what outcomes they produce.
Muhassil is further associated with tawfiq and 'isma.
3
Understand tawfiq and 'isma
Tawfiq is the grace that leads to performing obligations, while 'isma is grace that prevents sin. Recognize that both apply only to the muhassil type.
Also note the alternative definitions involving God's knowledge.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
The passage ends by noting that lutf varies per person, leading to their interpretation of Q. 32:13 as a will of compulsion. Consider how this preserves free will.
This shows the Mu'tazila's commitment to human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة اللطف ما يختار المكلف عنده الطاعة تركاً أو إتياناً أو يقرب منهما مع تمكنه في الحالين، ويسميان المحصل والمقرب، ويختص المحصل للواجب باسم التوفيق، وترك القبيح باسم العصمة، وقيل: التوفيق : خلق لطف يعلم الله أن العبد يطيع عنده والخذلان منع اللطف والعصمة لطف لا داعي معه إلى ترك الطاعة ولا إلى ارتكاب المعصية مع القدرة عليهما . قالوا : واللطف يختلف باختلاف المكلفين، وليس في معلومه ما هو لطف في حق الكل ومن ههنا حملوا المشيئة في مثل قوله تعالى ولو شئنا لآتينا كل نفس هداها ) (۲) على مشيئة قسر وإلجاء) .
And the Mu'tazila said: Grace (لطف) is that by which the responsible agent (مكلف) chooses obedience, whether abandoning or performing, or which brings him close to both, while he is capable in both states. And they are called the effecting (محصل) and the approximating (مقرب). And the effecting is specifically called success (توفيق) for obligations, and abandoning evil is called protection (عصمة). And it was said: Success is the creation of grace by which Allah knows that the servant will obey; abandonment (خذلان) is the prevention of grace; and protection is a grace with which there is no motive to abandon obedience or to commit sin while having the ability to both. They said: And grace varies according to the responsible agents, and there is not in His knowledge what is grace for everyone, and from this they interpreted the will in such as His statement 'And if We willed, We would have given every soul its guidance' as a will of compulsion and coercion.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite theory of divine grace (lutf). They define lutf as something that leads the morally responsible person to choose obedience, either by making him perform an act or abandon it, or bringing him close to that state, while he retains ability in both situations. They classify lutf into two types: muhassil (effecting) which actually brings about the action, and muqarrib (approximating) which brings one near to it. The muhassil specifically is called tawfiq when it leads to performing obligations, and 'isma when it leads to avoiding evil. There is also a definition of tawfiq as the creation of a grace by which Allah knows the servant will obey, and khidhlan as the withholding of that grace. 'Isma is defined as a grace without any motive to abandon obedience or commit sin, despite having the ability. They note that lutf varies per individual and that not everything is lutf for everyone, which leads them to interpret Q. 32:13 about God's will as referring to a will of compulsion, not guidance.

Key Terms
لطف
محصل
مقرب
توفيق
عصمة
خذلان
مشيئة قسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That lutf (divine grace) compels obedience, removing free will.
Correction: The Mu'tazila emphasize that lutf comes with the agent being capable (تمكنه في الحالين), thus preserving free choice. Lutf facilitates but does not force action.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the difference between lutf muqarrib and lutf muhassil?
A) Muqarrib brings one close to the act while muhassil achieves it.
B) Muqarrib is for obligatory acts and muhassil for prohibitions.
C) Muqarrib is for obedience and muhassil for avoiding sin.
D) Muqarrib is general and muhassil is specific.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view lutf as divine aid that facilitates but does not compel obedience, reflecting their emphasis on human freedom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself able to obey Allah, do you attribute that to your own strength or to Allah's grace (lutf)? How does the Mu'tazilite definition of lutf as that which facilitates free choice challenge the way you think about your own obedience? Reflect on one recent act of worship: was your ability to perform it a result of your own will or a specific grace from Allah? Commit to thanking Allah specifically for that grace.
?
The Mu'tazila say that lutf varies per person and that Allah knows exactly what grace each soul needs. Have you ever considered that the trials and aids you experience are uniquely tailored to you? Stop and ask yourself: What grace am I receiving right now that I might be overlooking? How would my day change if I saw every small ease as a personalized lutf from Allah?
?
The definition of 'isma here is a grace with no motive to sin. You are not infallible, but can you think of times when you were protected from a sin you were capable of committing? Do you acknowledge that as a specific protection from Allah, or do you take it for granted? Today, recall one such moment and thank Allah for that 'isma, no matter how small.
